Chapter 1: Rehearsal Pulse

Kestrel Gate made a sound before it woke.

It was not a sound any unassisted ear could hear. The field vanes stood thirty kilometers beyond Halcyon Ring, black ribs against the white boil of Kestrel's star, and vacuum kept its old silence around them. But the station translated stress into audible warnings for the people who had to work near danger. The first tone came through Lena Ors's wrist console as a pressure-deep hum, the kind of note that made teeth remember childhood fevers and old hulls. Then came the harmonic ladder: five rising tones, a pause, and a sixth that arrived flat.

Lena froze with one gloved hand inside a maintenance cabinet and the other braced against the tug's vibration frame.

"Say again, Kestrel Control," called Jori Pell from the pilot cradle. His voice cracked on the first word, not from fear yet but from the effort of sounding older than seventeen. "Tug Larkspur is receiving vane precession alarm on local. Confirm training artifact?"

The console in Lena's sleeve showed green across the formal board. Power buffers stable. Magnetic wells within rehearsal tolerance. Transit envelope cold. No live ship in the throat. The gate was not open. It was only supposed to be thinking about opening, running a rehearsal pulse through fields that had slept for six years while committees, insurers, and grieving governments decided whether Kestrel deserved to matter again.

The sixth tone stayed flat.

Lena pulled her hand out of the cabinet. A ribbon of sealant followed her glove and snapped back into the panel with a wet little recoil. "Control," she said, overriding Jori's channel before the boy could apologize for existing. "This is Ors on Larkspur. I have a phase disagreement on Vane Three local instrumentation. It is not on your board."

The delay was less than a second, purely procedural, but it felt long enough for the star to move.

"Larkspur, Control." Sivek Hale's voice carried the dry calm that had once steadied entire convoys. Age had roughened it. Command had not. "Your board is secondary telemetry. We show green."

"Secondary telemetry is bolted to the thing making the complaint."

Jori twisted in the cradle. The tug was a utility beetle, all engine block and clamp arms, certified for two awake crew and four uncomfortable passengers. There was nowhere for his knees to go except into the panel beneath him. He had freckles across the bridge of his nose and a half-moon bruise under one eye from some workshop accident he had considered too ordinary to report.

"Do we back off?" he asked on internal.

The right answer, by manual, was no. A rehearsal pulse required close tugs to maintain position unless Control ordered retreat, because drifting metal near a field vane could become debris, and debris near gate infrastructure became politics. Lena knew the manual. She had written two of its postwar cautions while lawyers watched her hands.

On the external channel Sivek said, "Ors, hold station. We are checking your feed."

The first tremor passed through Larkspur's frame.

Jori flinched. The tug's attitude jets answered with a hard chatter. Lena pushed off from the cabinet and caught the back of the pilot cradle before her boots had time to decide which surface was down. Out the forward blister, Vane Three cut across the star like a needle laid over an eye. It should have looked still at this range. It did not. The vane's distal edge shimmered with waste heat, a thin fever line wavering where vacuum should have been clean.

"Jori," Lena said, "give me manual deadband two degrees wide, nose out."

"Control told us to hold."

"Control sees green."

His hands moved. Good hands, she noticed despite everything. Not elegant, not yet disciplined, but quick and honest. The tug's nose yawed away from the vane by the width of a coin held at arm's length. The harness webbing across Lena's chest tugged as acceleration invented a direction.

The flat tone split into two tones beating against each other.

Every screen went amber.

Control erupted. Three voices spoke over one another, then vanished as Sivek cut them. "All service craft maintain assigned positions. Rehearsal pulse is inside containment. Repeat, maintain assigned positions."

Lena tasted metal. She had not tasted metal in a cockpit since the war, when blood under acceleration had found the back of her throat as easily as memory. She checked the local feed again and felt the old part of her mind unfold without permission: vectors, time-to-threshold, energy that had no ideology and no mercy.

Vane Three's phase was not drifting. It was being pulled, then released, then pulled again in a pattern too regular for corrosion. The central board showed green because the error was being corrected before each official sample. Local instrumentation saw the corrections. Control saw the lie between them.

"Sivek," she said, forgetting title and channel discipline. "Abort the rehearsal."

"We are not at abort threshold."

"Your threshold is being groomed."

Another pause. Short. Fatal.

The rehearsal pulse arrived as light.

The star ahead of them wrinkled. There was no better word for it. Kestrel's bright disk folded into bands, not dimmer but wrong, as if something immense had pressed a transparent thumb against the universe. The gate vanes accepted power from the buffer ring and shaped it into a question spacetime had been trained to answer. In a real opening, the answer would have been a corridor: brutal, narrow, expensive, and precious. Today there should have been no corridor, only the mathematical rehearsal of one.

Instead, a seam of black opened along Vane Three's inner edge.

Jori whispered, "That isn't on the sim."

"Burn aft," Lena said. "Full."

"Larkspur, hold position," Control ordered.

Lena reached past Jori and struck the burn key herself.

The tug kicked. The harness bit into her collarbone. A clamp arm, still extended toward the service socket, screamed through the hull as torque tried to leave it behind. The forward blister filled with warning glyphs. Something outside flashed white and became pieces.

For one suspended instant the black seam widened. Lena saw stars through it, but not the stars that belonged beyond Kestrel. These were crowded, blue-white, bent into a crescent by a gravity that should not have been there. Then the seam snapped shut and the universe slapped Larkspur sideways.

The tug spun.

Jori's shoulder hit the cradle frame with a sound Lena felt in her own bones. A loose tool clipped the ceiling, ricocheted, and vanished into the aft compartment. The attitude jets fired in panicked bursts as the tug's simple systems tried to turn motion into categories they knew how to correct.

"Hands off," Lena said.

Jori did not answer. His right hand floated free, fingers open. Blood globed from his temple in bright beads.

Lena locked her boots, leaned across him, and killed the automatic correction. The spin became honest. Nausea rose hard and immediate. The star, the vane, the dark of Kestrel's yard, the white curve of Halcyon Ring, and the star again: all of it strobed through the blister in a rhythm that would make most pilots chase it and die.

She counted instead.

One: the tug had angular velocity around two axes and a leak alarm from the damaged clamp trunk.

Two: the main drive was alive but misaligned.

Three: the gate vane was shedding debris in a fan that looked slow only because it was large.

Four: Jori was breathing.

Five: Control was shouting, and none of the words mattered.

She waited until the star crossed the blister's left edge, fired the dorsal jets for half a second, waited through darkness, fired port aft, waited through Halcyon, fired main for a quarter pulse that made the tug groan like an animal. The spin flattened into a roll. She let it roll. Roll she could use.

"Control," she said. Her voice sounded borrowed. "Larkspur is damaged. One injured. We are clearing debris fan on manual."

"Ors, you were ordered to hold position."

There it was. Not are you alive, not what did you see, not how many others. The first institutional reflex: preserve the record in the shape least likely to bleed upward.

Lena laughed once. It hurt. "Put that in the inquiry."

She reached for the medical tab on Jori's harness. The patch foamed against his neck and read his blood through the skin. Amber. Concussion likely, clavicle fracture, pressure response acceptable. The boy's eyelids fluttered.

"Did I ruin it?" he murmured.

"No."

"My aunt said don't touch pilot hands."

"Your aunt is right about most things."

"Am I fired?"

"You are concussed."

"That's not a no."

The tug cleared the debris fan by forty meters. A flake of vane shielding struck the blister and left a silver flower in the transparent alloy. Lena did not blink until the crack stopped growing.

Halcyon Ring filled the forward view as she stabilized. It was not beautiful in the way routeholder brochures still rendered it, all clean arcs and celebratory windows. Six years of scarcity had made the ring practical. External tanks clustered where promenade gardens had been planned. Emergency radiator cloths patched one quadrant in mismatched grays. Cargo spines extended like splints. The whole habitat hung beside the gate because history had promised it traffic, and history had then gone to war.

Beyond the ring, Kestrel Gate dimmed. The vanes bled heat into vacuum. Service lights flickered along their length, tiny and frantic.

Control's channel returned stripped of background voices. Sivek again. "Larkspur, proceed to Dock Twelve medical priority. Do not discuss event telemetry on open channels."

Lena looked at the local recording buffer. It had copied everything to the tug's sealed maintenance core, unless the pulse had fried more than the status board admitted.

"Acknowledged," she said.

Jori made a small sound between a laugh and a groan. "You always sound like that?"

"Like what?"

"Like the door is already locked and you're deciding who gets blamed."

Lena turned the tug toward Halcyon. The cracked blister caught Kestrel's light and split it into three pale ghosts across her gloves.

"No," she said. "I used to sound worse."

They crossed the approach lane under emergency beacon. Traffic parted reluctantly: repair drones, inspection sleds, a fuel tender with its paint burned to primer along one flank, two passenger shuttles crowded with faces at the ports. Everyone would have felt the rehearsal pulse through station translation. Everyone would know by now that the gate had not simply failed to wake. It had opened one eye.

On the private maintenance band, someone began asking for Jori by name. A woman's voice, older, trying to remain official and failing. "Larkspur, this is Scrubber Works Three. Confirm apprentice Pell is conscious. Confirm. Larkspur, answer me."

Jori's mouth tightened.

Lena switched channels. "Scrubber Works Three, Ors on Larkspur. Pell is injured and conscious. Medical patch shows amber, not red. We are six minutes from Dock Twelve."

The woman exhaled so hard the channel clipped. "You keep him that way."

"That is the plan."

"Plans are what people say before they spend us."

The channel cut.

Jori kept his eyes closed. "Aunt Tamsa."

"I guessed."

"She hates pilots."

"She has reasons."

"Do you?"

For a moment Lena saw another ship, another board, another official green that had not meant safety. She saw the face of a captain who had trusted her and the list of names that had followed her through six years of civil rooms and hostile corridors. She saw Sivek younger by a decade, telling the inquiry that her decision had been within emergency doctrine, which was not the same as saying she had been right.

"Yes," she said.

Jori opened one eye. "Then why are you one?"

The docking clamps caught Larkspur before she could answer.

Dock Twelve received them with light too bright for a damaged tug. Medics came through the hatch first, two humans and a narrow service frame with four folded arms. Lena lifted her hands away from the controls and let them work. Jori tried to apologize to the medics for bleeding. One of them told him to save his manners for someone with time.

When they cut his harness, Lena saw the bruise spreading under his collar. Her own shoulder throbbed in sympathy, but she refused the second medic's scanner until Jori was out of the cradle and sealed in a transport sling.

Tamsa Reet arrived as the sling cleared the hatch.

She was shorter than Lena expected from the voice, built like a person who had spent her life persuading machines through leverage rather than permissions. Her gray coverall bore the green band of Halcyon life-support workers and the black thumbprint stains of someone who had come straight from a membrane bay. She put one hand on Jori's forehead, briefly, lightly, as if checking that he belonged to the same world as before.

"Aunt," he said.

"Quiet."

"I didn't touch the wrong thing."

"I said quiet."

Only after the medics moved him did she turn to Lena. "You were in command?"

"No."

"Don't polish it. Were you flying when he got hurt?"

"I took control after the pulse."

Tamsa looked past her into Larkspur's cockpit. The cracked blister. The scorched panel. The stain where Jori's blood had drifted and struck the underside of the console. Her expression did not change, but something in it settled deeper.

"They said maintenance error on the first public bulletin."

Lena glanced toward the dock status wall. There it was already, phrased in the velvet language of blame prepared before evidence: Preliminary reports indicate a service craft anomaly during scheduled rehearsal pulse. No gate-integrity threat confirmed. Maintenance compliance review pending.

"Of course they did," Lena said.

Tamsa's gaze sharpened. "Was it?"

A Compact security officer stepped through the dock hatch before Lena could answer. Two more followed. They wore soft gray pressure jackets and carried no visible weapons, which meant they expected cooperation or had better weapons elsewhere. The lead officer nodded to Tamsa as if she were furniture of recognized civic value and to Lena as if she were a file reopened against his wishes.

"Lena Ors," he said. "Scheduling Officer Hale requests your immediate presence in Control."

Tamsa did not move aside. "My nephew's blood is still on that seat."

"Medical has him."

"That was not my point."

The officer gave the smallest sigh. "The gate event is under Compact authority."

Lena unsealed her gloves finger by finger. Her hands shook only when the gloves hid them. "Tell Sivek I need ten minutes to secure the tug's local recorder."

"The recorder is evidence."

"That is why I said secure."

"We will handle evidence."

Lena looked at the officer, then at the status wall, then at Tamsa Reet. The worker's face held fury, fear, and a question she hated needing to ask a pilot.

Was it us?

Lena thought of the flat sixth tone. The tug's local board. The black seam. The way the official thresholds had been groomed clean between samples.

She could say nothing and be wise. She could go to Control, let Sivek place the event inside the old institutional grammar, and perhaps the convoy would still come. Halcyon would breathe another season. Her name would remain what it was: a warning, not a participant. No one would have to decide whether to trust her again.

The cracked blister ticked as it cooled.

"It was not maintenance error," Lena said.

The security officer's jaw tightened.

Tamsa Reet did not look grateful. Gratitude would have been too simple and much too soon.

"Then what was it?" she asked.

Lena removed the local recorder from its slot, felt its warm sealed weight in her palm, and knew that whatever peace she had built out of obscurity had just ended.

"Something that wanted Control to see green."

Chapter 2: The Shape of a Lie

Kestrel Control had been designed when people still trusted curves.

The room occupied the forward crown of Halcyon Ring's gateward bulge, a hemisphere of layered consoles looking out through armored panes at the six field vanes and the power-buffer arc behind them. Before the war, visiting ministers had stood at the central rail and admired the symbolism: human judgment nested inside transparent engineering, the gate visible to anyone whose decision might open it. After the war, half the panes were covered in radiation shutters that had never been retracted. The remaining view came in strips.

Lena had not entered the room for six years.

Nobody announced that fact. They did not need to. Conversations thinned as she crossed from the lift to the inquiry table. Operators looked at her and away with the practiced speed of people who had read about a disaster but not the testimony. A junior scheduler whispered something and received an elbow from the woman beside him. Near the central rail, Sivek Hale stood with his hands clasped behind his back, facing the wounded gate as if it could be reasoned with by posture.

He turned before she reached him.

"You are injured," he said.

"Not enough."

"That is not an answer."

"Then ask medical."

For a moment, age broke through the official arrangement of his face. Sivek had taught Lena how to read transit shear from peripheral light, how to distrust elegant numbers, how to sleep in twenty-minute pieces before a convoy approach. He had also sat three chairs away from her at the inquiry while committee counsel transformed the worst hour of her life into a diagram.

"I am glad you survived," he said quietly.

She hated him for saying it where others could hear.

"Jori Pell may have a fractured clavicle and a concussion," she replied. "The tug is salvageable. Vane Three is not green."

The official mask returned. "Vane Three is in automatic safing. We do not yet know why local and central telemetry diverged."

"Yes, we do."

Sivek looked at the sealed recorder in her hand. "You removed evidence from a damaged service craft."

"I preserved evidence from a damaged service craft before your officers could normalize it into a maintenance compliance review."

A few operators pretended not to listen more intensely.

Sivek lowered his voice. "Lena."

"No."

That stopped him. Not because no one refused him. People refused Sivek Hale constantly, in the small permitted ways by which bureaucracies breathed. They appealed, delayed, qualified, requested clarification, invoked jurisdiction. Lena's refusal had none of that lace around it.

"No private correction," she said. "No asking me to remember what is at stake. No telling me the convoy will turn back if Halcyon panics. I know what is at stake. That is why I am still holding the recorder."

His eyes moved over her face, searching perhaps for the younger pilot who had once mistaken his approval for a law of physics. "The convoy cannot turn back."

The words landed harder than she expected.

"Last packet said they were still outside burn commitment."

"Last public packet," Sivek said. "We received a compressed schedule update during the rehearsal handshake. Captain Dax advanced by thirty-one hours to avoid debris from the Varda side."

Lena glanced toward the gate. "And you rehearsed on the old timing?"

"We rehearsed on a window that still had margin."

"Had."

"Yes."

For the first time since Dock Twelve, fear found a clean path through her anger. A convoy did not approach a meridian gate like an aircraft approaching a runway. It fell into mathematics arranged weeks in advance. Its ships burned along corridors shaped by fuel, mass, legal priority, and the expectation that the gate would be available within a tolerable envelope. Too early and they wasted reaction mass correcting. Too late and they arrived with holds full of medicine, membranes, and passengers who might as well be poems sent to the dead.

"How many ships?" she asked.

"Nine primary, fourteen auxiliary, three medical packets riding escort."

"Population?"

"Crew and passengers, eleven thousand two hundred registered. Unregistered likely higher."

Halcyon Ring had two hundred thousand residents and another thirty thousand temporary workers living in cargo districts that had stopped being temporary years ago. Eleven thousand sounded small only to people who had learned to sin by column.

Sivek gestured toward the inquiry table. "That is why you will give us the recorder and your statement in sequence. We will determine what can be released without compromising order."

"Order was compromised when Vane Three opened a rehearsal seam."

"The seam is not confirmed."

Lena stared at him.

He did not look away. That was something. Cowards glanced aside when they lied. Sivek held position and let the lie wound both of them.

"You saw it," she said.

"I saw an optical artifact during a field instability."

"Say that again after you read the tug's local."

"I intend to."

"With whom in the room?"

He inhaled once through his nose. "Compact safety, House Ceyr technical liaison, Halcyon administration, and Magistrate Om if she arrives before second bell."

"House Ceyr has no authority over evidence."

"House Ceyr owns forty percent of the buffer debt and underwrites the reopening liability."

"That is not authority. That is leverage wearing perfume."

This time a scheduler actually smiled before fear erased it.

Sivek extended his hand. "The recorder."

Lena did not give it to him.

Behind Sivek, on the upper tier, Ilya Ceyr descended the narrow stair as if the room had been built for her entrance. She wore a dark suit cut in the old routeholder fashion, severe at the collar and loose at the wrists, the fabric threaded with a sheen that caught status lights and returned them as faint gold. Her hair was silver-white, not from age but from a family modification fashionable among people who considered inheritance a design problem. Lena had seen her in hearings, always smiling with her mouth and never with the timing of her breath.

"Pilot Ors," Ilya said. "Or should I say Contractor Ors? The records vary by jurisdiction."

"Use Lena. It wastes less air."

Ilya's smile adjusted by a millimeter. "A practical answer. We need practical people today."

"Then you should leave."

Sivek's expression hardened. "Enough."

Ilya lifted a hand, forgiving them both. "No offense taken. I would be suspicious too, in her place."

"My place is not available."

"No," Ilya said. "It never is, after an inquiry."

There it was, the delicate knife. Not accusation, not sympathy. Recognition shaped to remind everyone listening that Lena came with history already attached.

The lift doors opened again before Lena could answer. A man stepped out carrying an old-fashioned case in one hand and a legal slate in the other. He was taller than most station-born people, with the slightly careful posture of someone whose body had been regrown after injury or selected from a template that had not quite matched his habits. His face was brown, narrow, and tired around the eyes. A thin blue line ran from his left temple to the hinge of his jaw: not decoration, then. Glassward continuity mark.

He crossed to the table and set down the case.

"Maro Venn," he said. "Compact Accident Reconstruction, provisional authority under reopening statute twelve."

Ilya's smile cooled. "I was not informed the Compact had requested Glassward personnel."

"It requested an accident investigator," Maro said. "The Glassward did not come as luggage."

Lena liked him before she trusted him, which was dangerous and therefore useful to notice.

Sivek nodded to Maro. "You are early."

"I was on Halcyon for the rehearsal."

"Why?"

"Because gates that have slept through wars sometimes wake badly."

Maro opened his case. Inside lay three witness seals, two sensor keys, and a compact forensic cradle for data cores. He looked at the recorder in Lena's hand, then at Sivek's extended one.

"Has chain of custody begun?" he asked.

"It will begin when Contractor Ors stops obstructing evidence transfer," Ilya said.

Maro's gaze shifted to Lena. "Did you alter the core?"

"No."

"Did anyone order you not to remove it?"

"A security officer implied the universe would become tidier if I did not."

"Implication is not an order." He placed a witness seal on the table and activated it. Three points of blue light formed a triangle over the surface. "Place the recorder inside the field. State your name and the circumstances of removal."

Sivek's hand lowered slowly.

Lena set the recorder in the triangle. "Lena Ors. Licensed route pilot, inactive by sanction in three jurisdictions, maintenance calibration contractor on temporary Halcyon authority. I removed this local telemetry recorder from service tug Larkspur after rehearsal pulse failure at Kestrel Gate. I removed it because central telemetry did not match local instrumentation before the event, public bulletin language assigned probable maintenance responsibility before inquiry, and Compact security attempted to take custody without independent seal."

The blue triangle pulsed once.

Maro looked to Sivek. "Scheduling Officer Hale, do you contest factual elements of that statement?"

Sivek's face had become very still. "I contest characterization, not sequence."

"Recorded." Maro looked to Ilya.

She spread her hands. "House Ceyr reserves position on all conclusions."

"Position is not evidence. Also recorded."

Lena nearly laughed again, but this time it would not have hurt.

Maro seated the recorder in the cradle. The device hummed softly. A screen unfolded from his case and filled with local telemetry: dense columns of numbers, phase curves, heat gradients, vane flexion, tug attitude, the audio translation of stress rendered as waveform. Lena stepped closer before she meant to.

There was the flat sixth tone.

On the central board above them, the same timestamp showed harmony within tolerance.

Maro did not speak for almost a minute. That was the first professional thing he did. People eager to own a disaster named it too soon. He moved through the data with the patient hands of someone opening a body.

"Local drift begins twelve seconds before rehearsal pulse," he said at last. "Correction pattern appears at four-second intervals. Central sample rate?"

"One per second for rehearsal," Sivek said.

"Local?"

"Eight per second," Lena said. "Because service craft do not enjoy dying between official truths."

Maro magnified the curve. "The error is visible on local and absent on central. Not averaged out. Removed."

Ilya leaned forward. "Sensor fault."

"Which sensor?"

"The tug's."

Maro tapped another sequence. "Vane Three hull stress local also records the correction. Independent system."

"Then a shared calibration fault."

"Conveniently timed across independent systems and invisible to central?"

"I said fault, not miracle."

Lena watched the curve. The correction pattern rose, clipped, and vanished before each central sample. Too clean. Too respectful of procedure.

"It knew the sample timing," she said.

The room absorbed the sentence.

Sivek's voice came out low. "Be careful."

"No. Be accurate." She pointed at the magnified sequence. "A physical failure wanders. It rings, heats, drags neighboring tolerances with it. This is being pulled inside the line before the official board looks. Whoever wrote that correction had your sampling schedule."

"Many people have rehearsal specifications," Ilya said.

"Not many people have vane-level correction authority."

"Kestrel has suffered six years of unglamorous decay. Before we invent saboteurs, perhaps we should exhaust ordinary neglect."

Tamsa Reet's voice came from the stair. "Ordinary neglect has a badge number if you follow it long enough."

She had changed out of the stained coverall into a pressure jacket, but the green life-support band remained on her sleeve. Two security officers trailed behind her, both unhappy and neither willing to touch her in front of Control staff.

Sivek said, "This is a restricted inquiry."

"My crew is named in your bulletin."

"No individuals were named."

"Maintenance compliance review pending," Tamsa quoted. "On Halcyon that is a name with every worker's face under it."

Ilya sighed. "This is exactly why raw evidence must be handled carefully. Panic will not repair the gate."

Tamsa looked at the magnified telemetry. Lena saw the moment she understood enough. Not the equations, maybe, but the shape: a failure combed smooth for the people above her.

"That is not one of ours," Tamsa said.

Sivek rubbed the bridge of his nose. "You cannot possibly know that from one display."

"No, Officer Hale. I know it because if one of ours could make a dying vane lie that politely, we would not still be begging Ceyr for scrubber membranes."

Something like a laugh moved through the lower consoles and died before it became disciplinary.

Maro froze the telemetry. "I need full central logs, raw vane access records, maintenance authorization chains, drone traffic, coolant batch certificates, and all schedule revisions from the past thirty days."

"You will have relevant records," Ilya said.

"I did not ask you."

"House Ceyr's liability office co-maintains the buffer."

"Then House Ceyr may co-produce records."

Sivek nodded once to an operator. "Begin transfer to sealed inquiry partition."

The operator hesitated. "Sir, some of those records are under debt covenant."

"I am aware."

"Releasing them may trigger review."

"Everything triggers review," Sivek said, and for the first time all day Lena heard the exhausted man under the officer. "Send them."

The operator bent to the console.

Then every public screen in Control changed.

The status board, the traffic map, the internal ration notices, even the narrow entertainment strip above the rest alcove: all of them went white, then resolved into the same emergency bulletin.

REHEARSAL INCIDENT PRELIMINARY FINDING:
SERVICE-CRAFT MAINTENANCE NONCOMPLIANCE
NO EVIDENCE OF GATE SABOTAGE
CONVOY REOPENING REMAINS ON SCHEDULE

For three seconds nobody moved.

Then Tamsa Reet crossed the room and punched the nearest console hard enough to crack the protective laminate.

"That went station-wide," an operator whispered.

Sivek stared at the bulletin as if it had appeared on the inside of his own skin. "I did not authorize that."

Ilya's expression had become unreadable.

Maro closed the forensic case around the recorder without powering it down. "Then the inquiry has a second question."

Lena looked through the narrow exposed strip of window toward Vane Three. Heat still shimmered along its injured edge. Somewhere beyond it, eleven thousand people were falling toward a gate that had just learned to lie in public.

"No," she said. "It has the same question twice."

Chapter 3: Halcyon Breath

Tamsa Reet did not take Lena to a conference room.

She took her down.

Halcyon Ring's public elevators had names from the old donor era: Ascent, Prosper, Welcome, Horizon. The maintenance lift behind Control had a number stenciled on its inner door and a smell of warm insulation. It dropped through the gateward bulge with an honesty Lena appreciated more than comfort. The walls sweated condensation where a chilled feed line ran too close to the lift shaft. Every ten meters, the car shuddered past another deck whose access panel had been opened, patched, relabeled, and opened again.

Tamsa stood beside the emergency stop bar with her arms folded. She had not spoken since Control. The cracked laminate mark from her fist was still red across two knuckles.

Lena held the overhead strap and let the lift's vibration climb through her boots. A medic had scanned her on the way out of Control and found bruised ribs, a strained shoulder, and the old elevated stress markers that made every station clinician suggest rest in the same doomed tone. She had accepted a pain patch because refusing would have been theater. It softened the edges of her body but not the day.

Maro had remained above with the recorder, the sealed inquiry partition, and a room full of people discovering that evidence could acquire enemies faster than advocates. Sivek had let Tamsa take Lena away only after Maro observed that a material witness could inspect affected maintenance systems if escorted by the responsible supervisor. Ilya Ceyr had objected to the phrase responsible supervisor and then, for reasons Lena did not yet understand, stopped herself.

The lift passed Deck G-12. Someone had scratched a sentence into the door frame and filled the letters with blue grease pencil.

WE ARE NOT THE LEAK.

Lena read it twice before the shaft wall swallowed it.

"Does Control know this route?" she asked.

"Control knows every route after someone bleeds on it," Tamsa said.

"That isn't an answer."

"It is on Halcyon."

The lift slowed so abruptly Lena's injured shoulder complained. The door opened on wet heat and voices.

Scrubber Works Three occupied a crescent of the ring's inner service layer, where spin gravity pressed everything outward and the air tasted of mineral cartridges, human breath, and tired plants. The main corridor had once been painted white. It was now a history of repairs: gray patches over impact gouges, yellow sealant around old conduit, strips of handwritten warnings where printed signs had worn illegible. Workers moved through it in green-banded coveralls, some with tool frames riding their backs, some carrying lunch tins, some guiding carts stacked with membrane sleeves wrapped in sterile cloth.

They looked at Tamsa first. Then at Lena.

The change in the corridor was not silence. Halcyon did not have enough spare silence for drama. Pumps beat behind the walls. Fans whined. A child cried somewhere beyond a half-open hatch. A valve chattered in need of adjustment. But human voices fell away, and that was worse.

"She is here to see what they blamed," Tamsa said.

No one welcomed Lena. No one spat. A woman with silver repair tape along both sleeves stepped aside just enough for them to pass and said, "Jori's awake."

Tamsa's jaw moved once. "Clear?"

"He asked whether he was fired."

"Then clear enough."

The woman looked at Lena. "You tell him no?"

"I told him he was concussed."

"Pilot answer."

Lena accepted that. It was accurate.

Tamsa led her through an access hatch into a chamber that should have smelled green and did not. The scrubber nursery was three decks high, a vertical garden of air-cleaning biotowers and membrane stacks arranged around a central service spine. Once, before Kestrel's route closed, the chamber must have been almost beautiful: leaves trained along mist frames, algae columns glowing with soft internal light, children from school tours pressing hands to the glass while guides explained how breath became breath again.

Now a third of the towers were dark. Another third glowed in the wrong shades: pale yellow where they should have been blue-green, bruised purple where nutrient balances had drifted. Technicians worked from suspended rails, clipping infected growth, reseating membrane cartridges, reading diagnostics from handheld slates held together with tape. A boy no older than twelve sat at a sorting bench, separating usable fasteners from a tray of mixed salvage.

Lena looked up despite herself. "This is primary life support?"

"Primary, secondary, and whatever we can steal from the decorative gardens without starting a referendum." Tamsa took a mask from a wall rack and handed it over.

"Is the air unsafe?"

"The air is expensive."

Lena put the mask on.

Tamsa did not. "We were certified for replacement membranes four years ago. Shipment delayed by blockade, then by liability review, then by route closure, then by the argument about whether Halcyon counts as gate infrastructure or civilian dependency. House Ceyr says civilian when we need parts and infrastructure when they need collateral."

"The convoy is carrying membranes."

"So they tell us."

"You do not believe the manifest?"

Tamsa walked to the nearest dark tower and opened its inspection panel. Inside, a lattice of fine ceramic sheets sat in a frame designed to slide out. Three sheets were missing. Others had been patched with translucent film and wire tensioners. Not repaired. Persuaded.

"I believe mass," she said. "I believe crate volume. I believe the name of the person who counted a thing before sealing it. I believe what arrives through a hatch. Manifests are prayers with accounting codes."

Lena stepped closer. "How long?"

"Before people start dying because of air? Depends how honest you want the word because to be."

"Honest."

"Ninety days if nothing else breaks and we keep the children's wards on full priority. Sixty if the convoy delay triggers another ration protest and people start overriding district fans. Thirty if a hot month hits the agricultural arc and the mold comes back. Yesterday, if you count the pneumonia cases the administrators call independent."

The chamber seemed to tighten around Lena's ribs.

Tamsa shut the panel. "Control knows those numbers. Ceyr knows. Compact knows. Everyone knows enough to arrange their face when we say them."

"Then why risk the gate?"

"Ask the people who told the station my crew broke it."

"I am asking you because you brought me here."

For the first time, Tamsa looked directly at her without anger as a shield. The fury remained, but behind it was calculation and something older than either.

"I brought you here because if you are lying, I need to know how much damage you can do. If you are telling the truth, I need you to understand what truth costs when it misses a delivery window."

A technician above them cursed. A membrane sleeve slipped from the rail and fluttered down like an exhausted flag. The twelve-year-old at the sorting bench darted out, caught it before it struck the wet floor, and held it against his chest with both hands.

"Careful," Tamsa called.

"I was," the boy said.

"Be more than careful."

He carried the sleeve away with solemn concentration.

Lena watched him go. "Is he supposed to be in here?"

"No. He is supposed to be in mathematics block. Mathematics block has thirty-eight children, one teacher, and a ceiling leak over the dry wall. Here he can sort screws and feel useful."

"That is not the same as being safe."

"Almost nothing is."

They left the nursery through a side hatch and entered a narrower passage where warm pipes ran behind mesh. Tamsa moved quickly. People stopped her every few meters: a filter request, a ration appeal, news of Jori, a rumor from Control, an argument over whether the public bulletin meant work crews would lose hazard credit. She answered each without softening and without promising what she could not deliver. Lena began to see that Tamsa's authority did not come from title. It came from being the person who would still be there after an answer became inconvenient.

At a junction, three residents waited beside a food dispenser whose screen read PROTEIN CREDIT DELAYED. One of them, an old man with a portable oxygen loop across his chest, pointed at Lena.

"That's the pilot?"

Tamsa did not slow. "That is a pilot."

"They opening the gate or not?"

"When I know, you will know what I can prove."

"My daughter is on Dax's convoy."

That stopped her.

The old man's mouth trembled with rage or effort. The oxygen loop clicked softly at his collar. "She got work on the medical packet. First good contract in eight years. They told us the gate was certified. They told us today was ceremony."

Lena felt every person in the junction turn toward her.

There were answers that protected institutions. There were answers that protected panic from itself. There were answers that felt honest because they were incomplete.

She said, "The gate is not certified by what I saw today."

The old man's eyes closed.

Tamsa's voice cut in, hard. "And the convoy is still inbound, which means shouting at a pilot in a corridor will not save your daughter. Go to district desk. Register her ship name. If there is a family packet channel, we will find it."

"They always say register."

"Then register twice. Make the system trip over you."

He nodded once, hating the advice and needing it. The residents moved aside.

Lena and Tamsa walked on.

"You should not have asked me in front of him," Lena said.

"I did not ask."

"You let him."

"He lives here."

The passage opened into a meeting bay built from an old cargo lock. Two hundred people had packed themselves into a space rated for half that, standing between crates, sitting on folded pressure blankets, perched on rails. A public ration board glowed at one end. At the other, a cheap projector showed the station-wide bulletin that had blamed service-craft maintenance. Someone had drawn a black circle around the phrase NO EVIDENCE OF GATE SABOTAGE.

Tamsa climbed onto a crate.

The room did not quiet. It leaned.

"Jori Pell is awake," she said.

The room exhaled.

"He is injured. He is not dead. He was doing assigned work on Tug Larkspur during a certified rehearsal pulse. Any bulletin implying crew misconduct is premature and will be challenged."

"Challenged by who?" someone shouted.

"By me."

"They do not listen to you."

"Then I will become more repetitive."

A few people laughed because the alternative was worse.

Tamsa pointed to the ration board. "Membrane priority remains children's wards, medical decks, agricultural arc, then residential fans by vulnerability index. I know district four thinks the index cheats them. District four can come see the pneumonia logs before calling it politics."

"It is politics," a woman near the front said.

"Everything with a queue is politics. I said it is not cheating."

Lena stood near the hatch, feeling more exposed than she had in Control. There, people had wanted her contained. Here, people wanted something from her that no gate pilot could give: a future with a delivery schedule.

Tamsa turned slightly. "Lena Ors was on Larkspur."

The room found her.

Lena kept her hands visible. Old habit. Bad rooms liked hidden hands.

"She says the fault was not maintenance," Tamsa continued.

Noise broke open. Questions, curses, a man's short prayer, three people demanding to know why Control had lied. Tamsa let it rise until the shape of it became dangerous, then stamped once on the crate. The sound cracked through the bay.

"Listen," she said. "If Control is lying, panic helps the liar. If Ceyr is lying, breaking our own ducts helps Ceyr. If the Compact is lying, giving them a riot to manage writes their report for them."

"So we wait?"

"No. We document. Every crew lead seals logs twice. Every district writes down shortages by name and time. Nobody edits angry. Nobody invents. Nobody hides bad maintenance to protect pride. If we have a broken valve, we say broken valve. If we have sabotage, we say sabotage when we can prove it. We do not hand them one false word."

Lena heard the discipline in it and felt a reluctant respect settle beside her guilt. Tamsa Reet understood evidence as a survival system.

A young woman with a shaved head raised a hand. "And the convoy?"

Tamsa looked at Lena.

This time it was a question.

Lena stepped forward. The bay's heat closed over her. "I do not know yet whether Kestrel can open safely. I know the central board was given a lie. I know the timing of that lie required access to systems ordinary maintenance crews do not control. I know the convoy is inbound and cannot be treated like an argument that can wait for a better day."

"Can you fix it?" the young woman asked.

Lena thought of Vane Three's black seam. Of the official green. Of Sivek saying the convoy could not turn back. Of Jori asking whether he was fired because he lived in a world where injury did not cancel debt.

"Not alone," she said.

The room did not like that answer. It liked it better than a promise.

Tamsa climbed down from the crate. The meeting began to reorganize itself around tasks: logs, family packet registration, district shortage copies, watch rotations to stop anyone from damaging life support in anger. People moved with the grim relief of having work that might matter.

At the hatch, Tamsa handed Lena a slate. It showed a schematic of Scrubber Works Three, not the clean official drawing but a working map layered with patches, substitutions, undocumented reroutes, and warnings written in maintenance shorthand.

"You shared route geometry in Control," Tamsa said.

"I said one sentence."

"It was the right sentence."

"Do not make a habit of trusting my sentences."

"I trust maps better." Tamsa tapped the slate. "This is ours. Not for Ceyr. Not for Compact unless I say. You want to know how a lie reached a field vane? Start with how legal parts become illegal paths."

Lena looked at the map. Lines crossed between life support, drone storage, coolant access, and gate service locks in ways the official architecture would never admit. Not sabotage yet. Possibility.

"Why give this to me?"

"Because someone above us made a board see green." Tamsa's face hardened. "I want someone below them to see everything."

Before Lena could answer, her wrist console vibrated with an inquiry alert. Maro's name appeared over a compressed message.

COOLANT CERTIFICATE LOT 88 CONTRADICTS LOCAL SUPPLY LOGS. NEED ORS AND REET. QUIETLY.

Tamsa read it over her shoulder.

"Lot 88," she said.

"You know it?"

"I rejected it three times."

"For what?"

Tamsa was already moving back toward the lift.

"For being too clean."

Chapter 4: Forked Witness

Maro Venn had turned a disused insurance office into an investigation room in less than an hour.

That told Lena three things before he said a word. First, he had expected trouble before the rehearsal pulse. Second, he trusted official rooms only after he had counted every door. Third, he had done this kind of work often enough to know that accidents became stories while metal was still cooling.

The office sat between a shuttered claims desk and a corridor of empty negotiation booths. House Ceyr's crest still ghosted the wall where someone had peeled away a smartfilm banner years before. Maro had covered the remaining glass with foil insulation, turned the central table into a data surface, and placed three independent recorders at different heights in the room. One faced the door. One faced the table. The third faced Maro.

Tamsa noticed. "Expecting yourself to lie?"

"Expecting someone to accuse me of it," Maro said.

He looked less composed than he had in Control. His jacket hung open. A bruise had surfaced along one cheekbone, old yellow under brown skin, not from today. The blue continuity line at his temple glowed faintly where it met a diagnostic patch. He had removed his gloves and arranged them with exact care beside the forensic cradle that held Larkspur's recorder.

On the table, two documents floated side by side. One was a certified coolant transfer record stamped by Compact safety and House Ceyr liability. The other was a local supply log written in Halcyon's ugly maintenance shorthand. Both named Coolant Lot 88. They disagreed about almost everything else.

"Who has access to this room?" Lena asked.

"Me, now you, now Supervisor Reet. Compact security thinks I am in Booth C with Officer Hale. Officer Hale knows I am not in Booth C and has chosen not to correct them for nine minutes."

"That is a narrow kind of courage."

"Most useful courage is narrow." Maro tapped the certified transfer record. "Lot 88. Officially delivered eight days ago from Ceyr bonded storage, purity ninety-nine point nine eight percent, inhibitor profile within Kestrel vane tolerance, signed into gate service by Halcyon maintenance at twenty-one forty station time."

Tamsa leaned over the table. "No."

Maro tapped the local log. "Unofficially rejected by you eight days ago at sixteen ten, again at sixteen forty, and finally at seventeen fifteen with the note: too clean, no bittering trace, no chain stink, do not feed to vanes unless command signs in blood. I assume the last part is not a formal technical category."

"It should be."

Lena studied the records. Gate-vane coolant was not simply cold fluid. It was a chemically tagged medium designed to carry heat, reveal contamination, and leave a traceable history on the pipes it touched. Every manufacturer added benign bittering compounds and isotope signatures so batches could be tracked after accidents. Too clean meant someone had removed more than impurities.

"If the bittering trace is gone," Lena said, "the coolant can be anything."

"Not anything," Tamsa said. "It still has to pass thermal sniffers."

"Thermal sniffers sample bulk behavior," Maro said. "Not memory."

Tamsa looked at him. "Coolant does not remember."

"Pipes do. Seals do. Valves do. So do people, badly."

He enlarged a set of spectra. Even with the labels, Lena needed a moment to place what bothered her. The official purity curve was too smooth, a line drawn by someone who understood certification better than machines. The local scan had tiny scars in it: trace metals, shipping residue, a faint organic bitterant left from the original manufacturer, and something else just above the threshold of instrument noise.

"There," she said.

Maro nodded. "A phase-responsive polymer. Very low concentration. It would not affect ordinary heat transfer."

"But a rehearsal pulse is not ordinary heat transfer."

"No."

Tamsa's eyes narrowed. "Say it in words for the person who rejected the poison without knowing its name."

Lena placed two fingers on the table and moved the spectra apart. "If that polymer migrates under field stress, it could change the coolant's dielectric behavior during a pulse. Small change. Local. Enough to tug a vane inside tolerance if someone knew where to put it."

"And enough," Maro said, "to let an automatic correction system hide the tug until the next official sample."

Tamsa was still for a long moment. "We did not install it."

"I know," Maro said.

"You do not know."

"I know your local logs reject the batch before the official record says you accepted it. I know the acceptance signature on the certified transfer is not yours, though the file claims your authority token. I know the token was used from a terminal inside Scrubber Works Three during a shift when you were in a public ration hearing."

"That proves someone used my token."

"It proves more than that." Maro opened another file. "This is the terminal's visual audit."

The image was poor: a ceiling-corner view of a maintenance desk, three people passing in blur, a tool cart blocking half the frame. At the timestamp in question, the terminal activated. No one stood before it. The authorization completed anyway.

Tamsa's mouth went flat. "Remote injection."

"Or a ghost key."

"Ghost keys are war rumors."

Lena said, "Many war rumors are procurement programs with better names."

Maro looked at her, and for a second his professional reserve thinned into recognition. "Yes."

Tamsa saw the exchange. "You two want to share the part where the rest of us get killed by vocabulary?"

Maro straightened. "During the Fracture Wars, several security groups developed authorization tools that could replay a person's access pattern without stealing the token itself. Timing, pressure signature, error habits, command sequence, even hesitation. Systems trained to recognize a worker became systems trained to accept a performance of that worker."

"A Quiet Directorate tool," Lena said.

"The Directorate is officially dissolved."

"And yet nobody buried the tools."

Tamsa folded her injured hand around the edge of the table until the knuckles whitened. "Can you prove that was used?"

Maro did not answer immediately.

Lena appreciated that, too.

"Not yet," he said. "I can prove the acceptance record is false. I can prove the batch you rejected entered gate-service records under your authority. I can prove the coolant found in Vane Three's local residue includes a polymer absent from the certified profile. I cannot yet prove who put it there, who authorized the substitution, or whether the polymer alone caused the event."

"So they will say bad maintenance."

"They will say several things. Bad maintenance. Panic. Faulty local instrumentation. A bitter supervisor protecting her crew. A disgraced pilot needing a conspiracy to excuse disobedience."

Lena smiled without warmth. "You have met inquiry counsel."

"I was inquiry counsel once."

Tamsa turned on him. "For whom?"

Maro reached to the diagnostic patch at his temple and pressed it until the glow dimmed. "For a Glassward recovery court after the Aurelian breach. Not for routeholders. Not for Compact safety. I argued whether memory forks from dead station residents could be used to reconstruct evacuation orders."

The room changed around the words. Even Tamsa, who had looked ready to strike him with the nearest certification slate, waited.

Everyone in the Reach had heard of the Aurelian breach. Few knew facts. The war had turned facts into rationed goods. Aurelian had been a habitat cluster near Pall, neutral by charter, valuable by position, dead by sunrise depending on whose clock counted. Afterward the Glassward had recovered partial mind states from emergency implants, backup caches, and damaged domestic assistants. For years, courts had argued whether those fragments were testimony, property, persons, or grief in machine-readable form.

"You represented the forks?" Lena asked.

"Some of them. Then I became one of the people arguing against admitting certain fork records because the dead had not consented to being made useful."

Tamsa's voice lost none of its hardness, but it lowered. "And now you record yourself from three angles."

"Now I know evidence can eat the people who carry it."

He touched the blue line at his temple. The gesture was small, almost private. "My continuity mark means there are authenticated state records that can verify parts of my perception and decision process. It does not mean I am a walking truth machine. It does not mean I cannot misremember. It does not mean my private fear belongs to the court. And if I unseal a fork record broadly enough to satisfy hostile counsel, I may expose unrelated people who never agreed to be evidence."

Lena thought of him in Control, calm while Ilya sharpened jurisdiction around him. She had assumed the blue line made him powerful. It did. Not in the way she had thought.

"Can your records prove the bulletin went station-wide without Sivek's authorization?" she asked.

"They can prove what I saw and heard in the room. They cannot prove the negative. They cannot prove his mind."

"Useful distinction."

"Expensive distinction."

Tamsa studied him as if recalibrating a tool. "Then why help us?"

"Because the false record blamed your crew and the false coolant could kill the convoy."

"That is the official answer."

"It is also true."

"I asked why."

Maro looked at the sealed windows. Behind the insulation, Halcyon vibrated with pumps, traffic, breath, and rumor. "Because Aurelian taught me what happens when institutions protect future legitimacy by smoothing present terror. The official record always arrives clean. People do not."

No one spoke for several seconds.

Then Lena pointed at the official transfer record. Work was kinder than reverence. "Who signed physical custody?"

Maro's mouth curved faintly, grateful or amused. "A bonded Ceyr logistics clerk named Pavo Irren. He departed Halcyon two hours after signature, officially for family leave on Pall."

"Convenient."

"His shuttle is still docked."

Tamsa blinked. "Then he did not depart."

"The passenger manifest says he did. The lock camera says his luggage did. No biometric exit record."

"Someone wanted him gone on paper," Lena said.

"Or wanted us to chase an absent man."

Tamsa stepped toward the door. "Where is he?"

"I do not know."

"Then we find him."

"Quietly," Maro said.

Tamsa gave him a look of open contempt. "Quietly is how the bulletin got written."

"Quietly is also how frightened clerks survive long enough to answer questions." He slid a file to her. "I pulled his housing assignment before access closed. Temporary berth in Ceyr Quarter, Block Blue-Nine. His door has not opened in six days."

Lena felt the day shift. Sabotage had been a shape on telemetry, a chemical trace, a forged authorization. A missing clerk turned it into flesh.

"Security?" she asked.

"Compact security will notify House Ceyr the moment they request entry. House Ceyr will arrive with liability counsel and a humanitarian concern for Mr. Irren's privacy. We will learn nothing until a polite person has removed everything interesting."

"You have done this before," Tamsa said.

"Yes."

Tamsa took the housing file. "Blue-Nine is not easy for outsiders. Ceyr leases it, but Halcyon owns the waste lines."

Lena looked at her.

"Every fancy quarter sits on a pipe," Tamsa said.

Maro closed the coolant files and transferred copies to two sealed chips. He gave one to Lena and one to Tamsa.

"Do not network these," he said. "Do not show them to anyone whose first question is whether they can make a copy. If I am removed from the inquiry, these are enough to force a second review but not enough to win it."

"If you are removed?" Lena asked.

"I was provisionally attached under reopening statute. Provisional people are easy to thank for service."

His wrist console chimed once. He looked down and went still.

"What?" Tamsa asked.

Maro turned the console so they could see. A formal notice filled the screen in Compact gray.

PROVISIONAL INVESTIGATOR MARO VENN:
YOUR ACCESS TO KESTREL REOPENING EVIDENCE PARTITION IS SUSPENDED PENDING REVIEW OF GLASSWARD CONFLICT-OF-INTEREST DISCLOSURES.

Below it, a second line appeared.

PLEASE REPORT TO COMPACT SECURITY FOR CONTINUITY VERIFICATION.

Lena felt cold pass under the pain patch.

Tamsa read the notice twice. "That was fast."

"No," Maro said. "That was ready."

Someone knocked at the office door.

Not a hard knock. Not security forcing entry. Two taps, a pause, and two more, polite as a clerk with bad news.

Maro looked at the recorders. Lena looked at Tamsa. Tamsa looked toward the rear wall where, if her map could be trusted, a maintenance crawlspace ran behind the waste reclamation conduit and into the Ceyr quarter's service spine.

The knock came again.

"Mr. Venn," called a woman's voice through the door. "Compact security. We need to confirm your continuity status."

Maro reached for his gloves.

Lena caught his wrist. "If you go with them, how long before you get evidence access back?"

"If the review is honest, hours. If it is not, after the convoy."

"Then the answer is no."

He looked at her hand on his wrist until she released him. "I do not enjoy fleeing lawful orders."

"Good. You can dislike it accurately."

Tamsa crossed to an access panel half-hidden behind a decorative strip. "I can get two people through here, maybe three if the third does not breathe like a guilty executive."

"The recorders?" Maro asked.

Lena picked up the smallest one and tossed it to him. "One witness is portable."

The door latch flashed amber as someone applied an override.

Tamsa popped the panel. Warm, sour air breathed out of the wall. "Move."

Maro took one last look at the office he had built to survive accusation. Then he climbed into Halcyon's hidden infrastructure with the careful dignity of a man entering a sewer for legal reasons.

Lena followed, shoulder scraping the frame.

Tamsa pulled the panel shut behind them just as the office door opened.

In the dark conduit, with waste lines humming under her palms and a suspended investigator breathing ahead of her, Lena heard Compact security enter the empty room.

"Mr. Venn?" the woman called.

Maro did not answer.

For the first time since the rehearsal pulse, Lena felt the investigation leave the rails completely. The old fear came with it. So did something cleaner.

Choice.

Chapter 5: House Ceyr Smiles

Ceyr Quarter had carpets.

That was the first absurdity. Not pressure mats, not washable traction grid, not the thin anti-slip film used in public corridors until it curled at the corners. Carpet. A muted blue weave ran the length of Block Blue-Nine under warm ceiling panels that imitated late afternoon on a planet none of Halcyon's children had ever seen. The air was cooler than Scrubber Works Three by at least two degrees. It smelled of citrus, sterilized fabric, and money pretending not to have a smell.

Lena emerged from the service hatch behind a sculptural planter and had to resist the urge to laugh. The planter held actual soil. Not nutrient foam. Not decorative lichen. Soil under a dwarf tree with glossy leaves and three yellow fruits no one had rationed by district vulnerability index.

Tamsa climbed out behind her, saw the tree, and whispered a word that would have cost a junior worker a discipline mark in the nursery.

Maro came last, brushing condensation from one sleeve. The crawlspace had not improved his elegance, but he managed to look like a court officer inconvenienced by architecture rather than a fugitive from continuity verification.

"Camera left," Tamsa murmured.

They moved before the lens completed its sweep. Tamsa's knowledge of the quarter was partial and practical: where pipes ran, which wall panels opened, which decorative vents were real enough to carry sound. She did not know the carpets. That, too, meant something. People who maintained comfort rarely got to use it.

Pavo Irren's temporary berth was three doors down from the block lounge. Its status panel glowed occupancy-private, a setting that on most of Halcyon meant someone had paid for quiet. On Ceyr leasehold, it meant the door would ask lawyers before admitting physics.

Tamsa knelt and slid a thin tool from her sleeve.

Maro raised an eyebrow. "Do I want to know why a life-support supervisor carries a lock tool?"

"You want to breathe on Halcyon?"

"Yes."

"Then no."

Lena watched the corridor. At the far end, two Ceyr staffers in dark uniforms spoke beside a beverage niche. One laughed softly. The sound did not belong to the day.

The lock clicked. Tamsa did not open it. She glanced at Maro.

"Legal consequences?"

"Several."

"Fatal?"

"Less likely than the gate."

She opened the door.

The room beyond was dark and cold.

Not cool like the corridor. Cold enough that Lena saw her own breath. The berth's environmental panel blinked with a local override warning. A bed folded into the wall. A desk sat beneath a narrow screen showing a paused travel authorization in Pavo Irren's name. Two suitcases stood near the door, one open and empty, one closed with a luggage seal from the shuttle that supposedly had carried him away.

No body.

Lena let herself exhale.

Tamsa went straight to the environmental panel. "Temperature drop started six days ago. Manual setting. Vent baffles closed."

"To preserve something?" Maro asked.

"Or hide smell."

They all understood the word she did not add.

Lena checked the desk without touching it. A cup with a skin of frozen tea. A legal slate in sleep mode. A strip of medication ampules, three used, one cracked. A family image in a cheap frame: Pavo Irren, if the file image was accurate, standing in low gravity beside an older woman and two small children. His smile had the strained uncertainty of a person visiting people he had not supported well enough.

"He expected to come back," Lena said.

Maro opened his portable recorder and began narrating softly. "Entry to temporary berth Blue-Nine-Seventeen at station time fourteen twenty-two. Door status occupancy-private. Environmental anomaly. No occupant visible from entry. Evidence preservation limited by exigent circumstances."

Tamsa snorted quietly. "That sentence doing much work?"

"Heroic work."

Lena pointed to the closed suitcase. "That seal is too obvious."

"Agreed," Maro said.

Which meant they had to inspect it anyway.

Tamsa scanned the casing. "No pressure charge. No heat bloom. Basic luggage lock."

"Basic on Ceyr property?" Lena asked.

"Basic looking."

Maro photographed the seal from three angles. Then Tamsa broke it.

Inside were clothes, folded with nervous precision, a pouch of personal hygiene tabs, a sealed packet of paper letters, and a coolant certificate wafer.

No bomb. No revelation written in blood. Just the sort of object a missing clerk might hide because he had realized too late that paperwork could kill people.

Maro lifted the wafer with evidence tongs. "Lot 88 original manufacturer certificate."

Lena leaned close. "Can you read it?"

"Not here. It is sealed to a manufacturer key."

"Does the official file have this?"

"The official file has a derivative certification from Ceyr bonded storage. It should reference an original. It did not."

Tamsa looked around the room. "Where is he?"

A soft tone sounded from the corridor.

All three stilled.

The room's wall screen woke. The paused travel authorization vanished. Ilya Ceyr's face appeared in its place, composed and regretful, as if she had been waiting inside the furniture.

"Please do not damage Mr. Irren's belongings," she said. "He has had a difficult week."

Tamsa whispered, "Of course."

Lena stepped into view of the screen. "Where is he?"

Ilya's eyes moved over her, then past her. "Investigator Venn, I believe Compact security is concerned for your continuity status."

Maro placed the certificate wafer into a shield sleeve. "My continuity is touched by your concern."

"I do not want this adversarial."

"Then you could stop hunting evidence before we find it."

Ilya's smile was almost sad. "You mistake liability management for predation."

Tamsa barked a laugh. "No, that one is dressed exactly right."

"Supervisor Reet," Ilya said, "your anger is earned. It is not always useful."

Tamsa moved toward the screen. "My anger kept Lot 88 out of the vanes until someone forged my hand."

"A serious allegation."

"A true one."

"Truth still needs sequence."

Lena felt the sentence hook the room. Ilya did not deny. She arranged.

"Where is Pavo Irren?" Lena asked again.

Ilya looked at her fully. "Alive."

"That was not the question."

"It is the answer you needed first."

Maro's recorder caught the words. Ilya knew it and did not care.

"He came to our internal counsel after discovering irregularities in a transfer file," she said. "He was frightened. He had reason to be. House Ceyr placed him under protective quiet."

Tamsa stared. "Protective quiet."

"A temporary security status."

"You mean detention without a door log."

"I mean a frightened clerk is not helped by being dragged into a public machinery of accusation before we know whether he is guilty, negligent, or being used."

Lena hated that the argument had bones. That was Ilya's talent. She did not offer lies without structure. She built rooms around partial truths and invited people to live there until the doors disappeared.

"You filed him as departed," Maro said.

"To reduce risk."

"To whom?"

"To him. To the gate. To Halcyon. To a convoy whose passengers do not benefit from your theatrical trespass."

"If he is alive, produce him to the inquiry."

"There is no inquiry at present. You are suspended."

"My evidence authority is suspended. My eyes are not."

Ilya's expression warmed by one carefully measured degree. "That sort of line plays well in public. In court it gets people hurt."

Lena stepped closer to the screen. "Did House Ceyr alter the Lot 88 certificate?"

"No."

"Did House Ceyr install Lot 88 in Vane Three?"

"No."

"Did House Ceyr know the certified file did not match the local batch?"

For the first time, Ilya paused.

There were many kinds of silence. Lena knew cockpit silence, when people waited for an alarm to decide their lives. She knew inquiry silence, when counsel saw a witness approach the shape of a forbidden answer. Ilya's silence was neither. It was the gap of a person calculating which truth could be afforded.

"House Ceyr knew Lot 88 was under review," Ilya said.

Tamsa swore.

"Under review before or after the rehearsal pulse?" Maro asked.

"Before."

The cold room seemed to narrow.

Lena said, "And you let the rehearsal proceed."

"House Ceyr does not schedule Compact rehearsal pulses."

"You underwrite the liability. You sit in Control. You had a duty to disclose."

"We disclosed to the appropriate channel."

"Which channel?"

Ilya's gaze shifted away from Lena for half a second.

That was enough.

"Sivek," Lena said.

"Scheduling Officer Hale received a narrow risk advisory."

"Narrow," Tamsa said. "That word should be spaced out an airlock."

Maro remained very calm. "Did the advisory include the polymer trace?"

"No."

"Did it include the missing original manufacturer certificate?"

"No."

"Did it include the possibility that Supervisor Reet's authority token had been performed by a ghost key?"

"No."

"Then what did it include?"

Ilya's face hardened. "It included a warning that hostile disclosure of unresolved supply-chain questions could collapse the reopening schedule."

For a moment Lena could hear the gate again: five rising tones, a pause, and the sixth arriving flat.

"You warned him about panic," she said. "Not poison."

"We warned him about systemic risk."

"People always call it systemic when they want the victims spread thin."

Ilya absorbed that without flinching. "And pilots always discover moral clarity after someone else owns the timetable."

Tamsa took a step, but Lena lifted a hand.

The old shame rose fast. Ilya had meant it to. Lena could feel the inquiry chamber around her again, the diagram, the names, Sivek's careful defense that saved doctrine and abandoned judgment. She let the memory hit. Then she let it pass through.

"Maybe," Lena said. "Where is Irren?"

Ilya studied her. Whatever response she had expected, that had not been it.

"Safe."

"No. Located."

"I cannot provide that."

"Cannot or will not?"

"Both, if you insist on simplicity."

Maro slid the coolant wafer into an inner pocket. "Then we will continue without House Ceyr's cooperation."

"You are holding House property."

"I am holding potential evidence from a room you hid from the inquiry."

"A suspended investigator, an inactive pilot, and a maintenance supervisor with a forged token." Ilya's smile returned, gentler and more dangerous. "Please consider how this looks from outside your own urgency."

Tamsa leaned toward the screen. "From outside my urgency, children are breathing through patched membranes because your house can make carpet arrive faster than filters."

Ilya's mouth tightened.

There. Not guilt exactly. Irritation at an accusation too visible to elegantly reframe.

A chime sounded from Maro's console. He did not look down, but the screen reflected in his eyes. Lena saw text scroll across the glass.

SECURITY ENTERING BLUE-NINE.

Tamsa saw it too. She moved to the door and cracked it open. Footsteps approached along the carpeted corridor. More than two people. Measured pace. Not rushing. People with authority rarely ran when doors opened for them.

Ilya's image remained on the wall. "Stay where you are. This can still become orderly."

Lena almost laughed. The word orderly had begun to smell like coolant.

"Tamsa," she said, "service route?"

"Not from inside this room."

"Window?"

"To vacuum."

Maro looked at the ceiling vent. "Too small."

The footsteps stopped outside the door.

A man's voice called, "Compact security. Open and stand visible."

Tamsa's eyes flicked to the environmental panel. "Vent baffles are closed."

"I noticed," Lena said.

"But not welded."

Lena understood. "If you open them?"

"The room equalizes with the service plenum. Loud. Cold. Fog everywhere. Alarms."

Maro adjusted his grip on the portable recorder. "That sounds like evidence destruction."

"It sounds like building conditions," Tamsa said.

Ilya said sharply, "Do not."

The security override engaged. The door began to unlock.

Lena reached the environmental panel in two strides and braced her shoulder against the wall. "Do it."

Tamsa drove her tool into the manual release.

Every vent in Pavo Irren's room snapped open at once.

Cold air became a white wall. The pressure difference screamed through the ceiling baffles. Loose papers lifted from the desk and spun. The door slid open on three security officers, who entered into fog, alarms, and Tamsa Reet shouting that Ceyr's private leasehold had created an unsafe environmental condition in a residential berth.

It was not an escape plan. It was a maintenance incident with witnesses.

Lena moved through the fog low and left, one hand on Maro's sleeve. Tamsa blocked the doorway by falling, convincingly and loudly, into the lead officer's path while accusing him of ignoring a pressure hazard. Maro stumbled once but kept the coolant wafer inside his jacket.

Behind them, Ilya's voice cut through the wall screen.

"Pilot Ors."

Lena turned despite herself.

Ilya's face floated in the fog, pale and perfectly framed. "You think I want the gate to fail."

"I think you want to own the meaning of failure."

For the first time, Ilya Ceyr stopped smiling.

Then the fog swallowed the screen, and Lena ran.

Chapter 6: The First Hearing

Magistrate Vale Om convened the emergency hearing in a theater built for tourists.

Before the war, visitors to Halcyon Ring had sat in reclining seats under a transparent dome while guides projected the history of meridian transit across the stars. Children had learned to cheer when the first convoy emerged from Kestrel Gate in archived light. Routeholder delegates had given speeches beneath animations of clean vectors and cooperative flags. The dome had been shuttered since the first blockade year, partly to conserve power and partly because no one wanted to watch a celebratory film about promises that had failed to arrive.

Now the seats had been locked upright. The dome showed only armored gray. A portable judicial rail stood where the projection guide used to stand, and Magistrate Om sat behind it in a plain black pressure jacket with a seal of the Meridian Compact pinned at her throat.

She was smaller than Lena expected and older than the file image, with white hair braided tight against her skull and a face built from patience that had survived many attempts to exploit it. Two clerks stood behind her. Neither looked rested. A status ribbon above the rail counted down to the convoy's outer correction burn.

Sixteen hours, twelve minutes.

The number had not been present when Lena entered. It appeared after everyone sat down, as if Om wanted no one to pretend the hearing was happening outside physics.

Sivek Hale occupied the left table with Compact safety counsel and two operators from Control. Ilya Ceyr sat at the right table with three House attorneys, all young, immaculate, and grave in the theatrical way of people who billed grief by the quarter hour. Halcyon administration had sent Deputy Administrator Keva Lorn, a broad-shouldered woman with silver implants along both temples and a ration board slate clutched like a shield. Tamsa stood behind Lena's chair rather than take her assigned seat. Maro sat beside Lena with a temporary legal restraint glowing amber around his evidence slate.

The restraint did not prevent him from speaking. It only announced that every word he spoke was administratively suspect.

Lena had offered to break it.

Maro had said, "Please do not solve legal problems with assault before opening statements."

Now he folded his hands on the table and looked almost serene, which Lena was beginning to understand meant furious.

Om touched the rail. The theater's murmurs folded into recorded silence.

"This emergency session concerns the safety status of Kestrel Gate following today's rehearsal incident, the admissibility and preservation of evidence related to that incident, and the pending arrival of Convoy Dax-Varda-Seven. It is not a criminal trial. It is not a public relations exercise. It is not a substitute for engineering reality. Anyone who forgets those limits will be removed."

Her voice was soft. The room adjusted to hear it.

"Scheduling Officer Hale," she continued, "state the current operational posture."

Sivek rose. He had changed jackets since Control. The new one carried the Compact seal and no stains. His face, however, still looked like a man who had found an old crack in a pressure wall and realized it had his name scratched beside it.

"Kestrel Gate is in restricted safing. Vane Three is thermally unstable but not shedding additional debris. Power buffers remain isolated. Central telemetry has been placed under audit hold. Convoy Dax-Varda-Seven is inbound and cannot return to Varda without unacceptable fuel expenditure and passenger risk. It can delay final approach by no more than fourteen hours beyond current schedule without entering a high-casualty logistics profile."

Om looked at the countdown. "High-casualty logistics profile is a phrase. Numbers."

Sivek swallowed. "If the gate remains closed beyond the delay margin, projected mortality among convoy passengers and dependent Halcyon medical cases rises from low single digits to twelve percent within thirty days. If the convoy attempts ungated fallback burn, mortality could exceed forty percent over the return interval, assuming no drive failures."

The room received the numbers badly. Even the Ceyr attorneys stopped arranging their slates.

"Thank you," Om said, as if he had handed her a cup of water and not a ledger of possible deaths. "House Ceyr."

Ilya stood before her counsel could. "House Ceyr shares the court's concern for immediate safety. We support continued restricted safing, disciplined evidence review, and avoidance of speculative public claims that could trigger station unrest or convoy miscalculation."

"Do you support release of all bonded storage records for coolant Lot 88?"

A tiny pause. "Subject to relevance and proprietary limitation."

Om wrote something on her slate. "That is not yes."

"It is the legally possible version of yes."

"This room is full of legally possible deaths. Try again."

One of Ilya's attorneys leaned toward her. She did not look down.

"House Ceyr will release Lot 88 bonded storage records to sealed judicial partition," she said.

"Original manufacturer certificate?"

This time the attorney could not restrain himself. "Magistrate, the original certificate is subject to supplier confidentiality and chain-of-trade protection under Compact commercial article--"

"I asked House Ceyr, not its reflex." Om's eyes remained on Ilya.

Lena felt Tamsa shift behind her. Appreciation, maybe. Or hunger.

Ilya said, "We will produce what we possess."

"You possess the original certificate?"

"We possess a derivative certification."

Maro leaned toward his microphone. The amber restraint brightened.

Om turned to him. "Investigator Venn, you are under provisional access suspension. I will hear you on preservation only."

"Then preservation," Maro said. "A wafer believed to contain the original manufacturer certificate for Lot 88 was found in Pavo Irren's temporary berth. It is currently in my physical custody, sealed and unnetworked. I request immediate judicial custody and independent manufacturer-key verification."

The theater went very still.

Ilya did not turn her head, but Lena saw the muscles tighten at the side of her jaw.

Om's expression did not change. "You entered Mr. Irren's berth?"

"Yes."

"Under what authority?"

"Exigent evidence preservation after House Ceyr filed Mr. Irren as departed despite no biometric exit record and after Compact security attempted to remove me from the evidence partition on a conflict notice I had not been permitted to answer."

"That is a long authority."

"It was a long afternoon."

Someone in the upper seats made a sound and covered it as a cough.

Om said, "Produce the wafer."
