Ilya's counsel stood. "Magistrate, we object to admission of evidence obtained through trespass by a suspended investigator and accompanied by an inactive pilot and a maintenance supervisor whose own authority token is implicated in the transfer under review."

Tamsa said, "Implicated by forgery."

"Supervisor Reet," Om said, "you will be given room to be angry in sequence."

"Looking forward to it."

"Quietly."

Tamsa folded her arms.

Maro removed the shield sleeve from inside his jacket and set it on the table. A judicial clerk crossed the room with a lockbox, scanned the sleeve without touching it, then sealed it in transparent custody. Om watched the process until the clerk returned to the rail.

"The wafer is preserved," she said. "Admissibility will be determined after key verification. The existence of the wafer is now part of the record. House Ceyr, you will not retaliate against Mr. Irren, his family, his coworkers, or any person believed to have handled this object."

Ilya's face sharpened. "We have no intention of retaliating."

"Good. Then the order burdens no one."

Keva Lorn, the Halcyon deputy administrator, raised a hand. Her implants caught the theater light like thin blades.

Om nodded. "Administrator Lorn."

"Halcyon requests that all sabotage speculation remain sealed until gate safety is determined. We are managing ration compliance across nine districts after an unauthorized bulletin and environmental incident in Ceyr Quarter. Public release of partial evidence will endanger life support."

Tamsa laughed once, hard.

Om looked at her.

"Apologies," Tamsa said, without apology. "It is only that Halcyon administration calling for sealed evidence after a station-wide false bulletin is a rich nutrient medium."

Keva turned in her seat. "Your crew has already used this crisis to inflame district meetings."

"My crew has used this crisis to keep people from tearing ducts out of walls while your office calculates how many children can share a membrane bed."

"Enough," Om said.

The word was not loud. It cut anyway.

She looked from Keva to Tamsa. "Halcyon administration will produce the authorization chain for the preliminary bulletin. Supervisor Reet will produce all local maintenance logs concerning Lot 88 and Vane Three access. Both will do so within one hour. If either side edits for embarrassment, I will assume the embarrassment was evidence."

Keva sat back. Tamsa nodded once.

Lena watched Sivek. He had not reacted to the mention of the bulletin. That told her nothing. Sivek had once flown a convoy through a two-day communications blackout while a coolant leak ate through an engine-room wall. His calm could hide guilt, fear, arithmetic, or all three.

Om turned to Lena.

"Pilot Ors."

The title moved through the room in a visible wave. Inactive pilot would have been safer. Contractor would have been easier. Om had chosen the word with intent.

Lena stood.

"You were aboard Tug Larkspur during the incident."

"Yes."

"You removed the local recorder."

"Yes."

"You disobeyed Control's order to hold position."

"Yes."

"Why?"

Lena felt every prepared argument behind her teeth. The official board was wrong. The local telemetry showed drift. The seam opened. Jori was injured. All true. All partial.

"Because the gate was changing faster than Control admitted," she said, "and the tug was inside the debris fan that would exist if the change continued."

"Did your action prevent greater damage?"

"I do not know."

That answer irritated the room. People liked pilots more when they claimed certainty.

Om's eyes narrowed with something like approval. "What do you know?"

"The central board showed green while local systems recorded vane-phase correction. The correction pattern occurred just before official samples. The rehearsal pulse produced a transient aperture or aperture-like optical event along Vane Three. The failure signature does not match ordinary deferred maintenance. It is consistent with deliberate manipulation of what Control could see."

Sivek said, "Consistent is not conclusive."

Lena looked at him. "I agree."

That cost him half a prepared objection.

"But if we treat it as inconclusive in the direction of convenience," she continued, "we will open a gate using a board that has already been taught to lie."

The phrase carried. Lena wished it had not sounded memorable. Memorable things were easier to misuse.

Om made a note. "What do you recommend?"

Sivek turned sharply. "Magistrate, with respect, operational recommendations must come from certified active personnel."

"I asked what she recommends. I did not say I would obey."

Lena kept her gaze on Om. "Do not reopen on central telemetry alone. Independent local sampling at service-craft rate on all vanes. Physical inspection of Vane Three's coolant loop and field actuators. Verification of Lot 88 composition against original manufacturer key. Audit of all authority-token use related to gate service over the last thirty days. Delay the convoy to the outer margin and send a low-bandwidth warning packet that gate certification is under active review."

"A warning packet could cause convoy panic," Sivek said.

"Not sending one assumes they prefer dying surprised."

The words were too sharp. Lena knew it when Om's eyes shifted. She steadied herself.

"Captain Dax deserves operational truth," she said. "Not politics. A narrow packet: gate under restricted safing, maintain delay posture, prepare for alternate approach instructions."

Sivek's mouth tightened. "Every packet through the rehearsal carrier is monitored by Varda liability. If Varda sees uncertainty, they may invoke convoy recall authority."

"You said recall is impossible."

"Legally impossible is not physically impossible. They could order an attempt. Captains have obeyed worse orders."

There it was: not cowardice. Fear of another institution making a lethal gesture to preserve its own record. Lena hated that she understood.

Maro leaned toward his microphone again. The amber restraint pulsed.

"Magistrate, I request permission to address evidence risk."

"Briefly."

"If the convoy receives no warning and evidence later proves sabotage, every institution represented here will have preserved discretion by stealing agency from eleven thousand people. That is not safety. That is custody."

Om looked at the amber restraint around his slate. "Your conflict review concerns Glassward memory status?"

"So the notice claims."

"Do you have memory evidence relevant to this hearing?"

Maro's face went still.

Lena felt him withdraw without moving. A door closing inside his skin.

"I have personal sensory records from Control during the unauthorized bulletin," he said. "They may confirm sequence, visible reactions, and statements made in the room."

Ilya's counsel rose again. "Magistrate, Glassward fork records are notoriously prejudicial. The mere existence of authenticated sensory evidence can overwhelm witness testimony and introduce private cognitive material far beyond scope."

"A correct warning from an interested mouth," Om said. "Investigator Venn, are you willing to submit a narrow sensory extract to judicial review, excluding cognitive and emotional metadata?"

Maro did not answer immediately.

Lena remembered his words in the insurance office. Evidence can eat the people who carry it.

Tamsa leaned forward, not touching him. "We need the sequence, not your insides."

Maro's eyes flicked to her. Something human passed between them, wary and temporary.

"Yes," he said. "Narrow sensory extract only. No cognitive layer. No relational tags. No private biometric inference."

"So ordered," Om said. "Clerk, arrange Glassward-compliant extraction with independent witness. If anyone attempts to broaden the extraction without this court's order, I will treat it as assault."

Maro sat back. The amber restraint dimmed by one shade. Not gone. Less hungry.

Om turned to Sivek. "Send the narrow warning packet to Convoy Dax."

The room shifted again.

Sivek stood very still. "Magistrate, I advise against wording that suggests sabotage."

"Draft wording that suggests uncertainty."

"Uncertainty may be enough."

"It will have to be. Send it."

He bowed his head once. "Yes, Magistrate."

Ilya rose. "House Ceyr requests that the packet include affirmation that reopening remains the intended outcome."

Om considered her. "Granted, provided it also states that gate certification is under active review."

"That phrasing damages confidence."

"Then the gate should not have damaged itself in public."

Lena saw Ilya's counsel flinch. Ilya did not.

Om touched the rail again. "Orders. One: all Lot 88 records to judicial partition within one hour. Two: original wafer to independent key verification. Three: local maintenance and bulletin authorization logs to partition. Four: narrow convoy warning packet to be drafted by Compact scheduling and approved by this court within thirty minutes. Five: Investigator Venn's suspension remains in place as to unsupervised evidence access, but he is retained as a material reconstruction witness pending conflict review. Six: Pilot Ors and Supervisor Reet will remain available and will not be detained absent further order."

The lead Compact security officer near the door looked unhappy.

Om noticed. "If that order disappoints anyone's hallway plans, they may file disappointment in writing."

The hearing ended not with a gavel but with the countdown ribbon brightening.

Sixteen hours, four minutes.

People stood in clusters and began converting orders into maneuvers. Sivek spoke urgently with a Control operator. Keva Lorn sent messages with both hands. Ilya's attorneys formed a dark shell around her, but she looked through them toward Lena.

Tamsa bent close. "We got more than I expected."

"Did we?"

"The packet goes. Lot 88 comes out. Maro is not in a cell."

Maro closed his slate. "A high bar for civic triumph."

"On Halcyon, any bar above the floor is architecture."

Lena watched Sivek across the theater. He had finished with the operator and now stood alone for half a second, facing the sealed dome. His shoulders sagged in a way no one looking for villains would notice.

"Sivek knew something," she said.

Maro followed her gaze. "The Ceyr advisory."

"Maybe more. Maybe not enough. I need to ask him before he decides what version of himself is useful."

Tamsa's expression soured. "That sounds like giving him a chance to tidy up."

"It is."

"Why?"

Lena looked at the countdown. "Because if the gate opens, he still has the hand on half the locks."

Maro stood. "Then I am coming."

"You are under restraint."

"I am under many things. Most are negotiable."

They reached the aisle before Ilya's voice stopped them.

"Pilot Ors."

Lena turned.

Ilya had dismissed her attorneys with a glance. Up close, without the screen or Control's status lights, she looked almost tired. Almost.

"You were right about one thing," Ilya said. "I do not want the gate to fail."

"That leaves many sins available."

"Yes. It does." Ilya looked toward the countdown. "Ask Hale what he received from Varda before the rehearsal."

Lena's pulse changed.

"You said your advisory went to Sivek."

"It did."

"What came from Varda?"

Ilya's smile returned, faint and joyless. "A reason to hurry."

Then she walked away, leaving Lena with the first gift she had given them that looked like it might cut the giver too.

Chapter 7: Coolant Lot 88

Sivek Hale did not retreat to Control.

He went to the old guide booth above the theater, the place where projection techs had once managed stars for schoolchildren. The booth overlooked the shuttered dome through a smoked window. Its consoles were dead except for a portable scheduling board, a secure packet terminal, and a cup of tea gone cold beside a stack of printed timing sheets.

Printed. Lena stopped at the threshold when she saw them.

"You always told me paper was confession," she said.

Sivek did not look up. "Paper is also difficult to edit from a hallway."

Maro remained outside the booth with Tamsa, partly because the room was too small and partly because Lena had asked for two minutes. Tamsa had granted one with visible distrust. Maro had said nothing, which might have been worse.

The booth door closed behind Lena. The theater below had emptied into procedural noise: clerks, security, attorneys, workers, all moving under the countdown's quiet tyranny. Through the smoked window, people looked less like factions and more like particles under weak gravity.

Sivek slid one printed sheet aside. "If you came to ask whether I knew Lot 88 was compromised, the answer is no."

"I came to ask what Varda sent before the rehearsal."

His hand stopped above the scheduling board.

Lena felt the answer before he built words around it. Pilots learned to read hesitation because ships spoke through it. The tiny interval between command and correction. The half-second before a system admitted it had been failing for hours.

"Ilya told you," he said.

"Ilya told me enough to make asking useful."

"That is one of her talents."

"What did Varda send?"

He turned the secure packet terminal so she could see the header but not the body. Varda Convoy Authority. Compressed Burst Through Rehearsal Handshake. Priority: Operational. Timestamp: forty-two minutes before the pulse.

"Open it."

"This is restricted convoy command material."

"Sivek."

He closed his eyes once. When he opened them, he authorized the display.

The message was short. Burst packets favored brutality.

DAX-VARDA-SEVEN ADVANCED FINAL BURN BY 31H TO AVOID DEBRIS CASCADE V-SIDE. MEDICAL PACKETS CARRY UNSTABLE PEDIATRIC GEL REQUIRING HALCYON COLD CHAIN WITHIN 42H ARRIVAL. FUEL MARGINS BELOW PUBLIC SCHEDULE ASSUMPTION. REQUEST KESTREL REOPENING CERTIFICATION WITHOUT DELAY. VARDA LIABILITY WILL NOT AUTHORIZE RETURN BURN EXCEPT UNDER CONFIRMED GATE LOSS.

Below the text, Captain Olan Dax had added a personal line outside the formal compression block.

Do not make us choose between math and children.

Lena read the message twice.

"Medical gel," she said.

"Pediatric neural scaffold cultures. Halcyon's wards have nine children waiting. Varda added twelve more from the convoy after an outbreak on one auxiliary ship."

"Why was this not in the hearing?"

"Because Varda liability marked it restricted and because if Halcyon parents hear that nine named children are tied to a forty-two-hour cold chain, the hearing becomes a siege."

"You do not get to keep facts because people will have feelings about them."

Sivek's mouth tightened. "That is exactly what command is, Lena. Choosing which facts arrive when people can still use them."

"That is what command tells itself when it is frightened of witnesses."

He flinched. Not much. Enough.

For a moment the booth held six years between them. Not the public version, not the inquiry diagrams. The hour when Lena had commanded three ships through a collapsing wartime route and left the fourth outside the feasible envelope. The hours after, when Sivek had told her she had followed emergency doctrine and she had asked him whether doctrine had screamed when the fourth ship burned. He had not answered then.

He did now, though not to the old question.

"I was frightened," he said. "I am frightened. The gate is damaged, the convoy is early, House Ceyr sat on a risk advisory, Halcyon is two rumors from life-support sabotage, and every path from here contains dead civilians who will not care whether our evidence handling was pure."

Lena sat in the second chair because her legs had begun to feel less theoretical.

"Did you know the central telemetry was being groomed?"

"No."

"Did you suspect?"

He looked through the smoked window. "I suspected our systems were more fragile than the reopening certificate admitted. That is not the same."

"No. It is adjacent."

"Yes."

The admission did not satisfy her. It made the room heavier.

"Why rehearse on old timing after Varda advanced?"

"Because the new timing gave us less margin, not more. We needed a clean rehearsal or authority to delay would fracture across four jurisdictions. Varda would push. Ceyr would demand emergency control. Halcyon administration would accept anything that promised the medical gel. The Compact would debate until the convoy passed the outer correction line."

"So you gambled."

"I scheduled a rehearsal inside certified tolerance."

"You gambled with better nouns."

His face hardened. "Yes."

Lena had expected denial. The directness struck worse. It left her nothing to push against except the fact of him.

The booth door opened before she could answer. Tamsa stepped in without permission, followed by Maro, whose restraint slate glowed at his wrist like a patient infection.

"Minute is over," Tamsa said. "Also, your corridor security is pretending not to listen badly."

Sivek looked at Maro. "You should not be here."

"Many people have expressed that today."

Tamsa saw the packet terminal. "That Varda?"

Lena looked to Sivek.

He shut the display, but not before Tamsa caught enough. Her expression changed at medical and children.

"What children?" she asked.

Sivek rubbed both hands over his face. The gesture aged him ten years. "Pediatric neural scaffold cultures. Cold-chain deadline. Nine Halcyon cases, twelve convoy cases."

Tamsa went silent in a way Lena had not seen before.

"Names," she said.

"I do not have authority to release--"

"Names."

Sivek opened another file and transferred it to her slate. "You cannot put this on a district board."

Tamsa looked at the list. Her anger did not vanish. It found a chair, sat down, and became something more dangerous. "I know three of these families."

"That is why it was restricted."

"No. It was restricted because everyone in charge thinks love is a riot hazard."

Maro leaned over the terminal. "This message changes the motive landscape. Varda's pressure, Ceyr's advisory, Halcyon's medical need, and the false coolant record all converge before the rehearsal."

"Converge is polite," Tamsa said.

"Politeness helps documents survive court."

Lena stood and began pacing because the booth was too small for the shape forming in her head. Lot 88 rejected by Tamsa. Acceptance forged through a ghost key. Polymer trace in Vane Three. Ceyr advisory warning of supply-chain questions but framed as panic risk. Varda packet pressuring certification without delay. Unauthorized bulletin blaming maintenance. Maro suspended before he could tie records together. Pavo Irren hidden as departed.

"The sabotage was not meant to blow the gate today," she said.

Sivek looked up. "You cannot know that."

"If someone wanted a clean destruction, they had better chances. The rehearsal pulse was low power. Service craft close enough to see local anomalies. Too many witnesses."

Maro nodded slowly. "A visible incident that creates a manageable crisis."

"Managed by whom?" Tamsa asked.

Lena looked at Sivek.

He understood and recoiled. "No."

"Emergency doctrine," she said. "Gate under uncertainty. Convoy inbound. Local administration compromised. Maintenance blamed. Compact authority strained. Who gets control?"

Maro answered. "The largest liability underwriter with certified technical assets and routeholder emergency capacity."

"House Ceyr," Tamsa said.

Sivek stood. "Ceyr emergency control still requires Compact concurrence."

"Yours," Lena said.

"And magistrate review."

"Under a countdown, after a public maintenance-blame bulletin and before Lot 88 records clear verification."

He looked away.

Tamsa's voice was low. "They were building a box."

"Maybe," Maro said. "But House Ceyr alone does not explain a ghost key if the method is Directorate-derived. Nor does it explain why Irren is alive and hidden instead of dead."

"Because dead clerks attract doctors," Tamsa said. "Frightened clerks sign statements."

The secure packet terminal chimed.

All of them turned.

The narrow warning packet to Convoy Dax had been approved by Magistrate Om and sent through the gate's low-band carrier. The terminal now displayed receipt confirmation, then a compressed reply. Sivek authorized playback.

The audio was thin, stripped almost empty by bandwidth limits. A man's voice emerged through static.

"Kestrel, Dax Actual. Received restricted safing advisory. Holding delay posture at outer margin. Be advised, auxiliary Three reports coolant bloom in port thermal bus after Varda departure. We assumed local contamination. Rechecking against your Lot query. Sending trace profile. Also be advised passengers know something is wrong. You have fourteen hours before fuel chooses for us. Dax out."

The terminal displayed an attached chemical trace.

Maro was already moving. "Transfer to local."

Sivek did it without argument.

The trace resolved into a ragged spectrum. Not identical to Lot 88. But there, barely above the threshold, was the same phase-responsive polymer.

Tamsa whispered, "It is on the convoy."

Lena felt the floor tilt though spin gravity remained unchanged.

If the polymer had traveled with the convoy from Varda, Lot 88 was not just a poisoned batch at Kestrel. It was part of a supply chain that touched both sides of the route. The rehearsal incident was not local sabotage in the narrow sense. It was a corridor shaped to fail wherever institutional pressure forced people to hurry.

Sivek gripped the back of his chair. "Auxiliary Three. That ship carries one of the medical packets."

Tamsa looked down at the list of children on her slate.

No one spoke for a moment. The countdown continued below them.

Fourteen hours, fifty-eight minutes.

Maro broke the silence. "We need the manufacturer key on the original certificate."

"How fast?" Sivek asked.

"Too slow, if we ask politely through Ceyr."

Lena looked at Tamsa. "Can your people test for the polymer directly?"

"In scrubber works? Not clean enough. In gate service chemistry? Yes, if Ceyr has not sealed it."

"They will have," Maro said.

Sivek straightened. Command returned to him not as certainty but as function. "I can order access to gate service chemistry under safety authority."

"Ceyr will challenge," Maro said.

"Let them challenge while we are inside."

Tamsa stared at him. "That sounded almost useful."

"Do not spread it."

Lena moved toward the door. "We need a sample from Vane Three, Lot 88 storage, and any coolant loaded to convoy-linked medical packets before departure."

"The last is on Varda," Sivek said.

"Dax has Auxiliary Three. They can sample their bloom if we tell them how."

"Bandwidth."

Tamsa held up her slate. "A polymer test protocol can be small if you stop making it pretty."

Maro looked at her. "You can write one?"

"I can write one a frightened engineer can follow while her ship sweats poison."

The booth door opened again, this time without Tamsa's impatience. A Compact clerk stood outside, pale and breathless.

"Officer Hale. Magistrate Om requests all parties return to the theater. House Ceyr has filed an emergency petition."

Sivek closed his eyes. "For what?"

"Temporary technical control of Kestrel Gate under routeholder stabilization doctrine."

The box, Lena thought.

Not closing around them. Already closed, waiting for someone to notice the seams.

Tamsa's hand tightened around the children's list until the slate creaked. Maro saved the convoy trace to his portable recorder. Sivek gathered the printed sheets. Lena looked once through the smoked window at the theater below, where the judicial rail stood under the dead tourist dome.

The first hearing was over.

The real one had begun.

Chapter 8: Apprentice's Fear

Jori Pell had been moved out of emergency medicine and into a recovery bay with a window.

The window did not look outside. No hospital administrator on Halcyon would waste structural shielding on a view for concussed apprentices. It looked inward across the pediatric ward, through two layers of smartglass and a corridor where nurses moved with the brisk quiet of people conserving both time and alarm. Beyond the glass, nine children lay in membrane tents under soft blue lights. Their beds were arranged like small boats tied to a dock. Each tent carried a name tag, a cold-chain countdown, and a tangle of tubes thin enough to make Lena angry at the universe for requiring such delicate work.

Jori sat propped against pillows on the adult side, one arm locked in a bone-knit sling, his forehead sealed under a translucent patch. He had the pale, offended look of someone whose body had betrayed his schedule. A diagnostic halo floated above his bed and flashed warnings whenever he tried to sit higher.

Tamsa stood beside him with her hands in her pockets.

"You look awful," she said.

"Medical said I look stable."

"Medical lies to the injured."

"You lie to everyone."

"Only with purpose."

Jori smiled, then winced. The smile had pulled at the bruise along his cheek.

Lena stopped at the edge of the privacy field. She had not wanted to come. That was how she knew she had to. The boy had been in her cockpit when the gate lied. He had been injured after following her order, though she could build a dozen arguments about command authority and survival and the fact that Control had been wrong. Arguments did not mend clavicles.

Maro waited in the corridor, speaking with a nurse about witness capacity. Sivek had gone to Control to fight Ceyr's emergency petition in the language of schedules. The magistrate had granted them forty minutes before all material witnesses were summoned again. Forty minutes in a crisis felt like theft.

Tamsa looked up. "He is awake. He is stubborn. He has been told three times that testimony is voluntary."

Jori scowled. "I was told once by the nurse, once by him with the blue face-line, and once by Aunt Tamsa using the tone she uses before people volunteer to avoid more tone."

"So you understood."

"Yes."

Lena stepped through the privacy field. It hummed across her skin. "Jori."

His eyes shifted to her. For an instant she saw the cockpit again in his face: amber boards, spinning star, blood drifting bright in the air.

"Pilot Ors," he said.

"Lena is fine."

"My aunt says first names make authority lazy."

"Your aunt has many policies."

"Most are correct," Tamsa said.

Lena took the chair beside the bed only after Jori glanced at it. He noticed. Good. Fear had not made him stupid.

"How is your head?" she asked.

"Full of bells."

"Memory gaps?"

"Some. I remember the pulse. I remember you stealing my controls."

"I took your controls."

"You did not ask."

"No."

He looked at her, not accusing exactly. Measuring. "If you had asked, I would have said yes."

"I know."

"Then you could have asked."

Tamsa's expression sharpened, but Lena raised a hand before she could defend or attack.

"You are right," Lena said.

Jori blinked. He had expected doctrine, maybe apology shaped to avoid liability. Not agreement.

"I did not ask because I had already decided you were too young to spend time on consent while we were inside the debris fan," Lena continued. "That decision may have helped keep us alive. It also took something from you. Both can be true."

The diagnostic halo noted Jori's rising pulse and flashed amber. He ignored it.

"Are you always like this?"

"Bruised?"

"Annoyingly direct."

"No. Sometimes I am annoyingly silent."

Tamsa gave a short breath that might have been almost laughter.

Jori looked through the inner window at the children in membrane tents. "Aunt Tamsa says the convoy has medgel for them."

"Yes."

"And something wrong with coolant on one of the convoy ships."

Tamsa turned. "I said people were checking a coolant question."

"I am concussed, not decorative."

Lena followed his gaze. The blue-lit tents made every adult argument feel obscene. "There is a trace on one auxiliary ship that may match something found in Lot 88. We do not know yet what it means."

"Means the bad batch got on both sides."

"Maybe."

"Do they know?"

"The convoy knows enough to test."

He nodded slightly, then shut his eyes.

Tamsa stepped closer. "Jori, you do not have to do this now."

"Yes, I do."

"No."

"If I sleep, people decide what I saw."

That landed in the room with adult weight.

Maro entered carrying a small recorder and no visible pity. Lena was grateful. Pity could smother testimony as effectively as threat.

"Jori Pell," he said, "I am Maro Venn. We spoke briefly when you were in emergency care, but you were under analgesic fog and asked whether my face-line meant I had been drawn on by committee."

Jori reddened. "Sorry."

"A fair question. I have been drawn on by several committees." Maro placed the recorder on the bedside table. "Your nurse confirms you are medically capable of giving a limited voluntary statement, with breaks. Your aunt is present. Pilot Ors is present because she was on the tug and because you requested to speak where she could hear corrections if needed. You may stop at any time."

"Will stopping make people say I lied?"

"People may say many things. Stopping will not make them true."

"That is not reassuring."

"It is accurate."

Jori considered him. "I like you better than reassuring people."

"It fades."

Tamsa pulled the privacy field tighter and darkened the inner glass. The children became soft shapes in blue instead of witnesses to a witness.

Maro activated the recorder. "Begin with your assignment on Tug Larkspur."

Jori inhaled carefully. "I was assigned pilot cradle for service support during rehearsal pulse. Not command pilot. Utility cradle. Mostly station-keeping, clamp readiness, feed checks. Pilot Ors was assigned calibration cabinet."

"Had you performed that assignment before?"

"Four times in sim. Twice live during pre-pulse tests. Never during actual rehearsal."

"Who cleared you?"

His eyes flicked to Tamsa.

She said nothing.

"Crew Lead Sann cleared me. Aunt Tamsa argued because she argues with weather. I was qualified."

Lena heard the plea inside the last sentence. Not to be treated as a child who had wandered into danger. To have earned the danger properly.

Maro said, "What did you observe before the alarm?"

"Normal board. Tug jitter from service arm. Pilot Ors complaining at the cabinet without words."

"How does someone complain without words?"

"Shoulders."

Lena said, "Accurate."

Jori's mouth twitched. "Then the local vane tone came in wrong. Like one of the old pumps when the bearing is about to decide philosophy."

Tamsa closed her eyes briefly. That was a family phrase, then.

"I asked Control to confirm training artifact. Pilot Ors overrode and said local disagreed with central."

"Before that tone," Maro said, "did you see anything outside the tug?"

Jori's right hand moved under the blanket, searching for a gesture his injured arm could not complete. "A service drone."

The room tightened.

"Describe it," Maro said.

"Small. Vane-crawler class, I think. But not one of ours."

Tamsa leaned in. "How do you know?"

"Ours limp."

"Jori."

"They do. The old crawlers have a lag in the rear left magfoot because Works Two keeps trading us refurbished joint stacks. This one moved clean."

Lena felt the shape from the outline harden into evidence. "Where was it?"

"Near Vane Three inner service track. It came from shadow behind the radiator baffle, crossed to the coolant coupling, then stopped when Larkspur's nose light caught it."

"Did it carry markings?" Maro asked.

"No paint. Matte shell. I saw a maintenance beacon for maybe half a second."

"Color?"

"White."

Tamsa frowned. "Service drones use green on Halcyon."

"Compact inspection uses blue," Lena said.

Maro said, "House Ceyr?"

"Gold," Tamsa and Lena said together, then glanced at each other.

Jori swallowed. The halo flashed again. "White is quarantine or dead-system handling, right?"

"Sometimes," Lena said. "Also Directorate equipment during the war."

Tamsa looked at her sharply.

Jori's voice thinned. "I thought I imagined that part."

Maro leaned closer, still not touching the bed. "What part?"

"There was a mark on the shell. Not paint. Like it had been filed off and left a raised edge. A circle with a cut through it."

Maro's eyes went to Lena.

Quiet Mark. The symbol was not official. That was why it had traveled so well. During the last years of the Fracture Wars, some Directorate field equipment had carried a manufacturing stamp used only on deniable hardware: a broken ring etched by toolpath rather than printed code. Most people who recognized it had learned to pretend they did not.

Tamsa saw their faces. "What?"

Lena said, "A dissolved security apparatus that keeps being dissolved at us."

Jori breathed too fast. The halo turned orange.

Tamsa touched the bed rail. "Break."

"No."

"That was not a request."

Jori looked at her with sudden anger. "If I stop now, you will all leave and decide how brave I was allowed to be."

Tamsa's face changed. Hurt first, then control over hurt. "You are alive. That is the first bravery I require."

"It is not enough."

Lena heard herself at seventeen in that sentence. The dangerous hunger to be counted before age, rank, or fear could make a person invisible.

"Jori," she said, "testimony is not payment for being alive."

He turned on her. "Easy for you. Everyone listens when you say a thing."

Tamsa actually laughed this time, sharp and incredulous.

Lena accepted the blow. "People hear reputation before they hear me. That is not the same."

"But they hear."

"Yes."

"Then help them hear me."

There it was. Not courage as performance. A demand.

Maro watched Lena with professional stillness, leaving the choice where it belonged. Tamsa looked ready to refuse on behalf of blood and love. The children behind the darkened glass breathed through machines that cared nothing for witness capacity.

Lena nodded. "All right. We help them hear you accurately. Not bigger. Not smaller."

Jori's breathing slowed by effort. The halo retreated to amber.

Maro resumed. "After you saw the drone, what happened?"

"It moved away when the alarm tone split. Not back along the service track. It dropped."

"Dropped?"

"Like it let go of the vane."

Tamsa said, "A crawler would drift."

"It did not drift. It fired a cold puff and went under the vane lip. Then the pulse came and I lost it."

Lena pictured the geometry. A drone under the vane lip, shielded from Larkspur, near the coolant coupling. A cold-gas maneuver would not show as active drive on distant scans if the system was busy looking at rehearsal noise.

"Could it have attached something?" she asked.

Jori frowned. "Maybe. It was already near the coupling when I saw it. But there was a glint."

"Where?"

"On the service track behind it. Like a washer or shaving. It spun when the nose light hit."

Maro made a note. "Physical fragment."

Tamsa said, "If it is still there after the pulse."

"If no one retrieved it," Lena said.

The same thought reached all of them at once.

Maro turned off the recorder. "We need an exterior inspection."

"Ceyr's emergency petition is still active," Tamsa said. "They will lock every gate service route if Om lets them."

"Then we go before Om rules."

Tamsa stared at him. "You just proposed unauthorized exterior work near a damaged gate."

"No. I proposed urgent preservation of physical evidence before a party under suspicion obtains technical control. The exterior work is merely how reality insists it be done."

Jori closed his eyes. "I want to go."

"Absolutely not," Tamsa said.

"I know where I saw it."

"You have a fractured clavicle and a head full of bells."

"I can mark the track on a map."

"That you can do."

Lena pulled the local vane schematic onto the bedside slate. The official drawing loaded first, clean and useless. Tamsa overlaid her maintenance map with the undocumented camera shadows and service-light dead zones. Jori leaned forward too fast, hissed in pain, then pointed with his good hand.

"There," he said. "No. Closer to the baffle. Larkspur was yawed two degrees after Pilot Ors told me. The light cone would hit from here."

His finger trembled. Lena adjusted the angle.

"There," he said.

The mark landed just outside a certified inspection route, in a maintenance blind pocket between the coolant coupling and a radiator support strut.

Tamsa made a low sound. "That pocket is a trash catcher."

"Debris?" Maro asked.

"Every shaving, seal splinter, and lost bolt in that quadrant likes to end up there. If your glint survived, it could still be wedged."

Lena saved the coordinate. "Who can suit fastest?"

Tamsa's eyes narrowed. "Not you."

"I have exterior gate hours."

"You have bruised ribs and a talent for getting teenagers hurt."

The sentence hit harder because Tamsa regretted it as soon as it left. Jori opened his eyes.

"Aunt."

Lena said, "Fair."

"No," Tamsa said, jaw tight. "Not fair. Useful maybe. Not fair."

The privacy field chimed. A nurse stepped through with the expression of someone prepared to sedate authority itself.

"Statement is over," she said. "The patient is done."

"I am not," Jori objected.

"Your pulse says otherwise. Your pulse has legal standing in this ward."

Tamsa looked relieved enough to hide it poorly. "You heard medical."

Maro collected the recorder. "Jori Pell, your statement has been preserved. You may amend later. You did well."

Jori made a face. "That sounded reassuring."

"I apologize."

Lena stood. "Thank you."

He caught her sleeve with his good hand before she could step away. His grip was weak. He hated that too.

"Do not let them make me the reason," he said.

"The reason for what?"

"For stopping. For opening. For whatever they already want."

Lena looked at the children behind the darkened glass, at Tamsa's rigid shoulders, at Maro's portable recorder with the boy's fear and precision sealed inside it.

"No," she said. "You are evidence. Not permission."

Jori released her sleeve.

In the corridor, the countdown display had been mirrored onto the hospital wall.

Thirteen hours, twenty-six minutes.

Tamsa walked beside Lena without speaking until the lift doors closed around them. Then she hit the stop bar between floors.

The lift halted with a sigh.

"I need one minute," Tamsa said.

Maro looked at the emergency panel, then at Lena, then at Tamsa. "I will face the wall."

"That is not necessary."

"No, but it is procedurally elegant."

He turned around.

Tamsa gripped the rail. Her breathing shook once, twice, then steadied. Lena said nothing. Silence, for once, was not evasion.

"When he was eight," Tamsa said, "Jori took apart a district fan because he thought it sounded lonely. Not broken. Lonely. Flooded the corridor with pollen from the nursery and made three administrators sneeze through a budget meeting."

Lena waited.

"His mother died in the blockade winter. His father is on a cargo chain somewhere between Pall and debt. I taught him tools because tools answer when adults do not." She looked at Lena. "If this eats him, I will not become reasonable."

"I would not ask."

"You might."

Lena considered lying kindly. "I might. If the gate needs him."

Tamsa's eyes flashed.

"And you should hate me for it," Lena said. "But I will know what I am asking."

The lift seemed very small.

Maro turned back around slowly. "For what it is worth, the strongest testimony often comes from witnesses who are protected from becoming symbols."

Tamsa barked without humor. "Do you sell that line by the hour?"

"Usually. This one was free."

She restarted the lift.

When the doors opened on the service level, a message waited on all three of their consoles. Magistrate Om had delayed ruling on Ceyr's emergency petition for seventy minutes. During that delay, no party could alter gate service hardware without court notice.

Seventy minutes.

Lena looked at the coordinate Jori had marked.

"We need a lawful reason to go outside," Maro said.

Tamsa wiped her face with both hands, and when they came away the maintenance supervisor had returned.

"No," she said. "We need a broken thing in exactly the right place."

Chapter 9: Maps for Workers

The right broken thing was a radiator flutter alarm on Vane Three's auxiliary heat curtain.

It had been active for eleven months.

In a well-funded gate yard, the alarm would have triggered a service ticket, a drone inspection, and a replacement hinge assembly before the next scheduled opening. On Halcyon, it had become part of the weather. The flutter appeared during thermal swings, complained into a maintenance queue no one had parts to satisfy, and was acknowledged twice a week by workers who knew the difference between a dying system and a dramatic one.

Tamsa promoted it from weather to emergency with three lines of code and a great deal of moral confidence.

"Is that legal?" Maro asked, watching over her shoulder in Scrubber Works Three.

"The alarm is real."

"That is not exactly an answer."

"It is exactly the part of the answer that survives audit."

Lena stood at the central worktable while the life-support crew gathered around. Twenty-three workers had answered Tamsa's call: scrubber technicians, drone wranglers, coolant plumbers, two hull patchers, a retired vane rigger with one artificial leg, and the twelve-year-old screw sorter whose name turned out to be Niko and whose official purpose in the room was bringing tea no one drank. Their coveralls were stained, patched, and marked with district bands. Their faces held the same expression Lena had seen on pilots before a bad approach: fear made disciplined by the insult of having no better option.

On the wall, Tamsa projected Jori's marked coordinate over the official vane schematic.

The official schematic was beautiful. Uselessly beautiful. It presented Vane Three as a clean engineered object with certified access paths, standard couplings, drone tracks, and hazard zones shaded in polite colors. Tamsa's overlay made it honest. Here were hand-labeled dead cameras, pressure-shadow pockets, old patch plates, nonstandard cable loops, and a warning near the auxiliary heat curtain that read DO NOT TRUST BLUE HANDLE; BLUE HANDLE TRUSTS NO ONE.

Lena studied the room before she spoke. Pilots guarded geometry like priesthood because gate math could kill when half-understood. Routeholders had built entire hierarchies out of that danger. They were not entirely wrong. That was the problem. Half-knowledge near a meridian gate was not liberation. It was another way to become debris.

But no knowledge had also become a weapon.

She picked up a marker.

"I am going to explain only what you need for the inspection," she said. "If I simplify, ask. If I make something sound mystical, assume I am hiding ignorance or habit. Gate geometry is hard. It is not sacred."

The retired rigger with the artificial leg grunted. "Tell that to the people who charge by the blessing."

"I have."

"And?"

"They charged more."

A ripple moved through the room. Not laughter exactly. A loosening.

Lena drew a line from the vane's inner edge to the gate throat. "During a rehearsal pulse, the vanes shape a field that never becomes a full corridor. Think of it as stress testing the question without accepting the answer. Vane Three drifted here." She marked the phase zone. "Local systems corrected here. Official central sampling looked here, too late to see the drift."

A coolant plumber named Doss raised a hand. "Corrected by actuator or field trim?"

"Both, likely. The polymer trace suggests coolant behavior nudged field trim, and the system compensated mechanically."

"Coolant cannot command field trim."

"No. But it can change the conditions the trim system reads."

Doss frowned. "Like lying to a thermostat with a match."

"Yes, if the thermostat steers a city."

Niko whispered, "Bad thermostat."

Tamsa pointed at him without looking. "Tea duties do not include commentary."

"Yes, Supervisor."

Lena enlarged the blind pocket Jori had marked. "A drone was seen here. It moved under the vane lip before the pulse. There may be a fragment in this debris catcher. We need eyes, physical retrieval, and residue sampling."

The drone wrangler, a thin woman with silver hair braided through a sensor band, folded her arms. "Ceyr sealed all gate service drones after the petition."

"Halcyon drones?" Maro asked.

"Two working, one philosophical."

"Meaning?"

"It moves when observed and fails when needed."

Tamsa said, "We are not sending drones first."

The room went quiet.

Lena looked at her. "You said we needed a broken thing."

"We do. Radiator flutter alarm justifies exterior inspection. If we send a drone, Ceyr can claim hardware alteration. If we send suited workers to verify a preexisting mechanical hazard under court notice, they can object loudly while we are already outside."

"Who?"

Tamsa gestured to the retired rigger. "Pellam has the most vane hours."

Pellam tapped his artificial leg. "Had."

"Still have the hours. Leg is newer than your jokes."

"Everything is newer than my jokes."

"Doss for coolant residue. Sann for rig safety. I go because I signed the alarm escalation."

"No," Lena said.

The word came out too fast. Too much like Tamsa in the hospital.

Tamsa's eyes sharpened. "Interesting."

"You are needed inside."

"I am needed where my people are."

"You have not worked exterior vane in years."

"And you have?"

"Yes."

"While inactive by sanction?"

The room absorbed that. Most already knew who Lena was. Hearing the status attached to practical risk made it heavier.

Lena set the marker down. "I am qualified for exterior gate geometry. I know what not to touch."

"You are injured."

"So is half this station."

Pellam leaned against the table. "If the pilot can still read a phase shadow, I would rather have her outside than watching us from a screen and swearing too late."

Tamsa turned on him. "Helpful."

"That's why you keep me."

Maro spoke from near the wall. "There is also evidence integrity. Pilot Ors as independent technical witness outside, Supervisor Reet as authorizing maintenance officer inside, and my recorder as legal witness. That gives the retrieval a better chance of surviving challenges."

"Your recorder is under restraint," Tamsa said.

"My recorder remains less restrained than common sense in this matter."

Lena looked at Tamsa. "You can stop me for family reasons or operational ones. Pick the honest objection."

Tamsa's face closed.

That was unfair. Lena knew it and did not withdraw. Time had made all of them cruel in practical shapes.

After a moment Tamsa said, "Operationally, I need someone outside who can identify whether the fragment is gate hardware, drone hardware, or trash. Family reasons remain available if you get Jori killed by extension."

"Accepted."

"Do not accept like that makes it noble."

"It makes it clear."

Tamsa looked away first. "Suit roster: Pellam, Doss, Sann, Ors. I stay inside on command link. Drone wrangler Fei runs tether cam. Maro records from lock."

The crew began moving. Tools came out of lockers. A retrieval capsule was packed with evidence sleeves, microsponges, residue vials, a field camera, and three old-fashioned mechanical tags that could be scratched by gloved hand if electronics failed. Niko distributed tea anyway and was ignored with gratitude.

Lena followed Tamsa to the side console. "You have to file court notice."

"Already drafting."

"Show me."

Tamsa angled the slate. The notice was short, factual, and shaped like a spanner dropped on a lawyer's foot.

PREEXISTING RADIATOR FLUTTER ALARM ON VANE THREE HAS ESCALATED UNDER CURRENT THERMAL CONDITIONS. HALCYON MAINTENANCE WILL CONDUCT EXTERIOR VISUAL INSPECTION AND NONINVASIVE DEBRIS DOCUMENTATION. NO GATE SERVICE HARDWARE WILL BE ALTERED WITHOUT FURTHER ORDER. TIME-SENSITIVE DUE TO CONVOY SAFETY.

"Noninvasive debris documentation," Lena said.

"If the debris enters a sealed capsule of its own free will, who are we to refuse?"

Maro glanced over. "Please never testify exactly that way."

The notice went out to Magistrate Om, Compact safety, House Ceyr, and Halcyon administration. Ceyr objected in forty-two seconds. Om responded in seventy.

INSPECTION MAY PROCEED. RETRIEVAL OF LOOSE DEBRIS PERMITTED IF DOCUMENTED CONTINUOUSLY. NO SURFACE ENTRY OR HARDWARE REMOVAL.

Tamsa displayed the order to the room. "We have permission to pick up trash."

Pellam grinned. "A career pinnacle."

The suit lock for exterior vane work was on the service rim, three tram stops and one security argument away. By the time Lena reached it, Ceyr had filed two supplemental objections, Keva Lorn had requested that inspection footage be delayed from public release, and Sivek had sent a private message consisting of three words: Be very exact.

She almost replied. Then did not.

The suit tech assigned her a medium hard-shell with additional rib bracing. It smelled of disinfectant and old panic. Lena stepped into it one limb at a time, letting muscle memory take what dignity it could. The shoulder seal hurt. The pain patch blurred it. She checked wrist jets, tether locks, helmet feed, glove sensitivity, boot magnets, emergency foam, suit clock.

Pellam suited beside her with the slow competence of someone whose body had renegotiated with vacuum and won enough. Doss muttered chemical inventories under her breath. Sann, the crew lead who had cleared Jori, said nothing at all.

Lena watched him through the reflection in her visor. He was broad, young for a crew lead, with tired eyes and hands that kept checking the same tether clasp.

"Sann," she said on local.

He looked up.

"You cleared Jori because he was qualified."

His jaw tightened. "You do not know that."

"I read his handling before the pulse."

"Handling is not certification."

"No. It is evidence that certification meant something."

He swallowed. "Tamsa blames herself. So I blame me. It saves time."

"Blame is not maintenance."

"People keep saying pretty things today."

"That one is ugly and true."

The lock alarm interrupted before he could answer.

Maro appeared outside the suit line, recorder mounted at his collar. "Continuous record active. Judicial notice confirmed. House Ceyr objection logged. Compact safety monitoring."

"Comforting," Pellam said.

"It was not intended to be."

Tamsa's voice entered the suit channel from interior command. "All exterior crew, comm check."

One by one they answered.

"Ors?"

"Clear."

A pause. Then, quieter on a private sideband: "You know the difference between useful risk and old guilt?"

Lena sealed her helmet. The world narrowed to suit air and her own breathing.

"I can usually identify it afterward."

"Bad answer."

"Honest one."

"Do not make me explain you to my nephew."

"I will try not to."

The outer lock cycled.

Vacuum took sound first, then the idea of ordinary distance. The service rim opened beneath Kestrel's star, and Vane Three lay ahead like a black blade wounded by light. Halcyon Ring curved behind them, patched and crowded and alive. Far beyond, invisible except as numbers on boards and fear in people's mouths, the convoy was falling toward them with contaminated coolant and children whose names Tamsa now carried in her slate.

Lena stepped onto the exterior track.

Her boots caught.

The suit translated contact into a tone, solid and low. She took another step. Around her, the maintenance crew emerged in a line, tethers glinting. The radiator heat curtain trembled faintly at the edge of sight, fluttering for the first time in eleven months with political importance.

"Interior, exterior team clear of lock," Pellam said. "Proceeding to Vane Three auxiliary heat curtain."

Tamsa answered, "Copy. Cameras clean. Ceyr is already asking whether Ors is touching anything."

Lena looked at the dark length of the vane and the blind pocket where Jori had seen a drone drop under the lip.

"Tell them," she said, "not yet."

Chapter 10: The Edited Hour

The exterior of Kestrel Gate did not forgive scale.

From inside Halcyon, the vanes were icons on status boards, long black shapes rendered with labels and tolerance bands. From the service track, Vane Three was a continent turned on edge. It rose beside Lena in a blade of carbon-black lattice and embedded superconductive veins, its inner surface drinking Kestrel's light until the stars around it looked sharper in protest. Heat shimmered where the rehearsal pulse had wounded it. The suit translated radiation warnings into restrained clicks, as if politeness could make exposure cumulative instead of fatal.

Pellam led the line with the confidence of a man who trusted his tether because he had inspected the person who inspected it. Sann followed him, every motion exact. Doss came third, carrying the residue kit against her chest. Lena brought up the rear, where she could see all three workers and the curve of Halcyon behind them.

"Exterior team at heat curtain approach," Pellam said. "Flutter visible."

The auxiliary heat curtain was a segmented foil-and-ceramic sheet stretched along the vane's service flank. It trembled at one corner in the slow rhythm of thermal strain. The movement was tiny against the structure's size, barely more than a shiver. Enough for a legal maintenance notice. Enough to put human eyes near Jori's blind pocket.

Tamsa's voice came over the command channel. "Interior copies. Court observer has inspection feed. Ceyr has filed an objection to the angle of Camera Two."

"Camera Two has a better lawyer than I do," Maro said from the lock.

Pellam chuckled. "Tell Camera Two to bill them."

Lena kept her eyes on the service track. The closer they moved to Jori's coordinate, the more the official access path became fiction. The certified rail ended at a handhold whose left side had been scarred by an old micrometeor strike. Someone had welded a supplemental grip below it, then added a caution tag whose print had faded to a ghost. A cluster of cable loops crossed the next two meters in defiance of the clean schematic. Beyond them, under the vane lip, a shadowed recess opened between the coolant coupling housing and a radiator support strut.

The blind pocket.

"Hold," Lena said.

The team stopped.

Pellam looked back. "Seeing something?"

"Geometry."

"That sounds expensive."

"It usually is."

She anchored her boots, raised her left hand, and overlaid Jori's marked light cone on her visor. The suit built a pale triangle through memory and measurement: Larkspur's yaw angle, nose light spread, Vane Three's edge, the place a frightened apprentice had pointed from a hospital bed. The triangle touched the blind pocket and spilled over a ridged strip of service track.

"Jori's glint would have been here," she said. "Not inside the pocket. At the lip."

Doss leaned carefully, tether taut. "I have loose particulate along the ridge."

"Do not touch yet," Maro said. "Camera confirm?"

Fei, the drone wrangler inside, shifted Camera Two despite Ceyr's objection. The feed brightened across Lena's visor. There: a fleck caught between two ridges, no larger than a clipped fingernail, rotating slightly under electrostatic tremor.

Tamsa said, "I see it."

Pellam whistled. "Ugly little thing."

"Ugly little things built most of history," Doss said.

Sann extended a stabilizing arm and locked it against the track. "Retrieval path clear."

Lena checked the surrounding surface. No obvious charge traps. No fresh scorch. No active coolant leak. The pocket itself was darker than it should have been, not from shadow alone but from a film along the inner curve.

"Doss," she said, "residue on the pocket wall."

"I see it."

"Sample before debris retrieval. If the fragment moves, we still have chemistry."

"Agreed."

Doss unfolded a microspoon and sponge assembly with the slow grace of someone handling a sleeping infant beside an open furnace. She touched the dark film once, rotated the sponge, sealed it in a vial. The vial's indicator flickered as it took a preliminary read.

"Polymer presence," Doss said. Her voice had lost all humor. "Low concentration. Same family marker."

Inside command, someone swore off-channel and failed to keep it off-channel enough.

Tamsa said, "Documented. Continue."

The fleck did not want to be evidence. It clung to the ridge, skittered when Doss's tool approached, caught on a static edge, and nearly escaped into the pocket before Pellam brought a grounded tag beside it. The fleck snapped to the tag with a tiny movement that Lena felt in her teeth more than saw. Doss sealed both inside a transparent capsule.

"Loose debris retrieved," Maro said. "Continuous record confirms no surface entry."

"Ceyr says the grounding tag counts as surface interaction," Fei reported.

Tamsa replied, "Ceyr can marry a definition and be unhappy with it."

"Please do not put that in the official log," Maro said.

"Already didn't."

Lena moved closer to the coolant coupling. Her suit alarm clicked faster, warning of residual heat. The coupling housing bore the serial marks she expected: manufacturer, retrofit, Halcyon inspection, Compact recertification. Under those, near the lower seam, a patch of surface had been abraded. Not by heat. By a tool.

"Camera Two, zoom here."

The image tightened.

At first the abrasion looked random. Then the light angle shifted, and a broken ring appeared in raised outline where material had been filed around it rather than over it.

Quiet Mark.

No one spoke.

Lena felt the old war move through the channel like a pressure change. Some of the workers would not know the mark. Some would know stories. Sivek, if he was monitoring, would know too much.

Maro's voice came quietly. "Documenting suspected deniable-manufacture stamp, broken-ring profile, located near Vane Three coolant coupling."

Pellam said, "I thought those people were dissolved."

"They dissolve like sugar," Doss said. "Still in the water."

A private channel opened in Lena's ear. Sivek.

"Do not touch the mark."

"I know."

"No, Lena. Do not even gesture near it. Directorate hardware triggers legacy evidence protocols. If Ceyr can argue contamination by unauthorized handling, they will bury it under security classification."

"Then get Om to seal it before Ceyr does."

"Working."

The channel closed.

Lena looked out beyond the vane, toward the invisible path the convoy would take if the gate survived them all. Somewhere out there, Auxiliary Three was testing its own coolant bloom with a protocol Tamsa had stripped down to a handful of steps. Somewhere in Halcyon's hospital, Jori Pell had either fallen asleep or was pretending to, failing both.

"Interior," Lena said, "we have physical evidence of an unregistered drone or hardware contact, polymer residue, and suspected Directorate manufacturing mark. Recommend immediate judicial seal of Vane Three coupling zone."

Tamsa answered, "Om is receiving. Ceyr is attempting to classify the feed."

"On what basis?"

Maro's voice cooled. "Legacy security contamination."

"That did not take long."

"No."

Pellam shifted his weight on the track. "Can we leave now? My jokes are aging faster out here."

The suit telemetry flashed. A vibration moved through Lena's boots, too faint for a structural alarm, too patterned for random thermal chatter.

"Hold," she said.

The vibration came again. Three pulses. Pause. Two. Pause. Three.

Not from the vane. From the service track behind them.

Sann turned first. "Movement."

A maintenance drone detached from the shadow under the radiator hinge.

For half a second Lena's mind refused it. The thing had been too still, tucked against a strut where the heat curtain's flutter hid its profile. Then it unfolded six magnetic feet and moved with clean, silent precision.

No Halcyon limp.

"Drone on track," Pellam said, voice suddenly flat. "Ten meters aft."

Tamsa: "No authorized drone deployment."

The drone's beacon flashed white.

Lena saw Sann reach for his tether cutter by reflex and shouted, "Don't."

The drone fired a cold-gas puff.

It did not attack them. It launched itself toward the blind pocket, toward the mark, toward the residue that could convict the hour. A retrieval arm extended from its underside, needle-thin and bright.

Pellam moved with impossible speed for a man with an artificial leg. He kicked off the service track, tether snapping taut, and swung the grounding tag assembly like a hammer. It struck the drone's side and sent it spinning, but the drone clamped two feet to the vane lip and held. Doss flattened herself against the track. Sann anchored one arm around her tether and fired a wrist jet to keep them both from drifting into the heat curtain.

Lena launched.

Not far. Not dramatically. A half-meter push across the track, one boot magnet released, gloved hand out. The drone's arm sliced across her suit forearm and scraped ceramic. Her suit shrieked about abrasion. She caught the drone by its rear frame and felt it pull with insect strength.

"Ors!" Tamsa shouted.

"Do not cut power," Lena said. "If it dies, it may wipe."

Maro: "Can you restrain?"

"Defining can."

The drone twisted. Its feet released and reattached in a wave, dragging Lena's shoulder against the vane housing. Pain went white across her ribs. She clamped her other hand around a service loop and held the drone away from the coupling mark. It vibrated, trying to shed her grip, then opened a tiny port along its flank.

"It is venting," Doss said.

Not venting. Spraying.

A mist of solvent jetted toward the abraded mark.

Sann threw his body between the spray and the coupling. The solvent hit his suit chest and froze into glittering rime. Alarms barked across the channel.

"Sann suit contamination," Fei reported. "Outer layer only. No breach."

Pellam recovered his tether and drove a clamp around the drone's front legs. "I have two feet."

"I have rear frame," Lena said through clenched teeth.

"Doss?" Tamsa's voice cut hard.

"I have the evidence capsule. Residue vial secure. Sann is swearing, so alive."

Sann said something that proved her point.

The drone stopped fighting.

That was worse.

Maro said, "Shield your feeds. It may attempt burst transmission."

Lena saw the white beacon brighten.

"Fei, jam local," Tamsa ordered.

"With what? The drone wrangler that thinks about movement?"

"Use the philosophical one."

For one absurd second, no one spoke.

Then Fei said, "Deploying."

From the service lock, Halcyon's malfunctioning third drone lurched into view. It was boxy, scarred, and asymmetrical, with one manipulator folded the wrong way and a diagnostic light that blinked amber in an apparently random pattern. It moved badly. It moved late. It moved straight into the white drone's line of transmission and opened every test channel it had.

Static flooded Lena's suit. Her visor filled with meaningless handshake requests, obsolete maintenance pings, nursery humidity logs, a music file from someone's private cache, and a diagnostic confession that Drone H-3 had lost confidence in its left motor.

The white beacon sputtered.

Pellam laughed like a man who had just won money from physics. "Philosophy prevails."

"Focus," Tamsa snapped, but Lena heard the relief under it.

The white drone locked rigid. Maro's voice returned through the static, broken but clear enough.

"Do not power it down. Isolate. Bag if possible. That drone is not just cleanup. It responded to evidence exposure."

"We cannot bag active hardware on the track," Pellam said.

Lena looked at the drone, then at the service loop under her hand, then at the heat curtain's trembling edge. "We can take the section."

Sivek cut in. "No hardware removal. Court order forbids surface entry or hardware removal."

"The drone is clamped to the hardware."

"Do not be clever with a magistrate's wording while hanging off a damaged vane."

Maro said, "Emergency preservation doctrine permits removal of foreign active hardware if continued operation threatens evidence integrity."

"You are under restraint," Sivek said.

"The doctrine is not."

Tamsa overrode them both. "Exterior team, secure drone in place. Om is on the channel."

Magistrate Om's voice entered with no preamble. "I have seen enough. Foreign active hardware is judicial evidence. Removal authorized if necessary to preserve evidence and crew safety. No alteration beyond restraint and transport. House Ceyr objection noted and denied."

Ilya's voice followed, colder than before. "House Ceyr requests identification of the hardware before any movement."

Om said, "House Ceyr may request many things. Exterior team, proceed."

Pellam and Sann rigged a restraint bag around the white drone while Doss held the evidence capsule against her suit as if it were a heart outside her body. Lena kept one hand on the rear frame until the bag sealed. The drone twitched once inside, then went still.

Only after it was tethered for transport did she realize her suit forearm alarm had gone from amber to red.

Tamsa noticed at the same moment. "Ors, report abrasion."

"Outer ceramic scored. No pressure loss."

"Return now."

"We have not inspected the radiator flutter."

"Lena."

Her first name on an open channel did what orders had not. She looked at the black vane, the mark, the solvent rime on Sann's chest, the bagged drone that had come alive exactly when evidence became undeniable.

"Exterior team returning," she said.

The path back to the lock felt longer. The philosophical drone drifted beside them, still babbling interference into dead channels like a drunk prophet. Halcyon grew larger in Lena's visor until it filled half the sky: patched radiators, cargo splints, windows, hidden hospitals, ration boards, rooms where people argued because argument meant air still moved.

Inside the lock, pressure returned with a roar. Sound found them in pieces. Suit techs swarmed. Tamsa was there before Lena's helmet came off.

"Arm," Tamsa said.

"No breach."

"Arm."

Lena extended it. The suit's forearm plating bore a clean diagonal cut through the outer ceramic and into the second layer. A centimeter deeper and the drone would have opened her to vacuum.

Tamsa stared at it, then at Lena. "You are impossible to explain."

"You said not to make you explain me to Jori. I did not."

"Do not use my own threats as comfort."

Maro had the drone bag transferred into a judicial lockbox under Om's remote seal. The fleck capsule and residue vial went into separate boxes. Doss refused to release the residue until the clerk repeated the custody phrase correctly. Sann submitted to decontamination while cursing at the solvent rime in three dialects.

Pellam sat on a bench and removed his helmet. His face was gray.

"You hurt?" Lena asked.

"Old." He looked toward the lockbox. "That thing waited."

"Yes."

"Machines do not wait unless someone gives them a reason."

Maro approached with his recorder still active. "Or unless waiting is the reason."

Tamsa's console chimed. She opened the message and went still.

"What?" Lena asked.

Tamsa turned the slate.

Maro's suspended evidence access had been restored for one file category only: central safety sensor audit. Magistrate Om's order sat above it.

Below that, a result from Maro's earlier partition request had arrived.

SAFETY SENSOR CHAIN: VANE THREE
STATUS: INCOMPLETE
MISSING SEGMENT: 00:57:12
WINDOW: ONE HOUR PRIOR TO REHEARSAL PULSE

Maro read it once. His expression changed less than anyone else's, which told Lena how bad it was.

"The edited hour," she said.

He nodded. "Someone cut the gate blind before the drone moved."

Chapter 11: Debt Ration

Halcyon announced the ration adjustment ten minutes after the exterior team returned.

It was a bad time to announce anything, which meant the timing was deliberate or incompetent. Lena had known institutions capable of both in the same breath. The notice appeared first on district boards, then on wrist consoles, then across the thin entertainment strips in waiting rooms, tram stops, market bays, and sleep-stack corridors. It used the gentlest possible language for making people poorer in air.

TEMPORARY LIFE-SUPPORT CREDIT REALLOCATION
MEDICAL PRIORITY AND CONVOY READINESS PROTOCOL
RESIDENTIAL FAN CYCLES TO REDUCE BY 6 PERCENT
NONESSENTIAL FABRICATION SUSPENDED
NURSERY AND PEDIATRIC WARDS REMAIN FULL PRIORITY
COMPLIANCE PRESERVES HALCYON

Someone in Scrubber Works Three threw a wrench at the board hard enough to crack the corner.

Tamsa did not reprimand them. She walked to the board, removed the wrench, set it on a bench, and said, "Next person who damages a display has to explain to District Four why their shortage appeal vanished with the glass."

"They cut our fans," Doss said. She had not changed out of her suit liner. A red band marked one cheek where the helmet seal had pressed too long. "After blaming us. After sending us outside."

"Yes."

"Say something better."

"I do not have something better."

The room was full now. Exterior crew, scrubber techs, district leads, two nurses, a cargo representative, Fei with grease up both arms from recovering the philosophical drone, and Maro standing in the corner with three judicial lockbox receipts. Lena leaned against a tool cabinet because her ribs had begun filing formal complaints. A medic had sealed her forearm scrape and ordered two hours of rest. She had accepted the seal.

Rest was under review.

The ration notice kept scrolling.

Keva Lorn appeared beneath it in recorded form, face composed, voice warm enough to curdle.

"Residents of Halcyon, today's gate incident and subsequent safety review require disciplined conservation. These measures are temporary. They protect the vulnerable, preserve convoy readiness, and demonstrate to Compact and routeholder partners that Halcyon remains capable of shared sacrifice. We ask every resident to reject rumor, avoid unauthorized gatherings, and trust the emergency allocation process."

The recording looped.

"Trust," Pellam said. He had a blanket around his shoulders and contempt in every syllable. "Cheap word. No shipping cost."

Tamsa muted the board. "District leads, report."

They reported in the clipped shorthand of people who had practiced crisis until it became a civic language. District One could absorb six percent for eighteen hours before elder care complaints rose. District Two had already been running below index because a fan controller had failed and no one believed the replacement queue. District Three's night shift would lose fabrication heat and had threatened to walk. District Four had three pneumonia cases and would not accept another cut without seeing the medical priority list. District Five's cargo stacks had unregistered residents who did not exist for allocation purposes but breathed anyway.

Maro listened with increasing discomfort.

Lena noticed. "Legal categories bothering you?"

"Yes."

"Good."

Tamsa heard without turning. "If you two are done enjoying moral education, we need to stop this from becoming a riot."

"Or a surrender," Doss said.

The word hung there.

Tamsa looked at her. "Explain."

"They cut residential fans now so we prove we can behave. Ceyr asks for technical control because the gate is dangerous. Halcyon administration says yes because medical priority. Compact says temporary. Then every future part, every membrane crate, every fan cycle comes through a Ceyr emergency schedule. We breathe by permission."

No one argued. That was the frightening part.

Lena pushed away from the cabinet. "The ration adjustment also creates pressure to open the gate quickly."

"Everything creates pressure to open the gate quickly," Pellam said.

"This creates public pressure that can be pointed. If people believe delay means children lose medgel and districts lose air, they will accept whoever promises certainty."

Maro looked toward the muted image of Keva Lorn. "Or they will attack whoever is blamed for uncertainty."

Every eye in the room moved, not to Tamsa, but to the green maintenance bands on everyone's sleeves.

Fei wiped her hands on a rag. "The station-wide bulletin already named us with polite words."

Tamsa said, "Then we answer with impolite evidence."

"Public release?" Maro asked.

"Public enough."

He looked wary. "The drone, residue, missing hour, and Quiet Mark are under judicial seal. If we leak sealed evidence, Ceyr can move to suppress."

"I did not say leak."

"I heard the shape of it."

"You hear like a lawyer."

"An injury I manage."

Tamsa pulled up the district documentation network. It was ugly, decentralized, and stubborn. Each district lead could post verified local conditions: fan cycles, medical load, shortage logs, maintenance tickets, ration appeals. It had been built during the blockade when official dashboards told residents that help was arriving after everyone could count the empty docks.

"We publish what is ours," she said. "Ration impacts by district. Local maintenance logs showing Lot 88 was rejected. Jori's testimony summary only with his consent and no drone mark detail. Exterior inspection notice and Om's authorization, without sealed evidence. The fact that evidence was recovered under judicial seal. The fact that central sensor logs have a missing hour, if Om allows."

Maro considered. "That is not a leak. It is a pressure map."

"Yes."

"It may still inflame."

"People are already on fire. I prefer lamps."

Lena looked at the district board. "Include what you do not know."

Tamsa turned. "What?"

"Do not make it sound cleaner than it is. Say the gate is dangerous. Say the convoy is endangered by delay and by false certainty. Say maintenance has not been cleared, only that evidence contradicts blame. If people smell polish, Ceyr wins."

Pellam snorted. "Pilot telling us not to polish."

"I learn slowly."

Tamsa studied her for a long second, then nodded to Niko, who had appeared at the edge of the room with a tray of tea again. "Get me a fresh board."

"For writing?"

"For writing."

He set the tray down so carefully it might have contained explosives and fetched a slate from the charging rack.

Before Tamsa could begin, Lena's wrist console pulsed with an incoming private call from Sivek. She stepped into the side corridor and accepted.

His face appeared in miniature, gray with Control light. "Where are you?"

"Scrubber Works."

"Of course you are."

"If this is about ration compliance, choose another tone."

"It is about the missing hour."

She straightened. "You found it?"

"No. We found where it should have been replicated. Backup archive on Halcyon's administrative mirror."

"Should have been?"

"The mirror was accessed during the same window. Segment removed."

"By whom?"

Sivek's eyes moved off-screen, then back. "Deputy Administrator Keva Lorn's emergency credential."

Lena looked through the open hatch at the room where Tamsa was beginning to draft the public pressure map. Keva's ration notice remained frozen on the cracked board, one hand lifted in a gesture of shared sacrifice.

"Ghost key?" Lena asked.

"Maybe. Maybe not."

"Does Om know?"

"In three minutes. I am telling you first because Tamsa will need to keep her people from storming administration when it breaks."

"You assume I can influence Tamsa."

"No. I assume you can stand near her and say something exact."

The line ended.

Lena returned to the room.

Tamsa looked up from the slate. "Bad?"

"The missing hour was also removed from Halcyon's administrative mirror."

The room went quiet.

Tamsa's face hardened. "Whose credential?"

Lena did not soften it. "Keva Lorn."

The room exploded.

Not into violence, not yet, but into the sound violence makes while choosing a direction. Doss shouted that administration had sold them. Fei demanded a lockout of every district fan from central override. Pellam tried to stand too fast and nearly fell. Three district leads began speaking over one another about emergency assemblies. Someone near the back said Ceyr had bought Keva years ago. Someone else said no, Keva had a daughter in the pediatric ward, which made it worse or better or impossible depending on who was yelling.

Tamsa climbed onto the worktable.

"Quiet."

No one quieted.

She picked up the wrench from the bench and slammed it onto the metal table.

The crack silenced them.

"We do not know if Keva used her credential," Tamsa said. "We know her credential was used. You will hear the difference or you will help the people who forged mine."

That landed because it was not generous. It was tactical and true.

"We publish nothing about Keva until Om confirms," she continued. "We lock district fan controls locally where safety permits, not as revolt but as anti-tamper protocol. Every lockout gets logged. No one goes to administration. No one threatens medical staff. No one touches Ceyr Quarter. If you need to shout, shout in here where the ducts are already disappointed."

Doss was breathing hard. "And if Om seals it?"

"Then we document the seal."

"And if they arrest you?"

"Then Pellam gets my chair because he is old and annoying enough to make meetings last forever."

Pellam raised one hand weakly. "I accept this sacred burden."

The room's anger bent, not gone but redirected into grim laughter and task lists. Tamsa began assigning local lockouts. Fei took drone feeds. District leads drafted fan-cycle explanations. Maro sent Om a preservation request on Keva's credential logs. Lena stood by the hatch and watched Tamsa hold a station district together with a wrench, facts, and the refusal to let fury become someone else's evidence.

Then the main board unmuted itself.

Keva Lorn's recorded face vanished.

A live emergency seal replaced it: Meridian Compact Judicial Notice. Magistrate Om appeared, still behind the theater rail, but the background was no longer ceremonial. People moved behind her in urgency.

"Residents of Halcyon," Om said. "This court confirms that material evidence has been recovered from Vane Three under judicial seal. The court further confirms that portions of the gate safety sensor chain and administrative backup archive are missing from the hour preceding today's rehearsal incident. The court has not assigned culpability. Any person claiming otherwise speaks without authority."

Tamsa looked at Lena once.

Om continued. "Because evidence integrity and life-support stability are now linked, I am issuing an immediate preservation order over Halcyon administration credentials, gate service records, House Ceyr bonded supply files, and Compact scheduling logs. Residential ration adjustments remain under medical review and may not be used to alter legal control of Kestrel Gate."

In Control, in Ceyr Quarter, in administration, in every district corridor, that sentence would hit like a pressure wave.

"Finally," Om said, "Convoy Dax has confirmed possible coolant contamination aboard Auxiliary Three. Testing is ongoing. Residents will receive verified updates as bandwidth permits. Unverified rumor endangers lives. Verified fear does not. This court will not ask you to be calm. It asks you to be exact."

The feed ended.

For several seconds nobody spoke.

Then Niko, still holding the tea tray, whispered, "I like her."

Tamsa sat down on the edge of the table as if her legs had been unplugged. Around her, the room resumed breathing.

Lena's console pulsed again. This time the message came from Maro, though he stood only four meters away. He had sent it silently because saying it aloud would change the room before they were ready.

DRONE LOCKBOX ATTEMPTED OUTBOUND BURST DURING OM NOTICE.
DESTINATION HASH PARTIAL MATCH: HALCYON ADMINISTRATION EMERGENCY SUITE.

Lena read it twice.

Across the room, Maro met her eyes.

The gate had not simply been taught to lie. It had been taught where to report.

Chapter 12: Sivek's Office

Sivek Hale's office had no window.

It was not modesty. Control officers with windows spent too much time explaining why the view did not change the numbers.
