Sivek had chosen an interior room behind the scheduling vault, surrounded by three layers of archive insulation and old conduit, where the gate existed only as telemetry, paper, and decisions. The walls were lined with printed route sheets from before the Fracture Wars, each sealed in a fading sleeve. Lena recognized some of the routes by the ache they left in her body: Kestrel-Varda emergency corridor, Pall relief braid, Asterion slow convoy, the broken approach to Nerio's Lamp.

The last one should not have been on display.

She stopped in front of it.

Sivek saw. "I keep it where I cannot avoid it."

"That is not the same as remembering."

"No."

He did not defend himself. That made the room harder to hate.

Maro stood near the door with his evidence slate held flat against his chest. His access restraint had been modified again, this time to permit viewing of judicially mirrored records in Sivek's presence. The amber glow had become a thin gold ring around the device, less shackle than reminder. Tamsa had come because the drone's outbound hash pointed toward Halcyon administration, and because no one could convincingly tell her not to.

Sivek locked the door, then placed four printed packets on his desk.

Tamsa looked at the paper. "Is this a meeting or a museum?"

"Both, if we fail."

The office's overhead light flickered once as the station shifted into another rationed fan cycle. Somewhere behind the wall, air moved more slowly.

Lena took the chair opposite Sivek. It was the same model as the chairs from the inquiry six years ago. That was probably coincidence. Her body did not care.

"You said the administrative mirror was cut with Keva's credential," she said. "Tell us the rest."

Sivek sat. He looked older than he had in the guide booth, as if every hour had begun billing him separately.

"The safety sensor chain has three routine mirrors," he said. "Primary in Control. Backup in the Compact partition. Administrative mirror for insurance and station governance. The missing segment is absent from all three. That requires either coordinated access, a privileged maintenance tool, or a record-cleaning package installed before the incident."

Maro opened his slate. "The removal marks differ."

Sivek glanced at him, surprised.

"I read the partial hashes," Maro said. "Primary was cut cleanly. Compact backup was overwritten with dummy continuity. Administrative mirror was deleted using emergency credential authority."

Tamsa leaned against the wall. "Translate for those of us who do not flirt with archives."

"Three doors were opened by three different hands," Maro said. "Or by one hand wearing three gloves."

"Ghost keys."

"Possibly. But the Compact partition overwrite is not a simple replay of a person's habits. It required system-level knowledge."

Sivek nodded. "Which points back toward old security tools."

"Quiet Directorate," Lena said.

The name sat in the office like a device no one wanted to touch.

Sivek folded his hands. "I need to be precise. The Directorate no longer exists as a lawful Compact organ. Its emergency authorities were dissolved by treaty, its budgets closed, its command structure disbanded."

Tamsa said, "And its toys?"

"Supposed to be surrendered, destroyed, or sealed."

"Supposed to be is doing the work of a whole station crew today."

"Yes."

Lena looked at the route sheets. "You knew some tools were missing."

Sivek's silence was brief. Not denial. Selection.

"After the war," he said, "several transit-security packages could not be accounted for. Gate intrusion tools. False telemetry generators. Authority emulators. Most were designed for sabotage prevention behind enemy lines, which is a polite way of saying they could do sabotage well enough to stop it."

Maro's expression tightened. "Were routeholders given access?"

"Not officially."

"Again with the useful adverb."

Sivek accepted it. "Some routeholder houses cooperated with Directorate cells during the war. House Ceyr among them. So did Compact scheduling, including my office's predecessors. So did Free Hab defense committees that now claim they would never. The war made hypocrites efficient."

Tamsa pushed off the wall. "And now someone is using those tools to make my station beg for the people who poisoned it."

"That is one plausible structure."

"Do not call it structure like it isn't a knife."

Sivek looked at her. "It is a knife. I am trying to determine whose hand is on it before we grab the blade."

Lena opened the nearest packet. It contained a timeline: Varda packet, Ceyr advisory, Lot 88 rejection, forged acceptance, safety-chain missing hour, rehearsal pulse, false bulletin, Ceyr emergency petition, ration adjustment, drone burst. Printed, the day looked less like chaos and more like architecture.

"This is too much for one emergency," she said.

"Yes."

"Then the reopening was targeted before today."

"Yes."

Tamsa swore softly.

Maro scanned the timeline. "The false bulletin went station-wide after the accident, but the ration adjustment framework was drafted before it."

Sivek nodded. "Keva Lorn's office prepared it two days ago as a contingency."

"That is not automatically suspicious," Lena said.

"No. Halcyon is short on everything. Administrators draft ugly contingencies because reality keeps being ugly."

Tamsa's voice went cold. "Keva has a child on the pediatric list."

Sivek looked down. "Her niece."

That changed the room.

Tamsa closed her eyes once. "Name?"

"Meli Aronn. Seven. Neural scaffold candidate. Cold-chain dependent."

Tamsa knew the name. Lena saw it in the way her mouth tightened around grief before anger could cover it.

"If Keva cooperated," Maro said carefully, "medical coercion becomes a possibility."

"Or motive," Tamsa said.

"Both can be true."

Lena looked at Sivek. "Where is Keva now?"

"In administrative suite under judicial preservation order. Not detained. Om is trying to keep her reachable without giving Ceyr grounds to claim Compact overreach."

"And Pavo Irren?"

"House Ceyr now admits he is in protective quiet. Om has ordered production within two hours."

"They will stall."

"They are stalling."

Tamsa crossed to the desk and planted both hands on it. "What are you not saying?"

Sivek met her glare. "Many things. Most because I do not yet know them."

"Pick the thing you are ashamed of."

Lena expected him to snap. He did not. He looked at the route sheet for Nerio's Lamp.

"During the war," he said, "I authorized Directorate telemetry filters on three reopening routes. Kestrel was one of them."

The office seemed to lose air.

Lena heard her own voice from far away. "When?"

"After the Pall relief braid was ambushed. Directorate analysts argued that gate boards were too easy to flood with false alarms. Their filter would suppress hostile noise and protect command attention."

"Protect command attention," Maro repeated.

"Yes."

Tamsa stared. "You installed a system designed to decide what Control deserved to see?"

"We installed a wartime filter designed to resist external spoofing."

"On Kestrel."

"Yes."

Lena stood. The chair slid back and struck the wall. "Was it active today?"

"It was removed from active certification after the war."

"That is not the question."

Sivek's face changed. Grief, guilt, command, all trying to use the same muscles.

"I do not know."

The sentence almost broke the room.

Lena put both hands on the desk and leaned toward him. "You do not know whether a filter you authorized may have helped central telemetry see green while local boards screamed."

"Correct."

"And you did not tell Om."

"I told her twenty minutes ago."

"After the exterior drone."

"After the missing-hour analysis showed Compact partition manipulation."

"After," Lena said.

He took it. That infuriated her more.

Maro spoke before she could. "The filter architecture may explain the sample grooming. If a dormant wartime filter remained embedded below certification level, a Directorate-derived package could use its trust relationships."

Tamsa looked sick. "So the gate was not just taught to lie. It had an old tutor."

Sivek flinched. "Yes."

Lena turned away because she did not trust herself facing him. The route sheets blurred. Nerio's Lamp waited under its faded sleeve, the line of the fourth ship's failed approach preserved in ink. During the inquiry, Sivek had said Lena followed doctrine. Now she wondered how much doctrine had been written by fear and never uninstalled.

"Did the filter affect Nerio's Lamp?" she asked.

Sivek did not pretend not to understand.

"No. It was not deployed there."

"Can you prove that?"

"Yes."

"Then do."

He opened a drawer, removed another printed packet, and slid it across the desk.

Lena did not touch it.

"You keep that ready?"

"For six years."

"Why?"

"Because I knew one day you would ask the exact question instead of the one the inquiry asked for you."

Silence gathered in all the old places.

Tamsa looked between them, anger complicated now by the knowledge that she had walked into a room where history had sharp edges. Maro remained still, but his gaze had softened, which Lena did not want.

"Do not make this absolution," she said.

Sivek's voice was quiet. "It is not. Nerio's Lamp died because every available option killed someone and you chose the option that killed the fewest. That is not innocence. It is not guilt in the way the inquiry wanted. It is command."

"I did not come here for your theology."

"No. You came because the same machinery is forming around Kestrel now. A crisis, a doctrine, a record clean enough to bury the people inside it."

That landed because it was true.

Lena sat slowly. Her hands were shaking. She placed them flat on her knees where no one needed to remark on it.

"What do we do?" she asked.

Sivek exhaled. "We prevent emergency technical control from passing to House Ceyr. We get Om enough evidence to order a joint technical board: Compact, Halcyon maintenance, independent reconstruction, convoy engineering, and no single routeholder authority. We identify the embedded filter and isolate it without crashing gate safing. We get Captain Dax a test protocol and a maneuver option if Auxiliary Three's contamination worsens."

Tamsa crossed her arms. "And Keva?"

"We find out whether she is compromised, coerced, or guilty. In that order."

"Convenient order if you like administrators."

"Necessary order if you dislike ghost keys."

Maro nodded. "If Keva is being performed by an emulator, detaining her publicly helps the emulator. If she is coerced, pressure may break her or reveal the coercer. If she is guilty, the evidence must survive her lawyers."

Tamsa hated every part of that. Lena could see her choosing discipline anyway.

"Where is the filter?" Lena asked.

Sivek brought up a gate systems map. "That is the problem. Certification says removed. Current architecture shows a blank trust node where a removed filter used to handshake with central sampling. Blank nodes should not answer. During the missing hour, that node answered three times."

Maro leaned in. "Can we query it?"

"If we query it wrong, we may trigger whatever used it."

"Can we physically isolate?"

"It sits inside Control's sampling bridge. Pulling hardware blind could take down central safing during convoy approach."

Tamsa gave a humorless laugh. "So the lie is load-bearing."

"Possibly."

Lena studied the map. The sampling bridge connected central telemetry, vane local boards, rehearsal controls, and the low-band carrier used to warn the convoy. Too many systems, too little time.

"We need someone who understands old Directorate filters," Maro said.

Sivek looked at Lena.

"No," she said.

He did not look away.

"No," she repeated. "Absolutely not."

Tamsa's eyes narrowed. "Who?"

Lena stood again, this time slowly. "A dead woman."

Maro said, "Legal dead or biological dead?"

"Inquiry dead. Professionally buried. Unfortunately breathing."

Sivek said, "Vela Cho is on Halcyon."

The name cut through six years of sediment.

Vela Cho had been a Directorate systems architect during the war, then a protected witness, then a scapegoat, then a recluse no one admitted consulting. At Lena's inquiry, Vela had testified behind a distortion field that command boards were never neutral because someone always chose what counted as noise. Committee counsel had treated her like contamination. Lena had treated her like a coward for surviving by partial truth.

"Why is Vela here?" Lena asked.

"Quiet contract work for Halcyon administration's archive migration."

Tamsa's expression sharpened. "Administration hired Directorate talent?"

"Former Directorate. Under amnesty."

"I am running out of distinctions to respect."

Maro looked at Lena. "Can she identify the filter?"

"Yes," Lena said. "If she chooses to."

"Will she choose to?"

Lena thought of Vela behind the distortion field, voice rough from refusing to cry while lawyers extracted what they could use and sealed the rest.

"Not for Sivek," she said. "Not for Compact. Not for Ceyr."

Tamsa understood first. "For you?"

Lena shook her head. "No. But maybe for the chance to be exact."

Sivek sent the location to her console. Dockside archive annex, four decks below Ceyr Quarter.

Before anyone could speak, the office alert chimed with a priority convoy packet. Sivek opened it.

Captain Dax's voice filled the room, flattened by compression and still unmistakably human.

"Kestrel, Dax Actual. Auxiliary Three confirms polymer family match. Thermal bus degradation accelerating. Medical packet cold-chain holding, but margin dropping. We can isolate Auxiliary Three from convoy main approach if ordered, but passengers aboard include pediatric cases and nineteen unregistered riders. Need gate status and contamination guidance. Twelve hours to decision. Do not send comfort. Send instructions."

The packet ended.

No one moved.

Then Tamsa said, "I like him too."

Lena looked at Sivek. "Send Dax the truth: we are isolating a compromised sampling bridge, testing for filter manipulation, and building a joint board. Tell him to prepare Auxiliary Three for independent thermal shedding but not separation until he hears from us."

Sivek nodded. "And Vela Cho?"

Lena opened the door.

"I will ask the dead woman to talk."

Chapter 13: The Drone Hull

The drone's hull had not been designed to be opened by honest people.

That was Maro's first observation in the judicial evidence bay, and it annoyed the Compact technician enough to make her forget to be intimidated by him.

"No hull is designed to be opened by dishonest people either," she said. "It is designed to stay shut."

"This one is designed to punish curiosity."

The technician, whose name tag read Arvi Sen, bent closer to the sealed lockbox and adjusted the scanning field. "That is unfortunately accurate."

The evidence bay occupied a former customs inspection room near Dock Twelve. Magistrate Om had converted it into a temporary forensic lab under judicial seal. Transparent partitions separated the drone lockbox, the residue vials, the fleck capsule, and the coolant certificate wafer. Every object had its own recorder. Every recorder had a watcher. The room smelled of sterile plastic, overheated processors, and fear kept professional.

Lena stood behind the observation line with Tamsa and Sivek. Her arm throbbed where the drone had scored the suit, an echo more than injury. Maro stood beside Technician Sen inside the evidence field, permitted to observe but not touch unless invited. He looked painfully happy to be near a puzzle and morally upset by that happiness.

The drone sat in a restraint cradle, still sealed inside its transparent bag. It was smaller than it had seemed on the vane: a matte-black crawler with six folded feet, a needle arm, two cold-gas cartridges, a solvent microtank, and a beacon lens clouded white from Fei's improvised jamming. The broken-ring mark was not visible from this angle. That made it worse, somehow. Ordinary until turned.

Om's voice came through the room speaker. She was in the theater again, handling Ceyr petitions, Keva's credential order, and convoy packet approvals while monitoring the evidence bay. Lena wondered if magistrates were grown in vats without fatigue.

"Technician Sen," Om said, "status."

"External scan complete. Active transmission suppressed. Power core isolated but not removed. Internal memory housing appears tamper-reactive. Opening the hull without bypass may trigger data purge, thermal spike, or chemical wipe."

Ilya Ceyr's voice entered from a remote legal channel. "House Ceyr renews objection to invasive examination before hardware provenance is established."

Tamsa muttered, "It crawled out of my gate and sprayed solvent at evidence."

Om said, "Objection renewed and denied. Technician, proceed by least destructive method."

Sen glanced at Maro. "You said curiosity. Suggestions?"

Maro folded his hands behind his back. "Assume the drone expects standard forensic sequence. Do not begin at the service seam."

"Where then?"

"The feet."

Sen smiled despite herself. "Finally, someone with sense."

Tamsa leaned toward Lena. "Why feet?"

"Crawler identity lives in gait," Lena said. "If it moved clean, it had newer joint stacks or better control code. Either way, feet remember routes."

"You people make everything sound haunted."

"Machines are very physical ghosts."

"That did not help."

Sen deployed two microclamps and isolated the rear left magfoot. The scanner built a layered image above the table: joint housing, magnetic pad, dust embedded in microgrooves, tiny deformations from stress. She did not open the drone. She read where it had touched the world.

"Residue match," Sen said. "Vane Three service track. Coolant film. Radiator ceramic. Also..."

She magnified a cluster of particles.

Sivek stepped forward despite the observation line. "Is that blue sealant?"

"Yes. Not from Vane Three."

Tamsa's eyes narrowed. "Halcyon uses yellow and gray on exterior."

"House Ceyr uses blue in bonded storage locks," Lena said.

Ilya's remote channel remained silent.

Maro said, "Could be transfer contamination."

"Could be," Sen agreed. "But the pattern is in the foot groove, not surface smear. It walked through uncured sealant."

Sivek brought up Ceyr storage maps on his console. "Lot 88 bonded tanks are in Blue Vault Six. Sealant maintenance occurred last week."

Tamsa looked toward the speaker. "How strange and proprietary."

Om said, "Technician Sen, preserve foot residue. Continue."

Sen sampled the magfoot groove without opening the hull. The drone remained still, but the beacon lens flickered once.

"Power fluctuation," Sen said.

Maro took one step back. "It noticed."

"Machines do not notice," the Ceyr attorney said.

Sen did not look up. "This one changed power state in response to noninvasive foot sampling. Choose your verb in your own time."

Lena liked her.

The second foot held metal dust from the vane's abraded coupling. The third held a trace of cleaning solvent matching the spray reservoir. The fourth held something that made Sen stop humming.

"What?" Om asked.

Sen enlarged a fiber thinner than hair, curled around the edge of the magnetic pad. It was translucent with a dark line through the center.

"Pressure-sleeve filament," Tamsa said immediately.

"Human suit?" Maro asked.

"Not exterior suit. Interior pressure sleeve. The kind clerks wear under formal jackets if they expect to visit low-pressure storage."

Lena thought of Pavo Irren's empty berth, the frozen tea, the family image, the suitcase with the manufacturer certificate.

"Can you identify type?" she asked.

Sen ran the scan. "Ceyr leasehold supply. Common in bonded storage."

Ilya finally spoke. "House Ceyr has thousands of employees and guests using standard safety garments."

Om said, "And now we know this drone touched at least one environment where they are used. Continue, Technician."

Sen moved to the front foot. The scanner flickered. For a second the layered image distorted, then cleared.

"There," Maro said.

"I saw it," Sen replied.

"Saw what?" Tamsa asked.

Maro pointed without touching the projection. "The drone is carrying a false wear profile. It has a software layer trying to make the feet look older."

Sen's mouth tightened. "Yes. But physical deformation disagrees. This hardware has maybe forty hours of operation, not four hundred."

"New drone," Lena said. "Made to look old."

"Made to look like salvage," Tamsa said. "So maintenance gets blamed if anyone finds it."

The room absorbed another piece fitting where everyone hated it fit.

Om's voice remained level. "Can provenance be determined without opening the memory housing?"

Sen looked doubtful. "Maybe through manufacturing microstress. If the frame was printed in a known facility, lattice irregularities can match a production bed."

Sivek said, "Directorate deniable hardware used distributed beds."

Maro nodded. "But distributed beds still have scars."

Sen reoriented the scanner toward the hull frame. The image deepened. Under the matte outer shell, beneath the filed-away marks, structural lattice appeared in blue-white lines.

The pattern was almost beautiful.

Then Sen overlaid known manufacturing signatures from Compact legacy archives. Most failed. A few flickered partial. One turned amber.

"Partial match," she said. "Legacy bed class QD-17."

Sivek went very still.

Lena saw it. "You know it."

"QD was not Directorate originally," he said. "It was a Compact emergency fabrication class assigned to route-security shelters."

"Where?"

He checked the archive. His face changed.

Maro read the result aloud because Sivek did not.

"QD-17 production license transferred after demilitarization to Kestrel Gate Reconstruction Consortium."

Tamsa looked from one to the other. "Who is the consortium?"

Lena already knew from the sinking feeling in her ribs.

Sivek answered. "House Ceyr, Halcyon Administration, and Compact Scheduling Authority."

For one sharp second, the room became too small to hold all its implications.

Ilya's attorney began speaking quickly. "A production license does not establish manufacturing event, custody, intent, or House involvement. Reconstruction assets have been subcontracted across multiple jurisdictions--"

Om cut him off. "Noted. Technician, can you narrow bed use?"

Sen's fingers moved. "The drone frame includes a maintenance metadata scar. Not memory. Physical print artifact from a miscalibrated strut nozzle. If we have access to consortium fabrication logs, I can compare."

"You have judicial access," Om said.

"Magistrate," Ilya said, "those logs include proprietary reconstruction templates and security-sensitive gate repair components."

"Then I will be careful with them."

"House Ceyr requests independent review before disclosure."

"Denied. Produce logs."

Silence from the Ceyr channel. Then a clipped, "Under protest."

Tamsa whispered, "Frame that and hang it in the nursery."

While Sen waited for the logs, Maro examined the drone's beacon lens through the projection. "The outbound burst attempted during Om's public notice. Destination hash partial match to administration emergency suite. Can we identify whether the burst carried data or command receipt?"

Sen shook her head. "Not without memory."

"Can we read the antenna residue?"

"Antenna residue?"

"High-power microbursts leave thermal patterns. If it sent a large package, the lens and antenna substrate heat differently than if it pinged."

Sen looked at him with a new respect she clearly resented. "You do this often?"

"Stand near better technicians and ask for miracles? Yes."

She adjusted the scan. The beacon lens bloomed in infrared history: a ring of heat near the base, brief and narrow.

"Small packet," Sen said. "A ping or status code. Not a data dump."

"It reported that it was captured," Lena said.

"Or asked what to do," Maro said.

Tamsa's console chimed. She looked down, then up. "Keva Lorn is requesting private contact with Magistrate Om."

Om's voice came from the speaker. "She is requesting more specifically than that."

The room waited.

"Deputy Administrator Lorn requests protective testimony status," Om said. "She claims her credential was performed by an emulator and that her niece's medical priority was used to compel silence."

Tamsa closed her eyes.

Lena thought of the ration notice, Keva's recorded composure, the word sacrifice polished until it could hide a threat. Coerced, guilty, compromised. In that order.

"Is she naming who compelled her?" Maro asked.

"Not yet. She says she will speak only if her niece's treatment priority is placed under court protection and not administrative discretion."

Ilya's channel opened. "House Ceyr supports protection for all pediatric patients and cautions against accepting self-exculpatory claims from an administrator facing credential irregularities."

Tamsa stepped toward the speaker. "You people hear a woman saying her sick niece was used as leverage and your first instinct is admissibility."

Ilya answered directly, not through counsel. "My first instinct is that desperate people lie under pressure."

"You would know."

"Yes," Ilya said.

That stopped Tamsa, and everyone else.

Ilya continued, voice quieter. "Which is why evidence matters."

For once, no one had an immediate answer.

Sen's console chimed. Fabrication logs arrived under protest. She loaded them into the scanner comparison. Lines of production metadata scrolled past. Most failed. One matched the strut nozzle scar.

"Frame print match," Sen said. "Consortium fabrication bay K-RC-4. Timestamp nine days ago."

Sivek spoke carefully. "K-RC-4 is in the reconstruction annex."

"Access?"

He checked. "Joint facility. Physical entry controlled by Halcyon administration. Template authority shared with House Ceyr. Scheduling access for certified gate repair parts."

"Who entered nine days ago?" Om asked.

Sivek's face closed. "Logs show Pavo Irren."

"The missing clerk," Tamsa said.

Maro looked at the drone. "Alive, hidden, and now positioned as manufacturer."

Lena felt the box shift. Not close. Shift.

"Too neat," she said.

"Yes," Maro said.

Tamsa looked from them to Sivek. "If he printed it, why hide the certificate in his luggage?"

"Because he discovered what his access had been used for," Lena said. "Or because someone wants us to believe that."

Sen's scanner flashed red.

"Power spike," she said.

The drone's beacon lens lit white.

"I thought it was suppressed," Tamsa said.

"It is."

The lens did not transmit. It cracked.

A hairline fracture ran across the beacon housing, then another. Inside the transparent bag, a dark fluid seeped from the lens assembly and spread over the drone's upper shell.

"Chemical wipe," Sen snapped. "Memory housing is flooding."

Maro moved to the edge of the field and stopped himself before crossing without permission. "Freeze it."

"With what?"

"Cold chain."

Everyone looked at him.

"Medical cold-chain packs," he said. "The gel stabilizers. If we can drop the housing temperature fast enough, the wipe reaction may slow."

Tamsa was already at the wall cabinet. "Evidence bay has emergency organ transport packs."

Sen said, "Those are not rated for reactive solvents."

"Neither are my nerves."

Om said, "Use them."

Tamsa slammed the pack into the field transfer slot. Sen grabbed it, cracked the seal, and wrapped the drone's memory housing through the bag with insulated clamps. Vapor rolled off the pack. The dark fluid thickened, slowed, stopped short of the rear casing.

The beacon went out.

Sen watched the thermal readout. "Reaction arrested. Maybe."

Maro exhaled. "Memory may be partially recoverable."

"And if not?" Lena asked.

Sen looked at the drone, then at the frozen smear. "Then the attempt to destroy it is still evidence."

Outside the observation line, Tamsa leaned both hands on her knees and laughed once, breathless and angry.

"Pediatric cold chain saves the day," she said. "Do not tell the children. They will demand licensing fees."

No one smiled much, but the room did not collapse.

Om's voice returned, sharper now. "Technician Sen, secure the drone under cold preservation. Investigator Venn, prepare a preservation summary. Officer Hale, produce full access logs for K-RC-4. Supervisor Reet, remain available regarding cold-chain custody. Pilot Ors."

Lena straightened. "Magistrate."

"Find Vela Cho. If this drone used a legacy filter pathway, I want the architect before Ceyr, Directorate ghosts, or our own caution reach her first."

"Understood."

Sivek turned from the console. "I am coming."

Lena looked at him. "No."

"She may speak to me."

"She may shoot you."

"Vela does not use firearms."

"That was not literal."

Tamsa pushed away from the observation line. "I am coming."

"You have Keva, cold-chain custody, and district lockouts."

"I also have legs."

Maro said, "I should come. Vela Cho's testimony may require legal handling."

Lena looked at the three of them: Sivek, carrying old authorization like a stain; Tamsa, carrying a station's lungs; Maro, carrying evidence that kept trying to eat him back.

"No," she said. "Vela will not talk to a delegation. She barely talked to a court."

Tamsa studied her. "Will she talk to you?"

"I do not know."

"Then why go alone?"

Lena looked through the evidence partition at the frozen drone, the new hardware made old, the mark filed down but not erased.

"Because she once told the truth and I hated her for not telling all of it."

Maro's expression softened again. This time she let it.

"That may not be enough," he said.

"No," Lena said. "But it is exact."

Chapter 14: Bad Rehearsal

Vela Cho lived under the place where Halcyon stored things it could not afford to throw away.

The archive annex four decks below Ceyr Quarter had been built as a customs overflow vault, then repurposed for reconstruction records, then half-abandoned when the war made every inventory into a political argument. Its corridors were low and overbraced, with old pressure doors left open on manual locks and labels from three administrations layered over one another. Smartfilm signs failed in patches, leaving letters that blinked into accidental poetry: BONDED MEM, HAZARD HIST, AUTHORIZED REGRET.

Lena passed a row of sealed crates marked KESTREL OPENING CEREMONY: FABRIC BANNERS. Someone had written NO AIR FOR FLAGS across them in black sealant.

She did not request directions. Sivek had sent Vela's contract location, but following it exactly would have announced her through every standard corridor sensor. Instead Lena took the maintenance side route Tamsa had grudgingly added to her slate before saying, "Do not mistake alone for unsupported." The route smelled of dust, cold wiring, and old plastic. Twice she had to climb over archive pallets whose tracking tags had died before the people arguing over ownership.

At the end of the side route stood a pressure door with no nameplate. A cheap optical chime had been attached beside it, wired badly and recently.

Lena raised a hand.

"If you knock like a pilot," a voice said through the door, "I will pretend not to be here."

Lena lowered her hand. "How do pilots knock?"

"As if doors are late."

The voice was rougher than she remembered. Older. Less distorted, because no witness screen stood between them now.

"Vela," Lena said.

"There are several dead women in this archive. Be specific."

"Vela Cho. Systems architect. Protected witness. Coward. Prophet. Pick the name least likely to waste time."

A pause.

"That sounds like Lena Ors," Vela said. "Still using wounds as identification."

The door opened.

Vela Cho had gone thin. Not fragile. Wire was thin. Cutting tools were thin. She wore a gray thermal coat over a pressure sleeve, her black hair clipped close to her skull, her left eye replaced by a matte sensor lens that did not try to look human. The right eye was human enough to be tired. A small workroom stretched behind her, crowded with archive cores, portable filters, disassembled sensor blocks, and handwritten notes pinned to insulation panels. Three mugs sat on a crate, each at a different stage of abandonment.

Vela looked Lena up and down. "You look like the gate bit you."

"It sent a drone."

"Worse manners every year."

She stepped aside. Lena entered.

The room's air tasted overfiltered. A private scrubber hummed under the desk, old but well maintained. On one wall, Vela had projected a live feed of Kestrel Gate through public maintenance channels. Vane Three sat in wireframe, judicial seals glowing around the coupling zone. The feed was six minutes out of date.

"You have been watching," Lena said.

"Everyone has been watching. Some of us know what we see."

"Then you know why I am here."

Vela closed the door and engaged three locks, one electronic, two mechanical. "You need someone to tell you whether the old filter is still inside the sampling bridge."

"Is it?"

"Yes."

The answer was so immediate that Lena felt robbed of approach.

"You knew?"

"I suspected. Now I know."

"From public feeds?"

"From the shape of what was missing." Vela crossed to her desk and cleared a chair by moving a stack of data wafers onto the floor with her foot. "Sit if you plan to accuse me thoroughly."

Lena did not sit. "Why did you not tell Om?"

"Because Om is a magistrate, not a systems sink. If I sent a message saying an illegally retained Directorate-era filter might be active below Kestrel sampling, it would travel through compromised channels, trigger security classification, and vanish into the same mouth that ate the hour."

"You could have sent it to Sivek."

Vela gave her a look. "I try not to hand matches to men standing in dry forests they planted."

"Fair."

"That is new."

"Many things are on fire."

Vela's expression shifted, not softer but more attentive. "Tell me what you have."

Lena did. Not everything, because everything would take longer than the convoy had. She gave Vela Lot 88, the polymer trace, Jori's drone sighting, the Quiet Mark, the exterior retrieval, the drone's QD-17 fabrication scar, Keva's credential, the outbound burst toward administration, Sivek's admission about wartime filters, and Captain Dax's contaminated auxiliary ship.

Vela listened without interrupting. Only once did her sensor eye brighten, when Lena described the central board seeing green while local systems saw drift.

When Lena finished, Vela sat on the edge of the desk and looked at the old feed of Vane Three.

"The filter was called Lattice Mercy," she said.

Lena almost laughed. "Of course it was."

"Do not blame me. I argued for Ugly Compromise Six."

"You named it?"

"I built parts of it. Names were committee damage." Vela folded her hands. The knuckles were scarred. "Lattice Mercy was designed after Pall. Enemy drones flooded gate sensors with false micro-errors before attacks. Operators began ignoring local alerts because ninety-nine percent were hostile noise. So the Directorate asked for a filter that could recognize coordinated spoofing and suppress it before it broke command attention."

"Suppress local truth for central calm."

"Yes."

"That was a bad idea."

"It was an effective bad idea."

Lena heard no defense in that. Only history refusing to become simple.

Vela continued. "The filter sat between high-frequency local telemetry and Control's lower-frequency command board. It did not alter raw local logs. It shaped what command saw as actionable. In wartime, that meant fewer panic aborts. It also meant whoever owned the filter could decide what counted as panic."

"It was supposed to be removed."

"It was supposed to be burned out of every bridge before demilitarization." Vela's mouth twisted. "But gate infrastructure is like law. Nothing is ever fully removed if someone might later need it and can call the need temporary."

"Can it be isolated?"

"Yes."

Lena waited.

Vela pointed to a cracked kettle. "Tea?"

"Vela."

"If I am going to save several thousand people from my own old sin, I would like tea."

"Fine."

Vela poured hot water over leaves that smelled metallic and bitter. Her hands shook only when she set the kettle down.

"The filter cannot be pulled physically without risking sampling bridge collapse," she said. "If it is active and entangled with current safing, a blind removal could cause every central board to distrust every local board at once."

"So we leave it?"

"No. You starve it."

"Of what?"

"Authority. Lattice Mercy ranks signals by trust. During the war, Compact command, routeholder security, and certain emergency administrative channels outranked local maintenance. That was the sin. If someone reactivated the filter through a legacy trust path, it is accepting high-authority calm over low-authority alarm."

"How do we starve authority?"

"Flood the bridge with authenticated local truth from enough independent sources that suppression becomes statistically visible. Not raw noise. Signed, time-synced, local-board feeds at service rate, from every vane and service craft you can attach. Make the filter either pass them or reveal itself by clipping them."

Lena thought of Tamsa's workers around the schematic. Gate geometry is hard. It is not sacred.

"Workers can do that."

"Workers have always been able to do that. That is why systems are designed to make them ask permission."

The room's lights flickered. Once. Twice.

Vela looked up.

"That is not ration cycling," Lena said.

"No."

Her wrist console came alive with simultaneous alerts: Tamsa, Maro, Sivek, Control emergency, station public safety.

Tamsa's voice punched through first. "Passenger dock incident. Dock Eighteen. Pressure surge, guidance failure, shuttle impact risk."

Sivek overrode on command band. "All personnel, Kestrel Control declaring local dock emergency. Passenger shuttle Nine-Arc has lost clean approach after a false green from dock guidance. Not gate vanes. Repeat, not gate vanes."

Vela's face went still. "It adapted."

Lena was already at the door. "Meaning?"

"The same trust abuse. Smaller system. Dock guidance instead of gate sampling. It is proving it can hurt civilians without touching the sealed evidence."

Maro's voice entered, clipped by motion. "Lena, Nine-Arc carries evacuees from the outer cargo stacks. Two hundred aboard. Dock Eighteen reports alignment boards green, local beacons red."

Green again.

Vela grabbed a battered tool case and a coil of optical line. "I am coming."

Lena stopped. "I thought you avoided dry forests."

"Now it is my match."

They moved through the archive side route at a run. Vela ran badly but with commitment, one hand against the wall, sensor eye dimmed to conserve processing. Lena's ribs protested every turn. The corridor speakers began issuing calm instructions for residents to avoid Dock Eighteen. Calm had become a smell.

"Can your starvation method work on dock guidance?" Lena asked.

"Yes, if we can get independent beacon feeds and if the trust layer is related."

"How fast?"

"How on fire?"

"Shuttle impact risk."

"Then too slow unless someone at the dock ignores central green."

Lena opened Tamsa's channel. "I have Vela. We need Dock Eighteen local feeds authenticated, not routed through central guidance."

"Already pulling," Tamsa said. "Fei has two maintenance drones in the dock. One reliable, one philosophical."

"Use both."

"It will be insufferable."

"Let it."

Sivek came on. "Nine-Arc pilot is requesting central clearance. Guidance board shows clean capture."

"It is lying," Lena said.

"I know."

"Does the pilot?"

"She sees conflicting beacon lights. She is asking which authority to obey."

There it was again: the old violence of hierarchy. Green board above red light. Command above local. Calm above alarm.

Lena and Vela burst from the side corridor into a tram junction. Residents were pressed against the walls under emergency amber, faces turned toward public boards that showed DOCK EIGHTEEN TRAFFIC HOLD in three languages. A child asked whether the gate was falling. No one answered.

Vela shoved past a cargo worker twice her size. "Move if you want to keep atmosphere."

He moved.

The tram to Dock Eighteen had been commandeered by emergency control. Lena hit the manual request. Denied. She hit it again with Sivek's authorization. Denied. Vela opened the panel below the request plate and pulled two wires with her teeth.

The doors opened.

"Legal?" Lena asked.

"Nostalgic."

The tram launched hard enough to make Lena grab a strap. Vela fell into the opposite rail, cursed, and began unpacking her tool case on the floor. Out came optical taps, a hand compiler, three old Directorate-format connectors filed smooth, and a paper notebook.

Lena looked at the connectors.

Vela caught the look. "I surrendered what they asked me to surrender."

"And kept what?"

"What they were too ignorant to name."

"That is why I hated you."

"No." Vela connected the compiler to the tram's emergency port. "You hated me because I said command boards lie by design and then let counsel seal the examples."

"Yes."

"I sealed them because unsealing them then would have let three houses blame dead operators while keeping the filters. I was a coward about many things. That was strategy."

"Did it work?"

Vela's laugh was dry enough to scrape. "Look around."

The tram screamed into Dock Eighteen's service stop.

The dock concourse beyond was chaos forced into lanes. Passengers who had been waiting for transfers were crouched behind pressure barriers. Dock workers in orange bands dragged emergency foam units into position. Through the wide shielded window, shuttle Nine-Arc hung outside the capture frame, nose canted five degrees too low, maneuver jets firing in sharp white bursts. Its hull lights strobed distress amber. Beyond it, the dock collar's capture arms held open like hands unable to decide whether to receive or strike.

The central guidance board above the window glowed green.

The local beacon strip along the dock frame flashed red-red-red.

Lena's stomach went cold.

Maro met them at the service hatch, breathing hard. "Nine-Arc has three minutes before drift carries it into the collar edge. Central says commit. Local says abort. Pilot is holding manual, but fuel margin is thin."

"Where is Tamsa?"

"Command booth."

They ran.

Dock Eighteen's command booth was smaller than Kestrel Control and less ceremonial. Three operators sat at consoles under emergency light. Tamsa stood behind them with Fei, who had both hands buried in a drone feed interface. Sivek appeared on a wall screen from Control, arguing with someone off-camera. The shuttle pilot's voice came through the speaker, tight and professional.

"Dock Eighteen, Nine-Arc. I need a decision. My capture corridor is green by board and red by eye. I can hold ninety seconds."

Tamsa saw Vela and did not waste surprise. "You can fix this?"

Vela dropped to the floor under the main console. "No. I can make the lie visible."

"Same family," Lena said.

"Less comforting."

Vela plugged her compiler into the local beacon maintenance port. Fei fed her drone streams. Lena leaned over the operator's shoulder and watched the numbers split. Central guidance showed a clean capture corridor. Local beacon rangefinders showed a slight rotational offset in the collar, invisible if sampled once per second, obvious at eight.

"Same clipping pattern," Lena said.

Vela's fingers flew across the compiler. "Trust layer is accepting central smoothing over local range jitter. Not full Lattice Mercy. A child of it. A dock-scale mimic."

"Starve it."

"Working."

She pushed a script into the local beacon ring. Feeds multiplied across the display: beacon A, B, C, maintenance drone one, philosophical drone, manual range laser, worker suit cam from outside the lock, shuttle nose lidar. Each signed at source. Each time-stamped. Each one local. The central board remained green.

Then the green flickered.

"Nine-Arc," Lena said into the channel before anyone could grant her authority. "This is Lena Ors. Do not commit to central capture. Your board is being smoothed. Trust local red. Prepare manual abort yaw plus two point three degrees starboard and down-thrust at my mark."

Sivek's voice cracked from the wall. "Ors, you are not dock control."

The shuttle pilot answered. "I know that name. Are you certain?"

Lena looked at the local feeds. At Vela's hands. At Tamsa's face. At the red beacons telling the truth in a language built to be outranked.

"No," Lena said. "I am exact enough."

A half-second pause.

"Good," said the pilot. "Exact beats dead."

The central board flashed green again, brighter, as if insistence could become physics.

Vela hissed. "It is clipping the drone feeds."

Fei slapped the interface. "Not that one."

The philosophical drone's channel burst across the board in a flood of malformed but signed telemetry. Its rangefinder was miscalibrated by a constant offset, its motor confidence logs were still attached, and its timestamp contained a formatting error that made the trust layer hesitate. It was too strange to classify quickly and too authenticated to discard.

The green board shattered into amber.

Local red became authoritative.

"Mark!" Lena shouted.

Outside, Nine-Arc fired thrusters. The shuttle rolled away from the collar edge with a grace born of terror. Its port stabilizer clipped a foam boom, tearing it loose, but the hull cleared the capture arm by meters. The dock shook as the shuttle passed the frame and burned into a holding loop.

For one second nobody breathed.

Then the pilot's voice returned. "Dock Eighteen, Nine-Arc clear of impact. Fuel low but alive. Please tell whoever wrote that green board that I would like to have a conversation with a wrench."

Tamsa closed her eyes.

Fei kissed the drone interface. "You beautiful useless box."

Vela sat back against the console, sweating. "Not useless. Unclassifiable."

The command booth erupted in overlapping relief, orders, damage reports, and legal warnings. The public board shifted from TRAFFIC HOLD to SHUTTLE SAFE HOLDING PATTERN. In the concourse beyond, people began crying, swearing, cheering, and demanding explanations all at once.

Lena looked at the central guidance board. Amber now. Honest in the worst way.

Sivek's face on the wall screen had gone pale. "Vela Cho."

Vela looked up. "Sivek Hale."

"Can the same method expose the gate filter?"

"Yes."

"Can it do so before the convoy decision?"

Vela's human eye moved to the countdown strip.

Eleven hours, twenty-one minutes.

"Only if you stop asking whether workers are allowed to know what keeps them alive," she said.

Tamsa looked at Lena.

Lena nodded.

"Maps for everyone," Tamsa said.

Chapter 15: Arrest of Tamsa Reet

The arrest order arrived disguised as concern.

Tamsa saw it first because it came through Halcyon administration's labor channel, not Compact security. She read the header, made a small sound of disgust, and threw it onto the Dock Eighteen command booth's wall display where everyone could enjoy the craftsmanship.

TEMPORARY PROTECTIVE REMOVAL
SUBJECT: TAMSA REET, LIFE-SUPPORT SUPERVISOR
BASIS: EMERGENCY REVIEW OF UNAUTHORIZED SYSTEM ACCESS, DISTRICT FAN LOCKOUTS, EXTERIOR INSPECTION ESCALATION, AND POSSIBLE COERCIVE INFLUENCE ON MINOR WITNESS JORI PELL
PURPOSE: PRESERVE EVIDENCE, PROTECT PUBLIC ORDER, AND SAFEGUARD SUBJECT FROM RETALIATORY HARM
ISSUED BY: HALCYON ADMINISTRATIVE SECURITY
NOTED TO: MERIDIAN COMPACT JUDICIAL SESSION

For three seconds, the booth was silent.

Then Fei said, "Protective removal can lick a coolant pipe."

Vela, still seated under the console, looked at the notice with professional interest. "That phrase used to mean arrest."

"It still means arrest," Tamsa said. "It just learned manners from debt contracts."

Lena turned to Maro. "Can they do this under Om's preservation order?"

Maro was already reading. "They are attempting to sidestep by naming her subject rather than suspect and claiming protective custody. Since Halcyon administrative security issued it, not Compact, they will argue station order jurisdiction."

"Can Om block it?"

"Yes, if she receives a challenge before they take physical custody."

Tamsa looked toward the concourse where residents from the disrupted shuttle were being guided into emergency lanes. "Too late."

Four administrative security officers entered the command booth.

They wore blue-gray Halcyon jackets with no visible weapons, which fooled no one. The lead officer was a woman Lena had seen at Dock Twelve after the rehearsal pulse. Same controlled face. Same talent for making violence look like procedure.

"Supervisor Reet," she said. "You are requested to accompany us for protective review."

Tamsa folded her arms. "No."

"This is not optional."

"Then your word requested is leaking."

The officer's gaze moved around the booth: Lena, Maro, Vela, Fei, dock operators, wall screen Sivek, all the local feeds showing exactly how badly central authority had just failed. "You may bring counsel."

Maro stepped forward. "I am available."

"You are not registered as her counsel."

"I can become tedious until registration catches up."

"Investigator Venn, do not obstruct."

Lena moved between Tamsa and the officers. Her ribs disliked it. She ignored them. "Magistrate Om has jurisdiction over evidence preservation tied to the gate incident. Supervisor Reet is material to that evidence."

"Magistrate Om has been notified."

"Notified is not approval."

"Pilot Ors," the officer said, "please step aside."

Pilot. Not contractor. Not inactive. The title had become useful to people who wanted her visibly disobeying again.

Tamsa touched Lena's shoulder. "Move."

Lena did not look back. "No."

"Move," Tamsa repeated, quieter. "If they drag me out over you, they get what they want."

"And if you go quietly?"

"Then I choose the shape of the problem."

Maro said, "Supervisor, I advise against submitting without written terms."

"I am not submitting. I am relocating the argument." Tamsa looked at the lead officer. "Terms. I walk. No restraints. Maro Venn accompanies as legal witness whether registered or tedious. My district leads receive a live notice from me before I leave. Jori Pell is not contacted by administrative security without medical and judicial approval. My tools stay with my crew. My chair does not go to Pellam unless I am gone more than six hours."

Fei said, "He will be unbearable."

"Exactly."

The lead officer hesitated. She had expected refusal or fear. Tamsa offered logistics.

"You are not in a position to set terms," the officer said.

Tamsa pointed at the command booth window. Beyond it, the concourse was full of residents who had just watched local truth save a shuttle from central green. Dock workers, passengers, maintenance crews, and nurses were looking up. Not gathering yet. Not riot. Witness.

"Neither are you," Tamsa said. "But here we are, surrounded by positions."

Sivek's voice came from the wall. "Administrative security, this is Compact Scheduling Officer Hale. Delay action pending Magistrate Om review."

"Officer Hale, with respect, this is a station-order matter."

"With respect returned, your station-order matter is standing in a dock that nearly lost two hundred passengers because a compromised guidance layer outranked local beacons. Choose your next sentence like it will be evidence."

Vela glanced at Lena. "He has improved."

"Low bar."

The officer's jaw tightened. Her console chimed. She read, then looked at Tamsa.

"You may make a live notice. Investigator Venn may accompany as observer. No restraints unless you resist."

"Define resist."

"Supervisor."

"I enjoy terms."

"Physical refusal to proceed."

"Fine."

Tamsa turned to Fei. "Open district channel. All districts. Maintenance priority."

Fei patched her through. The wall filled with district lead icons, then with public audio markers as the channel rebroadcast into workrooms and local boards. Tamsa stood in the center of the booth, face lit by emergency amber, and spoke as if calling a shift change.

"This is Tamsa Reet. Administrative security is taking me for protective review. I am walking. I am not under Compact arrest. Maro Venn is accompanying. No one leaves their post. No one blocks a corridor. No one touches a fan controller except assigned leads. District lockouts remain anti-tamper protocol and are logged. If anyone uses my name as an excuse to break life support, I will come back and make that person inventory filter screws until the next century."

A district lead's voice broke in. "Tamsa, is this legal?"

"Legal is currently having a difficult day. It is recorded."

"Do we gather?"

"You breathe. You document. You wait for verified orders. You do not become someone's picture of disorder."

Another voice, older: "What about Jori?"

For the first time, Tamsa faltered.

Lena stepped into view. "Jori remains under medical protection. I will check on him."

Tamsa looked at her, gratitude and warning in equal measure. Then she faced the channel again.

"You heard Pilot Ors."

The title landed differently when Tamsa used it. Not endorsement. Assignment.

She cut the channel.

Maro adjusted his coat and slipped his recorder into visible mode. "For clarity, I am observing under protest."

"Everything about you suggests that," the lead officer said.

"Thank you."

Tamsa turned to Lena. "Vela's method. Do it. Do not wait for me."

"I need you for the workers."

"You have the workers. You just dislike admitting it." She leaned closer. "And if Keva is coerced, do not let my people eat her before truth does."

"I will try."

"Bad answer."

"Honest one."

Tamsa gave a grim half-smile. "Mine sounded better."

The officers escorted her out. Maro followed two steps behind, recorder glowing. The concourse watched them pass. No one shouted at first. Then Niko's young voice rose from somewhere near the emergency barrier.

"Supervisor says breathe!"

Someone laughed. Someone else repeated it. The phrase moved strangely through the concourse, not a chant exactly, more instruction than protest.

Supervisor says breathe.

By the time Tamsa reached the far hatch, the dock was murmuring it in dozens of voices.

Lena stood in the command booth doorway until the hatch closed behind her.

Then the shaking started.

Not fear. Not only. Rage seeking a place to become useful. She gripped the doorframe until her injured arm protested.

Vela's voice came from behind her. "If you break your hand, you will be less useful."

"I am aware."

"Awareness has a poor record against fists."

Lena let go.

Sivek remained on the wall screen. "Om is challenging the removal."

"After?"

"Yes."

"Convenient word."

He accepted it. "Administrative security moved during Dock Eighteen confusion. Keva's office claims the order was prepared before Om's broadcast."

"With Keva's credential?"

"Deputy chain. Not Keva directly."

"Ghost key?"

"Maybe."

Vela pulled herself up from the console floor. "Enough maybe. We need to expose the trust layer before every compromised office uses concern as a weapon."

Fei wiped her face with the heel of her hand. "Maps for everyone, she said."

Lena turned to the dock operators. "Can you keep Nine-Arc stable?"

The senior operator nodded. "Holding loop for forty minutes. After that, either capture by corrected local or redirect to Dock Sixteen."

"Do not trust central green."

"Never again."

That was dangerous too. Lena knew it. Systems could fail from too much trust or none. But there was no time for the full sermon.

She opened a channel to Scrubber Works Three. Pellam answered from Tamsa's chair with a blanket still over his shoulders.

"I have inherited authority and regret," he said.

"Congratulations. Tamsa has been taken for protective review."

"I know. Half the station knows. The other half is pretending not to know while listening harder."

"Can you coordinate worker feed signing?"

"Already doing it. Tamsa left instructions marked IF THEY GET CLEVER."

Of course she had.

"We need every independent local feed tied to gate sampling bridge. Vane boards, service drones, suit cams, coolant sensors, dock beacons, anything with source authentication and time sync. Vela Cho will provide the filter exposure method."

Pellam was silent for one beat. "Cho as in inquiry ghost Cho?"

"Yes."

"Should I trust her?"

Lena looked at Vela.

"No," she said. "Use her accurately."

Pellam laughed. "That I can teach."

The next hour became labor.

Not heroics. Not speeches. Labor. Fei dragged drone feeds into the signing mesh. Dock operators exported local beacon clocks. Scrubber Works pulled coolant timestamps from systems that had never been designed to speak outside their own rooms. Sann, still flecked with solvent decontamination marks, patched suit-cam channels from exterior teams. Nurses contributed cold-chain monitor feeds after verifying that no patient data would ride with them. District leads sent fan-cycle logs, not because the gate needed them but because the filter needed to see the station's physical truth from below.

Vela built the exposure lattice on the Dock Eighteen floor with two slates, her old compiler, and curses so precise they felt like code comments. Lena moved between workers, translating when gate terms became priest language. She explained service-rate sampling until her throat hurt. She told three different crews that no, they were not opening the gate from a workshop; they were making any hidden suppression visible by forcing it to choose between contradictions.

Sivek argued from Control for bridge access. Om argued from the theater for legal cover. Maro sent brief updates from wherever administrative security had taken Tamsa: alive, not restrained, refusing water because it came in a cup labeled visitor, demanding written custody terms, making the officers miserable.

At ten hours, thirty-two minutes to convoy decision, they pushed the first exposure test.

The gate sampling bridge received local truth from forty-six sources.

For one second, nothing happened.

Then the central board in Control went green.

Too green.

Every local feed showed minor disagreement: thermal lag, vibration, coolant noise, dock beacon jitter, human suit-cam drift, ordinary living mess. The central board flattened it into confidence.

Vela stared at the result with a face like stone. "There."

Lena's pulse slowed. Not relief. Aim.

"Can we prove it?"

"Yes."

The central board flickered.

Green became amber.

Then red.

Then green again.

Sivek's voice came through, sharp. "What did you do?"

Vela leaned over the compiler. "Nothing."

Across every feed, timestamps began sliding out of phase by fractions of a second. Not enough to crash. Enough to make local truth look untrustworthy. The hidden filter had stopped only suppressing. It was now attacking synchronization.

Fei swore. "It is making our feeds look forged."

"Can you stop it?" Lena asked.

Vela's hands hovered above the compiler. "If I stop it wrong, the bridge marks every worker feed as hostile noise."

The wall screen flashed with an incoming public notice from Halcyon administration. Not Om. Not Compact. Administration.

PROTECTIVE REVIEW UPDATE:
SUPERVISOR TAMSA REET DETAINED FOLLOWING EVIDENCE OF COORDINATED MAINTENANCE DATA MANIPULATION.
RESIDENTS ADVISED TO DISREGARD UNVERIFIED WORKER CHANNELS.

The station inhaled.

Lena felt it through the deck.

The hidden system had not just attacked their feeds. It had prepared the story for the attack.

Maro's message arrived a second later.

THEY ARE MOVING TAMSA FROM ADMIN SECURITY TO PRIVATE HOLDING.
NO DESTINATION GIVEN.

Lena looked at Vela. At Fei. At the dock operators. At the worker feeds beginning to jitter on the screen.

Then she opened the all-worker channel Tamsa had used.

"This is Lena Ors," she said. "Do not disregard your instruments. Do not break ranks. The data manipulation claim is part of the attack. Keep feeds live. Keep clocks visible. Keep breathing."

Her console lit with warnings about unauthorized broadcast.

She kept talking.

"They want local truth to look like disorder. So we will be exact in public."

On the screen, the hidden filter clipped another feed.

This time, everyone saw it.

Chapter 16: Lena's Old Inquiry

The first clip became a wound in public.

Not a metaphorical wound. Halcyon had too much practice with those. It became a visible cut in the station's shared instruments: forty-six local feeds entering the exposure mesh, one central bridge smoothing them into impossible agreement, then a worker camera vanishing from the composite exactly when its timestamp contradicted the official green. The feed did not fail. It did not drop. It was clipped, and Vela's lattice forced the absence to render as a black rectangle with a source name and a time.

SANN SUIT CAM 03
SUPPRESSED BY TRUST LAYER
OFFSET: 0.42 SECONDS

The label appeared on every district board still subscribed to Tamsa's worker channel.

Then another:

FEI DRONE H-3
SUPPRESSED BY TRUST LAYER
OFFSET: 0.39 SECONDS

The philosophical drone had finally become intelligible by being censored.

Lena stood in Dock Eighteen's command booth with her hand on the open channel and felt the station listening. Not obediently. Not calmly. Listening in the hard way people listened when their own eyes had been called unreliable by someone else's machine.

Vela was on the floor under the console, hair plastered to her forehead, one hand on her old compiler and the other holding an optical tap against the local beacon port. "Do not narrate too much," she said.

Lena kept the channel open. "You are seeing local feeds clipped by the hidden trust layer. Do not alter your instruments. Do not reboot. Keep source clocks visible."

A district lead's voice came through. "They say Reet falsified feeds."

"Then keep them visible."

"They say maintenance channels are compromised."

"They are. By the thing clipping them. Keep them visible."

Sivek's voice arrived on a private line. "Lena, Compact counsel is warning that your broadcast could contaminate admissibility."

"Tell Compact counsel to look at the black rectangles."

"I did. They looked offended by geometry."

"Progress."

Maro's channel cut in, low and strained. "Tamsa is in transit. I am still with her. Administrative security is refusing destination disclosure under protective movement protocol. She is loudly composing complaints for each officer by name."

"Can you keep recording?"

"Yes. If they remove me, I will make that legally expensive."

The wall display flickered again. Administration's warning scrolled under the worker mesh: DISREGARD UNVERIFIED WORKER CHANNELS. Someone in District Four had overlaid a live fan-cycle graph beneath it. The graph showed a local fan sensor clipped and restored in rhythm with central smoothing. Someone else added cold-chain temperatures from the pediatric ward. Then Dock Sixteen added a beacon feed. Then Scrubber Works Three added membrane-flow variance.

Halcyon was turning itself inside out.

Vela looked up. "This is enough to prove a suppression layer exists. Not enough to prove intent."

"It attacked clocks."

"Intent in law. Not intent in reality."

Lena heard old committee voices in the distinction and hated that Vela was right.

Sivek said, "Magistrate Om is convening immediate evidentiary review. She wants you, Vela, and the exposure mesh."

"We cannot move the mesh."

"Then she is coming to Dock Eighteen."

That silenced even Vela for a second.

Magistrates did not usually leave rails. Om had been doing it all day with increasing contempt for her own procedure. Lena wondered how long one person could hold a legal system together by refusing to pretend it was abstract.

The public channel shifted. A new feed forced its way onto the boards, not from administration this time. House Ceyr legal seal. Ilya's face appeared beside the worker mesh, calm and clean, framed by crisis graphics.

"Residents of Halcyon," she said, "House Ceyr recognizes the fear generated by recent displays of technical disagreement. We caution that unvetted diagnostic overlays can misrepresent ordinary filtering safeguards as hostile manipulation. During the Fracture Wars, similar panic cascades led to fatal command errors, including the Nerio's Lamp incident under Pilot Lena Ors."

The booth went still.

Lena's open channel stayed live.

Ilya continued. "We urge residents not to repeat history by elevating disgraced emergency judgment over certified safety process. House Ceyr stands ready to stabilize Kestrel Gate under judicial supervision and restore confidence before the convoy window closes."

The feed ended.

For a heartbeat, the station had no sound.

Then every channel exploded.

Questions, accusations, old documentary fragments, people asking what Nerio's Lamp was, people claiming they remembered, people posting inquiry summaries, people demanding whether Lena had killed a ship, whether pilots were hiding behind workers, whether Ceyr had just threatened them or saved them. Administration's DISREGARD notice vanished under the flood.

Lena's hand hovered over the channel control.

Vela watched her from the floor. "This is the part they prepared for you."

"I know."

"Knowing does not mean you are ready."

"No."

Fei looked up from the drone console. "What is Nerio's Lamp?"

No one answered immediately. That was answer enough.

Lena closed the public channel.

Sivek's private line flared. "Do not respond without counsel."

Maro, from the transit channel: "Do not let Ceyr define the inquiry."

Tamsa's voice, faint behind him, shouted, "Tell the truth short!"

Administrative security ordered her to stop interfering. Tamsa told them to improve their vocabulary.

Lena opened the public channel again.

"This is Lena Ors," she said.

The flood did not stop. It bent toward her.

"Six years ago, during the Fracture Wars, I commanded an emergency route approach near Nerio's Lamp. Four ships were under my timing authority. The route collapsed faster than doctrine predicted. I committed three ships to a correction that saved them and left one outside the recoverable envelope. Two hundred eighty-six people died on the fourth ship."

The words did not get easier because she had said them in hearings. They became heavier with use, like tools handled by too many hands.

"The inquiry found that I acted within emergency doctrine and removed my active route authority in three jurisdictions because doctrine was not enough. That is the public record. It is incomplete."

Sivek said her name on the private line. She muted him.

"The incomplete part is this: command boards during the war were treated as if they showed reality cleanly. They did not. They showed what systems, filters, and authorities decided command should see. At Nerio's Lamp, no hidden Kestrel filter made my decision for me. I made it. I carry that. But the inquiry used my choice to avoid asking how many command systems were built to hide uncertainty until only catastrophe could be called evidence."

The booth had gone quiet around her. Even Vela had stopped moving.

"Today, on Halcyon, worker feeds are being clipped by a hidden trust layer. You can see it on your boards. If House Ceyr wants to discuss old inquiries, then let us discuss the one question every inquiry hates: who benefits when local truth is treated as panic?"

She transferred the channel to Vela's exposure display.

"Keep your instruments live. Keep clocks visible. Do not riot. Do not surrender your eyes."

She ended the broadcast before she could say anything shaped like a plea.

For several seconds, nothing happened.

Then District Four reposted its fan-cycle feed with a caption: OUR EYES ARE LOCAL.

Scrubber Works Three posted Lot 88 rejection logs.

Dock Eighteen posted the Nine-Arc local red beacons beside the central green.

Pediatric ward nurses posted cold-chain monitors stripped of patient names.

Someone in an agricultural bay posted humidity variance clipped by central smoothing and wrote: TOMATOES ALSO OBJECT.

Fei made a sound suspiciously close to a sob and a laugh.

Vela said, "That was not short."

"Tamsa can file a complaint."

"She will."

Magistrate Om arrived eight minutes later with two clerks, one security officer, and the expression of a woman who had run out of patience before breakfast and was operating on reserves of law older than etiquette. She stepped into Dock Eighteen's command booth, looked at Vela under the console, Lena by the board, and the worker feeds multiplying across the wall.

"Who is in charge of this mess?" Om asked.

Everyone looked at everyone else.

Vela raised one hand without getting up. "Technically, the mess is in charge."

Om closed her eyes. "Fine. I will question the mess."

She set her portable rail on a crate.

"This is an emergency evidentiary review concerning alleged hidden filtering or trust-layer manipulation across Kestrel Gate and associated dock systems. Technician Cho, state your credentials."

Vela's face tightened at Technician. Not Doctor, not former Directorate architect, not witness. Technician was practical and merciful.

"Vela Cho. Systems architect, former emergency telemetry contractor, amnestied under postwar disclosure statute, currently archive migration contractor for Halcyon administration."

"Did you design any component related to the exposed trust layer?"

"Yes. During the war, I contributed to Lattice Mercy, a Directorate telemetry suppression filter designed to reduce hostile spoofing at gate command boards."

The booth absorbed the admission.

Om did not blink. "Is Lattice Mercy or a derivative active in Kestrel systems?"

"A derivative or retained component is active below the sampling bridge. It is suppressing authenticated local feeds, manipulating trust rankings, and attempting timestamp desynchronization to discredit source-level telemetry."

"Can you demonstrate?"

"It is currently demonstrating itself."

Vela pointed. A black rectangle appeared over a coolant sensor feed.

Sivek entered the booth in person, breathless. "Magistrate, Compact confirms central bridge anomaly."

"Anomaly is a shy word," Om said.

"Compact confirms unauthorized trust-layer suppression consistent with Technician Cho's description."

"Better."

Ilya's remote feed appeared without invitation. Om allowed it, which meant she wanted the objection recorded.

Ilya said, "House Ceyr requests that any testimony from Vela Cho be treated as compromised by her admitted role in wartime telemetry manipulation and her current undisclosed contract with Halcyon administration."

Om looked at Vela. "Did you disclose your contract to this court?"

"No."

"Why?"

"No one asked, and I was not yet in court."

"Uncharming but responsive." Om turned to Ilya's feed. "House Ceyr's concern is noted. It does not erase what every district board can see."

"Public visibility is not validation."

"No. It is evidence requiring validation. That is why I am standing in a dock booth."

Ilya's gaze shifted to Lena. "And Pilot Ors's broadcast?"

Om looked at Lena. "Ill-advised."

Lena nodded once.

"Also materially clarifying," Om said. "A rare and irritating combination."

Vela snorted.

Om continued. "I am ordering immediate preservation of the exposure mesh and all source feeds. House Ceyr's emergency technical-control petition is suspended pending review of trust-layer compromise. Halcyon administrative security will return Supervisor Tamsa Reet to judicial custody within thirty minutes."

Maro's voice cut in from the transit channel, too controlled. "Magistrate, administrative security reports Supervisor Reet is no longer in their vehicle."

The booth froze.

Om's face changed by almost nothing. "Explain."

"They transferred her at Dock Blue service junction to a private holding team pursuant to protective movement update. The transfer order bears Halcyon administration seal and a Ceyr security countersignature."

Ilya said sharply, "House Ceyr authorized no such countersignature."

Maro answered, "Then your seal is being performed too."

Tamsa's channel was silent.

Lena felt all the public proof, all the exposed black rectangles, all the old inquiry truth, become suddenly insufficient. Evidence did not hold a person. It only told you where someone had been lost.

Om's voice cut through. "All parties: locate Supervisor Reet. This is now unlawful interference with a material witness."

Sivek was already moving. Fei pulled dock cameras. Vela dragged herself out from under the console and began tracing the transfer order path. Lena opened Tamsa's last channel and heard only static, then a faint mechanical pulse.

Three beats. Pause. Two. Pause. Three.

The same pattern the hidden drone had sent through the service track.

Lena looked at Vela.

Vela heard it too. "That is not static."

"Beacon?"

"No. Legacy handshake."

"From where?"

Vela's sensor eye brightened as she triangulated across the mesh. "Blue service junction. Lower route. Moving toward gate service chemistry."

Tamsa was not being taken away from the evidence.

She was being taken toward the next piece of it.

Chapter 17: The Offer

House Ceyr made its offer while Lena was running.

That was one of the family's talents. Timing. The message appeared on her wrist console as she followed Fei's improvised route from Dock Eighteen through a cargo lift, two service corridors, and a half-lit maintenance crawl that smelled of insulation dust and overheated bearings. She almost dismissed it as another objection. Then the header unfolded under her thumb.

PRIVATE COMMUNICATION
FROM: ILYA CEYR
TO: LENA ORS
PRIORITY: MATERIAL WITNESS PROTECTION

Lena kept running.

Fei's voice spoke in her ear. "Turn left at the yellow conduit. Not the first yellow. The yellow that looks ashamed."

"Specificity needs work."

"If you pass the mural of a smiling comet, you went too far."

The console pulsed again.

Ilya had attached no legal seal. No counsel copy. No House crest. Just text.

I CAN HELP RECOVER REET. CEYR DID NOT AUTHORIZE THE TRANSFER. SOMEONE IS USING OUR COUNTERSIGNATURE. YOU KNOW WHAT THAT MEANS.

Lena reached the ashamed yellow conduit and turned left.

Behind her, Vela and Sivek were coordinating through the exposure mesh to keep the hidden trust layer visible. Maro had left the abandoned administrative vehicle and was following the physical transfer trail with two Om-appointed officers. Magistrate Om had converted half the station into a court order and the other half into witnesses. None of it changed the fact that Tamsa Reet was moving through lower service routes with people willing to forge Ceyr's seal and use Directorate handshakes near gate chemistry.

The console pulsed a third time.

Ilya: ANSWER.

Lena opened a voice channel, breath rough. "Make it short."

Ilya's face appeared, not in a polished office but in what looked like a lift or secure capsule. The lighting was harsh. For the first time all day, she looked genuinely angry.

"The transfer countersignature used a House emergency key," Ilya said. "It is not mine, not counsel's, and not from our active security desk."

"Then whose?"

"A dead key."

"Useful phrase."

"A Fracture-era continuity key from a Ceyr-Directorate liaison cell. It should have been burned."

"Everyone's ghosts are very busy."

"Yes." Ilya's eyes flashed. "Spare me the moral neatness while your ally is being carried toward a chemistry bay rigged with evidence."

Lena ducked under a pipe and dropped from the crawl into a storage corridor. Her boots hit hard. Pain split up her ribs.

"You said you can help."

"I can open doors in Blue service that Compact orders will reach too late. I can also see which House systems wake when the dead key is used."

"Do it."

"I will, if you listen to the rest."

Lena almost cut the channel.

Fei said, "Ten meters, then right. Security shutter ahead. It is Ceyr leasehold."

Ilya said, "You are approaching a shutter I can open."

Lena stopped before a blue-gray pressure shutter with no handle. A camera above it turned toward her. The console showed Ilya watching from the same angle.

"Open it."

"First, hear the offer."

Lena laughed once. It sounded ugly in the corridor. "Tamsa may be in danger."

"That is why you will hear quickly." Ilya leaned closer to her camera. "When this ends, there will be a record. Records need names. Ceyr can restore your pilot authority under House guarantee, support a finding that your disobedience today prevented broader disaster, and fund an independent technical board under your chair. You would have authority to fix Kestrel instead of begging every frightened institution for seconds."

The corridor hummed around Lena. Somewhere behind the shutter, machinery cycled.

"In exchange?"

"Do not let the public record make House Ceyr the architect of sabotage before we know who is using our dead keys."

"That is the offer? A chair for restraint?"

"A chair, authority, resources, and protection from becoming the convenient villain twice in one life."

The words found the old bruise easily because Ilya knew where it was. Lena saw Nerio's Lamp. The inquiry. Sivek's printed packet waiting six years in a drawer. Her own voice on the public channel saying two hundred eighty-six people died.

"You think I want restoration."

"I think you want the power to prevent procedure from killing people while procedure debates itself."

"That is not the same."

"It is close enough to tempt you."

Lena looked up at the camera. "Open the shutter."

"Answer."

"No."

Ilya's face stilled.

"No to restraint?" she asked.

"No to the chair. No to your guarantee. No to being owned by the first institution that decides my usefulness has outweighed my contamination."

"Pride is expensive."

"This is not pride. If a public record makes Ceyr guilty without evidence, I will say so. If evidence makes Ceyr guilty, I will say that too. You get accuracy. Not loyalty."

Ilya's mouth tightened. "Accuracy did not save Nerio's Lamp."

"Neither did authority."

For a second neither spoke.

Then the shutter opened.

"Blue service route is open for three minutes," Ilya said. "Two hostiles ahead by the lower valve gallery. They are using a maintenance cart with false medical priority. Reet's biomonitor is not visible, but the dead key woke Gate Chemistry Lock C."

"Why help after I refused?"

"Because I said Ceyr did not authorize the transfer."

"That is reputation."

"It is also true."

Lena ran through the shutter.

The blue service route had been built for people who liked to pretend service did not exist. The walls were cleaner than Halcyon's public corridors, the lighting softer, the emergency labels printed in Ceyr's elegant narrow type. Behind access grilles, pipes carried the same water, waste, coolant, and air that made every other corridor honest. Money could soften infrastructure. It could not abolish it.

Fei rerouted her. "Cart signal ahead. I am feeding you camera when I get it. Wait, no, the camera is showing an empty corridor."

"Central?"

"Local too. But the philosophical drone is getting acoustic bounce from something moving."

"Tell it thank you."

"It will misunderstand."

Lena slowed before the valve gallery. The corridor opened into a cylindrical chamber crowded with vertical pipes and manual wheels. Condensation filmed the floor. A maintenance cart sat near the far hatch under a white medical-priority flag. Two people in pressure sleeves stood beside it, faces hidden by work hoods. One held a slate. The other held a compact shock tool low against the thigh.

Tamsa was not visible.

Lena stepped into the gallery.

"Wrong corridor?" she asked.

Both hoods turned.

The one with the shock tool raised it. "This route is closed."

"Ilya Ceyr says otherwise."

That landed. Not fear. Recalculation.
