The slate-holder said, "Pilot Ors, you are interfering with protective transfer."

"Everyone keeps protecting Tamsa from the strangest directions."

"Supervisor Reet is a material risk to station order."

"Where is she?"

"Being held for review."

The shock tool hummed.

Lena did not have a weapon. She had a bruised body, a wrist console, and enough experience with people pointing tools at her to know that talking was a form of distance.

"If you wanted me gone, you would have fired already."

"We prefer no injuries."

"Then release her."

"Cannot."

"Because she has seen something?"

The slate-holder's hand tightened.

Behind Lena, a soft clank sounded in the corridor. Maro's voice came through her earpiece, barely above whisper. "I am two minutes out with officers. Do not be heroic."

"Terrible timing," she murmured.

The shock-tool holder took a step. "Hands visible."

Lena raised her hands.

The maintenance cart beeped.

Not loud. Not dramatic. A medical-priority confirmation tone. Then a second tone beneath it: three beats, pause, two, pause, three.

The slate-holder slapped the cart console. "Quiet it."

Tamsa's voice came from inside the cart, muffled but very much alive. "It is already quiet. You are just bad at kidnapping maintenance workers."

Relief hit Lena so hard she almost missed the shock-tool holder move.

Almost.

She pivoted into the pipe rack instead of away, letting the first swing crack against a valve wheel. Sparks jumped. Lena drove her shoulder into the attacker's chest. Pain tore through her ribs. They both stumbled. The shock tool skittered across the wet floor.

The slate-holder bolted for the cart.

The philosophical drone dropped from an overhead inspection port directly onto his head.

It did not attack well. It mostly fell with conviction, legs flailing, diagnostic light blinking amber. The slate-holder shouted and crashed into the cart. Fei whooped over the channel.

"It meant to do that!"

Tamsa shouted from inside, "No it did not!"

Lena kicked the shock tool under the pipes. The first attacker recovered and reached for her. She caught a wrist, remembered too late that her left arm was injured, and turned the motion into a bad throw that dumped them both to the floor. Her breath vanished. The attacker scrambled up first.

Then Maro arrived with two judicial officers and a recorder already glowing.

"Under judicial order," Maro said, "step away from the cart."

The attacker looked at the officers, the drone, Lena on the floor, and the cart now rocking from Tamsa kicking inside it.

The slate-holder tried to run.

One judicial officer tripped him with a neatness that suggested prior career breadth.

The other secured the shock-tool holder against the pipe rack.

Lena rolled onto her side and dragged air back into her lungs.

Maro crouched. "You were heroic."

"I was delayed."

"That is not a defense."

"File it."

The cart door had been sealed with a portable medical lock. Maro produced a judicial override. It failed. Ilya's voice came through Lena's still-open console.

"Use House emergency blue."

"You are still here?" Lena gasped.

"Apparently accuracy is habit-forming."

Maro entered the code Ilya sent. The lock opened.

Tamsa Reet emerged from the cart with her hands bound in front of her, hair half-loose, face bruised, and eyes bright with murder she was carefully saving for later.

"What," she said, "took you so long?"

Lena lay on the wet floor, one arm wrapped around her ribs. "Negotiations."

Tamsa looked at Maro. "Tell me she is joking."

"Not entirely."

"Bad day for everyone."

The judicial officer cut Tamsa's restraints. She flexed her hands once, then crouched beside Lena.

"Can you stand?"

"Eventually."

"Eventually is not on the schedule."

She helped Lena sit. Her grip was gentler than her voice.

Maro searched the captured slate with one of the officers. "Transfer order route leads to Gate Chemistry Lock C. They were not taking her to holding."

"I gathered," Tamsa said.

"Why Gate Chemistry?" Lena asked.

Tamsa's expression changed. "Because I rejected Lot 88. Because I know the manual bypasses."

Maro looked up. "They needed you to open something?"

"Or needed it to look like I did."

The captured slate chimed. A countdown appeared.

CHEMISTRY LOCK C AUTHORITY WINDOW ACTIVE
TIME REMAINING: 08:12

Ilya inhaled sharply over the channel. "That lock leads to retained coolant samples."

Tamsa swore. "The original Lot 88 local reject sample."

Maro stood. "If they destroy or contaminate it--"

"They prove maintenance manipulated evidence," Lena said, forcing herself up with Tamsa's help.

Tamsa looked toward the far hatch. "Can you run?"

Lena took one breath. It hurt. The next hurt less because there was no time to honor the first.

"Eventually," she said, "has been moved up."

Chapter 18: Low-Bandwidth Warning

Gate Chemistry Lock C was not built for drama.

It was a squat reinforced door at the end of a service gallery, painted yellow because yellow meant caution before too many committees had diluted the color into advisory language. Beyond it lay retained samples, calibration solvents, emergency coolant standards, and the dull unglamorous materials that let a meridian gate prove what had touched it. If Control was the mind people photographed, chemistry was the bloodwork everyone ignored until a body failed.

The lock's status panel showed three active authorities.

HALCYON MAINTENANCE: TAMSA REET
HOUSE CEYR BONDED STORAGE: DEAD KEY C-47
COMPACT SAFETY: PENDING

Below them, the countdown continued.

TIME REMAINING: 05:38

Tamsa reached for the panel. Maro caught her wrist before she touched it.

"Do not," he said.

She stared at his hand until he released her. "We have five minutes."

"If you touch it while your authority is active in a forged window, the frame becomes real."

"If I do not touch it, the sample may be gone."

Lena leaned against the gallery wall and tried not to breathe like a damaged pump. The captured slate had been handed to a judicial officer, who was now sealing it in a portable evidence sleeve while looking as if he had joined the wrong profession. The two fake transfer agents were restrained at the valve gallery under guard. Ilya's channel remained open, her image small on Lena's wrist. Sivek was routing Compact safety from Control. Vela was screaming at an old trust layer through the exposure mesh. Fei was making three drones argue with door cameras. The station had become a committee of emergencies, every one of them with teeth.

"Can we freeze the lock?" Lena asked.

Tamsa shook her head. "Freezing a chemistry lock seals it for six hours unless you cut. We have a convoy in less than eleven."

Maro looked at the status panel. "Can someone other than Tamsa invalidate her active authority?"

"Her supervisor," the judicial officer said.

Tamsa gave him a look.

"You are the supervisor," he added, unhelpfully.

Ilya spoke from the wrist console. "Ceyr can revoke the dead key."

"Do it," Lena said.

"I am attempting. The key is not in active House registry."

Tamsa laughed once. "Then how is it active?"

"Because someone rebuilt a dead authority from archived trust records."

Maro's eyes narrowed. "A performed key."

"Yes," Ilya said. "But dead keys still look for burial rites. If I issue a House-wide revocation burn, it may collapse other dormant authorizations."

"Which means what?" Tamsa asked.

"Some reconstruction systems may lock."

"Gate systems?"

A pause. "Possibly."

The countdown reached 04:52.

Lena looked at the three active authorities. "We need a fourth authority that outranks the frame without using Tamsa's hand."

Maro said, "Judicial."

The officer shook his head. "Magistrate Om can seal evidence but not operate chemical locks without a safety officer present."

"Compact safety is pending," Tamsa said.

Sivek's voice cut in through Lena's console. "Compact safety officer is two minutes out."

"We have less than five."

"I know."

Lena stared at the panel until the words blurred. Halcyon maintenance. Ceyr dead key. Compact pending. The system did not care who was honest. It cared who had authority shaped correctly.

"Tamsa," she said, "who trained your authority?"

"What?"

"Your credential. Does it recognize only your token, or your work pattern?"

Maro turned sharply. "Lena."

She ignored him. "If ghost keys perform access patterns, can we make the lock see the difference between you and the fake?"

Tamsa looked at the panel, then at her hands. "Maybe. Chemistry locks require physical sequence for retained samples. Not just token. Pressure, rhythm, old manual habits."

"Could a ghost key mimic those?"

"If trained."

"Was it?"

Tamsa's mouth tightened. "Not for Lock C. I hate Lock C. I use the old left-hand bypass because the right pad sticks."

"Would the forged window know?"

"Only if it watched me curse at this specific door."

Maro said, "We need to document the distinction."

"Yes," Lena said. "So document it."

The judicial officer lifted his recorder. "State what you intend."

Tamsa stepped toward the lock, not touching yet. "I will attempt a non-opening authority challenge using the left-hand maintenance bypass sequence. It will not release the inner seal. It will force the lock to compare active authority against live habit pattern. If the active Reet credential is performed, it should reject one of us."

"One of you," Maro repeated.

"Me or the ghost wearing me."

Lena looked at the countdown.

03:41.

"Do it."

Tamsa placed her left hand on a panel that did not look like an interface at all, just a worn section of yellow plating beside the formal pad. She pressed twice, hard, then dragged her thumb down a seam and struck the lower edge with the heel of her palm. The panel blinked in offended blue.

MANUAL MAINTENANCE HABIT CHALLENGE
SUBJECT: TAMSA REET
ACTIVE AUTHORITY CONFLICT

The door vibrated. Somewhere inside, old mechanisms woke and complained.

Tamsa's jaw clenched. "Come on."

The panel flashed.

LIVE HABIT MATCH: 94.7
ACTIVE REMOTE HABIT MATCH: 61.2
REMOTE AUTHORITY SUSPENDED

HALCYON MAINTENANCE: LIVE CHALLENGE ONLY
HOUSE CEYR BONDED STORAGE: DEAD KEY C-47
COMPACT SAFETY: PENDING

Maro exhaled. "That is beautiful."

"It is annoying and badly documented," Tamsa said. "Beauty costs extra."

The countdown did not stop.

02:58.

Ilya said, "I have a revocation burn ready. It will suspend Ceyr dead key only if the lock has demoted Halcyon remote authority."

"Will it lock gate systems?" Lena asked.

"Less likely now."

"Less likely is not a number."

"It is the number we have."

Tamsa looked at Lena. "If she burns it and the lock freezes?"

"Then we cut."

"You should never comfort people."

"I have other skills."

Ilya's voice came cool and tight. "Executing revocation."

The panel went dark.

For one full second, Chemistry Lock C disappeared from every authority system.

Then the lights returned.

HOUSE CEYR BONDED STORAGE: REVOKED
COMPACT SAFETY: PENDING
JUDICIAL PRESERVATION: ACTIVE
HALCYON MAINTENANCE: LIVE CHALLENGE ONLY

The countdown vanished.

The gallery did not cheer. The people in it had been too close to seeing a door become a lie.

Sivek said, "Compact safety arriving."

A woman in a Compact pressure jacket rounded the corner at a run, breathless, scanner already in hand. "Safety Officer Jaya Voss. I have authority for Lock C under Om's preservation order."

Tamsa pointed at the panel. "Use live challenge only. Anything remote is dirt."

Voss looked at her bruised face, the judicial officer, Lena leaning against the wall, Maro recording, and Ilya Ceyr glowing on a wrist console. "I am going to ask later."

"Later is ambitious," Lena said.

The lock opened under joint Compact and judicial seal. Inside, the chemistry vault was colder than the corridor and lit in narrow blue strips. Retained samples sat in shock-mounted racks behind transparent doors: coolant lots, solvent references, residue standards, emergency contamination kits. Tamsa went directly to the Lot 88 reject shelf without consulting the index.

The slot was open.

Empty.

For one second, the entire race became a joke the universe had told badly.

Then Tamsa knelt and reached behind the rack.

"What are you doing?" Voss asked.

"Trusting my predecessor's paranoia."

Tamsa's fingers found a manual cold sleeve taped to the underside of the shelf. She peeled it loose and held it up. Inside, in a sealed vial with handwritten label and three scratched witness marks, was a cloudy sample.

LOT 88
LOCAL REJECT
T. REET / DOSS / PELLAM
TOO CLEAN

Tamsa closed her eyes. "Pellam, you old bastard."

Maro's recorder caught the words, and for once did not seem likely to object.

The official slot had been emptied. The hidden sleeve had not.

Voss scanned the vial without touching it. "Seal intact. Temperature within range. Judicial transfer?"

Maro held out a lockbox. "With pleasure."

Tamsa placed the sleeve inside. Her hand shook once after she let go.

Lena's console pulsed with a low-band convoy window alert. Captain Dax's next receive slot was opening. Sivek's voice came through immediately.

"We have ninety seconds to packet Dax before the carrier narrows."

"What does he need?" Lena asked.

"Contamination guidance, Auxiliary Three thermal options, and gate status. We cannot send everything."

Vela joined, voice flattened by too many channels. "Send the polymer field behavior. Short protocol. Tell him to separate contaminated thermal bus from navigation control if possible. Tell him not to trust smoothed green from any dock or gate-adjacent guidance."

Tamsa took the console from Lena's hand, still breathing hard. "Also tell him how to test with what a convoy auxiliary actually has. Not lab poetry."

"Can you write it?"

"In sixty seconds?"

"Yes."

"I hate pilots."

She wrote. Not elegantly. Usefully. Doss, reached through Scrubber Works, added a two-step heat response check. Vela compressed the filter warning into a brutal line: IF LOCAL HIGH-FREQUENCY DISAGREES WITH CENTRAL SMOOTHING, TREAT CENTRAL AS SUSPECT UNTIL SOURCE CLOCKS VERIFIED. Sivek packaged it in the low-band format. Maro added the evidence status in legal shorthand. Lena watched the packet shrink as the carrier demanded sacrifice. Words vanished. Nuance died. Instructions remained.

The final packet read:

POLYMER CONTAMINATION CONFIRMED KESTREL LOT 88 LOCAL REJECT AND AUX3 FAMILY MATCH.
TEST: HEAT SAMPLE 3C ABOVE BUS NOMINAL 11 SEC; WATCH DIELECTRIC DRIFT / LOCAL SENSOR ONLY.
ISOLATE CONTAMINATED THERMAL BUS FROM NAV/GUIDANCE WHERE POSSIBLE.
DO NOT TRUST CENTRAL SMOOTHED GREEN IF LOCAL HIGH-FREQ DISAGREES.
SOURCE CLOCKS VISIBLE. AVOID AUTO-CAPTURE.
GATE FILTER COMPROMISE UNDER ACTIVE EXPOSURE.
AWAIT MANUAL APPROACH OPTION.
NO COMFORT. INSTRUCTIONS FOLLOW.

Sivek sent it.

The carrier closed.

No one spoke until the terminal confirmed burst acceptance.

PACKET RECEIVED BY DAX-VARDA-SEVEN.

Tamsa handed Lena's console back. "No comfort."

"He asked."

"Good captain."

The judicial officer sealed the chemistry vault. Safety Officer Voss took custody of the hidden sample. Ilya's image remained on Lena's wrist, quiet.

"You helped," Lena said.

Ilya's face was unreadable. "House Ceyr did not authorize the transfer."

"Still reputation?"

"Still true."

Before Lena could answer, a new alert came through Control.

Pavo Irren had been produced to Magistrate Om.

Alive. Sedated. And asking for Lena Ors by name.

Chapter 19: Maro's Gap

Maro Venn did not like hospitals that had learned to sound like courts.

Halcyon's secure medical interview room had the worst habits of both. It smelled of antiseptic and legal caution. The bed at its center was surrounded by a patient monitor, two judicial recorders, a privacy field, and a chain-of-custody table for anything the patient said while half his blood still belonged to sedatives. Magistrate Om had ordered Pavo Irren examined before questioning. House Ceyr had objected to the word questioning. Halcyon administration had objected to the word secure. Maro had objected to three recorder angles, lost one objection, won two, and now stood behind a marked line with the sour satisfaction of a person whose principles had survived only partially.

Pavo Irren lay propped against white pillows, thinner than his file image, a pressure bruise along one side of his jaw. He was conscious, but consciousness seemed to have arrived in pieces and was still looking for somewhere to sit. His eyes moved too quickly. His hands worried the edge of the blanket until a nurse gently moved them apart.

Lena sat where he could see her. Tamsa stood near the door with her arms folded, against medical advice and several people's preference. Sivek was present as Compact officer. Ilya Ceyr attended by remote, her image confined to a small screen under Om's control. Vela had refused to enter, saying she was useful near machines and bad near frightened clerks. Fei was outside with the philosophical drone, which had been banned from secure medical after opening a bedpan inventory channel.

Om addressed the room from the portable rail. "Mr. Irren, you requested Pilot Lena Ors."

Pavo looked at Lena. "You were on the tug."

"Yes."

"It opened."

"Briefly."

His eyes filled with tears so fast Lena almost looked away. "They said no one would see."

The room tightened.

Om's voice softened without losing shape. "Who said that?"

Pavo swallowed. "I do not know her name."

Ilya's image sharpened. "House Ceyr requests clarification before implication."

Om muted her.

Pavo laughed weakly, then winced. "I always wanted someone to do that."

Tamsa muttered, "I like him enough."

The nurse checked Pavo's monitor. "Short answers. He is still clearing a neuroinhibitor."

Maro noted neuroinhibitor. He had already begun building three versions of the testimony in his head: what Pavo knew, what Pavo thought he knew, and what someone had wanted him to carry into this room.

Om said, "Tell us what happened with Lot 88."

Pavo closed his eyes. "I signed nothing."

Tamsa said, "My credential signed."

"Mine too." He opened his eyes. "Not my hand. Not my hand."

"We believe authority emulation was used," Maro said. "You are not being asked to prove that alone."

Pavo looked at him, confused by mercy in procedural form. "They used my access to print something. A frame. I saw the fabrication notice after. K-RC-4. My name. I thought it was a repair bracket. Then I saw the bonded transfer derivative for Lot 88. It referenced no manufacturer original. That is impossible. Ceyr clerks are lazy, not suicidal."

Ilya's screen unmuted under Om's control. "Mr. Irren, who did you report this to?"

"Internal counsel desk."

"Which counsel?"

"The desk. It was a woman. Not Ceyr legal. She had Ceyr colors, but the badge did not resolve when I looked later."

Maro leaned forward. "Did she give a name?"

"Sarr. Maybe Saren. I was scared."

"What did she do?"

"Told me I had found a hostile compromise. Told me if it became public before the reopening, I would be blamed for negligent custody, my family debt would accelerate, and the convoy medical packets might lose certification. She said protective quiet."

"Did you consent?"

He laughed again, smaller. "I signed a consent form."

Tamsa's face went dark. "That is not what he asked."

Pavo looked at her. "No."

Om made a note. "How did the original manufacturer certificate reach your suitcase?"

"I put it there. I pulled it from old cache before going to counsel. I thought if I kept it, they would have to keep me alive."

Lena glanced at Maro. His expression had not changed, but something in his posture had.

Om asked, "Who sedated you?"

"Medical quiet team. Not real medical. The woman said I was panicking. I woke in a room with no time display. Sometimes she asked me to repeat what I found. Sometimes she said my memory was unreliable. Sometimes she showed me my children's school debt and asked whether I wanted them protected from scandal."

The nurse's jaw tightened. Medical staff did not like counterfeit care.

Maro said, "Did they ask you to say Supervisor Reet instructed you?"

Pavo's eyes moved to Tamsa. "Yes."

Tamsa did not move.

"They said she hated Ceyr, hated pilots, wanted to delay the convoy to force better terms. They said if I testified to that, no one needed to know my key printed a drone frame."

"Did you agree?"

Pavo's mouth trembled. "I do not know."

Silence.

Maro felt the shape of it before anyone else. "What do you mean?"

Pavo looked at the blanket. "I remember saying yes. I remember saying no. I remember a recorder. I remember asking for water. I remember hearing my own voice say Supervisor Reet told me to accept Lot 88 after rejecting it, but I do not remember choosing the words."

Lena said, "Edited testimony."

Maro's continuity mark chilled along his jaw.

Om looked to him. "Investigator Venn?"

"It may be coached sedation, spliced recording, induced compliance, or memory contamination. His statement must be protected from both use and dismissal until medically reconstructed."

Pavo stared at Maro's blue line. "You know about not trusting yourself."

Every person in the room heard the shift.

Maro took a breath. "Yes."

Pavo reached weakly toward him. "They had a blue line."

Maro went still.

Lena turned. "Who?"

"The woman. Not like yours. Fake maybe. Or old. It glowed under skin when she leaned close." Pavo touched his own temple clumsily. "She said continuity was just what powerful people could afford to prove."

Maro's mouth had gone dry.

Om said, "Do you know this person?"

"No," Maro answered.

Too fast.

Lena heard it. So did Tamsa. So did Om.

Maro corrected himself with visible effort. "I do not know whether I know this person."

The nurse looked confused. Om did not.

"Explain," Om said.

Maro looked at Pavo, then at the recorders, then at his own hands. "During the Aurelian recovery courts, several continuity-marked investigators and legal advocates worked with partial forks, memory extracts, and trauma-state witnesses. Some later went into private evidence security. Some disappeared. A fake or altered continuity mark could be theater. It could also belong to someone from that world."

Tamsa said, "Your world."

"Yes."

The word cost him.

Pavo whispered, "She said you would understand the gap."

Maro did not move.

Om leaned forward. "Mr. Irren, exact words if you can."

"If Venn follows the gap, he will choose privacy over proof." Pavo's eyes filled again. "I did not know what that meant."

Maro did.

Lena saw it in the way he seemed suddenly alone in a room full of witnesses.

"Maro," she said.

He lifted one hand without looking at her. Not stop. Wait.

Om's voice lowered. "Investigator Venn, are you aware of an evidentiary gap relevant to this matter?"

His jaw worked once.

"Yes," he said.

Ilya's remote image watched sharply. Sivek looked from Maro to Lena with the dread of a man seeing another old system wake.

Maro continued. "When I was suspended, I submitted a narrow sensory extract from Control during the unauthorized bulletin. I excluded cognitive, emotional, relational, and private metadata by court order and by right."

"Correct," Om said.

"There is a gap in my own local continuity log from before the hearing. Not memory loss. A sealed privacy block. It predates this incident by years. I have carried it under Glassward privilege from Aurelian. It has never been relevant to Compact accident reconstruction."

Tamsa's voice was careful. "Until now."

"Until someone said gap."

Pavo began shaking. The nurse stepped in, checking his pulse. "He is done."

Om nodded. "Mr. Irren's testimony is paused. Medical priority."

"I need to say one more," Pavo whispered.

The nurse looked ready to refuse. Om waited.

Pavo looked at Lena. "The woman said the gate did not need to explode. It only needed to choose its owner."

His eyes rolled back. The monitors flared. The nurse pushed everyone away with the authority of actual care.

The privacy field went opaque.

In the corridor outside, no one spoke for several seconds.

Then Tamsa said, "Maro."

He looked like a man standing at the edge of an airlock and deciding whether the void had a point.

"The sealed gap," Lena said. "What is in it?"

"I do not know in accessible memory. That is what sealed means."

"Who sealed it?"

"I did. With court approval. After Aurelian."

Om emerged from the medical room. "Can it be opened?"

"Yes."

"With what consequence?"

Maro smiled without humor. "That depends whether you ask the law, my mind, or the people inside the memories."

Tamsa folded her arms. "Ask all three quickly."

Maro looked at the opaque field where Pavo was being stabilized. "The gap may contain privileged testimony from dead or partial persons. It may contain identities of protected fork witnesses. It may contain nothing relevant and much that would harm people who cannot consent again."

"And it may contain the blue-line woman," Lena said.

"Yes."

Om said, "I will not order a full unsealing on speculation."

Relief crossed Maro's face so briefly it hurt to see.

Then Om continued. "But I may order an indexed query if you consent: no content release, only whether specified patterns exist. Blue-line visual profile. Phrase: choose privacy over proof. Person connected to Aurelian recovery and private evidence security."

Maro closed his eyes.

Lena wanted to tell him he did not have to. That evidence should not eat the people who carry it. That they would find another route. All true. All incomplete. The convoy clock did not care about dignity. It only measured what dignity cost.

Maro opened his eyes. "I consent to indexed query only. No content extraction. No witness identity exposure. Query witnessed by Glassward protocol, Magistrate Om, and one advocate for nonconsenting memory subjects."

"We do not have a Glassward advocate on Halcyon," Sivek said.

Ilya spoke from the remote screen. "House Ceyr has one."

Everyone looked at her.

She met their suspicion evenly. "Before anyone vomits outrage, the advocate is independent and hates us. She is on retainer because dead investors also become legal problems."

Maro's expression shifted. "Name."

"Oriole-of-Archive."

Maro's breath stopped.

Lena said, "You know them."

"Yes," he said. "And they do hate everyone appropriately."

Om made a decision. "Contact Oriole-of-Archive. Indexed query only. Until then, Investigator Venn's gap remains sealed."

Tamsa leaned close to Maro. "You still with us?"

He looked at her. The question was rough, practical, and for that reason kinder than comfort.

"Yes," he said. "With reservations."

"Everyone worth having is."

Lena's console chimed. Low-band reply from Dax.

Sivek opened it on speaker.

Captain Dax's voice emerged, thin but steady.

"Kestrel, Dax Actual. Your no-comfort packet received. Aux Three confirms drift under heat test. We isolated bus from nav but lost cabin thermal margin. Passengers stable for now. We can hold convoy decision for nine hours, not twelve. Need manual approach option and gate trust status. Also, whoever wrote 'source clocks visible' saved us from a very elegant mistake. Dax out."

Nine hours.

Maro looked toward the evidence bay. Tamsa toward the sealed chemistry sample. Lena toward the corridor that led back to Control, where a hidden filter still sat load-bearing in a gate needed by thousands.

Om said, "Then we work inside the time we have."

Maro touched the blue line at his temple.

"And the time we sealed away," he said.

Chapter 20: Ring Without Sleep

Halcyon stopped pretending it had a night.

The station kept a nominal cycle because bodies still needed old lies: dimmed corridors, softer chimes, lower market noise, a gradual shift from work bands to sleep bands on the public boards. But after the trust layer showed its teeth, nobody believed in the dark. The ring remained lit in patches, awake in districts, red-eyed in clinics, humming in workshops and dock booths and courtrooms made from whatever flat surfaces could hold a recorder.

From the tram between Gate Chemistry and Scrubber Works, Lena watched the ring's inner arc slide past through scratched glass. In one section, residents stood in a corridor under fan-cycle graphs, arguing without shouting while a district lead pointed at source clocks. In another, children slept in a schoolroom converted to overflow ward while nurses checked cold-chain monitors with the solemnity of priests handling fire. Near Dock Eighteen, passengers from Nine-Arc sat wrapped in thermal blankets, alive because a bad drone had been too strange to censor quickly.

The public boards no longer showed a single official feed. Magistrate Om had ordered multiplex display: judicial notices, Compact schedule, worker source mesh, medical priority, and administration statements, all labeled by origin. It made every board ugly. It also made them harder to weaponize.

Tamsa sat across from Lena in the tram, one eye swelling, wrists marked where the restraints had bitten. She had refused medical twice and accepted a cold pack only after Jori sent a message consisting of three words: DO NOT BE STUPID. The cold pack sat against her cheek now, held there with the irritation of a person obeying a child.

Maro stood by the door, one hand on the rail, quiet since Pavo's testimony. His face had settled into a stillness Lena did not like. Not calm. Compartmentalization. The kind built to survive a room until the room ended.

Sivek was in Control. Vela was in Dock Eighteen, building an exposure method into something that would not collapse when ported to the gate. Ilya had vanished from Lena's wrist after sending a final line: DEAD KEY REVOCATION SPREADING. HOUSE SYSTEMS UNSTABLE. THIS IS NOT AN APOLOGY. Lena had not answered.

The tram slowed for a crossing delay. Through the window, a group of residents had gathered around one of the new multiplex boards. Someone had written SOURCE CLOCKS VISIBLE on a strip of tape and stuck it under the display. Beside it, in smaller handwriting: SUPERVISOR SAYS BREATHE.

Tamsa saw it too.

"They are going to make that insufferable," she said.

"It is already insufferable."

"Good."

Maro spoke without looking away from the door. "Insufferable phrases often survive litigation."

Tamsa lowered the cold pack. "You are still with us?"

He almost smiled. Almost. "With reservations."

"You said that already."

"The reservations multiplied."

The tram started moving again.

Lena watched him in the reflection. "Oriole-of-Archive?"

"Inbound by Glassward burst proxy. Not physically, obviously. A certified advocacy instance can run in a sealed legal substrate if Om accepts remote presence."

Tamsa frowned. "That sentence did not earn trust."

"It should not. It should earn procedure."

"Procedure kidnapped me in a medical cart."

"Counterfeit procedure did."

"I am running low on patience for distinctions."

Maro nodded. "Then keep this one anyway. If the sealed gap contains protected fork testimony, those people may be dead, partial, copied, or legally discontinuous, but they are not raw material. Oriole's role is to keep my urgency from robbing them."

Tamsa looked at him for a long second. "Fine. I hate it less now."

"That is a generous review."

Lena's console chimed. A message from Jori.

IS SHE ACTUALLY HOLDING THE COLD PACK?

Lena angled the console so Tamsa could not read it and typed back: YES.

The reply came instantly: PROOF.

Lena looked at Tamsa.

"No," Tamsa said.

"He asked for proof."

"He can ask the void for a pony too."

Lena sent: WITNESSED BY PILOT AUTHORITY.

Jori replied: PILOTS LIE.

She almost laughed. It hurt enough to discourage the habit.

At Scrubber Works Three, the tram doors opened onto controlled disorder. The workshop had become a civic engine. Tables had been pulled into clusters. Every wall displayed a different layer of the problem: gate vanes, sampling bridge, local feeds, convoy trajectory, district fan cycles, Lot 88 chain, dock mimic incident, legal orders, medical timers. Workers moved between them with tools, slates, food packets, anger, fatigue, and the strange courtesy of people who had decided panic was less useful than precision.

Pellam still occupied Tamsa's chair, blanket over his shoulders like a ridiculous judicial robe.

"You are back," he said. "I have improved the chair."

"Get out."

"As tyrannies go, brief but meaningful." He stood carefully, then surprised everyone by hugging her with one arm.

Tamsa went rigid. Then she patted his back twice, like resetting a valve.

"Enough," she said.

"Obviously."

Niko appeared with tea and a grin he tried to hide. "Supervisor says breathe?"

Tamsa pointed at him. "Supervisor says inventory filter screws."

"Worth it," Niko whispered.

The room laughed. Not much. Enough to prove they had not surrendered to the night.

Vela's face appeared on the main board from Dock Eighteen. She had acquired a smear of grease on one cheek and a strip of tape holding a connector to her collar. "I see Reet survived bureaucracy."

"It was touch and go," Tamsa said.

"For bureaucracy?"

"Yes."

Vela grunted approval. "We have nine hours minus drift. Less if Dax's cabin thermal falls faster. The exposure lattice proves the trust layer exists. It does not yet give us a safe isolation procedure."

Sivek joined from Control. "Central safing remains stable. The hidden filter is load-bearing in the sampling bridge. If we cut it abruptly, the gate may drop into local-only mode without reconciliation. That could be acceptable if no convoy were inbound."

"But one is," Lena said.

"Yes."

Vela said, "We need a bridge that does what the filter pretends to do: reconcile local telemetry for command without suppressing disagreement. A temporary truth bridge."

Pellam snorted. "Name needs work."

"Everything needs work."

Tamsa stepped to the central table. The room subtly reoriented around her. Even exhausted, bruised, and furious, she carried authority by becoming useful before anyone else.

"What does it need physically?"

Vela began listing. "Independent source clocks. Worker feed signing. Service-rate local telemetry from all vanes. A noncentral confidence calculation. Manual override hooks that cannot be performed by remote authority. A way to display disagreement without letting every minor variance abort the gate."

Doss whistled. "Build a new command board in a workshop, then?"

"No," Vela said. "Build a jury."

The word landed strangely.

Maro looked up. "Explain."

"A board decides by hierarchy. A jury decides by visible disagreement and rules of weight. We cannot replace Control. We can give Control a parallel verdict from local sources, with every dissent visible. If the hidden filter says green and the jury says amber, Sivek cannot pretend green means certainty."

Sivek's expression on the screen tightened. "Nor can Dax."

"Good," Lena said.

Tamsa glanced at her. "You like this?"

"No. I trust it more than elegance."

Vela's human eye narrowed. "It will be ugly."

"Ugly is harder to worship."

The workshop began turning the idea into parts. Not a revolution. Not a miracle. A jury. Twelve source clusters at first, then eighteen: each vane, each coolant loop, each service drone class, Dock Eighteen beacons, exterior suit telemetry, chemistry sample references, convoy auxiliary packet checks, cold-chain thermal monitors stripped of patient identity. They argued about weights. Doss insisted coolant drift deserved more authority near pulse. Pellam argued structural vibration had to outrank field elegance if anything started shaking. Sann, quiet until then, said apprentice visual testimony should not be a live feed but should determine where blind pockets were watched. Tamsa agreed. Jori, patched in from medical against Tamsa's first three objections, suggested labeling any feed that came from "stuff that has already tried to kill us" in red. Vela took the wording seriously enough to create a hostile-provenance tag.

Maro sat at a side table while they worked, preparing the indexed query protocol with Om and the remote Glassward advocate. Oriole-of-Archive appeared first as a legal seal, then as a voice: layered, androgynous, patient in a way that made human patience look rushed.

"Investigator Venn," Oriole said through a sealed speaker, "you request index-only access to a self-sealed continuity gap containing protected Aurelian recovery material."

Maro looked as if every person in the workshop had vanished and left him alone with the speaker.

"Yes."

"State necessity."

"A witness reports a continuity-marked coercer referencing the gap and predicting I would choose privacy over proof. The gap may contain indexable association with that person or phrase. The Kestrel convoy decision window is under nine hours."

"State limit."

"No content extraction. No identity exposure except if the index returns a living unsealed public identity already outside protected recovery status. Pattern query only: visual mark profile, phrase, Aurelian recovery affiliation, private evidence security link."

"State risk."

Maro swallowed. "The query may reveal that my sealed material is relevant, forcing a future choice between broader unsealing and evidentiary incompleteness. It may also confirm that someone has knowledge of sealed persons or records, implying a breach of protected dead and partial witnesses."

"State personal risk."

He closed his eyes. "I may become less able to pretend the seal is only care and not avoidance."

The workshop had quieted around him. Not completely. Work continued in softer motions.

Oriole said, "Noted. Query permitted under protest on behalf of sealed subjects."

"Your protest is accepted."

"It is not offered for your comfort."

"I know."

The speaker went silent for seven seconds.

Then Oriole returned.

"Index result: positive."

Maro did not move.

"Pattern phrase appears in sealed testimony cluster Aurelian-Blue-17. Visual continuity mark profile partial match to one person with public legal death and sealed operational alias. Private evidence security link: probable. Content remains sealed. Name status: protected, but public death record may be disclosed under current threat protocol."

Om's voice entered, formal. "Disclose."

Oriole paused. "Public legal death name: Cassian Vale."

Sivek's face changed on the Control screen.

Lena saw it. "You know the name."

"Cassian Vale was Quiet Directorate legal liaison to several route-security cells," Sivek said. "Reported dead after Aurelian."

Maro opened his eyes.

"Not dead," Tamsa said.

Oriole answered, "Legally dead. Ontological status unknown."

Pellam muttered, "Of course law found a worse way to say alive."

Maro's voice was very quiet. "Cassian Vale argued against admitting certain fork testimonies. He said continuity belonged to those with infrastructure."

"The woman told Pavo continuity was what powerful people could afford to prove," Lena said.

Maro nodded. "Same doctrine."

Vela leaned toward her feed. "If Vale had access to Lattice Mercy legal seals and recovery gaps, he could know the old trust structures, the dead keys, the evidence protocols, and how to make every institution fear exposure."

"Motive?" Tamsa asked.

Sivek answered. "Control. Not for Ceyr alone. Not Halcyon alone. Emergency control doctrine restored through manufactured uncertainty."

Lena looked at the jury board taking shape across the workshop wall: local truths, weighted disagreement, hostile provenance tags, source clocks visible. Ugly, alive, unowned.

"Then we make uncertainty public enough that no one can own it," she said.

The room went back to work.

Outside Scrubber Works, Halcyon stayed awake. District boards filled with local feeds. Residents learned to read source clocks badly, then better. Children slept under blue cold-chain lights. Dock workers recalibrated beacons by hand. Ceyr systems burned dead keys and found old doors they had forgotten they owned. Compact counsel filed warnings nobody had time to admire. Somewhere in medical, Pavo Irren slept under real care. Somewhere in Control, Sivek sent Dax another no-comfort update: MANUAL LOCAL JURY UNDER CONSTRUCTION. TRUST STATUS NOT GREEN. AWAIT APPROACH LOGIC.

At seven hours, forty minutes, the jury board rendered its first full verdict on Kestrel Gate.

Not green.

Not red.

Amber, with reasons.

Vane Three coolant drift: amber.
Sampling bridge trust: red.
Physical vane stability: amber-green.
Power buffers: green.
Hostile provenance active: red.
Manual override integrity: amber.
Convoy contamination coordination: amber-red.

At the bottom, Vela added a line no official board would have tolerated:

CERTAINTY UNAVAILABLE. DECISION STILL REQUIRED.

No one cheered.

They had not built safety.

They had built a way to stop lying about its absence.

Chapter 21: Geometry in the Workshop

By the second hour of the local jury, the workshop had become a cockpit large enough for a station.

Lena hated that comparison at first. Cockpits belonged to pilots, and pilots had done enough hoarding for one civilization. But as the source feeds settled into the wall and people found places by instinct, the old structure emerged in altered form. Tamsa at the central table, not commanding so much as routing urgency. Vela on the main compiler, one foot tucked under her chair, translating old sin into temporary function. Doss and Pellam arguing coolant against structure. Fei managing drones like unreliable witnesses. Sann and two exterior riggers feeding vane conditions into hostile-provenance tags. Nurses on a side channel correcting anyone who tried to use medical timers as political leverage. Jori in a hospital bed, limited to three messages every ten minutes by nurse decree and spending them with tactical precision.

Maro handled admissibility, which meant he kept asking whether a thing could survive hostile review without making it useless.

Sivek handled Control, which meant he kept discovering how much command had depended on nobody seeing the machinery underneath.

Lena moved between them, explaining gate geometry until the explanations changed in her own mouth.

She had learned meridian transit in rooms where knowledge narrowed as it rose. Apprentices received diagrams. Junior pilots received tolerances. Route pilots received failure modes. Senior route pilots received the parts of doctrine that made tolerances feel like superstition. Routeholder families received liability privileges and pretended they were wisdom. Everyone below was told the math was too dangerous to share.

Now Niko stood on a crate with a slate almost as wide as his chest and asked, "If the corridor is not open yet, how can it have a bad edge?"

The room kept working. No one told him to go sort screws.

Lena crouched beside the crate and pulled the rehearsal model onto his slate. "It does not have an edge like a door. It has a boundary where the field question stops being harmless."

"That is worse."

"Yes."

"Why call it a corridor then?"

"Because pilots like words that make impossible things sound like hallways."

Niko considered this with grave contempt. "Bad naming."

"Very."

She drew three curves. "During a full opening, ships enter here, inside the stable envelope. The vanes hold the boundary long enough for transit. During rehearsal, the envelope should never become usable. Today, Vane Three created a partial boundary here. That is the bad edge."

"The black seam."

"Yes."

"Can it happen again?"

The right answer would be comforting and therefore wrong.

"Yes," Lena said. "If we open without understanding what pulled the vane and how the filter hid it."

He nodded, accepting fear more easily when it was not wrapped. "Then do not."

"The convoy may die if we do not."

"Then do it better."

Tamsa called from the central table, "Niko, stop solving policy and bring Doss the blue sensor tags."

"Yes, Supervisor."

He jumped down and ran.

Lena stood slowly. Her ribs had stiffened. Fatigue made every light too sharp.

Maro appeared beside her with two cups of tea. He handed one over. "The child has reduced our strategic framework to three words."

"Do it better?"

"A devastating memo."

"How is the gap?"

He looked toward the sealed speaker where Oriole-of-Archive had retreated into standby. "Still sealed. Louder than before."

"Cassian Vale?"

"Legally dead. Operationally implied. Morally tedious."

"That last one is not a status."

"It should be."

Lena drank the tea. It had been steeped too long and tasted like boiled decisions. "If Vale is the architect, why use Ceyr? Why not just destroy the gate?"

"Because destruction creates martyrs and investigations. Control creates governance."

"You sound like him."

Maro's mouth tightened. "Yes. That is why I dislike understanding it."

On the main board, Dax's latest packet arrived as a compressed text block rather than audio. Bandwidth was narrowing as the convoy approached the decision geometry.

AUX3 CABIN THERMAL 5H SAFE / 7H DANGEROUS.
PASSENGERS TRANSFERRING PARTIAL TO AUX2 VIA HARD DOCK IMPOSSIBLE UNDER CURRENT BURN.
LOCAL TEST CONFIRMS POLYMER DRIFT SPREADING IN SECONDARY LOOP.
MANUAL APPROACH OPTION REQUIRED WITHIN 4H.
DAX.

No comfort. He had learned the rhythm quickly.

Tamsa read it aloud once, then pinned it beside the jury verdict. "We need approach logic."

Sivek on the Control screen said, "Certified approach logic depends on central sampling bridge."

Vela replied, "Then uncertify your imagination."

"I am serious."

"So am I."

Lena stepped to the main board. "What if Dax does not approach as a convoy?"

The room quieted by degrees.

Sivek's eyes narrowed. "Explain."

"The hidden filter exploited hierarchy. One central board. One convoy approach corridor. One confidence state. If Dax approaches in standard formation, Kestrel must provide a clean envelope for all mass profiles at once."

Pellam said, "And Vane Three is sulking."

"Yes. But the convoy has nine primary and fourteen auxiliary ships. If Dax splits the approach into staggered mass groups, we can tune a narrower manual envelope for the least contaminated, highest medical-priority ships first, then decide whether Auxiliary Three attempts later or sheds passengers by emergency pod after the first group clears."

Sivek's face hardened. "That breaks convoy doctrine."

"Doctrine is not load-bearing if the bridge lies."

"Staggering mass groups through a restricted gate increases shear risk for every subsequent opening."

"So does pretending one opening can carry all of them."

Vela leaned back. "A narrow envelope may reduce dependence on Vane Three's compromised range if we weight opposite vanes higher."

"Can we?" Tamsa asked.

"Not elegantly."

"We are fresh out."

Doss pulled coolant data from Vane Three. "If we flush the affected loop with inert reserve, we can stabilize drift for one narrow pulse. Maybe two. It will contaminate the reserve and kill any chance of a standard full opening until parts arrive."

Pellam looked at her. "Sacrifice future capacity for first transit."

"Future capacity is theoretical if the children die."

Nurses on the side channel did not speak. Their silence counted.

Sivek rubbed his face. "A staggered manual approach requires Dax to trust us without certification."

"No," Lena said. "It requires him to trust the data. Source clocks visible. Jury verdict. Local disagreements included."

"And who pilots the counter-geometry from here?"

The question found the old room inside Lena. Command chair. Inquiry chair. Every chair that turned a person into a place blame could sit.

Before she could answer, Tamsa said, "Not just you."

Lena looked at her.

"You were about to get noble and stupid," Tamsa said.

"I had not decided."

"Your shoulders did."

Maro sipped tea. "Her shoulders are notoriously admissible."

Tamsa pointed at the board. "We built a jury. We fly like one. Lena handles route geometry. Vela watches trust layer and bridge behavior. Sivek holds Compact scheduling and legal gate authority. Doss owns coolant go/no-go. Pellam owns structure. Fei owns drone and blind-zone feeds. Medical owns priority sequence. Dax owns his ships. No single heroic idiot."

"You left yourself out," Lena said.

"I own yelling."

"And manual maintenance authority," Doss said.

"Fine. And that."

Sivek listened from Control, then nodded slowly. "A distributed manual approach board. It has no certification category."

Om's voice entered from the judicial channel. "It has one now. Provisional emergency local jury under court supervision. I will draft the order as soon as someone gives me something less ridiculous to call it."

Niko, passing with blue sensor tags, said, "Do it better board."

Om was silent for a beat. "Absolutely not."

Vela said, "Too late, Magistrate. It has local adoption."

The name appeared on three side feeds before Om could forbid it.

DO IT BETTER BOARD.

Tamsa closed her eyes. "I hate all of you."

"Documented," Maro said.

Work accelerated.

They built the board in layers. Vela forked the exposure lattice into a decision display, stripping out anything that depended on central smoothing. Doss added coolant thresholds in blunt color: blue for stable, yellow for drift, red for stop, black for unknown because she refused to let unknown borrow red's drama. Pellam overlaid structural stress and vane flexion, using old rigger shorthand until Lena made him translate it. Fei mapped blind pockets and drone reliability, tagging H-3 as UNCLASSIFIABLE / USEFUL / DO NOT TRUST ALONE. Medical ranked convoy ships by cold-chain urgency and passenger transfer impossibility, with privacy stripped but stakes visible. Sivek sent convoy mass profiles and burn constraints. Dax returned what he could in compressed bursts: ship mass, fuel margin, contamination status, passenger immobility, crew readiness.

The first approach model failed immediately.

Vane Three drift too high.

The second failed because Auxiliary Three could not decouple from the medical packet without losing thermal control.

The third required a burn Dax's oldest auxiliary could not make.

The fourth looked possible until Vela showed the hidden filter clipping Vane Five's minor disagreement, which meant the board was still ingesting one poisoned trust path.

Nobody threw anything. That was progress.

At five hours, thirty minutes, Oriole-of-Archive interrupted with a supplemental index result.

"Cassian Vale public death record includes disposal certification by House Ceyr emergency liaison and Compact Directorate officer. Certification witness: Sivek Hale."

The workshop went quiet.

On the Control screen, Sivek froze.

Lena felt the floor vanish in an old familiar way.

"Sivek," she said.

His voice came out rough. "I signed hundreds of demobilization death certifications. Directorate liaisons, missing agents, destroyed backups."

Maro's expression had gone cold. "Did you see Vale's body?"

"No."

"Did you verify continuity destruction?"

"The certificate said no recoverable substrate."

"Did you verify?"

Sivek closed his eyes. "No."

Tamsa swore softly. "Another green board."

Sivek opened his eyes. "Yes."

The admission did not solve anything. It did something worse: added motive, shame, and another old trust failure to the same collapsing stack.

Om spoke from the judicial channel. "Officer Hale, you remain in place for now because removing you would endanger the convoy. Your authority is countersigned by this court and the local jury. You will preserve all death certification records related to Cassian Vale."

"Understood," Sivek said.

Lena expected anger. It came, but behind it was something stranger: recognition. Not forgiveness. A map of how people who signed papers because systems showed green could become load-bearing in crimes they never intended.

Vela broke the silence. "If Vale is using dead-key doctrine and death certifications, he may have access to systems that treat him as absent. That is power. Absent people are rarely challenged."

Maro nodded. "We need to make him procedurally alive."

Pellam frowned. "Can law resurrect a saboteur faster than we can open a gate?"

Om said, "Watch me."

The magistrate's feed vanished.

At four hours, fifty-eight minutes, the fifth approach model produced amber with reasons.

First group: medical packets one and two, primaries Dax Actual, Kestrel Mercy, Varda Thread, auxiliary heat-stable ships only.

Second group: cargo and passenger auxiliaries after coolant flush, pending Vane Three drift.

Auxiliary Three: hold outside envelope, attempt passenger thermal stabilization, prepare emergency micro-pod transfer only if first group succeeds and gate remains stable.

Risk: high.

Certainty: unavailable.

Decision still required.

Lena read the model twice. Everyone did.

Tamsa looked at her. "Can you fly this from the ground?"

"No."

The room went very still.

Lena touched the model where the manual envelope narrowed around the first group. "Control can guide. Dax can fly. But someone needs to ride the calibration tug inside the local envelope and feed real-time vane disagreement back to the board, outside central smoothing. Close enough that the filter cannot lie before the source clocks scream."

Pellam said, "That sounds like volunteering for a bad obituary."

"It is not certain death."

"Pilot standard for optimism?"

"Yes."

Tamsa's voice dropped. "Larkspur is damaged."

"There are other tugs."

"Not with your local recorder modifications."

Fei raised a hand. "We can move Larkspur's recorder stack to another tug."

Doss said, "And load independent coolant sniffers."

Pellam added, "And weld a better clamp arm."

Tamsa looked around the workshop as her people began turning Lena's dangerous thought into tasks. "You are all impossible."

Lena met her eyes. "Distributed heroic idiocy."

"I did not approve that phrase."

"No single heroic idiot."

"Barely better."

Maro stepped closer. "If you ride the calibration tug, you become central to both evidence and operation. Ceyr will argue your old inquiry, your public broadcast, and your sanction make you reckless."

"They already did."

"They will be louder."

"Then make the record exact."

He studied her, then nodded. "With reservations."

"Everyone worth having," Tamsa said.

At four hours, forty minutes, they sent Dax the manual approach option.

The packet was longer than comfort and shorter than justice. It carried the ugly board, the staged approach, the local jury weights, the Vane Three coolant flush plan, the requirement for source clocks visible, the warning that central green was not proof, and the sentence Lena insisted remain despite bandwidth cost:

COMMAND AUTHORITY DISTRIBUTED; DISAGREEMENT WILL BE SHOWN, NOT HIDDEN.

Dax's reply took twelve minutes to arrive.

"Kestrel, Dax Actual. We accept ugly. Preparing first group. Aux Three captain objects to being left outside. I told her objection is noted and I dislike it too. Send tug pilot name."

The room looked at Lena.

She keyed the reply herself.

"Dax Actual, Kestrel local. Tug pilot Ors."

His answer came after the carrier delay, text only.

UNDERSTOOD. I KNOW THAT NAME. BRING BETTER THAN DOCTRINE.

Lena looked at the Do It Better Board, at Tamsa's workers, at Maro's sealed gap, at Vela's old filter exposed in public, at Sivek on the screen under court countersign, at the countless local feeds refusing to be smoothed into obedience.

"That," she said, "is the plan."

Chapter 22: Keva's Arithmetic

Deputy Administrator Keva Lorn asked to testify in the agricultural dark.

It was not poetic. She did not trust administration offices, judicial chambers, medical rooms, or anywhere with a console that recognized her credential without hesitation. She asked for Arc Green-Seven, a section of Halcyon's suspended crop belt currently running at half light to conserve power. Magistrate Om granted the request after making three people explain why a tomato corridor could be more secure than a hearing room and receiving three different answers, all persuasive.

So they met among vines.

The arc curved above and below them in spin-soft perspective, rows of trellised greens and nutrient lines following the station's inner hull. The plants had been trimmed hard during the ration years. Leaves grew where calories justified them. Flowers had been allowed only on crops that promised fruit. The air smelled wet, mineral, and faintly peppery. Work lamps cast narrow gold lanes through blue shadow. Every recorder had been checked by Tamsa's people, then by Om's clerks, then by a nurse who said she trusted neither law nor maintenance near a frightened witness.

Keva Lorn sat on an overturned harvest crate with her hands folded between her knees.

Without the recorded warmth of official notices, she looked exhausted rather than false. Her silver temple implants were dull. One had been covered with a medical block to prevent remote credential triggers. She wore a plain station jacket, not the administrator's formal blue, and she had a child's woven bracelet around one wrist.

Meli Aronn's, Lena guessed. Seven years old. Neural scaffold candidate. Cold-chain dependent.

Tamsa stood across from Keva with a stillness that made Lena more wary than shouting would have. Maro stood beside the portable rail, recorder active. Om presided from a crate stacked with nutrient sacks, perfectly dignified because she had apparently decided dignity was portable. Sivek attended by screen, bandwidth reduced to still images unless he spoke. Vela remained in the workshop with the Do It Better Board, muttering about tomato humidity feeds being more honest than senior counsel. Ilya Ceyr was permitted to observe without speaking unless addressed. Her image hovered small and sharp over Om's rail.

Keva looked first at Tamsa. "I did not order your removal."

Tamsa's face did not change. "Begin where you did something."

Om said, "Deputy Administrator Lorn, you are under protected testimony status. You are not immune. You are protected from retaliation for truthful disclosure and from immediate use of coerced statements pending review. Do you understand?"

"Yes."

"You requested medical priority protection for Meli Aronn."

Keva's fingers tightened around the bracelet. "Yes."

"It is granted. Her treatment priority may not be altered by Halcyon administration, House Ceyr, Compact scheduling, or any party to this hearing without medical review and court order."

Keva bent forward as if the words had struck her between the shoulders. For a moment Lena thought she would cry. She did not. Maybe administration trained that out. Maybe fear had.

Om waited. Plants clicked softly in the nutrient lines.

Keva said, "The first contact came nine days ago."

Maro's stylus moved. "From whom?"

"A woman using the name Saren Vale."

Maro's face tightened.

Om glanced at him. "Noted. Continue."

"She appeared on an administrative emergency channel with valid Ceyr liaison countersignature and a Compact reconstruction reference. She knew Meli's diagnosis. She knew the cold-chain dependency. She knew which convoy packet carried the scaffold cultures."

Tamsa said, "Medical files are sealed."

"Not from everyone."

"They should be."

Keva looked at her. "I know."

"Did she threaten the child?"

"Not at first. That would have been easier." Keva's voice was thin but steady. "She showed me a model. If Kestrel reopened under ordinary schedule, Halcyon received membranes, medical packets, and temporary relief. Then debt review resumed. Ceyr kept buffer leverage. Compact restored route certification. My office lost emergency authority in six weeks. District fan debt remained. Pediatric cases survived this month and died by budget next year."

"And if it failed?" Lena asked.

Keva's eyes moved to her. "If it failed visibly but recoverably, under controlled conditions, Halcyon became too politically expensive to abandon. Emergency relief triggered. Ceyr took technical control, yes, but under Compact public shame. Medical priority expanded. Debt suspended. Membranes guaranteed. The model projected fewer deaths over two years."

Tamsa's hands curled once.

"You believed her?"

"I believed the math was possible."

"That is not the same."

"No."

Maro said, "What did she ask you to do?"

"Prepare ration contingencies. Keep public order language ready. Allow administrative credential mirror access for gate safety backups. Do not challenge Ceyr's supply-chain advisory unless the rehearsal caused actual casualty beyond threshold. If a maintenance group became implicated, treat it as stabilizing narrative until technical control was secured."

"Stabilizing narrative," Tamsa repeated.

Keva flinched. "Yes."

"You mean blame workers before facts could interrupt."

"Yes."

The word did not absolve. It landed, ugly and necessary.

Om asked, "Did you know Lot 88 was contaminated?"

"No. I knew there was a supply-chain advisory. I did not know about the polymer. I did not know Tamsa had rejected the batch until after the bulletin."

"Did you authorize the bulletin blaming service-craft maintenance?"

Keva looked down. "My credential did."

"Did you?"

"No. But I wrote the language template."

Tamsa turned away.

Keva spoke faster, as if the words would rot if left inside. "I wrote six templates. Maintenance noncompliance. Routeholder equipment failure. Compact scheduling error. Unknown hostile action. Passenger misinformation. Civic unrest. I wrote them because administrators write ugly possibilities before ugly days. Saren told me the maintenance template would only be used if evidence supported it."

Maro's voice was quiet. "You gave a liar a loaded sentence."

"Yes."

Lena thought of command doctrine. Of boards showing green. Of Sivek signing death certifications. Of herself choosing three ships over one. The room offered no clean place to stand.

"Why continue after the false bulletin?" Lena asked.

"Because by then my credential was in the missing hour. Because Meli's priority was under review by a committee Saren could reach. Because if I said I had been compromised, Halcyon administration collapsed in the middle of the convoy window and Ceyr's emergency petition looked like the only adult in the room."

Tamsa turned back. "You thought hiding compromise preserved authority."

"Yes."

"It never does."

"I know that now."

"No. You know that because it failed."

Keva accepted the hit. "Yes."

Om said, "Did you cooperate in Supervisor Reet's removal?"

"No. The order used deputy-chain authority, not mine. But I created the protective review category after the blockade riots, and I did not sunset it. It should not have existed in a form administrative security could use without judicial sign-off."

Tamsa laughed without humor. "You built a tidy arrest box and someone put me in it."

"Yes."

"Why Gate Chemistry?"

Keva shook her head. "I did not know until the alert. When I saw Lock C, I tried to revoke the deputy-chain order. My implant triggered a credential conflict and locked me out."

Maro looked at the medical block on her temple. "The implant was used as part of the performance."

"I think so."

"Do you have logs?"

"Yes. Hidden. Agricultural archive."

For the first time, something like surprise crossed Tamsa's face. "Here?"

Keva looked at the vines. "Administration backs up crop yield disputes more honestly than personnel decisions. Food audits are ruthless."

Pellam's voice came through Tamsa's side channel from the workshop. "I have never been prouder of lettuce."

Tamsa muted him.

Keva stood carefully and walked to a nutrient control post. The nurse moved as if to stop her, but Om raised one hand. Keva opened a manual panel, removed a moisture log chip, and handed it to Maro.

"Credential conflicts, Saren contacts, medical priority threats, ration template edits, deputy-chain protective review changes," Keva said. "Not enough to make me innocent. Enough to make me useful."

Maro took the chip without softening the moment. "Evidence custody begins."

Om watched the seal. "Deputy Administrator Lorn, did Saren Vale identify any objective beyond Ceyr technical control?"

Keva returned to the crate. "She said Kestrel would become the precedent. First citizen-supervised failure, then proof citizen supervision was dangerous, then emergency doctrine restored for every unstable route."

Sivek's image flickered. "Vale wanted the Compact back through fear."

Ilya spoke before Om could stop her. "Not the Compact. A dead version wearing its seal."

Om let the statement remain.

Lena looked at Ilya's small image. "You know more about Saren."

Ilya's jaw tightened. "I know House Ceyr employed a Saren Vale during the Fracture Wars as liaison to route-security legal cells. She died at Aurelian with Cassian Vale."

Maro went very still. "Cassian had a sister."

"Officially."

Oriole-of-Archive's sealed voice entered the channel without flourish. "Index note: Aurelian-Blue-17 includes plural Vale reference. Content sealed. Public death record lists Cassian Vale and Saren Vale as co-certified losses."

Sivek closed his eyes.

Tamsa said, "You signed both?"

"Yes," Sivek said.

Keva whispered, "She is dead?"

Maro answered, "Legally."

Keva's laugh broke on the way out. "She told me dead people make the best administrators because no one audits their ambition."

The plants clicked in the dark.

Om straightened. "This testimony establishes probable coercion, administrative compromise, and use of dead-key identity connected to Cassian and Saren Vale. House Ceyr's technical-control petition remains suspended. Deputy Administrator Lorn is relieved of operational authority pending further review but remains under witness protection. Medical priority order stands."

Keva's shoulders lowered. Not relief exactly. Collapse under controlled conditions.

Tamsa stepped toward her.

Lena tensed. So did the nurse.

Tamsa stopped an arm's length away. "I am not forgiving you."

"I know."

"I am also not letting them use you as the whole story."

Keva looked up, and the first tears came then. Quiet, furious, humiliating.

"Thank you," she said.

"Do not thank me. Make your logs clean."

"I can do that."

"You had better."

The hearing dissolved into work. Maro sealed the agriculture chip. Om issued preservation orders. Ilya cut her feed after promising to produce Ceyr wartime liaison rosters under protest, which had become almost a greeting. Sivek returned to the Do It Better Board's approach model. Tamsa sent a clipped update to district leads: KEVA TESTIMONY PROTECTED. COERCION PROBABLE. DO NOT HARASS MEDICAL OR ADMIN STAFF. LOG EVERYTHING.

Lena lingered among the vines after the others began moving.

Keva remained on the crate, holding the child's bracelet.

"Why did you ask for me in the room?" Lena said.

Keva wiped her face with both hands. "Because Saren used you."

"How?"

"She said every system survives by choosing who to spend. She said you proved that at Nerio's Lamp and were punished only because you lacked the courage to call it governance."

Lena felt the words slide under her skin, looking for old wounds.

"She was wrong."

"I know." Keva looked toward the dim rows of plants. "You looked sick when you talked about the fourth ship. Saren looked alive when she talked about casualties."

Lena had no answer worth giving.

Her console chimed. Workshop priority. Vela.

TRUTH BRIDGE READY FOR TUG INSTALL.
LARKSPUR RECORDER STACK TRANSFER UNDERWAY.
ORS NEEDED AT DOCK TWELVE.
ALSO TELL REET TOMATO HUMIDITY FEED CAUGHT TWO MORE CLIPS. TOMATOES REMAIN CIVIC HEROES.

Lena showed Tamsa as she came back down the row.

Tamsa read it and sighed. "I refuse to decorate tomatoes."

"Noted."

"You going to the tug?"

"Yes."

Tamsa looked at Keva, then at Lena. "I will be there after I make sure nobody turns this testimony into a corridor riot."

"Supervisor says breathe?"

"Supervisor says stop enjoying that."

Lena left the agricultural dark with the smell of wet leaves clinging to her jacket and Keva's arithmetic behind her: a calculation built from terror, scarcity, a sick child, and the seductive violence of managed disaster. It had not made Keva innocent. It had made the enemy clearer.

Vale, dead and not dead, had not needed people to be monsters.

Only frightened enough to accept clean math.

Chapter 23: The Magistrate Delays

Dock Twelve looked like a shipyard had collided with a courtroom and neither side had yielded right of way.

The tug chosen for the manual approach was not Larkspur. Larkspur's frame had survived the rehearsal pulse, but its clamp trunk was warped and its forward blister still carried the silver flower from the debris strike. Fei had argued for using it anyway because "haunted machines learn faster." Tamsa had said no with enough force to settle the matter three decks away.

The replacement tug was Brindle, a squat, ugly service craft with two good clamp arms, overbuilt attitude jets, and a cockpit that smelled faintly of hot dust and old coffee. Its paint had once been orange. Years of patching had turned it into a map of practical regret. Workers had opened three panels along its spine and were feeding Larkspur's recorder stack into a cradle welded behind the pilot seat. Doss's coolant sniffers hung in a rack like glass organs. Fei's drone interface had been bolted to the aft console with the philosophical drone's diagnostic charm taped to one side for luck, despite Maro's objection that luck had no chain of custody.

Pellam supervised clamp reinforcement from a rolling stool because Tamsa had forbidden him from standing on ladders. He ignored the spirit of the order by making everyone else climb faster.

"No, not there," he barked at a young rigger. "If Ors shears that arm off, I want it to be because physics insisted, not because you aligned bolts like poetry."

Lena entered with a fresh medical brace under her jacket and a pain patch she had not told Tamsa about. The dock's noise wrapped around her: welding clicks, tool calls, recorder calibration tones, distant public announcements, the low animal hum of a station awake too long.

Fei looked up from the aft console. "Your tug is offensively ready to become interesting."

"I prefer boring."

"Then you should not fly near sabotaged gates."

"Career advice accepted late."

Maro stood beside Brindle's hatch with a slate. "Before you enter, I need assent on recording scope."

"Now?"

"Especially now."

Lena stopped. "Say it."

"Brindle will carry local telemetry, source-clock mesh, audio, pilot biometrics, and cockpit visual feed. Cognitive or medical inferences beyond immediate safety will be blocked. If you are injured or incapacitated, emergency medical override releases relevant biometrics to rescue only. If you die, the record remains evidence but not spectacle."

The last line was not legal boilerplate. It was Maro.

"Agreed," Lena said.

Tamsa's voice came from behind. "If she dies, I reserve the right to call her an idiot in the record."

Lena turned. "You handled Keva?"

"No one handles Keva tonight. We routed her into protected administrative quarantine with medical counsel and district witness logs. People are angry. They are being precise about it, mostly."

"Mostly."

"A tomato got thrown at an admin board."

"Decorated?"

"Do not start."

Maro added a note. "Tomato incident excluded from operational record."

"Coward," Tamsa said.

A judicial chime cut through the dock. Every public board shifted to Magistrate Om's seal.

Om appeared from the theater rail, but the image was duplicated across Dock Twelve, Scrubber Works, Control, Dock Eighteen, agricultural arc, medical wards, and House Ceyr's liaison room. She looked as tired as everyone else and less willing to admit it.

"Emergency session," she said. "Subject: gate opening authority, House Ceyr technical-control petition, local jury approach board, convoy decision window."

The dock quieted. Tools lowered. Welding stopped. Even Pellam shut up.

Om continued, "Evidence now establishes unauthorized trust-layer suppression in Kestrel systems, probable authority emulation, coercive use of medical priority, falsified or performed credentials involving Halcyon administration and House Ceyr dead-key infrastructure, and contamination of coolant associated with Lot 88 and Convoy Auxiliary Three."

Hearing it laid out did not make it clearer. It made the day enormous.

"House Ceyr's emergency technical-control petition is denied."

The dock erupted for half a second before Om's expression crushed the sound.

"Quiet. Denial of that petition does not make the gate safe. It prevents a compromised authority pathway from becoming sole command. Kestrel Gate remains under Compact operational authority countersigned by this court, with mandatory local jury oversight."

Ilya's image appeared in a smaller side frame. "House Ceyr objects to the implication that its current authority is compromised in entirety."

"Objection noted. House Ceyr's current authority is not compromised in entirety. It is compromised enough."

Someone in Dock Twelve whispered, "Ouch."

Om's gaze shifted, as if she could hear even that.

"The proposed manual approach for Convoy Dax-Varda-Seven is not certified under ordinary reopening law. Ordinary reopening law did not anticipate an illegally retained wartime trust layer, contaminated coolant, a coerced administrator, a nearly destroyed passenger shuttle, or a convoy whose medical cargo will begin failing inside the next few hours."

Vela's voice entered from the workshop. "For the record, ordinary reopening law lacks imagination."

Om closed her eyes for a beat. "Technician Cho, restraint."

"Under protest."

"Everything is."

A few people laughed, carefully.

Om looked back at the rail. "I am delaying final gate-opening authority by ninety minutes."

This time no one laughed.

Sivek appeared in another frame from Control. "Magistrate, Dax's window--"

"I know the window, Officer Hale. I am delaying because the current approach board shows amber with reasons, but Brindle's truth bridge is not installed, Vane Three coolant flush has not been performed, and Maro Venn's indexed query implicates Cassian and Saren Vale without operational location. I will not convert urgency into permission while required mitigations remain unfinished."

Tamsa muttered, "I like her less when she is right against me."

Om continued. "During the delay: Brindle truth bridge installation and test. Vane Three coolant flush simulation and physical readiness. Convoy first-group sequence confirmation. Public update every thirty minutes. No party may represent delay as cancellation, sabotage, cowardice, or victory."

Ilya said, "House Ceyr requests permission to assist in dead-key tracing."

"Granted under supervision and with read-only tools."

Ilya's mouth tightened. "Read-only may limit effectiveness."

"That is one of its charms."

Maro raised a hand from Dock Twelve. Om nodded.

"Magistrate, what about the Vale location?"

"All systems will treat Cassian Vale and Saren Vale as procedurally alive for purposes of warrant, access revocation, and evidence preservation. Public death protections remain for unrelated records. Oriole-of-Archive has agreed to monitor boundary compliance."

Oriole's voice entered. "Agreed is an overstatement. I will attend."

"Noted," Om said. "Vale-linked dead keys are to be burned, challenged, or quarantined. If burning risks operational collapse, quarantine and mark. Do not hide red under green."

Lena looked at the Do It Better Board mirrored above Brindle. Amber, with reasons. Delay, with reasons. The shape was ugly and honest.

Om's gaze settled on her through the feed. "Pilot Ors."

"Magistrate."

"You are provisionally authorized to pilot Tug Brindle as local calibration craft if final opening authority is granted. This authorization is operational, not restorative. It does not reinstate your route license. It does not absolve prior sanction. It does not make you symbol, scapegoat, or savior under this court's authority. You are a pilot assigned a dangerous task with witnesses."

Lena felt the words enter places Ilya's offer had tried to buy.

"Understood," she said.

"Try to keep it that way."

The session ended.

Dock Twelve resumed all at once. Welding, shouting, tool handoffs, arguments over recorder mount angles, Tamsa demanding the coolant flush checklist, Fei asking whether Brindle's aft console could accept a drone feed labeled PHILOSOPHICAL OVERRIDE, Maro saying no, Vela saying yes from the workshop because it would irritate the filter, Sivek sending updated Dax timing, Ilya filing a read-only access complaint that nobody read aloud.

Lena climbed into Brindle.

The cockpit was smaller than Larkspur's and laid out by someone who loved redundancy more than ergonomics. Good. Redundancy had better morals. She ran her hands over the controls without powering anything yet. Thrust. Attitude. Clamp. Local recorder. Source-clock mesh. Emergency foam. Manual light. A place for every decision to leave a mark.

Tamsa climbed halfway into the hatch and handed her a tool pouch.

"What is this?"

"Things you will not need if everyone is competent."

"That many?"

"I am feeling optimistic."

Lena opened the pouch. Manual seal patches. A small cutter. Two analog pressure tabs. A grease pencil. A strip of tape reading SOURCE CLOCKS VISIBLE.

"The tape seems mission-critical."

"Jori sent it."

Lena stuck it above Brindle's central display.

Maro appeared behind Tamsa. "Dax confirms first group sequencing. Auxiliary Three remains outside first transit. Its captain has filed an objection using language I admire."

"Does Dax hold?"

"For now. Cabin thermal margin on Aux Three is narrowing."

Tamsa looked over Lena's shoulder at the cockpit. "You sure?"

"No."

"Good. If you said yes, I would pull you out."

Lena powered the local recorder. Brindle's boards woke in layers. Not green. Not yet. Amber diagnostics, red warnings, blue setup, black unknowns waiting to be named.

Vela's voice entered the cockpit. "Truth bridge handshake ready. When you connect, the hidden filter may attempt to classify Brindle as hostile noise."

"How do we prevent that?"

"Be noisier than a lie but more consistent than panic."

"That is not a procedure."

"It is now."

Fei said, "Philosophical drone agrees."

"It cannot agree," Maro said.

"It has emitted a confidence log."

"That is not agreement."

Tamsa leaned closer to Lena. "Come back so they can argue about this in person."

Lena looked at her. "That sounded almost sentimental."

"You are concussed by anticipation."

"Noted."

At the edge of the dock, through the open service aperture, Kestrel Gate hung in the dark: wounded, watched, still necessary. The convoy was invisible beyond it, but the board carried its pressure in every countdown.

Ninety minutes.

The magistrate had delayed final authority not because time was abundant, but because time was the last honest thing no faction had managed to forge.

Lena settled into Brindle's pilot cradle and began the checklist.

Chapter 24: Field Vane Walk

Brindle disliked the truth bridge.

The tug did not say so in words, but small craft had always possessed eloquent ways to complain. Its aft console rejected the first handshake with Larkspur's recorder stack. Its attitude jets pulsed half a degree out of rhythm during dry fire. The source-clock mesh produced a whining harmonic through the cockpit speakers until Fei discovered an old cargo proximity alarm still trying to decide whether the judicial lockbox counted as freight.

"It is not freight," Maro said from the dockside evidence station.

"It has mass and is annoying," Fei said. "Brindle is applying dockworker philosophy."

Lena sat strapped into the pilot cradle with her gloves unsealed, hands resting lightly on the controls. Around her, the cockpit had become less a vehicle than an argument made of wires.
