The strip of tape from Jori sat above the central display: SOURCE CLOCKS VISIBLE. Beside it, Tamsa had added another strip in grease pencil: COME BACK OR I INVENT PAPERWORK.

The local jury board was mirrored onto Brindle's left display, compressed until the categories looked like a weather map for a place nobody should visit. Vane Three coolant drift remained amber. Sampling bridge trust remained red. Physical vane stability had shifted from amber-green to amber after Doss ran the flush simulation and discovered an old valve delay. Power buffers stayed green, which only meant one system had not yet become interesting. Manual override integrity remained amber because every remote authority in the station now smelled faintly of fraud.

The countdown to Om's delayed authority ruling read forty-eight minutes.

The convoy decision board read four hours, eight minutes.

Tamsa leaned through the hatch, one boot on the dock, one hand braced against the frame. "Brindle truth bridge?"

Fei answered from the aft console, half inside an open panel. "Insulted but alive."

"Recorder stack?"

Maro: "Chain intact. Source-clock mesh visible. Cockpit recording scope as agreed."

"Coolant sniffers?"

Doss, from the service cradle outside: "Calibrated against Lot 88 reject, Vane Three residue, and Auxiliary Three packet trace. If the polymer sneezes, we will know."

"Clamp arm?"

Pellam: "Better than factory and less pretty."

"Pilot?"

Lena looked at her. "Annoyed but alive."

"Acceptable."

Sivek's voice entered from Control. "Brindle, Kestrel Control. Dry truth-bridge test in ten seconds. Central smoothing isolated from your local feed. You should see disagreement rendered, not reconciled."

Vela cut in. "If you see perfect agreement, scream."

"Procedural scream or personal?"

"Both."

The cockpit lights dimmed by one shade as the test began. Brindle's local feeds populated the mesh: attitude jitter, dock beacon range, suit-cam from Sann outside on the service arm, coolant sniffer baseline, Larkspur recorder echo, tug hull flexion, pilot biometric safety line, philosophical drone channel marked UNCLASSIFIABLE / DO NOT WEIGHT ABOVE 0.3.

Central display tried to render the old clean green. For half a second, it succeeded.

Then Vela's truth bridge forced the disagreement open.

The board went amber with reasons.

Dock beacon A lag: 0.03 seconds.
Hull flexion sensor B noisy.
Pilot pulse elevated.
Drone H-3 timestamp malformed but stable.
Central smoothing attempted confidence upgrade. Rejected.

Fei slapped the console. "It rejected the lie politely."

"Good," Tamsa said. "We cannot afford rude electronics."

Vela's voice came dry. "Rudeness is phase two."

Lena flexed her fingers inside the gloves. Her pulse was elevated. The board had not been wrong. Seeing it displayed beside hull flexion and malformed drone time made her body just another source of data. Humbling. Useful.

"Control," she said, "Brindle accepts truth bridge."

Sivek replied, "Kestrel copies. Next step: near-field drift walk. Brindle will move from Dock Twelve to Vane Three service perimeter, hold outside field hazard boundary, and compare live local against jury prediction. No pulse. No opening. No heroics."

"You always ruin checklists with wishful thinking."

"I am experimenting."

Tamsa reached into the cockpit and tapped the grease-pencil warning. "Read the paperwork clause."

"I saw it."

"Understanding?"

"Limited but improving."

She backed out. The hatch sealed.

For a moment, before launch, the cockpit held only Lena's breathing and the old intimacy of a craft waiting to leave a dock. Fear settled into its proper place: not gone, not dominant, one instrument among others.

Dock clamps released.

Brindle drifted.

The tug slid out of Dock Twelve under low thrust, nose toward Kestrel Gate. Halcyon Ring curved below and behind, a patched city lit in restless strips. Through the forward blister, the field vanes grew from icons into architecture. Vane Three was still scarred by the rehearsal pulse, judicial seals glowing around the coupling zone like small blue verdicts. Service drones hovered at respectful distance. Two exterior workers clung to the far track, finishing coolant flush readiness checks under Sann's supervision.

The Do It Better Board followed her onto the left display. Not just numbers. People. Doss's coolant threshold. Pellam's structural tolerance. Fei's blind-zone map. Vela's trust-layer exposure. Medical priority. Dax's mass groups. Sivek's scheduling constraints. Om's legal conditions. Tamsa's manual authority locks. Jori's marked blind pocket, still flagged in red.

No single heroic idiot.

Brindle crossed the dock perimeter.

The hidden filter noticed.

Central confidence tried to climb from amber to green.

Vela said, "There. It wants you clean."

"Flattering."

"Do not be flattered by predation."

The truth bridge rejected the confidence upgrade. Amber held. A black note appeared beneath the central feed: UNSUPPORTED SMOOTHING ATTEMPT.

Across Halcyon, that note would be visible on the public technical board. Not the raw cockpit, but the verdict. The station was watching disagreement instead of being protected from it.

Brindle approached the service perimeter. Lena trimmed speed by hand. The tug's new clamp arm flexed at the edge of view. Coolant sniffers inhaled vacuum through sample ports and converted nothing into readings. Nothing, in this case, was information.

"Brindle at perimeter mark one," she said.

Sivek: "Copy. Hold relative to Vane Three. Drift walk begins. Move along the predicted envelope boundary, five meters per second, no closer than safety line."

"Beginning walk."

The phrase had old roots. A field vane walk was not walking and not on the vane. It was the slow movement of a calibration craft along an invisible mathematical boundary, close enough to read how a gate's field might bend, far enough to avoid becoming an example. Before the war, route pilots did it as apprenticeship ritual. After the war, most gates automated it because rituals were bad at liability.

Brindle slid along the boundary.

Vane Three filled the right side of the blister. Surface scars moved past: old micrometeor pits, new judicial tags, the abraded Quiet Mark area sealed under blue light, radiator flutter hinge stabilized with a temporary brace. Beyond it, Kestrel's star washed everything in pitiless white. The tug's local sensors began picking up residual field tension from the damaged vane, not active, not safe, a sleeping muscle after injury.

"Coolant drift reading," Doss said. "Low but present. Matches jury prediction."

"Structure?" Tamsa asked from the workshop channel.

Pellam answered, "Flexion ugly but not lying."

"Trust layer?" Om asked.

Vela: "Still attempting to promote central confidence every twelve seconds. Brindle source mesh rejecting. Pattern consistent with a buried automatic doctrine, not live operator."

Maro: "Say that again for the legal record."

"The hidden filter is acting like a system with standing instructions, not a person steering each event."

Lena watched the boundary line on her display. It trembled by fractions. "Can standing instructions adapt?"

Vela paused. "If someone wrote them to treat exposure as hostile action, yes."

"Then what does it do when we keep exposing?"

The answer came as Brindle's right-side dock beacon vanished.

Not failed. Clipped.

The truth bridge rendered the absence black.

DOCK TWELVE BEACON R2 SUPPRESSED BY TRUST LAYER
OFFSET: 0.44 SECONDS

Then another beacon vanished.

Then Sann's exterior suit cam jittered.

Fei swore. "It is trying to make the walk look unstable."

Lena kept Brindle steady. "Is the walk unstable?"

Doss: "Coolant says no."

Pellam: "Structure says no."

Vela: "Trust says yes because trust is a liar."

Tamsa: "Operational language."

"Trust layer is generating false instability by source suppression," Vela said.

"Better."

Lena brought Brindle through mark two, then mark three. The false instability tried to blossom. The Do It Better Board held it apart, showing every missing source, every disagreement, every place the old system wanted a pilot to see one clean answer and obey it.

Her hands remembered Nerio's Lamp. Four ships. One collapsing route. A board that had not lied, exactly, but had been less than the universe. Doctrine had asked for a decision. She had given one. Now, for the first time in years, she felt the distinction between command and loneliness.

"Brindle," Sivek said, "your pulse is rising."

"Source clocks visible."

"That includes you."

"I know."

At mark four, the coolant sniffer spiked.

Doss snapped, "Hold."

Lena killed forward drift and fired a half-second counterthrust. Brindle steadied. "Holding."

"Polymer trace?"

"Yes. Not from Vane Three vent. Localized pocket near auxiliary return. It was not in the model."

Tamsa said, "Position?"

Lena tagged the coordinate. It appeared near the underside of the vane, away from the known Lot 88 coupling.

Pellam's voice changed. "That is an old service injection point."

"I do not have it on official schematic," Maro said.

"You would not. Pre-Compact retrofit. Used for emergency flush before the buffer ring upgrade. Supposedly welded closed."

"Supposedly," Tamsa said. The word had become a curse.

Lena angled Brindle's camera. The injection point was small, half-hidden behind a brace, a circular cap coated in old sealant. On its edge, barely visible under Kestrel's glare, was an abrasion line.

Not the broken ring. A tool mark.

"Camera lock," she said.

Fei sharpened the feed. The truth bridge tagged it as new evidence. Judicial seal pending.

Vela said, "If polymer was introduced through an old flush point, Lot 88 is not the only vector."

"Meaning?" Om asked.

Doss answered. "Meaning someone could contaminate Vane Three after the rejected batch. Or keep contaminating it even if we flush the known loop."

The cockpit seemed to shrink around Lena.

"Can first-group opening still work?"

No one answered quickly enough.

Tamsa finally said, "If we isolate that injection point physically before the flush."

"Exterior work?"

"Yes."

"Time?"

Pellam: "Forty minutes if the cap behaves. An hour if it hates us."

The magistrate had delayed authority by ninety minutes. Half that mercy vanished in a single hidden pipe.

Sivek said, "Brindle, return to dock. We need exterior team on that point."

Lena looked at the old injection cap. "No."

Silence.

Tamsa's voice went dangerous. "Ors."

"Brindle is already here. Clamp arm can hold a seal patch against the cap. Not a repair. A temporary physical block. Exterior team can verify from the track, but we do not need to wait to stop an open vector."

Pellam said, "Clamp arm is reinforced. It can hold pressure if she is gentle."

"If," Tamsa said.

Lena brought up the clamp controls. "Doss, tell me where not to touch."

"Do not touch anything warm, glowing, leaking, or older than my career."

"Narrowing."

The workshop burst into layered instruction. Doss marked the cap edge. Pellam gave clamp angle. Tamsa argued with both. Vela watched the trust layer for source suppression. Maro filed an emergency preservation note. Om authorized noninvasive temporary containment under active record. Sivek cleared local maneuver. Fei routed Brindle's camera through three source clocks because the filter kept trying to clip the view.

Lena moved Brindle sideways by centimeters.

The clamp arm extended, slow and ugly, carrying a flexible seal patch from the emergency kit Tamsa had given her. The old injection cap drifted into the center of the display. Field tension whispered across the hull. The hidden filter attempted another central green. The truth bridge rejected it.

"Two centimeters," Pellam said.

"One," Doss said.

"Stop," Tamsa said.

Lena stopped.

"Apply."

The clamp pressed the patch against the cap.

For a moment nothing changed.

Then the coolant sniffer reading dropped.

Doss exhaled. "Polymer spike falling."

The workshop erupted into disciplined noise.

Lena held the clamp steady. Sweat ran down her back. Her injured ribs ached with each breath. The tug trembled through the arm, a tiny vibration from the vane into metal into her hands.

"Temporary block holding," she said.

Om: "Recorded. Exterior team will verify. Brindle, maintain until replacement brace arrives or return if safety degrades."

Sivek: "Convoy packet window in three minutes. Dax needs update."

Lena looked at the patched cap. Another hidden vector. Another reason amber had been honest.

"Tell him," she said, "we found one more thing the green board missed."

Chapter 25: Quiet Mark

The quiet mark was older than its symbol.

Vela said that while standing in Dock Twelve with her hands buried inside a portable compiler, but she said it as if she were remembering a room no one else could see. Around her, workers prepared an exterior brace for the old injection point, Brindle held position on the field perimeter, and the Do It Better Board updated its verdict from amber to amber-with-new-reasons, which made everyone feel worse and better in equal measure.

Tamsa stood beside Vela, arms crossed. "Explain before I decide that sentence is decorative."

"The broken ring came late," Vela said. "The habit came first. Directorate cells needed ways to mark deniable hardware without leaving official codes. At first they used toolpath scars, manufacturing asymmetry, hidden timing signatures. Then some cell started using the broken ring because people are superstitious and bureaucracies like icons even when committing crimes."

Maro, listening from the evidence station, said, "The mark on the drone hull and the mark on Vane Three coupling match broken-ring profile."

"Yes."

"The injection cap does not."

"No."

Tamsa looked through the dock aperture where Brindle was a small hard shape against the vast vane. "So it is unrelated?"

"I did not say that."

"You enjoy being difficult."

"I have very few hobbies."

Vela pulled up Brindle's close scan of the old injection cap. No broken ring. No filed symbol. Just a set of tiny parallel abrasions near the sealant edge.

"This is older notation," she said. "Three-line bite. Used before the broken ring by route-security fabrication teams that wanted maintenance crews to ignore a part by making it look like a bad old repair. See here? The scratches are too even for accidental tool slip. Too shallow for functional grip. They say: this has been touched, do not ask by whom."

Pellam leaned in on his rolling stool. "I have seen that."

Everyone turned.

He scowled. "Do not look like I announced a pregnancy. I have been crawling on old infrastructure since half of you were hypothetical. Three-line bites show up on prewar emergency bypasses, sealed valve ghosts, old Directorate nonsense, routeholder nonsense, and one coffee heater that never worked right."

"Where on Kestrel?" Maro asked.

Pellam's scowl deepened as memory became evidence. "Vane One auxiliary purge. Buffer ring old charge splitter. Dock Sixteen pressure dump. Maybe two more."

Tamsa's face changed. "Are any tied to coolant?"

"Vane One purge, yes. Not active. Supposedly."

"There is that word again."

Vela pushed the scan to the Do It Better Board. "We need a Quiet Mark sweep."

Sivek appeared from Control. "Across all gate hardware? We do not have time."

"Not across all hardware," Vela said. "Across hidden trust-relevant infrastructure. Old bypasses, sampling paths, authority bridges, coolant emergency routes, dock guidance, anything that can affect what command sees or what the gate drinks."

Doss said, "The gate drinks. Lovely."

"Would you prefer ingests?"

"No."

Lena's voice came from Brindle, thin but steady. "I can hold another twelve minutes. After that, the clamp arm will overheat."

Tamsa turned toward the open channel. "Exterior brace team is launching in six."

"Make it five."

"Make fewer suggestions while attached to a sabotaged vane."

"Filed."

Fei began pulling old imagery. Maintenance archives, exterior scans, worker suit videos, drone footage, ceremony preparation reels, insurance walkdowns, tourist recordings from before the war. The workshop and dock systems chewed through them under Vela's scratch-pattern detector, a tool she wrote in eleven minutes and labeled NOT MAGIC / DO NOT WORSHIP.

Marks appeared.

At first three. Then nine. Then twenty-seven possible scratches, most false. Workers dismissed accidental gouges. Pellam confirmed old repairs. Doss flagged coolant relevance. Tamsa sorted by danger. Maro sorted by admissibility. Vela sorted by whether the hidden trust layer might care.

The pattern was not everywhere. That made it worse.

It formed a path.

Vane Three injection cap. Vane One auxiliary purge. Buffer ring old charge splitter. Dock Eighteen guidance maintenance bridge. Halcyon administrative emergency suite. Gate service chemistry Lock C. K-RC-4 fabrication bay. Ceyr bonded Blue Vault Six.

Not a random collection. A route through systems that could poison, hide, authorize, frame, and seize.

Maro looked at the board. "Quiet Mark is not just hardware provenance. It is an operational map."

"A dead route," Vela said.

Tamsa's voice was low. "A route for people who do not exist."

Oriole-of-Archive entered the channel. "Terminology note: legal nonexistence is often used to commit logistical violence."

Pellam stared at the speaker. "I do not know who that is, but I want it on a mug."

Om's voice followed. "Focus."

Nobody argued, though several looked tempted.

The exterior brace team launched from Dock Twelve: Sann, Doss's assistant Lira, and an older hull worker named Brek whose pressure suit bore more patches than original paneling. They moved along the service track toward Brindle's clamp position while Lena held the tug steady against faint field tension and rising arm temperature.

"Brace team at Vane Three track," Sann said. "Visual on Brindle clamp."

Lena watched their suit lights in the blister. "You look slow."

"You look attached to a problem."

"Accurate."

Tamsa cut in. "Children, work."

The team secured a mechanical brace around the patched injection cap, careful not to remove Brindle's pressure until the brace took load. The coolant sniffer readings wavered, rose, then settled lower than before. Doss called each number like a prayer she refused to believe in.

"Brace holding," Sann said. "Brindle may release on my mark."

"Waiting."

"Mark."

Lena eased the clamp arm back.

The patch stayed.

The sniffer held.

Brindle's overheat warning dropped from amber to blue.

For three breaths, the station allowed itself a microscopic victory.

Then the Quiet Mark detector flagged another three-line bite.

This one was not on hardware.

It was in a video still from six years ago: a demobilization ceremony after the Fracture Wars, held in Kestrel's old guide theater before the dome shutters failed. Officers and routeholder representatives stood in rows while sealed crates of surrendered Directorate equipment passed under recorder view. On the left edge of the frame, half-obscured behind a Compact banner, a woman with pale hair accepted a flat black case from a man whose face was turned away.

The case bore the three-line bite.

Fei enlarged the image.

The woman was younger, less polished, but unmistakable.

Ilya Ceyr.

Dock Twelve went silent.

Ilya's remote feed did not appear. For once, she was not already in the room.

Maro spoke first. "Timestamp?"

Fei swallowed. "2419. Formal demobilization. Kestrel guide theater."

Sivek's face appeared on the Control screen, ashen. "That was the surrender inventory."

"Was the case registered?" Om asked.

Maro searched the old manifest. "No matching case in public surrender record."

Tamsa's expression hardened. "Of course."

Lena, still in Brindle, looked at the frozen image on her display. Ilya's hand on a black case. A hidden mark before the broken ring became symbol. A bridge between House Ceyr and the dead route.

"Call her," Lena said.

Om did.

Ilya took nine seconds to answer. When her image appeared, she was in a dark room lit by blue emergency text. Her hair was less perfect than usual. Behind her, Ceyr staff moved in urgent silence.

"Magistrate," she said.

Om displayed the still.

Ilya looked at it.

For the first time since Lena had met her, Ilya did not arrange her face quickly enough.

"Explain," Om said.

Ilya sat down slowly. "That is me."

"Yes."

"The case contained Ceyr liaison keys surrendered by a Directorate route-security cell."

Maro said, "It is not in the manifest."

"Because the public manifest would have exposed Ceyr wartime cooperation beyond the amnesty terms."

Tamsa laughed once. It contained no humor. "There it is."

Ilya looked toward the sound. "Yes. There it is."

Om's voice sharpened. "Where did the case go?"

"House Ceyr internal burn vault."

"Was it destroyed?"

Ilya looked at the still again. "It was certified destroyed."

Sivek made a sound like pain.

Lena said, "By whom?"

Ilya's eyes moved to her small Brindle feed. "I signed the destruction certificate."

The dock's silence changed texture.

Ilya continued before anyone could speak. "I did not personally inspect the burn. I was twenty-six, newly made liaison heir, told the amnesty depended on clean closure and that every house had similar embarrassments. I signed. My father told me survival required knowing which truths needed graves."

Tamsa stepped closer to the screen. "Your grave just tried to poison my gate."

"I know."

"Do you?"

Ilya's eyes flashed. "Yes."

Maro said, "The dead key C-47 came from that case?"

"Likely."

"Likely."

"Yes, Investigator, likely. I am not going to invent certainty because it would sound better."

Om watched her. "Who had access to the burn vault?"

"Ceyr senior legal, liaison heir, two security trustees, and the burn auditor."

"Names."

Ilya closed her eyes briefly. When she opened them, she was less polished and more dangerous. "My father, Ivo Ceyr. Counsel Harlan Mott. Security trustees Parel and Jun Ceyr. Burn auditor Saren Vale."

Maro's blue line seemed to brighten.

"Saren Vale was your burn auditor," he said.

"Yes."

Oriole-of-Archive spoke. "Contradiction: Saren Vale public death certification predates that demobilization ceremony by seventeen days."

Ilya went still.

Sivek whispered, "What?"

Om said, "Repeat."

"Saren Vale was certified dead at Aurelian seventeen days before the ceremony in which Ilya Ceyr states Saren Vale audited a Ceyr burn vault."

Lena felt the gate, the filter, the convoy, all the boards and feeds and hidden marks, converge around a single impossible administrative fact.

A dead woman had audited the destruction of keys later used by dead people.

Vela entered the channel. "Then either the death certificate was false, the auditor identity was false, or Saren Vale survived by becoming a function."

Tamsa frowned. "A function."

"A role. A set of credentials. A mask multiple people could wear."

Maro said, "Or a fork."

No one liked that word in the room.

Oriole answered carefully. "No public evidence of lawful Saren Vale fork continuity."

"Lawful," Tamsa said.

"Yes," Oriole replied. "That adjective is carrying distressing weight."

Ilya looked sick. "If Saren audited the burn vault after death, then everything I signed was theater."

"Not everything," Lena said. "The signature was real."

Ilya looked at her, and something like hatred passed between them, clean because it did not pretend to be anything else.

"Yes," Ilya said. "Mine was."

Om made her ruling voice out of the silence. "House Ceyr will open its internal burn vault records immediately. Read-only no longer applies. Judicial and Glassward boundary monitors will attend. Ilya Ceyr is a material witness and may not exercise unilateral authority over Kestrel-related systems."

Ilya did not object.

That frightened Lena more than an objection would have.

Sivek said, "Magistrate, final authority window in thirty-eight minutes."

"I know."

"We have new evidence of unknown scope."

"We also have a convoy running out of thermal margin."

Brindle's cockpit alarm chimed. Dax packet.

Lena opened it.

Text only.

FIRST GROUP READY.
AUX3 THERMAL CRITICAL IN 3H 20M.
CREWS INFORMED OF AMBER STATUS.
PASSENGERS NOT LIED TO.
AWAIT FINAL AUTHORITY.
DAX.

Not lied to.

Lena looked at the Quiet Mark route, the Ceyr still, the patched injection cap, the amber board, the countdowns.

Om said, "We proceed with mitigation and evidence preservation in parallel. No more graves for useful truths."

Tamsa's voice came through Brindle's cockpit, low and steady.

"Field vane walk complete. Come back, Ors. We have a gate to open badly or not at all."

Lena released the last hold on Vane Three's perimeter and turned Brindle toward Dock Twelve.

"Coming back," she said.

Chapter 26: Public Channel

The public channel had become too crowded for speeches.

That was the first thing Lena noticed after Brindle locked back into Dock Twelve and the hatch opened on heat, voices, and the hard metallic smell of rushed work. Every board in the dock carried layered notices: Om's legal orders, the Do It Better Board verdict, Dax's latest text, Ceyr burn-vault preservation, medical cold-chain margins, Vane Three patch status, and a line from district coordinators reminding residents that emergency gatherings near fan nodes would be treated as self-harm first and disorder second.

People were not waiting for a single voice to tell them what was true anymore. They were comparing feeds. Badly sometimes. Angrily often. But comparing.

Lena climbed out of Brindle and nearly missed the dock plating when her ribs objected to spin gravity. Tamsa caught her by the elbow.

"You are moving like a cracked pressure bell."

"Poetic."

"Diagnostic."

"Brindle is intact."

"I did not ask about Brindle."

"That is new."

Tamsa's grip tightened once, then released. She looked past Lena into the cockpit, at the strip of tape from Jori and the grease-pencil threat above it. "Truth bridge?"

"Held. It hates central smoothing."

"Smart tug."

Maro arrived with a recorder slate and a face that said he had been reading things no one should have to read quickly. "Burn-vault records are opening."

"Already?" Lena asked.

"Ilya is cooperating at a speed that suggests either sincerity or a good attorney's panic."

Tamsa snorted. "Can we weaponize both?"

"We are trying."

They crossed Dock Twelve toward the temporary public rail Om had installed beside the service windows. It was not a courtroom this time. It was a broadcast station stripped of performance. Three cameras, all labeled by source. A public transcript feed. A delay buffer managed by Om's clerk and mirrored by worker channel so no single authority could cut context without leaving a mark.

Om stood behind the rail with her pressure jacket unfastened at the throat. She looked as if she had not slept since before law was invented.

"Pilot Ors," she said. "Supervisor Reet. Investigator Venn. You are in time to be useful and not early enough to be dramatic."

"A narrow category," Maro said.

"Cherish it."

Ilya's feed appeared on one screen. She was in the Ceyr liaison room now, surrounded by opened vault records, three unsmiling legal staff, and a Glassward boundary seal that cast a thin blue halo around the edge of the image. Oriole-of-Archive attended as an icon of shifting text beside her. Sivek appeared from Control. Vela from Dock Eighteen, still taped to half her own equipment. Dax had no live feed; the convoy was too bandwidth-poor for conversation. His latest packet sat pinned to the board like a hard little stone.

FIRST GROUP READY.
PASSENGERS NOT LIED TO.

Om opened the public channel.

Every board on Halcyon shifted. Not to one feed. To the labeled mosaic. Om had learned the station's new grammar quickly.

"Residents of Halcyon and crews of Convoy Dax-Varda-Seven receiving delayed packet summary," she said, "this is an evidentiary and operational update. It is not reassurance. It is not surrender."

The transcript rolled beneath her.

"Kestrel Gate remains damaged and compromised. The local jury has produced an amber manual approach option for the first convoy group. Amber means risk remains. Amber means reasons are visible. It does not mean safe. It does not mean hopeless."

On the public board, the Do It Better Board displayed the same amber verdict with categories. Someone in the dock whispered the nickname, and someone else shushed them as if Om might punish familiarity by legal theory.

Om continued. "House Ceyr's emergency technical-control petition remains denied. House Ceyr has produced burn-vault records showing that Fracture-era liaison keys were certified destroyed after the war. Those certifications are now suspect. Saren Vale, listed as legally dead before the relevant destruction audit, appears in records as burn auditor after her certified death. Cassian Vale and Saren Vale are now treated as procedurally alive for purposes of investigation and access revocation."

The dock reacted in a wave: murmurs, curses, one startled laugh from a mechanic who clearly regretted it.

Ilya's face did not move.

Om looked toward her feed. "House Ceyr may state its current position."

Ilya leaned forward. When she spoke, the polish was still there, but it no longer disguised exhaustion.

"House Ceyr admits that wartime cooperation with Directorate route-security cells was concealed beyond the spirit of postwar amnesty and that my signature appears on a destruction certificate I did not personally verify. That failure allowed dead-key infrastructure to remain credible enough to be weaponized. House Ceyr does not admit authoring the present sabotage. It does admit that its secrecy helped make the sabotage possible."

Tamsa muttered, "Put that on the carpet."

The transcript caught only the official feed, mercifully.

Ilya continued. "We are opening relevant burn-vault records under judicial and Glassward boundary supervision. We will not assert technical control over Kestrel Gate. We will provide material support under local jury and court order."

Om nodded once. "Noted."

Not forgiveness. Not trust. A fact placed where people could see it.

The public channel filled with district responses, but the delay buffer held them out of the main feed. Om's clerk summarized: anger high, corridor movement stable, no fan-node breaches, medical ward quiet, Dock Eighteen passengers requesting Dax updates.

Sivek spoke next. "Convoy Dax-Varda-Seven has accepted the manual first-group approach. Tug Brindle will serve as local calibration craft. The first group will include primary command vessel Dax Actual, medical packets one and two, and heat-stable escorts. Auxiliary Three remains outside first transit due to contamination and thermal instability. That is not abandonment. It is staged survival."

The phrase hurt. It had to. If it did not hurt, it would be too easy to spend people with it.

Om said, "Medical priority confirms staged approach?"

A nurse from the pediatric ward appeared in a small feed, face severe. "Medical confirms first group maximizes immediate cold-chain preservation without hiding Auxiliary Three risk. We object morally to all available options."

"Recorded," Om said.

Lena saw Tamsa look down, jaw tight. Moral objection recorded. Amber with reasons.

Then Om turned to Lena.

"Pilot Ors, operational statement."

Lena stepped behind the rail. The cameras did not zoom. Om had forbidden drama by lens.

"Brindle completed near-field walk at Vane Three," Lena said. "Truth bridge held. The hidden filter attempted multiple unsupported confidence upgrades and source suppressions. Those attempts were rendered and rejected. We found an additional polymer vector at an old injection point. A temporary brace is holding. Coolant drift is reduced but not gone."

She could feel the station wanting more. A promise, a posture, a reason to believe. She gave it what they had.

"If final authority is granted, I will fly Brindle inside the calibration perimeter. Dax will fly his ships. Control will guide under court countersign. The local jury will show disagreement, not hide it. If the board is red, we stop. If it is amber, we decide in public. If it is green, we distrust it until the reasons are visible."

Vela's voice from Dock Eighteen cut in. "That last sentence should be doctrine."

Om said, "Technician Cho, this is not a workshop channel."

"It should still be doctrine."

Several district feeds registered approval. Om ignored them professionally.

Maro stepped to the rail. "Evidence statement. The investigation currently supports probable use of dead-key authority, retained trust-layer manipulation, coolant contamination through multiple vectors, and attempted framing of maintenance personnel. It does not yet support final attribution to any living or legally dead individual. Pavo Irren's testimony, Keva Lorn's protected testimony, the Quiet Mark route, burn-vault records, and drone memory preservation remain under review. The absence of a complete culprit does not erase the presence of an active threat."

Tamsa glanced at him. "That was almost plain."

"I am adapting."

Om allowed one public question summary from the district channel. Her clerk read it: "Residents ask why the gate should be opened at all if the threat remains active."

No one answered immediately.

Then Tamsa stepped forward.

"Because not opening is also an action," she said. "Because children in the ward and people on the convoy are not abstractions we can protect by waiting until the universe becomes clean. Because the people who built this mess want us to choose between blind trust and paralysis. We are choosing neither. We are choosing dirty work under lights."

The dock went quiet around her.

"And because," she added, "if anyone breaks a fan node while I am busy, I will find them."

Om closed her eyes. "Supervisor."

"That was operational."

"Barely."

The public channel registered the first real laugh in hours. It spread through the dock, then through districts, then died before it became relief. Good. Relief would have been premature.

The burn-vault feed flashed. Oriole-of-Archive spoke.

"New record recovered. Saren Vale audit token includes post-death continuity checksum inconsistent with biological survival and inconsistent with lawful fork registration."

Maro straightened. "Meaning?"

"Meaning the Saren authority may be neither a single living person nor a lawful continuity. It may be an assembled operational persona."

Vela swore. "A role mask."

Ilya looked sick again. "Built from dead liaison records?"

"Probable," Oriole said. "Cassian Vale link remains uncertain."

Om absorbed the new horror at speed. "Can this persona still hold authority?"

Oriole: "Only where systems accept dead records, old seals, or performed trust over current embodied verification."

Tamsa looked at the boards. "Half the station, then."

"Less after tonight," Maro said.

Sivek's voice tightened. "Control reports final authority window in twenty-six minutes. Dax's first group reports burn alignment begins in forty-two."

Om's expression hardened. "Then we close public statement."

She faced the cameras.

"Final authority review begins now. Residents: remain at assigned posts. District leads: keep source clocks visible. Medical: update cold-chain status every ten minutes. House Ceyr: continue vault disclosure. Compact: preserve all bridge states. Maintenance: prepare Vane Three flush and brace watch. Convoy crews: you will receive compressed summary in the next packet. No one is being asked for calm. You are being asked for exactness."

She ended the channel.

The dock noise returned in a rush.

Lena stepped away from the rail and found Ilya's feed still looking at her.

"You refused my offer," Ilya said privately.

"Yes."

"Good."

That was unexpected enough to stop Lena's hand over the disconnect.

Ilya looked at the burn-vault records moving behind her. "If you had accepted, I would have known I was still thinking like my father."

"You are telling me this because?"

"Because after the first group, if we survive it, you will need Ceyr material support for the second. I would prefer you dislike me accurately rather than theatrically."

Lena studied her. "I can do both."

For the first time, Ilya almost smiled and failed. "I have earned that."

The feed cut.

Tamsa came up beside Lena. "Do I need to ask?"

"No."

"Good. Brindle is ready. Doss wants you to drink electrolyte paste. Jori wants proof that I am not doing anything stupid. Maro wants you to sign another recording scope addendum. Vela wants everything impossible. Sivek wants three minutes of your attention. Om wants no one to surprise her. I want a different life."

"Order?"

"Paste first. I am not explaining pilot dehydration to a teenager."

Lena accepted the packet Tamsa shoved at her. It tasted like salted glue and survival.

Above Dock Twelve, the board held amber.

Twenty-four minutes.

Chapter 27: The Convoy Burns In

Captain Olan Dax sent one audio packet before committing the first group.

It arrived stripped thin by low-band compression, all warmth squeezed out until only the bone of his voice remained. Dock Twelve played it through the operational channel while Lena sat in Brindle's cradle, helmet ring open, hands gloved and still.

"Kestrel, Dax Actual. First group aligned. Medical packets secured. Crews briefed on amber reasons. Passengers told we are attempting restricted manual transit under compromised gate conditions. Some prayed. Some cursed. One child asked whether amber means brave. I said amber means honest. Do not make me a liar. Dax out."

No one spoke afterward.

Then Vela said, quietly enough that the channel almost missed it, "Good captain."

Tamsa, from Dock Twelve command, answered, "Everyone keeps saying that like it helps."

"It does not help," Maro said. "It matters."

Lena sealed her helmet.

The cockpit narrowed to breath, boards, and the forward blister. Brindle's systems woke fully around her. Local jury feed on the left. Tug telemetry center. Control relay right. Dax packet queue below. Source clocks visible across the top like a row of stubborn witnesses.

Om's final authority ruling came at T-minus nineteen minutes.

"Kestrel Gate restricted manual opening authorized for Convoy Dax first group only. Authority distributed: Compact Control, local jury, Brindle calibration, convoy command, medical priority, judicial abort. No party may convert first-group authorization into general reopening claim. Amber with reasons is the operative state. Proceed."

Sivek's voice followed, steady and stripped of everything but function. "Kestrel Control to all stations. Restricted manual opening sequence begins. Power buffers to warm state. Vane teams stand by. Brindle, release dock on my mark."

Tamsa: "Vane Three flush team ready."

Doss: "Coolant loop isolated. Inert reserve staged. Patch and brace holding."

Pellam: "Structure watching. If something flexes ugly, I will say ugly."

Fei: "Drone feeds live. H-3 is emitting confidence in a fan controller, but otherwise useful."

Vela: "Truth bridge ready. Hidden filter still attempting low-level confidence promotion. We are rejecting."

Maro: "Recorder scope active. Evidence chain continuous."

Om: "Judicial abort active."

Sivek: "Brindle, release."

Dock clamps opened.

Brindle drifted into the dark.

The tug moved slowly at first, passing out of Dock Twelve's service aperture into the harsh light of Kestrel's yard. Halcyon Ring fell away behind Lena, its patched arc crowded with watching windows. Ahead, the gate vanes waited: six black ribs around a place the universe could be persuaded to change its mind. Vane Three wore its blue seals and new brace. The power-buffer ring glowed in sections, not bright enough to dazzle, bright enough to make every old fear in Lena's body wake.

She flew to the calibration perimeter and took her mark.

"Brindle on local point," she said.

"Control copies," Sivek replied. "Power buffer warm in ninety seconds."

The Do It Better Board shifted. Power buffers green. Vane One green-amber. Vane Two green. Vane Three amber. Vane Four green. Vane Five amber-green. Vane Six green. Trust layer red, contained. Hostile provenance red, monitored. Medical urgency red, accepted. Decision state: amber.

Not safe.

Enough to choose.

Far beyond the gate, the first convoy group began its burn.

They could not see it with the naked eye. The distance and angle made the ships abstractions on the board, moving numbers tied to names. Dax Actual. Varda Thread. Kestrel Mercy. Medical Packet One. Medical Packet Two. Heat-stable escorts. Each mass profile had been compressed, argued over, weighted, and placed into the manual approach model. Each carried people who had been told amber meant honest.

At T-minus ten minutes, Vane Three coolant flush began.

Doss called it like a surgeon. "Inert reserve entering loop. Temperature drop two degrees. Polymer response low. Drift falling. Patch holding."

"Structure?" Sivek asked.

Pellam: "Brace strain ugly but polite."

Tamsa: "Operational language, old man."

"Brace strain within temporary limit."

"Better."

The hidden filter made its first serious move at T-minus eight.

Central board climbed toward green.

Not slowly. Not subtly. It surged, assigning confidence where the local jury still showed amber. Vane Three drift? Acceptable. Trust layer? Resolved. Hostile provenance? Irrelevant to operation. The old doctrine trying to protect command attention from fear by removing reasons to fear.

Vela's voice cut sharp. "Rejecting central promotion."

The truth bridge held.

For one second.

Then Vane Five's local feed vanished.

Black rectangle: SUPPRESSED BY TRUST LAYER.

Then Dock Twelve beacon backup.

Then Brindle's aft hull flexion.

Lena kept the tug steady. "It is trying to blind the calibration point."

Fei: "Routing alternates. H-3, do your weird thing."

Static flooded a side channel. The philosophical drone sprayed malformed but signed telemetry into the mesh. The hidden filter hesitated just long enough for Vela to pin the clipped feeds and force them visible as absences.

Do It Better Board: amber-red.

Om: "Abort threshold?"

Doss: "Coolant stable."

Pellam: "Structure stable."

Vela: "Trust attack active but visible."

Lena watched Brindle's local instruments. The tug itself knew where it was. Source clocks visible. Hull flexion restored through alternate. Vane Five reappeared with a timestamp scar.

"Not abort," Lena said. "Continue with trust attack flagged."

Sivek did not hesitate. "Continue. Trust attack flagged."

At T-minus five, the power buffers rose.

Kestrel Gate woke again.

The hum came through Brindle's frame before it came through audio translation, a pressure in metal and bone. The vanes took power and turned it into disciplined stress. The star beyond them wrinkled, not yet opening, only listening. Lena's boards filled with live disagreement. Vane Three lagged by fractions. Vane One compensated. Vane Five complained about a vibration that central would once have smoothed away. The local jury showed every argument.

It was ugly.

It was magnificent.

"Convoy first group at outer manual envelope," Sivek said.

Dax's text flashed, carried on a tight packet riding their burn laser:

WE SEE AMBER. COMMITTING.

Lena's hands tightened on the controls.

At T-minus three, the first aperture boundary formed.

No black seam this time. A pale distortion along the gate throat, thin and trembling, like heat over a road no planet had built. Brindle's sniffers detected a polymer flutter and rejected it as below drift threshold. Vane Three's patched injection point held. The old filter tried again to call the boundary stable. The jury refused and labeled it: STABLE ENOUGH FOR FIRST GROUP MODEL, NOT GENERAL.

"Language matters," Maro said, voice barely audible.

"Everything matters," Tamsa replied.

At T-minus two, Auxiliary Three sent a burst:

WATCHING. NOT LEFT. DO NOT WASTE OUR WAIT.

No one had time to answer.

The convoy first group entered the inner approach.

Now the ships appeared on optical: six sparks in formation, too bright against dark, burning hard enough to become visible truth. Their vectors overlaid Brindle's board. Lena moved the tug along the calibration perimeter, matching the envelope's breathing. When Vane Three lagged, she tagged it. When Vane Five jittered, she tagged it. The Do It Better Board adjusted weights in real time, not hiding disagreement, giving it shape.

"Dax Actual yaw minus point zero six," Lena said.

Sivek relayed. "Dax, yaw minus point zero six."

"Medical Packet Two drift high," Tamsa said from the jury feed.

"Packet Two, correct starboard two," Sivek transmitted.

Dax text: CORRECTING.

At T-minus one, the aperture opened.

Not fully. Not cleanly. The gate throat folded into a narrow, shimmering corridor whose edges trembled with constrained violence. Kestrel's star bent around it. The vanes sang through Brindle's hull. Every board went briefly insane with too much truth.

Central tried to go green.

The local jury held amber.

Lena saw a flicker near Vane Three. A tiny black suggestion at the lower edge, where the bad seam had opened during rehearsal.

"Boundary sag Vane Three lower," she said.

Doss: "Coolant drift rising."

Pellam: "Brace strain near limit."

Vela: "Filter suppressing sag."

Om: "Abort?"

The first convoy group was seconds from commitment. Abort now meant scattering ships across fuel profiles the model had not built. Continue meant trusting amber with reasons.

Lena looked at the sag. At the ships. At the board. At every visible disagreement.

"Not abort," she said. "Narrow envelope upward point zero three. Dax, lift formation."

Sivek relayed without changing the words.

Dax lifted.

Medical Packet Two lagged.

For one awful second, the packet's icon brushed the modeled boundary. Its cold-chain cargo, its crew, its children represented only by medical priority and mass, all balanced on a number too small for human grief.

Brindle's local sensor clipped.

Black rectangle.

Lena did not wait for the feed to return. She had the optical. Her own eyes. Local truth does not become invalid because the board is attacked.

"Packet Two, burn dorsal now," she said.

"Authority?" Sivek snapped.

"My eyes."

A fraction of a second.

Then Dax relayed, "Packet Two, dorsal now."

The medical packet burned.

It cleared the sag by meters.

The first ship hit the aperture.

For a moment Dax Actual became a line of light drawn through a folded mistake. Then it vanished.

Varda Thread followed.

Kestrel Mercy.

Medical Packet One.

Medical Packet Two, still correcting, entered with a flare that turned every board red for half a heartbeat.

Then it vanished.

The escorts followed.

The aperture snapped down to rehearsal width, trembling but intact.

Brindle spun half a degree as the release wave slapped the calibration perimeter. Lena corrected manually. Her teeth clicked together. The boards screamed, then sorted themselves.

First group transit status: pending.

No one breathed.

Then the low-band carrier chirped.

Sivek opened it.

Text appeared.

DAX ACTUAL THROUGH.
VARDA THREAD THROUGH.
KESTREL MERCY THROUGH.
MED PACKET ONE THROUGH.
MED PACKET TWO THROUGH DAMAGED BUT PRESSURIZED.
ESCORTS THROUGH.
FIRST GROUP ALIVE.

Dock Twelve erupted.

Control erupted.

The public channels erupted.

Lena did not. She kept Brindle steady at the perimeter because the gate was still open at rehearsal width and Auxiliary Three was still outside with thermal death creeping through its cabins.

Tamsa's voice reached her through the noise. "Good work. Do not celebrate."

"Wasn't."

"I know."

The Do It Better Board updated:

FIRST GROUP: ALIVE.
GATE STATE: DAMAGED.
VANE THREE: AMBER-RED.
AUXILIARY THREE: CRITICAL.
SECOND DECISION REQUIRED.

Dax's next packet arrived eleven seconds later.

AUX3 CAPTAIN REQUESTS IMMEDIATE OPTION.
FIRST GROUP SURVIVAL BUYS TRUST, NOT TIME.
DAX.

Lena looked at the still-trembling aperture.

Amber had carried the first group. It had not saved everyone.

The convoy was still burning in.

Chapter 28: Ceyr's Injunction

House Ceyr filed its injunction eleven minutes after the first group survived.

It arrived through five channels at once: Compact legal, Meridian insurance relay, Halcyon public docket, the Do It Better Board's evidence sidebar, and Ilya Ceyr's private feed to Magistrate Om. The filing had clearly been prepared by more than one faction and reconciled by no one. Its title called for emergency preservation of life, emergency protection of House assets, emergency review of unauthorized distributed command, and emergency restraint against reckless second transit. Its body contradicted itself in places where counsel had not had time to sand down panic.

Om read the first page, sighed once, and sent it to every public board under the label: CEYR INJUNCTION REQUEST / UNRULED / DO NOT TREAT AS ORDER.

Tamsa saw that and said, "I like her again."

Lena stayed in Brindle at the calibration perimeter while the filing spread. The tug trembled in the residual field wash. The gate throat had collapsed to a narrow maintenance distortion, no longer open, not entirely quiet. Vane Three's coolant drift had risen after first transit, then settled just below Doss's red line. The old injection brace held. The truth bridge kept stamping central smoothing attempts as unsupported, though the hidden filter seemed weaker now, or more cautious, or only waiting.

Auxiliary Three remained outside the envelope.

On the board, its icon pulsed amber-red.

Tamsa routed the injunction summary into Brindle's cockpit. "Relevant portion: Ceyr says any second opening before dead-key audit completion risks deliberate destruction of evidence and mass casualty. They want all second-stage transit suspended pending House-supervised technical review."

"House-supervised," Lena said.

"Yes. They buried the old wish in a new coffin."

Maro's voice entered, clipped and irritated. "The filing is not entirely frivolous. Vane Three's post-transit drift is worse. The burn-vault records are incomplete. If Auxiliary Three fails during second transit, Ceyr will argue the local jury turned a rescue into proof of reckless governance."

Tamsa replied, "If Auxiliary Three dies waiting, what do they argue?"

"That the delay was tragic prudence."

"I hate law."

"With cause."

Sivek came on from Control. "Dax reports Auxiliary Three cabin thermal at two hours fifty-two minutes to red medical profile. Their captain is proposing a stripped transit: detach contaminated thermal bus, power down nonessential compartments, reduce crewed zones, attempt a narrow solo pass after first-group wake stabilizes."

"Can it fly?" Lena asked.

"Barely."

"Can the gate take it?"

No one answered quickly.

Vela broke the silence. "Maybe, if we do not pretend it is a second group. It is a needle pass. One ship. Lower mass than first-group packet, but contamination makes its internal guidance suspect."

Doss said, "If their secondary loop is drifting, their local board may be honest and sick at the same time."

Pellam added, "Vane Three brace can hold one more narrow event if coolant stays under red. Maybe."

"That word again," Tamsa said.

Om's channel opened. "All operational parties to emergency review. Now."

The review convened across places rather than in one. Om at the public rail in Dock Twelve. Lena in Brindle. Sivek in Control. Tamsa, Vela, Doss, Pellam, Fei, and the local jury in Scrubber Works. Maro in the evidence bay with the Ceyr injunction open on one slate and burn-vault records on another. Ilya in House Ceyr's liaison room, flanked by counsel she was no longer fully obeying. Dax by text only. Auxiliary Three's captain, Rena Kall, by audio packet delayed and rough.

Kall's packet played first.

"Kestrel, this is Auxiliary Three. I have three hundred eighty-two registered, unknown unregistered after cargo stack breach, medical overflow twelve, crew forty-one. Thermal control in crewed compartments is degrading. Contaminated loop isolated from nav but not from cabin life. I can power down half the ship and bring us through narrow if you give me an ugly corridor. If you deny, say deny. If you delay, call it what it is."

The packet ended.

Om looked at Ilya's feed. "House Ceyr, state your injunction position in one minute."

Ilya's senior counsel tried to speak. Ilya cut him off.

"House Ceyr requests suspension of second transit until Vane Three drift and dead-key manipulation are better understood. A failed second transit may destroy Auxiliary Three, further damage Kestrel Gate, and compromise evidence needed to identify the Vale persona or persons. We no longer request House-supervised technical control."

Her counsel visibly suffered.

Om said, "That last sentence amends your filing?"

"Yes."

"Recorded. Pilot Ors?"

Lena looked at Auxiliary Three's icon. "The ship has less time than the gate needs. That is the problem. A solo needle pass is physically less stressful than full second group, but less forgiving. If their internal guidance is contaminated, Brindle can provide external calibration, but only if we hold closer to the envelope than first transit."

Tamsa snapped, "Define closer."

"Inside previous safety margin."

"Absolutely not."

"I did not say I liked it."

"You said it like someone preparing to do it."

"Yes."

Sivek said, "Brindle inside previous margin risks calibration craft loss if the aperture sags again."

"Auxiliary Three loss otherwise."

Maro's voice was quiet. "We should distinguish risk from trade."

"Then do it fast," Tamsa said.

Maro took a breath. "If Brindle moves inward, Lena accepts higher personal risk to reduce ship-wide risk. That is not automatically ethical if her presence becomes necessary only because systems failed elsewhere. We should not launder institutional failure through pilot sacrifice."

Lena stared at the board.

Tamsa said, "Thank you."

"I am not finished," Maro said. "If Brindle does not move inward, Auxiliary Three's captain may be forced into blind self-guidance under worse conditions. That launders institutional failure through passenger death. There is no clean option."

"I liked the first half better," Tamsa said.

"So did I."

Om looked to the local jury. "Supervisor Reet. Board verdict."

Tamsa stood before the Scrubber Works display. "For solo needle pass: power buffers green. Vane Three amber-red. Patch brace amber. Trust layer red but visible. Auxiliary Three guidance amber-red due to contamination. Brindle close calibration reduces Auxiliary Three guidance risk and increases Brindle loss risk. Medical urgency red. Evidence preservation concern amber. Jury verdict: amber-red with reasons. Decision required in under forty minutes if attempting."

"Recommendation?"

Tamsa's jaw tightened. "Attempt, if Auxiliary Three crew consents with full amber reasons and if Brindle pilot consents without institutional pressure."

Om looked at Lena. "Pilot Ors?"

Every channel seemed to lean.

Consent without institutional pressure. An ideal phrase in a room made of pressure.

"I consent to fly closer margin," Lena said. "Not because it absolves anyone. Not because pilots are cheaper than passengers. Because with the board we have, my position can reduce risk for Auxiliary Three enough to matter, and I accept the personal risk under witness."

Maro said, "Recorded."

Tamsa said nothing.

That worried Lena more than objection.

Ilya leaned forward. "House Ceyr withdraws request to suspend if Auxiliary Three and Brindle both consent under court record."

Her counsel turned toward her in horror.

Om's gaze sharpened. "That is a major amendment."

"The dead-key audit will not matter to dead passengers."

"Recorded."

Sivek said, "Dax packet window in two minutes."

Om ruled. "Ceyr injunction denied as to blanket suspension. Evidence preservation concern remains active. Auxiliary Three solo needle pass conditionally authorized if Captain Kall confirms informed consent, Dax confirms formation clearance, local jury remains no worse than amber-red, and Brindle close calibration checks green for craft readiness."

"Brindle craft readiness is amber," Fei said.

Om closed her eyes. "Of course it is."

Vela said, "Amber with reasons."

"Then amber with reasons."

Sivek compressed the decision into a packet. It left on the next carrier, a bundle of life and caveat.

While they waited for Kall's reply, Tamsa opened a private line to Brindle.

"You meant what you said about consent?"

"Yes."

"I am trying to decide whether that helps."

"Tell me when you know."

"Do not get gentle with me from a cockpit."

"Wasn't planning to."

Tamsa exhaled hard. "I cannot order you not to do this."

"No."

"I cannot even say you are wrong."

"No."

"I hate that too."

Lena rested one gloved hand on Brindle's controls. "Tamsa."

"What?"

"If this goes badly, do not let them make Brindle the lesson."

"You think I would?"

"No. I needed to say it."

The private line held breath.

Then Kall's reply arrived.

Text and audio both. Sivek played the audio.

"Kestrel, Auxiliary Three. Crew and passenger representatives briefed. We understand amber-red. We understand Brindle risk. We understand gate risk. We do not consent to dying politely outside a usable chance. We attempt needle pass. Captain Kall out."

The cockpit became very quiet.

Om said, "Proceed."

Vane Three flush team reset the coolant loop. Brindle turned back toward the calibration perimeter, closer this time. The gate's residual field tickled the tug's hull before the buffers even warmed. Lena trimmed attitude and watched the Do It Better Board recalculate around a smaller target and a larger fear.

House Ceyr's injunction remained on the public docket, denied but visible.

That mattered. Not because it was right. Because it showed the refusal had not been hidden.

Auxiliary Three began its burn.

Chapter 29: Jori Testifies

Jori Pell refused to watch the needle pass from bed.

Medical refused his refusal.

The compromise, negotiated under threats from Tamsa, the pediatric ward nurse, and Jori himself, placed him in a mobile recovery chair inside the ward observation bay with his sling locked, head patch active, and a privacy curtain ready if his pulse climbed. He was allowed one slate, no direct command channel, and no unauthorized testimony.

He violated the last condition in seven minutes.

The ward had been quiet before that. Not calm. Quiet. Children slept or pretended. Parents watched boards with the sound muted. Nurses moved between cold-chain monitors and patient tents, their attention split between bodies in front of them and ships too far away to help by touch. The public feed showed Auxiliary Three's burn as a line, Brindle's calibration point as a dot, and the gate state as amber-red. Someone had switched off commentary. Nobody needed words to understand the shape of a desperate attempt.

Jori sat beside the inner window, pale but furious. His aunt had sent proof of the cold pack. Lena had sent nothing since climbing back into Brindle's cockpit. That was proper. Pilots flying did not owe injured apprentices comfort. He hated it anyway.

Then the Do It Better Board flickered.

Jori saw the first anomaly before the adults in the ward did because he was not watching the ship icons. He was watching blind pockets.

He had become obsessed with them after Vane Three. Places where official diagrams forgot that matter had edges. Places where drones could hide. Places where a boy in a utility cradle might notice something and be told later that noticing was less authoritative than a green board.

On the ward slate, Brindle's close calibration route curved beneath Vane Three and toward the lower envelope sag from first transit. Auxiliary Three burned toward the needle corridor. The local jury had every known blind pocket tagged.

Except one.

Near Vane One auxiliary purge, where Pellam had remembered a three-line bite.

A tiny source-clock mismatch appeared there and vanished.

Jori sat forward. The halo over his chair blinked amber.

"No," the nurse said without turning.

"I have to send something."

"You have to stay conscious."

"Both can happen."

"Historically overclaimed."

He tried Tamsa's channel. Blocked by medical. He tried Lena. Blocked. He tried worker general. Blocked. He stared at the slate, at the blind pocket that was not yet a blind pocket because the board had no reason to care.

Then he remembered the district documentation network.

He was still registered as Scrubber Works apprentice, injured but not suspended. His access could not reach command, but it could file a maintenance observation if tied to a local map.

He opened a report template.

OBSERVATION TYPE: VISUAL / MAP DISCREPANCY

LOCATION: VANE ONE AUX PURGE / THREE-LINE BITE ROUTE / POSSIBLE UNTAGGED BLIND POCKET

DESCRIPTION:

He stopped. Description mattered. Too much and the system would queue it. Too little and no one would listen.

He wrote:

SOURCE CLOCK MISMATCH 0.4 SEC SAME AS OLD DRONE CLIPS. POCKET NOT TAGGED. IF AUX3 NEEDLE PASSES VANE ONE WE ARE LOOKING AT VANE THREE TOO HARD.

Then he added, because he knew Tamsa:

NOT PANIC. MAP ERROR.

He submitted.

The nurse turned at the chime. "Jori."

"It is documentation."

"You are weaponizing forms."

"Aunt Tamsa says forms started it."

The report entered Scrubber Works queue, then bounced to Pellam because it named his three-line bite route. Pellam saw it while arguing brace strain and swore loudly enough that the ward feed carried it as a text flag: AUDIO EXCLUDED / PROFANITY LIKELY.

Tamsa opened Jori's file in the next second.

"Jori Pell," she said through the ward speaker, "you are in violation of medical quiet."

"Did you look?"

A pause.

"Yes."

"Was I wrong?"

Another pause. Longer.

"No."

The ward changed around him. Adults looked from the public board to Jori's chair. The nurse said something under her breath that sounded like surrender.

Tamsa's voice moved to the operational channel. "Local jury, new apprentice observation. Vane One auxiliary purge untagged blind pocket, source-clock mismatch similar to trust-layer clipping. Fei, camera."

Fei: "Routing."

Vela: "I see the mismatch. It is tiny."

Pellam: "Tiny killed better people."

Sivek: "Auxiliary Three approach intersects Vane One weighting in the needle model."

Lena's voice came from Brindle, very calm. "Show me."

The ward board updated. A new red tag appeared near Vane One: APPRENTICE OBSERVATION / UNVERIFIED / MAP ERROR POSSIBLE.

Jori felt absurdly proud of the wording.

Fei pushed drone H-2 toward the Vane One purge angle. The feed stuttered. Not suppressed fully, but smeared. H-3, the philosophical drone, was too far to help. A maintenance suit cam from exterior brace team caught a reflection off Vane One's old purge housing. Something there glittered, then went dark.

Vela said, "Trust layer is avoiding the area, not suppressing it. That is new."

Tamsa: "Meaning?"

"Meaning it learned that black rectangles draw attention."

Jori gripped the chair arm with his good hand. "It is hiding by not hiding."

The nurse said, "Do not talk to the board."

"I am talking near it."

On the operational channel, Lena said, "If Vane One purge is another vector, Auxiliary Three needle path may sag opposite predicted compensation."

Sivek: "Auxiliary Three is already committed to burn."

Kall's text arrived from the ship:

WE SEE NEW TAG. DO WE ABORT?

The ward went colder.

Jori looked at the children in blue tents. He looked at the board. He was seventeen, concussed, unqualified for command, and absolutely certain that being small in a system was how systems got away with ignoring you.

He opened another maintenance note.

NOT ABORT DATA. CORRECT MAP FIRST. IF TAG WRONG, IGNORE. IF TAG RIGHT, OLD PURGE IS VECTOR NOT WALL. CAMERA FROM LARKSPUR ANGLE MAY SEE.

Submit.

Tamsa shouted, "Jori!"

"What?"

"Stop being useful while injured!"

"No!"

The nurse stepped between him and the slate. He looked up at her, ready to argue.

She looked at the board, then back at him. "One more. Then I sedate civic virtue."

"Deal."

Fei rerouted the visual model to Larkspur's old accident angle using archived nose-light geometry. Not Brindle's current position. Larkspur's first drift. Jori's first fear. The model threw a cone of light across Vane One's auxiliary purge housing.

There it was.

Not a drone. Not a mark. A small capillary line of coolant vapor, visible only when lit from the wrong angle, venting from a purge seam that official maps had treated as welded dead.

Doss said, "Polymer source. Small but active."

Pellam: "If Vane One compensates during needle pass, that leak will heat and drift."

Vela: "The filter avoided showing it because it was not necessary to lie there until Auxiliary Three."

Kall: "Correction?"

Lena answered before Control could translate. "Shift needle path minus point zero four spinward. Reduce Vane One weighting. Increase Vane Four compensation. Brindle will move higher to watch sag."

Sivek: "Calculating."

Vela: "Possible."

Doss: "Coolant says possible."

Pellam: "Structure hates it but will not die immediately."

Tamsa: "Operational."

Pellam: "Structure amber-red but viable."

Om: "Auxiliary Three, Kestrel local jury revises path based on new source observation. Consent to continue?"

Kall: "We consent. Tell the apprentice thank you after we survive."

Jori stopped breathing for a second.

The nurse softened. "Breathe."

"Supervisor says," Jori whispered.

"Nurse says too."

Auxiliary Three burned correction.

On the ward board, Brindle moved closer to the envelope, a tiny calibration craft rising above the modeled sag. Lena's pulse appeared as a small line because her biometric safety feed remained visible. It climbed. The board did not hide it.

Jori watched his note become geometry. That was the terrifying part. Not being ignored was not comforting. It meant the thing you saw could now hurt people if you were wrong.

"What if I am wrong?" he said.

The nurse stood beside him. "Then you were wrong where people could check."

He thought about that.

"Is that good?"

"It is better than being right alone."

Auxiliary Three reached the outer needle envelope.

The gate woke again, smaller this time, meaner. Vane Three still hurt. Vane One now showed amber-red. Vane Four carried compensation it had not expected. The old filter attempted no clean green. Perhaps it had learned. Perhaps it was gathering itself. Perhaps there was no ghost in it at all, only old orders continuing after the people who loved them had died or become masks.

Brindle moved higher.

Auxiliary Three entered the corridor.

For a moment, the ship's icon wavered between two bad predictions: old model and corrected map. Then the new path held. The Vane One vapor line heated, drifted, and tried to drag the lower boundary exactly where Jori's observation had warned it might. Vane Four compensation caught it badly, but enough.

Lena's voice cut through every channel.

"Aux Three, burn dorsal two. Now."

Kall obeyed.

The ship lifted.

The corridor narrowed.

Brindle's hull flexion went red.

Tamsa shouted something the ward did not receive. Maro's recorder flagged overlapping emergency audio. Sivek called numbers. Vela called source clips. Doss called coolant drift. Pellam called brace strain. Om said nothing because the law had reached the edge of physics and could only witness.

Auxiliary Three hit the aperture.

It did not vanish cleanly.

Its aft section flared. A strip of hull plating tore away in light. For one horrible second, the ship seemed caught, neither through nor here, stretched by the gate's narrow refusal.

Then Brindle fired its calibration thrusters and tagged the sag in real time.

Vane Four compensated.

The aperture flexed.

Auxiliary Three went through.

The ward board lost the icon.

Silence took the room.

Jori heard a child breathing in one of the tents. A nurse's sleeve rustled. Somewhere far away, someone in the station began to sob before knowing whether grief had arrived.

The low-band carrier chirped.

Text appeared slowly.

AUX3 THROUGH.
DAMAGE SEVERE.
PRESSURE PARTIAL.
CABIN THERMAL STABILIZING.
CASUALTIES UNKNOWN.
ALIVE.

Jori read the last word until it stopped moving.

Alive.

Tamsa's voice entered the ward channel, rougher than he had ever heard it.

"Jori Pell."

"Yes?"

"You are not fired."

He laughed, then cried because his head hurt, because the ship was alive, because being useful had not made him less afraid, because his aunt sounded proud and furious and alive.

The nurse took the slate from his hand.

"Now," she said, "civic virtue is going to sleep."

This time, Jori did not argue.

Chapter 30: Maro Unseals

Maro Venn watched Auxiliary Three's last word blink on the evidence bay wall until it began to feel accusatory.

ALIVE.

The word had no punctuation. No mercy either. Alive did not say whole, safe, uninjured, justified, or forgiven. It did not say how many pressure compartments had sealed too slowly, how many passengers had been strapped into cargo corners without names on Dax's manifest, how many crew had burned their hands on manual valves while Kall's ship fought the narrow corridor. It only said the ship had crossed from one impossible side of the gate to the other.

Alive was a verdict too small for what it carried.

The evidence bay had emptied after the needle pass except for the people who could not leave their own obligations. Technician Sen monitored the frozen drone memory under cold preservation. Two judicial clerks copied burn-vault records into sealed partitions. Oriole-of-Archive remained as a boundary presence in the Glassward substrate, a shifting mark on the wall display that sometimes looked like text and sometimes like a patient wound in the light. Magistrate Om had gone to the public rail to issue casualty-language restrictions before rumor could turn unknown into useful panic.

Maro stood before the sealed speaker with his hands clasped behind his back.

Tamsa arrived first, still in the jacket with blood on the cuff from someone else's scraped knuckles in Dock Twelve. She stopped at the observation line.

"You sent for me."

"I did."

"If this is about paperwork, I am choosing violence."

"It is about the gap."

She looked at him more carefully then. "Where is Lena?"

"Brindle medical check. She protested. Doss threatened to involve Jori's nurse. The nurse won by reputation."

"Good."

Maro almost smiled. It failed. "I am requesting expanded unsealing."

Tamsa said nothing for a long moment.

Outside the bay's inner glass, the frozen drone sat in its cradle, still wrapped in cold-chain packs that had begun as medical emergency supplies and become evidence preservation. Its beacon lens remained cracked. Its memory housing was half saved, half dissolved, like a mind that had tried to drown itself before testimony.

"Why tell me?" Tamsa asked.

"Because Pavo's coercion named my gap. Because Jori's observation just saved a ship by refusing to accept that small witnesses must stay small. Because I have been arguing privacy correctly and maybe using correctness to keep myself from knowing what someone else has already weaponized."

Tamsa folded her arms. "That sounded rehearsed."

"It was."

"Good. Means you were afraid enough to prepare."

Maro let that stand.

Oriole-of-Archive spoke through the sealed channel. "Investigator Venn has requested expanded index-plus-context review of Aurelian-Blue-17, limited to references concerning Cassian Vale, Saren Vale, Lattice Mercy, dead-key authority, and the phrase previously identified. Full witness identities remain masked unless the witness is also an implicated operational actor."

Tamsa looked at the display. "And you asked me here to approve?"

"No. You cannot approve. I asked because someone should be in the room who does not belong to Glassward law, Compact procedure, House history, or my own self-justification."

"You think I am neutral?"

"No. I think you are allergic to elegant excuses."

"That is fair."

Om entered with Lena beside her before Tamsa could answer. Lena looked pale but upright, one side of her jacket unsealed where a medic brace had been adjusted. She took in the room, Maro's face, Oriole's mark, and Tamsa's stillness.

"You are opening it," Lena said.

"Partly."

"Partly is a word that makes knives nervous."

"Yes."

Om set a portable rail on the evidence table. "This proceeding concerns voluntary limited unsealing of protected continuity material by Maro Venn, with Glassward advocacy present. It is not a fishing expedition. It is not permission to consume the dead because the living are hurried. Time pressure is relevant. It is not sovereign."

Oriole responded, "Boundary accepted with objection preserved."

"Everything tonight has objection preserved," Om said. "Proceed."

Maro sat in the witness chair. He had used similar chairs for other people. He knew how they disciplined the body: feet flat, hands visible, spine unsupported enough to discourage collapse. He placed his continuity-marked temple within range of the sealed query device and felt the old implants recognize a door he had locked years ago.

Lena stood where he could see her without turning. Tamsa stood beside the observation line. Om behind the rail. Oriole in the wall. Technician Sen pretended to check a scanner and failed to hide that she was listening.

Oriole said, "Maro Venn, state consent."

"I consent to expanded index-plus-context review as described. I do not consent to full content release. I do not consent to exposure of protected Aurelian witnesses unrelated to Vale, dead-key authority, or Lattice Mercy operational continuity. I understand that contextual fragments may affect my own memory integration and legal obligations."

"State personal risk."

His mouth went dry.

"I may recover knowledge I chose to seal because I believed privacy was care. I may learn that my care left other people vulnerable. I may learn that the seal was breached by someone else. I may become a worse witness or a better one. I do not know."

Oriole's mark pulsed once. "Consent recorded. Opening contextual index."

The device at Maro's temple cooled.

Memory did not return as a film. That was the lie people told about their own minds. It returned first as pressure. A smell: burnt insulation and human fear filtered through Aurelian's failing atmosphere. A sound: many voices compressed into emergency archive format, saying names that had become legal categories. A blue-lit room after the breach where Maro, younger and still convinced rules could protect grief from use, sat across from a man with a continuity mark too bright under his skin.

Cassian Vale had been beautiful in the efficient way of knives and surgical tools.

Maro inhaled sharply.

Lena took half a step. Om raised a hand, stopping her without looking away from him.

Oriole spoke. "Context fragment one. Cassian Vale, legal liaison, Aurelian recovery proceeding. Public death record conflict confirmed. Fragment contains conversation with Maro Venn concerning admissibility of partial fork testimony."

Cassian's voice came through the speaker, not full content but enough context to have teeth.

"Privacy is the last dignity of the dead, Investigator. Proof is what institutions demand after they fail to prevent the need for it. Choose privacy over proof, or become another scavenger in a robe."

Maro closed his eyes.

He remembered agreeing with that sentence.

Not entirely. Enough.

Oriole continued. "Context fragment two. Saren Vale referenced as operational counterparty to Cassian Vale. No lawful fork registration. Evidence of shared access to recovery testimony metadata."

Saren's voice did not play. Instead, text appeared. The fragment protected more than it revealed.

SAREN: LATTICE MERCY WAS NEVER ABOUT NOISE. IT WAS ABOUT WHO GETS TO CALL FEAR A SIGNAL.

VELA CHO: THEN BURN IT.

SAREN: BURNING tools is theater. Teach systems to need them again, and they resurrect themselves.

Vela was not in the room, but the channel showed she had been pulled in by Om's clerk. Her voice came from Dock Eighteen, suddenly hoarse. "I remember that."

Om said, "You were at Aurelian recovery?"

"Not officially."

Tamsa whispered, "Of course."

Vela continued, quieter. "I gave sealed testimony on Lattice Mercy failure modes. I thought Cassian buried it."

Maro opened his eyes. "I helped him argue to seal portions."

No one softened the fact for him.

Oriole said, "Context fragment three. Protected witness identity masked. Content relevant: dead-key audit vulnerability."

A distorted witness voice played.

"If a death certificate, a destruction certificate, and a trust credential all point to each other, no living officer looks behind the triangle. The dead become an office. Anyone with the triangle can sit there."

Maro gripped the chair.

Lena said, "The Vale persona."

Oriole: "Likely. Context fragment four. Cassian Vale proposed postwar containment of liability through strategic preservation of emergency doctrines. Phrase match: 'A gate need not explode. It only needs to choose its owner.'"

Pavo's words, now older than Pavo's fear.

Tamsa's face went cold.

"He planned Kestrel at Aurelian?" she asked.

Oriole answered carefully. "No direct Kestrel reference in released context. Pattern indicates doctrine: manufacture uncertainty, discredit distributed local authority, restore emergency ownership through survivable crisis."

"Survivable for whom?" Tamsa asked.

No one answered.

The sealed device warmed against Maro's temple. He felt something give inside him. Not a memory returning, exactly. A moral partition failing.

"I argued for sealing the triangle testimony," he said.

Om's expression did not change. "Why?"

"Because the witness was a partial fork from Aurelian's emergency archive. They had not consented to public strategic use. Cassian argued that exposing the testimony would turn every recovered dead person into a weapon. I believed him."

Oriole said, "Belief was reasonable under known conditions."

"Insufficient under actual conditions."

"Also true."

Lena's voice was low. "Maro."

He looked at her. "I chose privacy over proof. He counted on that."

"He used care as cover," Tamsa said. "That does not make care the crime."

Maro almost broke then. The kindness was too practical to deflect.

Technician Sen cleared her throat. "Drone memory cold pull is ready. If Vale used the triangle, memory may have authority traces matching the same certificate loop."

Om seized the motion back toward work. "Proceed."

Sen initiated the partial recovery. The drone's damaged memory unfolded in fragments: route hashes, dead-key handshakes, chemical wipe commands, a status ping to administrative emergency suite, a secondary ping that had failed during H-3's static flood, and one line of embedded instruction recovered from the solvent-wiped partition.

IF LOCAL JURY SURVIVES FIRST CRISIS, ESCALATE TO OWNERSHIP FAILURE.

Tamsa leaned forward. "Ownership failure?"

Vela answered from Dock Eighteen. "Make distributed authority responsible for the next casualty. Prove the public board kills."

Lena felt the room tilt toward the future. First group alive. Auxiliary Three alive but damaged. The enemy's next move would not be to hide the truth layer. It would be to make the truth layer look lethal.

Maro stood too quickly. The chair scraped. "We need to warn Dax and Kall. Any casualty aboard Auxiliary Three will be used to blame the local jury."

"Casualties may already exist," Lena said.

"Then we need the record of why."

Om nodded. "Public grief protocol. Medical causality. No casualty attributed to transit governance without evidence."
