Tamsa said, "People will not wait for evidence if someone gives them a face to hate."

The evidence bay wall flashed with a public feed request. House Ceyr. Ilya.

Om allowed it.

Ilya appeared, pale. "We found the burn vault subpartition. Saren Vale's audit token was copied three times after certified destruction. One copy accessed Ceyr legal archive twenty minutes ago."

"Twenty minutes ago?" Maro asked.

"During Auxiliary Three transit."

Vela swore over the channel. "Ownership failure."

Sivek came on from Control. "Public channels just received an anonymous casualty packet from Auxiliary Three claiming local jury correction caused hull breach and deaths."

Tamsa said, "Already?"

Om's voice hardened. "Do not display it."

"Too late," Sivek said. "It is spreading through nonofficial district relays."

On the evidence bay board, a wave of public alerts bloomed. Not official. Not worker source. Anonymous, clipped, emotionally precise.

LOCAL JURY KILLED AUX3 COMPARTMENT C.
ORS ORDER CAUSED HULL TEAR.
WORKER BOARD HID RISK.

There it was. Ownership failure.

Maro touched the fading cool spot at his temple and felt the gap inside him no longer silent.

"No," he said.

He opened the public evidence channel himself before fear could negotiate.

"This is Maro Venn, accident reconstruction. The casualty packet spreading now is unverified and matches a recovered sabotage doctrine intended to convert local-jury survival into blame. Preserve the packet. Do not amplify without source. Auxiliary Three is alive, damaged, and owed truth instead of use."

Om looked at him, not stopping him.

Maro continued, voice steady because it had found something stronger than comfort.

"Protected Aurelian context confirms the method: manufacture uncertainty, force public choice, then weaponize the first casualty to restore emergency ownership. That method is now active. We will not feed it with grief before evidence."

He cut the channel.

The room held him.

Tamsa nodded once. "Better."

It was not absolution. It was enough to keep working.

Chapter 31: The Third Pulse

The third pulse began without permission.

It did not come from the power buffers. That was the first mercy. It did not open the full gate, did not seize the convoy path, did not tear Brindle from the calibration perimeter or swallow Dock Twelve whole. It began as a tremor in the old trust layer, a command ripple through systems that had no business obeying anyone. Sampling bridge. Dock guidance. Vane One purge monitor. Administrative emergency suite. Ceyr burn-vault audit mirror. A dead triangle of certificate, destruction, and trust turning once more.

In Control, Sivek saw central confidence climb.

In Scrubber Works, Vela saw local feeds begin to phase.

In the evidence bay, Maro saw the recovered drone instruction execute its next line.

In Dock Twelve, Tamsa saw the Do It Better Board flash from amber-red to black.

UNKNOWN COMMAND PROPAGATION.

On Brindle, still holding near the perimeter after Auxiliary Three, Lena felt the tug's hull sing.

"Control," she said, "I have field tension rising."

Sivek's answer came too fast. "No authorized pulse."

"Then unauthorized."

"Power buffers are isolated."

Vela cut in. "It is not a main pulse. It is a trust pulse. The hidden layer is querying every old authority path and forcing systems to answer."

Tamsa: "Can a query move field hardware?"

Doss: "If field hardware believes the query is safing."

Pellam: "Vane Three brace strain rising."

Fei: "Dock Eighteen guidance just tried to enter capture mode with no ship present."

Om: "All systems, local manual holds."

The command propagated anyway.

It did what Lattice Mercy had been designed to do in reverse. Instead of suppressing hostile noise to protect command, it made local disagreement look like hostile intrusion and asked every high-authority system to defend itself. Defense meant safing. Safing meant moving hardware, closing valves, locking archives, isolating districts, freezing evidence, and in Kestrel Gate's damaged state, shifting field vanes by fractions large enough to kill.

The Do It Better Board came back in fragments.

Vane One purge: red.
Vane Three brace: red.
Sampling bridge trust: black.
Dock guidance: red.
Power buffers: green but queried.
Administrative emergency suite: hostile.
Ceyr burn-vault mirror: hostile.

Lena fired Brindle's attitude jets and backed away from the perimeter. The field distortion followed by half a meter, not physically but mathematically, the boundary twitching under commands that thought they were safety.

"Brindle retreating," she said.

Sivek: "Approved."

Vela: "No, hold above mark two."

Tamsa: "Cho."

"If Brindle leaves entirely, we lose the only independent field reference still outside the trust pulse."

Lena stopped retreat. The tug shuddered. "Holding above mark two."

Tamsa's voice went sharp. "That was not an order."

"It was correct."

"I will hate everyone after."

Om: "Focus. How do we stop a trust pulse?"

Vela: "Starve authority. Same principle, bigger teeth. Every manual authority must challenge its remote version. Tamsa did it at Lock C. We need live habit challenges across systems."

Sivek: "Control can challenge scheduling authority."

Tamsa: "Maintenance can challenge vane service."

Ilya entered, voice urgent. "Ceyr can challenge dead liaison keys in the burn vault, but if we do it wrong we may trigger archive destruction."

Maro: "Glassward boundary can challenge Vale persona continuity claims where they touch protected records."

Om: "Judicial can challenge emergency legal authority."

Vela: "Do it simultaneously. If one high-authority path remains uncontested, the pulse routes through it."

"Timing?" Sivek asked.

"Source clocks visible," Lena said.

For one beat, no one spoke.

Then Tamsa said, "All right. Everybody show your hands."

The station became a body fighting a seizure by proving it was inhabited.

In Control, Sivek placed both hands on an old manual scheduling plate that had not been used except for ceremony since before the war. He entered a challenge sequence taught to route officers as heritage and never expected to matter. The plate read his pressure, rhythm, hesitation, and scars.

In Scrubber Works, Tamsa struck manual maintenance plates in sequence with Doss, Pellam, Sann, and three crew leads, each challenging remote versions of their own authority.

In the Ceyr liaison room, Ilya opened a burn-vault panel and cut her palm on a physical witness edge because old House keys, naturally, had been built by people theatrical enough to require blood without admitting ritual.

In the evidence bay, Maro consented to Oriole's continuity boundary challenge against any Vale authority claiming protected Aurelian root. Oriole's voice became layered, less patient, almost immense.

"Continuity claim challenged. Produce lawful chain or be marked persona."

In the agricultural dark, Keva Lorn, under witness protection, pressed her blocked temple implant against a manual administrative plate and said, "My credential is not my consent."

In Dock Eighteen, Vela connected her compiler to the local beacon ring and challenged the trust layer with its own ancestor code.

"Lattice Mercy," she whispered, "ugly compromise six says no."

On Brindle, Lena had no authority plate. She had a tug, source clocks, and a local recorder stack born from the first lie. The field tension rose again, trying to smooth her out of relevance.

"Brindle," she said, because craft liked names even when people pretended they did not. "Local pilot challenge. Manual control."

The tug asked for confirmation.

She gave it hands, voice, pulse, and a control motion no ghost key could perform because it had never been in her body when Nerio's Lamp died, never sat in Larkspur with Jori bleeding, never held Brindle steady while Auxiliary Three tore through a narrow chance.

Manual control accepted.

The source clocks aligned across the board.

For one incandescent second, every live authority on Halcyon answered at once.

Not remote.

Not dead.

Here.

The trust pulse hit the challenges and shattered into visible paths.

The Do It Better Board exploded with lines: dead Ceyr key, false Saren audit token, Compact Directorate remnant, administrative emulator, dock mimic, drone route, sampling bridge, burn-vault mirror. The hidden system had been powerful because its parts were treated as separate accidents. Challenged together, it became a map.

At the center of the map was not a person.

It was a legal-operational construct: SAREN/CASSIAN VALE CONTINUITY MASK, assembled from dead-key records, sealed testimony metadata, Ceyr burn audit authority, Directorate telemetry doctrine, and private evidence-security scripts. It had human operators somewhere, perhaps several. But its authority did not require one body. It was an office made of graves.

Oriole's voice cut through all channels.

"Continuity mask has no lawful personhood, no protected witness status, no valid emergency authority. Marking as hostile construct."

Om: "Judicial recognition: hostile construct. All systems reject."

The station rejected it.

Not elegantly. Doors slammed. Archives locked. Some lights died. Dock Eighteen lost one guidance lane. Ceyr's liaison room vented smoke from an overheated vault reader. Scrubber Works fan control dipped for eleven seconds before Tamsa's crew caught it manually. Vane Three's brace screamed across the structural feed. Brindle spun a quarter turn before Lena corrected.

The unauthorized field tension collapsed.

For a moment, Kestrel Gate went quiet.

Too quiet.

Then every board showed red at once.

POWER BUFFER THREE: UNSCHEDULED DRAIN.

Doss shouted, "That is not the trust pulse."

Sivek's face appeared, pale under Control light. "Buffer Three is dumping into the old charge splitter."

Pellam swore. "Quiet Mark path."

Vela: "The construct used the trust pulse to open a physical drain."

Tamsa: "Can we stop it?"

Sivek: "Not from Control. The splitter thinks it is preventing overload."

Lena looked through Brindle's blister toward the buffer ring. One segment glowed brighter than before, an ugly hard white at the edge of the gate infrastructure. If Buffer Three dumped through the old splitter into the damaged vane network, it could trigger a real pulse without sequence, without corridor, without mercy.

The third pulse had only begun.

Om said, "Options."

Pellam: "Manual splitter cutout."

Tamsa: "Location."

Pellam: "Exterior ring access. Not reachable before dump completes."

Fei: "Drone?"

"Too slow."

Vela: "Can Brindle clamp the splitter feed?"

Pellam made a sound. "That is not what clamp arms are for."

Tamsa: "Can they?"

Pellam: "If you hate them enough."

Lena was already turning Brindle.

Tamsa's voice sharpened. "Ors."

"I am closest."

"You are always closest to bad ideas."

"This one has geometry."

Sivek sent the splitter coordinate. It appeared near the buffer ring's lower arc, just outside Brindle's safe thrust envelope. Safe had become advisory hours ago.

Maro said, "Record shows this action carries extreme pilot risk and insufficient review."

"Record also shows no better option," Lena said.

Om: "Pilot Ors, do not proceed until--"

The buffer flare doubled.

She proceeded.

Brindle burned hard for the old charge splitter, leaving the calibration perimeter behind. The tug shook under acceleration. The local jury tried to render risk and failed, then displayed black with reasons it could not compute fast enough.

The splitter housing grew in the blister: a bulky pre-Compact assembly half buried under newer conduits, marked by three-line bites, glowing at seams where power should never glow. Energy crawled through it toward the vane network like lightning deciding law.

Lena extended the reinforced clamp arm.

Pellam shouted angles. Doss shouted heat limits. Vela shouted about trust paths still flickering. Sivek shouted abort authority and then stopped because there was no one else to send. Tamsa did not shout.

That was how Lena knew she was truly frightened.

"Clamp must bite the feed throat, not the housing," Pellam said. "If you miss, you shear the arm and maybe yourself."

"Understood."

"No you don't."

"Probably not."

The feed throat came into alignment.

Brindle's hull alarm went red.

Lena fired port aft, rolled three degrees, and drove the clamp into the throat.

The universe turned white.

For a second she had no instruments, no body, no sound. Only pressure and light and the absurd memory of Jori asking whether he was fired.

Then the cockpit came back sideways.

Brindle was spinning.

The clamp arm was gone.

Buffer Three's drain fell to zero.

The old splitter cracked open, venting heat and frozen fragments into space. The unauthorized pulse died before becoming a gate event.

"Brindle," Tamsa said. Her voice was too calm. "Report."

Lena tried to answer. Nothing came out.

She found the controls. Stopped one axis. Failed the second. Tried again. The tug groaned. Her left display was dead. The tape above the central board had peeled halfway loose.

SOURCE CLOCKS VISIBLE.

"Brindle alive," she managed.

The channel erupted. She ignored it until Sivek cut everyone down to one voice.

Tamsa again. "Can you dock?"

Lena looked at the boards that remained. Attitude compromised. Main drive unstable. Clamp arm gone. Hull breach sealed by emergency foam in aft compartment. Pilot pulse very high, helpfully visible.

"No," she said.

Silence.

Then Fei: "We can send rescue tug."

Pellam: "Docking collar damaged by her dramatic nonsense. Use Tug Ashkey. It has ugly arms."

Maro: "Medical rescue scope active."

Om: "Authorized."

Tamsa said, "Stay awake, Ors."

Lena looked at Kestrel Gate. It hung quiet now, damaged but not exploding. First group through. Auxiliary Three through. Halcyon alive. The hostile construct exposed, if not yet captured. Brindle tumbling slowly with one arm torn away because sometimes better than doctrine still meant wreckage.

"I am awake," she said.

"Good."

Then, softer and not on private channel because nothing tonight stayed private, Tamsa added, "Supervisor says breathe."

Lena laughed once, or tried to.

This time it hurt less than silence.

Chapter 32: Counter-Transit Plan

Tug Ashkey approached Brindle like a large, suspicious hand.

Lena watched it roll into view through the cracked right edge of Brindle's blister, all patched plating and blunt clamp arms, its work lights sweeping over the torn remains of Brindle's forward assembly. Ashkey had no grace and no interest in pretending otherwise. It was the kind of tug built for hauling dead cargo, stubborn hulls, and mistakes that had survived long enough to be billed.

Fei piloted it remotely from Dock Twelve with Sann in the rescue cradle and Pellam giving unsolicited advice over three channels.

"You are drifting spinward," Pellam said.

Fei replied, "I am compensating for a tumbling target."

"Compensate less theatrically."

"I am going to compensate you out an airlock."

Tamsa cut in. "Everybody speaks only if it keeps Ors alive."

For three blessed seconds, the channels cleared.

Brindle continued its slow roll. Lena had arrested two axes but not the third. Each rotation brought Kestrel Gate into view, then Halcyon, then the hard white line of the buffer ring where the old charge splitter had cracked open. The broken splitter vented faint glitter into vacuum. The unauthorized pulse was dead. That fact remained unbelievable.

Her left arm had gone numb from shoulder to wrist. The emergency foam in Brindle's aft compartment had sealed the breach but not the cold that kept creeping into the cockpit through wounded systems. Her suit was intact. Her body was less cooperative. The pulse line on the board helpfully displayed elevated and irregular, as if she had needed data to know her heart objected.

"Ashkey at ten meters," Fei said. "Sann, ready external tether."

"Ready."

Tamsa's private voice entered Lena's helmet, though private was a fiction. "Still awake?"

"Yes."

"Say something that proves it."

"Your paperwork threat is poorly formatted."

"Good enough."

Ashkey matched the roll. Its clamp arm opened in front of Brindle's blister, slow as a docking sunrise. The first attempt missed by half a meter when Brindle's damaged thruster coughed and changed the roll. Fei swore. Pellam inhaled like a man about to become educational.

Tamsa said, "Pellam."

He exhaled silently.

The second attempt caught Brindle's dorsal tow lug.

The impact rang through the cockpit. Lena's vision narrowed to a tunnel of white points and old training. She gripped the controls, not because they could help, but because hands liked lies during pain.

"Capture," Fei said.

"Tow lug strain?" Tamsa asked.

Sann: "High but holding."

"Brindle, do you confirm capture?" Maro asked.

Lena looked at the board. It took effort to find the right line. "Captured. Ugly but real."

"Legal record accepts ugly but real," Maro said.

Ashkey burned gently, dragging Brindle out of its tumble and toward Dock Twelve. Halcyon grew larger by degrees. Station lights looked too warm. Lena knew better. Warmth was a system maintained by exhausted people.

On the public board mirrored in Brindle, the operational summary kept updating:

FIRST GROUP: ALIVE.
AUXILIARY THREE: ALIVE / SEVERE DAMAGE / CASUALTY COUNT PENDING.
UNAUTHORIZED THIRD PULSE: INTERRUPTED.
HOSTILE CONTINUITY MASK: EXPOSED / REMNANTS ACTIVE UNKNOWN.
KST BUFFER THREE: DAMAGED.
GATE STATUS: RESTRICTED / UNSAFE FOR GENERAL TRANSIT.

No one had tried to call it victory.

Good.

Dock Twelve received Brindle with foam arms and mechanical caution. Sann rode the hull in a suit, guiding the damaged tug through a collar not designed for half-dead craft. The docking clamps locked in a sequence Lena felt more than heard. Atmosphere returned around the hull in shuddering layers.

The hatch refused to open.

Tamsa arrived on the outer side with tools before the status light finished turning red.

"Do not cut through the recorder trunk," Maro warned.

"If the choice is recorder or pilot, I am choosing pilot."

"I was not objecting. I was orienting."

The hatch opened under Tamsa's third insult and one hydraulic override.

Cold air spilled out. Lena tried to stand and discovered that standing had become a theory.

Tamsa climbed in and crouched beside the pilot cradle. For once, she did not begin with accusation.

"Look at me."

Lena did.

"You are out of the tug," Tamsa said.

"Not yet."

"Close enough for emotional accounting."

Medical pulled her out on a rescue board while she objected in fragments. Doss threatened to use coolant tape on her mouth. Fei said the philosophical drone wanted to visit. Maro told everyone that the cockpit record had ended and therefore insults could proceed with reduced evidentiary burden. Lena drifted in and out of the noise, catching faces: Tamsa above her, furious and alive; Sivek on a wall screen with his hands pressed flat to the Control desk; Ilya's feed absent; Vela watching from Dock Eighteen, one eye human and wet, the other a dark lens; Jori's nurse sending a message to the medical team that said PILOTS LIE ABOUT PAIN.

The triage bay had once been a cargo inspection nook. Now it held three stretchers, a portable scanner, and a cold-chain monitor someone had forgotten to remove after Auxiliary Three. Lena lay under a thermal sheet while a medic scanned her ribs, shoulder, and left arm.

"Concussion borderline," the medic said.

"Everyone is using that word loosely tonight."

"Rib fractures aggravated. Nerve compression left arm. Radiation within acceptable emergency exposure."

"Acceptable to whom?"

"People who fill forms after you sleep."

Tamsa stood at the foot of the stretcher. "Can she sit for planning?"

The medic stared at her.

Tamsa did not blink. "That was not cruelty. That was schedule."

"She can sit for ten minutes after analgesic stabilization if she does not stand, fly, argue loudly, or make any decisions that affect her survival."

Lena said, "Define loudly."

"No," said the medic.

Ten minutes later, they held the counter-transit meeting around her stretcher.

Maro objected to the venue until Om said the venue had already heard worse. The Do It Better Board was mirrored onto the triage wall. The board looked changed now. Not broken. Older. Its categories had multiplied after the third pulse, and every new line was a scar.

Buffer Three damaged. Old charge splitter destroyed. Vane Three brace holding but not reusable indefinitely. Vane One purge leaking but tagged. Sampling bridge trust black-cleared after manual challenges, but deep audit incomplete. Hostile continuity mask exposed, remnants uncertain. General transit unsafe. Emergency limited transit possible only with major repair and distributed authority.

Sivek spoke from Control. "Remaining convoy cargo and passenger auxiliaries can hold outside full transit for twenty-six hours before fuel and life support enter severe decline. Dax first group and Auxiliary Three are through but damaged. They can receive medical cargo and stabilize some cases, but they cannot send physical help back through until Kestrel reopens."

"So the gate must open again," Om said.

"Not in current state."

Vela leaned into her feed. "We need counter-transit."

Pellam frowned. "I thought counter-transit was a maneuver, not a repair."

"It is both if you are desperate enough."

Tamsa folded her arms. "Explain without mythology."

Vela pulled a model onto the board. The gate throat appeared as a distorted shape, wounded at Vane Three, newly stressed near the buffer ring, and crosshatched where the trust layer had been stripped from command. "The unauthorized third pulse left residual phase stress in the old splitter path. If we try standard opening, the stress may shear through Vane Three or drag the aperture toward the damaged buffer. Counter-transit means we run a small, controlled inversion pulse using a calibration craft and the remaining healthy vanes to pull residual stress away from the damaged path before opening for the remaining convoy."

"Small," Tamsa said.

"In gate terms."

"Which means enormous."

"Yes."

Sivek looked grim. "Counter-transit requires a calibration craft inside the pre-envelope."

Every eye in the triage bay turned toward Lena.

The medic said, "Absolutely not."

Lena did not argue. Everyone noticed.

Tamsa looked from Lena to the board. "Can a tug do it without Ors?"

Sivek hesitated.

Pellam said, "Brindle cannot. Ashkey is too slow. Larkspur is damaged. Tug Morrow has the thrust profile but old sensors."

Fei said, "We can move the recorder stack again."

Doss said, "Coolant sniffers too."

Vela said, "The pilot needs to read boundary behavior without waiting for central interpretation."

Maro said, "Do not define the task around the person you expect to sacrifice."

That stopped the room.

Tamsa nodded once. "Correct. Define task. Then crew."

They built the counter-transit plan without naming Lena as its answer.

Required: a calibration craft with high lateral thrust, reinforced local recorder, truth bridge, coolant sniffers, drone blind-zone support, structural feed from exterior riggers, manual pilot capable of boundary reading, second operator capable of jury feed arbitration, remote abort controlled by Om and Sivek jointly, maintenance authority held by Tamsa or delegate, Vela watching trust remnants, Maro preserving evidence, medical oversight with actual veto over pilot incapacitation.

When the list was done, it did not say Lena.

It did not exclude Lena.

The medic looked at it with open hostility. "Pilot incapacitation veto matters if you choose a pilot who is not already impaired."

Lena said, "I am not flying."

The words surprised even her.

Silence rippled.

She forced herself to continue. "Not the counter-transit. My left arm is compromised, and I cannot trust my reaction under analgesic. If I fly, the board becomes theater."

Tamsa's face did something complicated and quickly hidden.

Maro exhaled.

Sivek looked down.

Pellam said, "Well. Growth is disgusting."

"I can still read geometry," Lena said. "I can train the pilot and ride the board."

"Who flies?" Om asked.

The room looked at the task list.

Fei spoke first. "Tug Morrow's best pilot is Sann."

Sann, who had been listening from the exterior team channel, said, "I am a rigger."

Tamsa said, "You have utility cradle certification."

"Not pre-envelope."

Lena looked at the board. At the task. At the person. "You read tethers under load. You corrected Doss's drift during exterior recovery. You kept Jori alive by clearing him properly. You are steady when insulted."

Sann's voice was very quiet. "That is not route pilot training."

"No," Lena said. "So we do not make you a route pilot. We make you the manual hands of a local jury. I read boundary. You fly what the board decides. Fei rides second operator. Tamsa holds maintenance authority. Sivek holds gate sequence. Vela watches old lies. Doss and Pellam call physical limits. Maro records. Om can abort."

Tamsa said, "Mixed crew."

"Yes."

"No single heroic idiot."

"Exactly."

The medic looked at Lena. "And you stay off the craft."

Lena swallowed the old reflex before it became speech. "Yes."

It hurt. Less in pride than in identity. She had spent years trying not to be a pilot and one day becoming one again under witness. Giving up the cradle now felt like losing the one clean thing the day had returned. That was how she knew it was right.

Om watched her for a long moment. "Recorded. Counter-transit planning proceeds with Sann as pilot candidate, Fei as second operator, Ors as geometry lead from board subject to medical limits."

Tamsa glanced at Lena. "You are going to be insufferable from a chair."

"Probably."

"I can work with that."

The board updated:

COUNTER-TRANSIT PLAN: DRAFT.
CRAFT: TUG MORROW.
PILOT: SANN / PENDING SIM.
GEOMETRY: ORS / BOARD.
AUTHORITY: DISTRIBUTED.
CERTAINTY: UNAVAILABLE.

Outside the triage bay, Dock Twelve kept working. Brindle sat broken but not dead. Ashkey vented heat. Somewhere beyond the gate, Dax held remaining ships in a patient crisis. Auxiliary Three reported casualties still unknown but cabins warming. Halcyon breathed with fan cycles visible. The hostile construct had lost its mask but not necessarily its operators.

Lena closed her eyes for five seconds.

Only five.

When she opened them, Sann was on the line.

"Pilot Ors," he said, awkward with the title.

"Sann."

"Teach me the part that kills me if I misunderstand."

Lena looked at Tamsa, then at the board, then at the dark beyond the dock where the gate waited.

"All right," she said. "First, stop thinking of the boundary as a line."

Chapter 33: Mixed Crew

Tug Morrow had been named by someone with a sense of humor or a grudge.

It was older than Brindle, narrower than Ashkey, and painted in a shade of gray that made it look permanently tired. Its thrust profile, however, was almost perfect for counter-transit: fast lateral response, redundant attitude clusters, a compact frame, and an engine block that had survived three decades of abuse by refusing to become elegant. It had bad sensors, no modern truth bridge, and a pilot cradle upholstered with material that stuck to pressure suits in warm air.

"This craft is an ergonomic crime," Fei said.

Pellam patted Morrow's hull. "Respect your elders."

"I respect elders who update their ports."

"Then you respect no one."

Sann stood beside the open hatch in a suit liner, helmet under one arm, looking at the tug as if it might ask him a moral question. He was broad-shouldered, steady-handed, and too young to have learned how to disguise fear as irritation. Lena preferred the honesty. Fear that knew its own name was easier to train.

She sat in a rolling medical chair because the medic, whose name turned out to be Bera, had discovered enforcement as a vocation. The chair had a brake lock, biometric monitor, and a small sign Tamsa had taped to the back: PILOT CONTAINMENT DEVICE.

Lena had not dignified it by removing it.

The mixed crew gathered around Morrow in Dock Twelve. Sann as pilot. Fei as second operator, drone feeds, and truth bridge interface. Tamsa as maintenance authority and crew lead. Doss for coolant and phase-reactive contaminants. Pellam for structure and old hardware hatred. Vela remote from Dock Eighteen because moving her equipment would take too long and she had threatened to bite anyone who tried. Sivek in Control. Om on judicial abort. Maro in the evidence station with recording scope. Lena as geometry lead, medically confined and therefore, according to Tamsa, "finally shaped like an advisor."

Sann looked at her. "I have run the sim twice."

"You crashed once."

"The first sim included a false Vane Three shear."

"The real gate may be impolite too."

"The second sim survived."

"You overcorrected at the pre-envelope boundary and survived because the model was generous."

His face reddened. "I know."

"Good. Show me where."

He pulled the sim trace onto the dock display. The path curved into the modeled pre-envelope, tagged the residual stress pocket, and backed out. At the edge of the curve, his correction flared wide.

Sann pointed before she did. "Here. I treated the boundary like an obstacle."

"And?"

"It is a pressure behavior."

"And?"

He took a breath. "If I shove away, I feed the oscillation. I need to slide with the change until the board calls thrust."

"Better."

Tamsa watched him with arms folded. "That sounded like learning."

"Terrible habit," Pellam said.

Fei installed the last truth bridge module into Morrow's aft console. "Handshake ready."

Maro stepped forward. "Recording scope for Morrow. Pilot Sann, second operator Fei, maintenance channel, geometry board, source clocks, craft biometrics. Private medical biometrics limited to safety. No cognitive inference. If crew are lost, operational record preserved under dignity restrictions."

Sann swallowed. "Agreed."

Fei said, "Agreed, but if I die and the drone does something stupid, do not let it speak at my memorial."

"Recorded in spirit," Maro said.

Tamsa looked at Lena. "Geometry lead?"

Lena studied Morrow's boards. The tug's sensor suite had been improved with Larkspur-Brindle recorder stack fragments, Doss's sniffers, and two external cameras pulled from dock spares. It was still inferior to Brindle's patched truth bridge, but better distributed. Morrow would not fly alone. That was the point.

"Truth bridge test," she said.

Vela initiated from Dock Eighteen. Morrow's feeds populated the board with their usual indignities: hull temperature variance, old sensor lag, Fei's drone interface producing a complaint about port syntax, Sann's pulse elevated but stable, the pilot cradle pressure sensor reading "legacy" because nobody had configured it since before the Compact's last revision.

Central attempted to smooth.

Morrow rejected.

The board went amber with reasons.

Fei smiled. "It has taste."

"It has trauma," Vela said.

"Same family."

Tamsa gave them both a look.

Sivek entered the channel. "Counter-transit window available in thirty-two minutes if Buffer Three isolation holds. Remaining convoy reports stable but deteriorating. Dax requests plan confirmation."

Om: "Local jury verdict?"

Tamsa looked to the board. "Counter-transit with Morrow: amber-red. Risks: pre-envelope shear, residual stress rebound, pilot inexperience with route boundary, old tug sensors, hostile construct remnants unknown. Mitigations: distributed authority, Ors geometry lead off-craft, Vela trust watch, exterior physical feeds, judicial abort, source clocks visible."

"Recommendation?"

Tamsa's jaw moved. "Proceed with one more sim under live board, then decide."

Sivek said, "We may not have another sim's worth of time."

Tamsa snapped, "We also may not have another tug."

Lena looked at the countdowns. Remaining convoy time. Buffer stability. Morrow readiness. Human fatigue, not on the board but in every face.

"Run half sim," she said.

Vela objected immediately. "Half sims teach bad confidence."

"Not if we define scope. Boundary entry only. No full inversion. We test Sann's slide response and Morrow's trust rejection under live source clocks. Five minutes."

Om said, "Granted."

The half sim began with Morrow still docked but slaved to live feeds. Sann sat in the cradle, helmet open, hands on controls. Fei sat behind him, fingers over the jury interface. Lena watched from the chair, feet locked against the dock rail because not leaning forward had become impossible.

"Boundary behavior rising," Vela said.

The sim rendered the pre-envelope as a pale surface, not a line. Lena had insisted. Lines lied. Surfaces at least admitted depth.

"Sann," Lena said, "enter at mark."

"Entering."

His hands moved. The sim tug drifted toward the surface. It bulged. He did not shove. He rode with it for half a second, then corrected lateral only when the board called amber-high.

"Good," Lena said. "Now trust attack."

Vela injected a suppression attempt modeled on the third pulse. Fei caught the clipped feed and routed H-3's malformed channel to force the absence visible. Sann's right hand twitched toward manual override.

"No," Lena said.

He stopped.

"Why?"

"Because I wanted a clean board."

"You will not get one."

"I know."

"Again."

They ran it again. This time his hand did not twitch. The board stayed ugly. The sim survived.

At the end, nobody cheered. That had become their standard for competence.

Sann removed his hands from the controls. "I can do the entry."

"Yes," Lena said.

"Can I do the inversion?"

She held his gaze. "I do not know."

He nodded. "Good."

Fei leaned forward. "For the record, second operator believes pilot can do it if everyone else stops trying to make him represent the moral future of distributed authority."

Maro said, "That may be the best crew assessment I have heard tonight."

Tamsa looked at Sann. "You want out?"

He looked at Morrow. At the board. At Lena. At the exterior riggers preparing feeds. "No."

"Want is not the question," Tamsa said.

"I know. I consent."

Om said, "Recorded."

Lena felt the weight of the word consent in a room full of pressures. It did not make the choice clean. It made the dirt visible.

Sivek transmitted the plan to Dax. The reply came as text:

REMAINING CONVOY ACCEPTS COUNTER-TRANSIT ATTEMPT.
CARGO AUX GROUP CAN HOLD 5H.
PASSENGER AUX GROUP CAN HOLD 3H.
DAX FIRST GROUP REPAIRING MED PACKET TWO.
AUX3 CASUALTIES: 17 CONFIRMED, 43 INJURED.
KALL SAYS JORI PELL'S MAP SAVED HUNDREDS.

The dock went silent at the casualty line.

Seventeen.

No number was merciful. But seventeen was a shape the room could hold without collapsing. Forty-three injured. Hundreds alive. Auxiliary Three not a myth, not an argument, not a proof. A damaged ship full of people, some gone, many not.

Tamsa closed her eyes. "We will need names."

Maro said, "Yes."

Lena thought of Nerio's Lamp. Two hundred eighty-six, once a number people used to end arguments. Names came later and stayed longer. That was how it should be. Numbers were for decisions. Names were for the dead.

Om's voice softened by one shade. "Casualty confirmation will not be used in operational blame pending evidence. Public release will include cause uncertainty, ship survival, and medical status."

Vela said, "The hostile construct wanted ownership failure. This does not give it clean purchase."

"Seventeen people died," Tamsa said.

"I know."

"Do not sound relieved."

Vela flinched. "Understood."

The exchange stung because both were right. That was becoming the night's specialty.

Morrow's final readiness board came up.

CRAFT: AMBER.
PILOT: AMBER.
SECOND OPERATOR: GREEN-AMBER.
GEOMETRY LEAD: MEDICAL AMBER / COGNITIVE CLEAR.
TRUST WATCH: GREEN-AMBER.
MAINTENANCE AUTHORITY: GREEN.
STRUCTURE: AMBER-RED.
COOLANT: AMBER.
JUDICIAL ABORT: GREEN.
OVERALL: AMBER-RED WITH REASONS.

Tamsa looked at the board, then at the crew. "Mixed crew proceeds."

Om said, "Authorized."

The dock moved.

Sann sealed his helmet. Fei strapped in behind him and immediately complained about the aft harness. Tamsa checked Morrow's hatch seal personally, then did it again because distrust had saved more lives than optimism. Pellam slapped the hull and told it to embarrass every elegant craft ever built. Doss taped a coolant threshold card beside the hatch even though it was already on three boards. Maro placed a small witness seal inside the cockpit and stepped back.

Lena rolled her chair to the geometry station facing Morrow's live feed. The medic locked the wheels.

"I need lateral board and boundary model closer," she said.

The medic moved the display. "You need less authority over furniture."

"Noted."

Tamsa stood beside Lena's chair as Morrow's hatch closed.

"How does this feel?" Tamsa asked.

"Letting someone else fly?"

"Yes."

Lena watched Sann's gloved hands settle over the controls. He did not look like a route pilot. Good. Tonight did not need a route pilot in the old shape.

"Terrible," she said.

"Good."

"You keep saying that."

"Because when terrible is accurate, it is better than false peace."

Morrow's clamps released.

The old tug drifted out of Dock Twelve with Sann at the controls, Fei at the jury interface, and half of Halcyon wired into its source clocks.

Lena placed her good hand over the geometry board.

"Morrow," she said, "this is Ors. Stop thinking of the boundary as a line."

Sann's voice returned, steadier than before.

"Copy. Boundary is behavior."

"Good. Go learn what it wants."

Chapter 34: Halcyon Evacuates

The evacuation order did not say evacuate.

It said PARTIAL PRESSURE-DISTRICT REBALANCE / GATE-SIDE CIVILIAN STANDOFF DISTANCE / MEDICAL AND LIFE-SUPPORT PRIORITY MOVEMENT. It said residents of the gateward arcs should proceed to assigned interior shelters with personal medication, local ident, and no more than one soft bag per person. It said cargo stack residents without formal berth registration would be processed at temporary desks and would not be penalized for appearing on the air ledger. It said families separated by medical duty should follow district lead instructions. It said remain exact.

Everyone knew it meant the ring was moving its people away from the side most likely to suffer if counter-transit failed.

Tamsa wrote the plain-language version herself and pushed it under the official notice:

GATE SIDE MOVES INWARD. BRING MEDS. DO NOT BLOCK FAN NODES. UNREGISTERED PEOPLE BREATHE TOO. SUPERVISOR SAYS BREATHE AND WALK.

Om let it stand.

Halcyon moved.

Not beautifully. Not like the emergency drills in old Compact training reels, where citizens made tidy lines under clean arrows and nobody asked what to do with a grandmother who could not climb ladders or a child who refused to leave a sleeping pod because it was the only thing she owned. Halcyon moved like a real station: angry, practical, uneven, full of people carrying too much, full of people carrying others, full of district leads arguing with maps and nurses arguing with everyone.

Tamsa stood in Scrubber Works Three with one ear on the counter-transit channel and both hands in evacuation work. The Do It Better Board filled the main wall. Morrow's icon drifted toward the pre-envelope. Sann's pulse line was visible. Fei's second-operator feed jittered with drone channels. Lena's geometry station showed her seated, pale, medically strapped, one good hand moving across the boundary model. Beside that, district evacuation flow maps crawled in yellow and blue.

"District Two is slow at cross-corridor Seven," said Niko, who had been assigned to runner coordination after three adults independently decided he could not be kept away from useful trouble.

"Why?"

"Mobility chairs and a cargo family with pressure pets."

Tamsa blinked. "Pressure pets?"

"Two sealed fish cylinders and something in a blanket."

"Alive?"

"The blanket or the fish?"

"Do not answer. Reroute mobility chairs through maintenance lift G. Fish go with them if sealed. Blanket gets inspected for teeth."

Niko ran.

Pellam, back on a stool and pretending he was not exhausted enough to fall off it, called over the structure channel. "Gate-side Arc Three pressure doors cycling slower than map says."

"Map is prewar," Tamsa said.

"Map is a liar with nostalgia."

"Can they close?"

"Yes, if nobody wedges them with luggage."

Tamsa opened the district channel. "Arc Three: if you wedge pressure doors with bags, I will personally classify your belongings as debris. Keep moving."

A district lead answered, breathless. "Copy. Threat effective."

On the board, Morrow crossed the first perimeter mark.

Sivek's voice came through Control. "Morrow, you are clear to pre-envelope staging."

Sann: "Morrow copies."

Lena: "Easy lateral. Let the surface come to you."

Fei: "Truth bridge ugly but live."

Vela: "Residual stress pocket visible. Trust remnants quiet."

"Quiet worries me," Tamsa said, not meaning to transmit.

Maro answered anyway. "Quiet is admissible only as absence, not innocence."

"You have a terrible bedside manner."

"I am not beside a bed."

"That explains nothing."

An alarm chimed from medical. Tamsa's stomach tightened before the label appeared: cold-chain transfer convoy from pediatric ward entering inner shelter route. Not failure. Movement. Nine Halcyon children, now with medgel from the first group stabilizing across the gate, were being moved away from the gate-side arc while their treatment packs adjusted to newly arrived supplies. Meli Aronn among them. Jori, asleep at last, remained in observation and had been barred from hearing that by nurse and aunt jointly.

Keva Lorn's protected channel opened. Her voice was rough. "District leads are asking whether administrative quarantine staff may assist with evacuation desks."

"Can you keep your people from touching authority systems?"

"Yes."

"Can they write names, count bodies, and stop pretending unregistered residents are a rounding error?"

"Yes."

"Then send them to cargo stack intake under worker supervision."

Keva paused. "Thank you."

"This is logistics, not mercy."

"I know."

"Good. Move."

Tamsa closed the channel and caught Doss watching her.

"What?"

"Nothing."

"Say nothing with your face elsewhere."

Doss smiled tiredly and returned to coolant thresholds.

Morrow reached pre-envelope staging.

The workshop quieted by fractions. Evacuation did not stop. It could not. But every person learned to listen with one part of themselves while doing something else. Halcyon had become a distributed cockpit again, larger now, with civilians as part of the machine not because they should have been, but because hiding them had been part of the danger.

On the board, the boundary model pulsed.

Lena's voice: "Sann, tell me what you see."

Sann: "Boundary is not a line. Surface behavior shows outward flex near old splitter scar. Residual stress is curling back toward Vane Three if I approach direct."

"So?"

"I enter shallow, let it slide, then Fei tags the curl and Vela calls inversion timing."

"And if the surface jumps?"

"I do not shove."

"What do you do?"

"Ride until the board gives thrust or Tamsa invents a new way to be angry."

In Scrubber Works, several people laughed despite themselves.

Tamsa said, "Operationally acceptable."

The evacuation board flashed red at Dockside Market.

"Report," Tamsa snapped.

Niko's voice came back, breathless from a corridor. "Crowd stopped. They heard Morrow is entering. People want to watch the public board."

"They can watch from shelters."

"They say if the ring breaks, they want to see."

Tamsa closed her eyes once. Then she opened a public local channel.

"Dockside Market, this is Reet. I understand the impulse to stare at the thing that may kill you. It will not improve the view. Move inward. The board follows you. Your children and elders cannot breathe your courage. Walk."

A few seconds. Too long.

The flow resumed.

Niko said, "Moving."

Tamsa exhaled. "Good."

Om entered her channel. "That was almost eloquent."

"Delete the record."

"Denied."

Morrow entered the pre-envelope.

Every board on Halcyon dimmed peripheral data and highlighted source clocks. Not to simplify. To focus. The tug's icon crossed the pale surface around the gate throat. Residual stress rose from the old splitter wound like weather in clear air. Sann held the slide. Fei's hands moved over the second-operator board, pinning drone feeds and local sensors as they began to disagree.

"Pre-envelope contact," Sann said. His voice shook once and steadied. "Surface flexing."

Lena: "Do not push."

"Not pushing."

Vela: "Trust remnant ping from burn-vault mirror."

Maro: "Boundary monitor rejects. Oriole confirms no lawful continuity claim."

Ilya's voice entered, strained. "Ceyr vault mirror is quarantined. If it pings again, burn it."

Om: "Authorized under supervision."

Pellam: "Structure on Morrow is amber-red."

Doss: "Coolant stable. Vane Three brace stable."

Sivek: "Power buffers holding at inversion-ready warm."

Tamsa watched Sann's pulse line rise.

The evacuation map flashed again. This time not red. Blue. Gate-side districts reached seventy percent interior sheltering. Cargo stack intake at forty-one percent and climbing. Pediatric transfer complete. Fan nodes unblocked.

Halcyon was moving while Morrow held the edge of a wounded universe.

The station did not become brave. Tamsa distrusted brave. It became busy in the face of fear, which was more reliable.

"Inversion window in thirty seconds," Vela said.

Lena's face on the geometry feed was still, too pale, eyes locked on a model only she and Sann seemed to share fully. "Sann. The surface will try to throw you outward when Vela calls it. Let it. Half a second. Then lateral downspin."

"Copy."

"Say it."

"Let it throw. Half second. Lateral downspin."

"Fei?"

"I tag curl and scream if the board eats a source."

"Operational language," Tamsa said automatically.

"I tag curl and report source suppression," Fei corrected.

Vela counted down.

"Three. Two. One. Invert."

The power buffers pulsed.

Morrow disappeared from ordinary motion.

It did not vanish like a ship entering transit. It blurred sideways while remaining exactly where it was, as if the eye had been given two incompatible truths and chose both. Residual stress from the splitter wound bent toward the tug, caught by the inversion pulse. The pre-envelope surface flexed outward.

Sann let it throw him.

For half a second, Morrow drifted beyond the predicted boundary.

Tamsa's hand closed around the edge of the table hard enough to hurt.

Then Sann fired lateral downspin.

Morrow slid along the behavior, not the line.

The residual stress followed, pulled away from Vane Three, away from the damaged buffer path, into a safe dump vector prepared by Control and doubted by everyone.

The dump vector lit.

For a moment, Kestrel's star bent wrong.

Then the stress released into empty space, a silent flare of folded light that would have been beautiful if beauty had not been so expensive tonight.

"Counter-transit pulse complete," Sivek said.

Pellam: "Structure holding."

Doss: "Coolant stable."

Vela: "Trust remnants failed to latch."

Fei: "Morrow still exists."

Sann, breathing hard: "Pilot agrees."

The workshop erupted, then immediately did not, because Tamsa raised one hand.

"Morrow exits," she said.

Lena: "Sann, shallow back. Do not celebrate inside the knife."

"Copy."

Morrow slid out of the pre-envelope, slow and ugly and alive.

Only then did Tamsa let herself sit down.

The chair was not there. She sat on the floor.

Niko, arriving with a new evacuation update, stared.

"Supervisor?"

"Report."

"Shelter movement eighty-two percent. Cargo stack intake sixty. Blanket thing had no teeth."

"Good."

"Are you on the floor?"

"Temporarily."

"Should I document?"

"If you value your future, no."

The Do It Better Board updated:

COUNTER-TRANSIT: COMPLETE.
RESIDUAL STRESS: REDUCED.
GENERAL OPENING: STILL UNSAFE.
REMAINING CONVOY: LIMITED TRANSIT POSSIBLE AFTER REASSESSMENT.

Tamsa looked at the line and laughed once under her breath.

Limited possible. Not saved. Not finished. But possible.

Around her, Halcyon kept walking inward, kept sealing doors, kept counting people who had once been invisible, kept breathing because thousands of ordinary decisions had lined up under an ugly board and refused to become panic.

Supervisor said breathe.

For the first time all night, she almost did.

Chapter 35: Inside the Envelope

Morrow returned to Dock Twelve with scorch along its port side and a pilot who could not stop apologizing for surviving.

"I overcorrected after the dump," Sann said as soon as the hatch opened.

Tamsa stood on the dock with her hands on her hips. "Are you alive?"

"Yes."

"Is Fei alive?"

From behind him, Fei said, "Mostly and offensively."

"Is Morrow intact enough to bill?"

Pellam leaned past them to inspect the hull. "Bill, yes. Praise, no."

Tamsa looked back at Sann. "Then apologize later, specifically."

Sann nodded, then saw Lena in the medical chair near the geometry station and lost some of his composure. "Pilot Ors."

"Do not call me that like I am about to grade you."

"You already are."

"True. You passed the part that mattered."

He looked down at his gloves. "I was terrified."

"Good."

"Everyone keeps saying that."

"Annoying, isn't it?"

He laughed once, shaky and young. Fei clapped him on the shoulder hard enough to make him stumble and then pretended she had meant to check suit integrity.

There was no time for the ceremony the moment deserved. The counter-transit pulse had reduced residual stress, but the remaining convoy still waited outside the gate with fuel and life support sliding toward mathematics no one liked. Dax's latest packet arrived as the mixed crew disembarked.

COUNTER-TRANSIT OBSERVED FROM CONVOY.
REMAINING SHIPS READY FOR LIMITED TRANSIT IF KESTREL CONFIRMS.
CARGO AUX GROUP STABLE 4H.
PASSENGER AUX GROUP STABLE 2H 20M.
AUX3 CASUALTY LIST TRANSMITTING TO MEDICAL ONLY.
DAX.

Medical only. Another good captain decision that did not help and mattered.

The Do It Better Board began reassessment. Vane Three shifted from amber-red to amber. Vane One purge remained amber-red but contained. Buffer Three damaged but isolated. Trust layer remnants red but failing to latch. Power buffers green-amber after counter-transit. Structural model amber. Coolant amber. Manual authority green. General opening unsafe. Limited transit possible.

Possible had become a dangerous word.

Om convened the next authority review in Dock Twelve because nobody had strength left to move the court elsewhere and because the dock had learned to hold law, medicine, engineering, grief, and bad coffee in one place. Evacuation continued in the background. Gate-side districts had reached ninety-one percent interior sheltering. Cargo stack intake had slowed at seventy-three because some unregistered residents feared appearing on any ledger despite Tamsa's plain-language guarantee. Keva Lorn's quarantined administrative staff were sent with district witnesses to count without threatening. House Ceyr supplied temporary pressure cots and did not attach branding after Ilya received one look from Om.

Sivek appeared from Control. "Remaining convoy can be handled in two limited groups if the first limited group passes cleanly. Cargo mass first or passenger mass first is the decision."

Tamsa said, "Passengers."

Doss said, "Cargo includes scrubber membranes and med supplies."

"Passengers."

Medical channel entered. "Some cargo is medically essential within days, not minutes. Passenger auxiliaries have life support margins under cargo. Medical recommends passenger mass first."

Doss nodded. "Then passengers."

Ilya spoke from Ceyr liaison. "Cargo loss will affect Halcyon recovery and future gate repair."

Tamsa looked toward her feed. "Passengers."

Ilya held up both hands. "I am not objecting. I am making the cost visible."

"Fine. Cost visible. Passengers."

Om said, "Passenger limited group first. Cargo after reassessment."

Lena studied the model. The counter-transit had opened a cleaner path, but cleaner was relative. The first group had passed under tight formation. Auxiliary Three had passed as a needle. The remaining passenger auxiliaries were not as damaged as Three but less agile than the first group. They would need the envelope widened beyond the needle and kept narrower than standard opening.

"This puts us inside the envelope," she said.

The room quieted.

Sivek nodded. "A calibration craft must ride inside the lower tolerance band during passenger group transit."

"Morrow?" Om asked.

Pellam said, "Morrow survived the counter-transit but needs thruster inspection."

Fei said, "I trust it more now and less physically."

Tamsa looked at Lena. "Can the craft be remote?"

Vela answered. "No. Delay and trust attack risk. Needs live manual response."

Maro said, "We are again approaching the shape of a sacrifice."

"No," Lena said.

Everyone looked at her.

She moved the model to the main display. "Not one craft. Two. Morrow rides lower band. Ashkey rides upper outside band with independent source mesh. Neither alone holds the envelope. They bracket it. If one feed clips, the other remains. If one craft has to withdraw, the board narrows instead of collapsing."

Pellam squinted at the model. "Ashkey is a brick."

"A useful brick."

"Upper band needs less finesse," Vela said slowly. "More mass tolerance. Ashkey could do it."

Fei raised a hand. "I cannot be in both craft."

"You stay Morrow," Lena said. "Ashkey gets remote second operator from Dock Twelve with hardwired drone support."

Tamsa said, "Pilot?"

Pellam coughed.

Everyone turned to him.

Tamsa said, "No."

"I have more upper-band tug hours than anyone still awake."

"You have one artificial leg, two bad habits, and a chair you nearly fell off."

"The leg does not steer."

"The habits do."

Pellam's expression became more serious than Lena had ever seen it. "Upper band is not pre-envelope. It is heavy station-keeping inside field wash. I can do that. Ashkey likes ugly hands."

Sann, still in his suit liner, said, "He can."

Tamsa turned on him. "You are not helping."

"He taught me half of what I used out there."

Pellam looked briefly embarrassed. "Only the half with profanity."

Om said, "Medical assessment?"

The medic Bera looked at Pellam. "Annoyingly viable if seated with leg lock and cardiac monitor."

Tamsa stared at her. "You too?"

"I assess bodies. I do not approve personalities."

Maro said, "Consent under pressure must be recorded."

Pellam snorted. "I consent to piloting Ashkey upper bracket because I am qualified, old enough to know better, and tired of letting children do all the exciting mistakes."

"That is not a model consent statement," Maro said.

"Then polish it after I land."

Om looked at Tamsa. "Supervisor Reet?"

Tamsa's face was rigid. She was being asked to approve risk for someone she loved badly, which was to say with insults and work assignments.

"If the task is upper bracket station-keeping in Ashkey," she said, "Pellam is qualified. I object emotionally."

"Recorded."

"With emphasis."

"Recorded with emphasis."

The two-craft plan took shape quickly because it had to. Morrow lower band: Sann pilot, Fei second operator. Ashkey upper bracket: Pellam pilot, remote second via Dock Twelve, drone support. Lena geometry lead. Tamsa maintenance authority. Sivek gate sequence. Vela trust watch. Doss coolant. Maro evidence. Om abort. Medical veto on both pilots if biometrics crossed red.

The board rendered:

PASSENGER LIMITED TRANSIT PLAN: AMBER-RED.
TWO-CRAFT BRACKET MITIGATION: ACTIVE.
CERTAINTY: UNAVAILABLE.
DECISION REQUIRED.

Dax received the plan. His reply:

PASSENGER GROUP ACCEPTS. CARGO GROUP WILL WAIT. CREWS UNDERSTAND BRACKET RISK. SEND PILOT NAMES.

Sivek looked at the room.

Lena transmitted:

LOWER BRACKET: SANN / MORROW.
UPPER BRACKET: PELLAM / ASHKEY.
GEOMETRY: ORS / LOCAL JURY.

Dax replied:

KALL SAYS TELL PELLAM AGE IS NOT ARMOR.

Pellam barked a laugh. "I like her."

Tamsa muttered, "Everyone likes the captain telling my people to be careful. Nobody listens to me saying it."

Pellam softened. "I listen."

"You do not."

"I hear."

"Worse."

He reached out and squeezed her shoulder once. "I will try to come back so you can continue the critique."

Tamsa looked away first. "Try hard."

The passenger group burn began.

Dock Twelve's lights dimmed as power buffers warmed again. Evacuation shelters received a plain update: SECOND LIMITED TRANSIT STARTING. REMAIN INSIDE. BOARDS FOLLOW. Children in the shelters watched amber lines move across walls. Adults watched source clocks. Cargo stack residents watched intake clerks write their names without calling security.

Morrow and Ashkey launched together.

Lena sat at the geometry station, medical chair locked, both craft feeds before her. Morrow moved like a learner who had survived the lesson and now respected the subject. Ashkey moved like a stubborn old tool in stubborn old hands. The bracket formed around the projected envelope, lower and upper, two local truths where one had been too easy to clip.

"Morrow lower mark," Sann said.

"Ashkey upper mark," Pellam said.

Lena's board rendered the envelope between them. "Hold bracket. Passenger group at outer approach."

The gate woke for the fourth time that day.

It sounded different after counter-transit. Less like a throat clearing. More like a wounded thing agreeing to stand. Vane Three lagged but did not sag as badly. Vane One hissed through its contained purge. Vane Four carried compensation. The buffer ring held with one dark segment where Brindle had torn the old splitter open.

The passenger ships appeared on optical: four auxiliaries, slow and heavy with people.

Central stayed amber.

The local jury stayed amber-red.

The hidden construct did not speak.

That was not comfort.

"Passenger group entering envelope," Sivek said.

Lena watched Morrow and Ashkey bracket the lower and upper behaviors. "Sann, drift downspin point zero two. Pellam, hold, do not chase him."

Pellam: "I know what bracket means."

"Prove it quietly."

Ashkey held.

Morrow drifted.

The first passenger auxiliary entered the aperture. The envelope flexed lower. Morrow tagged it. Ashkey confirmed upper stability. The board adjusted. The ship went through.

Second ship.

Third.

On the fourth, the Vane One purge flared.

Doss: "Coolant spike."

Vela: "Trust remnant trying to suppress Vane One feed."

Fei: "Not today."

H-3's malformed timestamp flooded the mesh. The suppression rendered black instead of invisible.

Lena: "Fourth ship lift point zero one. Morrow lower tag. Ashkey upper hold."

Sann: "Lower tag."

Pellam: "Holding."

The fourth ship scraped the modeled edge, lost a cargo blister, and passed through with pressure intact.

Low-band confirmation came faster this time.

PASSENGER GROUP THROUGH.
MINOR DAMAGE.
NO REPORTED FATALITIES.

Dock Twelve did not cheer. It exhaled.

The cargo group remained.

The gate remained damaged.

The day remained unfinished.

But for the first time since the rehearsal pulse, the work ahead looked less like preventing catastrophe and more like surviving consequence.

Morrow and Ashkey returned inside the field wash.

Tamsa's voice entered both craft channels.

"Pilots, report."

Sann: "Morrow alive."

Fei: "Morrow offended but alive."

Pellam: "Ashkey and elder dignity intact."

Tamsa closed her eyes. "That last part is contested."

Lena let herself smile for one second.

Then the cargo group board went amber-black.

Vela whispered, "Something just woke in Blue Vault Six."

The hostile construct had not spent its last move.

Chapter 36: Maro's Broadcast

Blue Vault Six had always been a polite name for a locked throat.

It sat inside House Ceyr's bonded storage quarter, two decks above the chemistry vault and one pressure door away from the old cargo spine. Before the war it had held certified parts, liability-controlled tools, and crates whose seals mattered more than their contents. During the war, if the burn-vault records could be trusted at all, it had become a place where emergency keys passed through clean hands into dirtier systems. After the war, Ceyr had called it dormant.

Dormant things, Halcyon had learned, were often only waiting for the right lie to wake them.

The alert from Blue Vault Six did not come as an explosion or a breach. It came as a document request.

REQUESTING RESTORATION OF EMERGENCY ROUTEHOLDER CUSTODY
BASIS: DISTRIBUTED AUTHORITY CASUALTY EVENT
SOURCE: SAREN VALE AUDIT CONTINUITY
EVIDENCE ATTACHED: AUXILIARY THREE CASUALTY PACKET / LOCAL JURY COMMAND TRACE / ORS VISUAL OVERRIDE

It hit House Ceyr systems first, then Compact legal mirror, then three public relays in the Ash Belt that had no business receiving Kestrel emergency filings in real time. Whoever or whatever remained of the Vale construct had stopped trying to hide inside machinery. It was making a legal move where law, grief, and distance would do its work faster than sabotage.

Maro read the filing twice in the evidence bay before he understood the cruelty of it.

The construct was not claiming the local jury had failed because the gate failed. It was claiming the local jury had succeeded in a way that proved it could kill. Auxiliary Three had survived with casualties. Lena's visual correction had saved the ship but preceded hull damage. Jori's map correction had altered the path. Tamsa's worker board had informed the decision. Distributed authority had touched every action.

Therefore, the filing argued, distributed authority owned every death.

Therefore, emergency routeholder custody should be restored before the cargo group.

"It is elegant," Maro said, hating himself for the word.

Technician Sen looked up from the drone memory cradle. "Do not admire hostile paperwork near me."

"Admiration is not approval."

"Still smells bad."

Om's channel opened. "Investigator Venn."

"I see it."

"Can you kill it?"

"Legally?"

"Preferably."

He almost smiled. "Not with a single objection. It is not meant to win here. It is meant to propagate faster than the full record."

Tamsa's voice entered from Scrubber Works. "Then we propagate the record."

"Raw record is too large. Too technical. Too ugly."

"Ugly has been working."

"Not across external relays where Ceyr rivals, Compact loyalists, and routeholder houses will strip it into slogans before the next cargo burn."

Lena, still at the geometry station and looking more tired than any living person should be allowed to look while conscious, said, "What does it need to be?"

Maro looked at the filing. At the recovered Aurelian context. At the frozen drone. At the casualty line from Auxiliary Three. At his own hands, which had once argued that privacy mattered more than proof and now knew the danger of letting proof belong only to predators.

"A broadcast," he said.

Om's eyes narrowed on the feed. "Public?"

"Public enough to outrun the filing. Not full protected content. Not casualty names before medical release. A reconstruction statement: what happened, what is known, what is unknown, why casualty does not equal ownership by the last visible decision, and how the Vale construct is attempting to convert grief into custody."

Tamsa said, "Plain."

"Plain."

Oriole-of-Archive entered. "Boundary condition: no protected Aurelian identity exposure beyond already disclosed Vale continuity mask context."

"Agreed," Maro said.

Ilya appeared from the Ceyr liaison room. "House Ceyr will carry the broadcast through its own relays."

Tamsa gave a humorless laugh. "That will make people trust it."

Ilya looked toward her feed. "No. It will make them unable to claim we hid it."

Maro nodded. "Useful."

Om said, "You have six minutes before Blue Vault Six filing hits the next route relay."

Six minutes was not enough time to craft anything. It was enough time to choose not to polish.

Maro stood in the evidence bay, facing three cameras labeled by source: judicial, worker mesh, Ceyr relay. He insisted on the labels being visible in the frame. He insisted the Blue Vault filing remain displayed behind him, marked HOSTILE / UNVERIFIED / PRESERVED. He asked for the Auxiliary Three status line to remain visible too:

ALIVE / 17 CONFIRMED DEAD / 43 INJURED / CAUSE REVIEW PENDING.

Not hidden. Not used.

Then he began.

"This is Maro Venn, accident reconstruction under Meridian Compact emergency review and Magistrate Om's court. A filing from Blue Vault Six is currently spreading through external route relays. It claims Auxiliary Three casualties prove distributed local authority is too dangerous and that emergency routeholder custody should be restored before the remaining cargo group transits. That claim is not evidence. It is the next step in the sabotage method."

He let the sentence stand long enough for transcripts to catch it.

"Here is the record in plain order. Kestrel Gate's central telemetry was manipulated before the rehearsal incident. Lot 88 coolant records were falsified. Maintenance personnel were framed. A hidden trust layer suppressed local truth. House Ceyr dead-key infrastructure and Halcyon administrative credentials were performed or coerced. A hostile continuity mask using the Vale names exploited sealed postwar records, burn-vault failures, and emergency doctrines. The local jury did not create this danger. It made the danger visible."

Tamsa's voice did not enter, but he felt her demand for plainness behind him like a hand between his shoulders.

"Auxiliary Three's casualties are real. They are not a slogan. They are not a custody argument. Current evidence shows Auxiliary Three was already contaminated and thermally failing before its needle transit. The local jury changed its path after an apprentice's map observation revealed an untagged coolant vector. That correction likely prevented total loss. It may also have been part of a chain that led to hull damage. Both can be investigated. Neither justifies handing authority to a system that helped hide the danger."

He heard his own voice try to become legal. He pulled it back.

"Do not let anyone make grief do their work before the bodies are named."

The evidence bay went very still.

Maro continued. "We will preserve the Blue Vault filing. We will preserve the casualty record. We will preserve Lena Ors's visual override, Jori Pell's map observation, Tamsa Reet's local jury channels, Captain Kall's consent, Captain Dax's packets, House Ceyr's burn-vault failures, Compact scheduling decisions, and my own sealed-context disclosure. No one gets a clean story tonight. Clean stories are how this happened."

Om's face on the side feed shifted by a fraction. Approval, perhaps. Or fatigue finding company.

"The cargo group remains outside Kestrel. It carries scrubber membranes, medical supplies, repair stock, and people in crew compartments whose risk is also real. The decision to transit or wait will be made under visible disagreement. If you receive a clean claim from any faction, including mine, ask what it clipped."

He looked directly into the judicial camera.

"Source clocks visible. Grief visible. Responsibility visible. Custody not restored by panic."

He ended the broadcast.

For three seconds nobody spoke.

Then Technician Sen said, "Plain enough."

Maro had not known he needed that until it arrived.

The broadcast propagated. Worker mesh. Judicial docket. Ceyr relay. Compact emergency. Dax packet summary. Ash Belt public nodes, where the Blue Vault filing had hoped to arrive first and clean. It did not stop the filing. Nothing did. But it met it with a record shaped for human attention rather than procedural burial.

Responses began to appear.

Free Hab League node: RECORD RECEIVED / BLUE VAULT CLAIM CONTESTED.

Varda Convoy Authority: DAX PACKETS MATCH VENN SUMMARY.

Ceyr Minority Trustee Board: BURN-VAULT FAILURE ACKNOWLEDGED / ROUTEHOLDER CUSTODY CLAIM NOT SUPPORTED.

Halcyon District Four: WE WANT NAMES BEFORE BLAME.

Cargo Stack Intake: UNREGISTERED CREW ARE PEOPLE TOO.

Tamsa read that last one aloud in Scrubber Works and said nothing for a moment.

Then Blue Vault Six made its physical move.

It opened.

Not the main vault door. A service hatch behind the bonded coolant racks, one that House Ceyr's current maps labeled sealed, one that Pellam's Quiet Mark route had flagged as probable but unreachable without Ceyr cooperation. Its opening sent a pressure tremor through the storage quarter and a status ping through the dead-key route.

Vela caught it first. "Vault hatch active."

Ilya: "No current Ceyr authorization."

Tamsa: "What is inside?"

Ilya looked at records scrolling beside her. Her face drained of color. "Emergency routeholder custody cores. Old."

Maro said, "Define cores."

"Portable authority packages. Legal templates, access emulators, telemetry filters, route-control claims. They were supposed to be destroyed."

"Of course," Tamsa said.

Om said, "Can they transmit?"

Ilya checked. "If powered."

Vela: "Something woke Blue Vault. Assume powered."

The cargo group board flickered. Amber-black deepened.

Sivek's voice from Control went hard. "Kestrel systems receiving custody-core handshake attempts. Rejected by current challenge state, but they are probing."

"Cargo group?" Lena asked.

"Still holding. But if the custody cores reach external relays, remaining convoy insurers may order Dax to suspend or surrender control."

"Dax will ignore them," Tamsa said.

"Dax may. His crews may receive conflicting legal orders."

Maro had just warned everyone against clean stories. Now another one was trying to write itself in machine-readable law.

Ilya stood. "I can go to Blue Vault."

Her counsel objected in three languages at once.

"No," Om said.

Ilya turned. "I know the old vault layout."

"You are a material witness and a compromised authority."

"Yes. I am also the person who signed the false destruction certificate. If the old cores respond to anyone alive, they may respond to me."

Tamsa said, "Or eat your authority and wear it."

"Possibly."

Lena looked from the cargo board to Ilya's feed to the live map of Blue Vault Six. The remaining convoy needed the cargo: scrubber membranes, repair stock, medical reserves. Halcyon needed it. The gate needed it. The hostile construct needed delay, confusion, and a story where distributed authority failed because it could not stop old ownership from reasserting itself.

Om said, "No one goes alone."

Tamsa smiled without warmth. "Finally, doctrine."

Maro picked up his recorder. "I will go."

"You are evidence lead," Om said.

"The cores are making a legal-evidentiary move. I can identify what they attempt to claim."

Tamsa said, "I am going."

"You are maintenance authority," Om said.

"The vault hatch is a physical system in my station."

Ilya said, "I am going."

Om looked at the three of them and seemed to consider resigning from civilization.

Lena, from the geometry station, said, "Take Niko."

Everyone stared.

Niko's voice squeaked over the runner channel. "Me?"

"No," Tamsa said immediately.

Lena continued. "Not inside. Outside the route. He knows the new intake paths and unregistered corridors better than Ceyr maps. If Blue Vault has an unlisted service exit, he may know which people it endangers."

Niko tried to sound older. "I can do that."

Tamsa's glare promised future consequences. "Outer route only. With an adult."

Pellam said, "I am not an adult, but I am available."

"Absolutely not," Tamsa said.

In the end, the Blue Vault team became Ilya, Tamsa, Maro, two judicial officers, Technician Sen, and a maintenance escort. Niko ran outer corridor mapping with Doss supervising from Scrubber Works and three district leads watching him like predatory relatives. Om authorized. Oriole monitored. Vela kept the custody handshakes visible. Sivek shielded Control. Lena stayed at the geometry station, because the cargo group still needed a gate.

As the team moved toward Blue Vault Six, Maro's broadcast kept spreading.

No clean story.

He hoped it would be enough to slow the old one until they could put hands on the machine writing it.

Chapter 37: Sivek's Choice

Sivek Hale had always trusted sequences.

Not institutions, despite what Lena thought. Not laws. Not people with polished authority. Sequences. The order in which a thing must happen so another thing could happen without killing everyone nearby. Charge before field. Field before alignment. Alignment before corridor. Corridor before burn. Warning before maneuver. Witness before record. Record before judgment, if civilization could afford the time.

The night had attacked every sequence he knew.

Now Control showed him three clocks and none agreed on mercy.

Cargo group life-support margin: three hours eleven minutes.

Blue Vault custody-core handshake escalation: twelve minutes to external relay burst if not contained.

Kestrel limited transit readiness after counter-transit: twenty-six minutes to next viable opening before buffers required cool-down.

Three sequences. All incompatible.

In Control, the room had changed since the rehearsal pulse. The central board no longer dominated. The Do It Better Board sat beside it, ugly and stubborn. Local feeds crawled across the lower tier. Worker source clocks blinked in the space where routeholder liability projections had once occupied pride of place. Sivek had ordered no one to remove the old displays. He wanted the shame visible.

An operator called, "Blue Vault handshake probing Control again."

"Reject through live challenge."

"Rejected. It is trying Ceyr custody template revised by Vale mask."

"Display."

The template unfolded in harsh legal shorthand: emergency routeholder custody on evidence of distributed casualty, gate instability, hostile local interference, compromised worker channels, need for rapid restoration of command hierarchy before cargo transit.

It cited Auxiliary Three's dead.

It cited Lena's visual override.

It cited Jori's apprentice report.

It cited Tamsa's public channels.

It cited Sivek's own countermanded green-board authority as evidence that Compact command had been undermined.

It was a filthy document with excellent structure.

Sivek hated that too.

The Blue Vault team feed appeared on a side display. Tamsa and Ilya walked side by side through Ceyr's bonded storage corridor like two people who would prefer vacuum to conversation. Maro followed with a recorder. Technician Sen carried a shielded case. The judicial officers kept hands near tools that were not weapons until urgently reclassified.

Tamsa's voice came through. "Approaching service hatch."

Ilya: "The hatch should not exist on current maps."

Tamsa: "Yes, we covered the theme."

Maro: "Record notes hatch visible, unlisted in current Ceyr map, Quiet Mark three-line bite present on hinge seam."

Sivek turned to the operator. "Cargo group packet window?"

"Four minutes."

Lena's voice from geometry station: "Cargo group plan still forming. Vane Three can handle one broad low-mass cargo transit if we avoid the damaged splitter vector. Passenger group wake settled. Morrow and Ashkey can bracket again after inspection."

"Pilot status?"

"Sann and Pellam medically cleared for one more bracket. Fei has threatened to haunt anyone who says the word heroic."

"Noted."

The cargo group was not glamorous. That made its risk harder to see. It carried scrubber membranes, medgel reserve cultures, vane repair ceramics, cold-chain stabilizers, fabrication templates, pediatric nutrient packets, and structural coils Halcyon had been promised across six years of delay. If it died, people would not all die tonight. They would die by maintenance debt over weeks and months, in pneumonia logs and scrubber triage and old valves failing one at a time. Slow casualties were still casualties. They were simply easier for authority to misplace.

Sivek looked at the cargo manifest and imagined each crate as breath.

The Blue Vault feed flashed. The service hatch opened.

Inside lay a narrow room packed with black cases.

Not many. Nine. Each sealed in obsolete Ceyr blue, each marked with no public code, each bearing either a broken ring or three-line bite. They sat on a powered rack that should have been cold. It was not.

Ilya whispered, "They were not burned."

Tamsa turned on her. "I can see that."

"No." Ilya's voice cracked. "I mean they were never even moved to burn. The certificate was not false after inspection. It was false from the start."

Maro said, "Who signed rack power maintenance?"

Technician Sen scanned. "Automated dead-key loop. Saren audit token. Last living validation..." She paused. "Ivo Ceyr."

Ilya closed her eyes. "My father."

The custody-core handshake in Control intensified.

Operator: "External relay burst charging."

Sivek said, "Can Blue Vault team cut power?"

Sen answered over the feed. "If we cut rack power cold, volatile cores may dump templates into relay as fail-dead."

"Fail-dead," Tamsa said. "I hate every designer in this vault."

Vela entered. "They built the rack to make destruction look like attack. If you burn them wrong, they scream their doctrine outward."

Om: "Options."

Sen: "Isolate one by one into shielded case. Slow."

"How slow?"

"Twenty minutes if none fight."

Sivek looked at the relay clock.

Ten minutes, forty seconds.

Cargo readiness clock.

Twenty-four minutes.

Lena said, "Sivek."

He knew that tone. She had used it once in a collapsing route, right before asking for permission no doctrine could grant.

"Say it," he said.

"If the relay burst goes out before cargo transit, Dax's insurers and convoy legal may order cargo group to hold or surrender to routeholder custody claims. Even if Dax resists, the cargo captains may be bound by contracts."

"Yes."

"Can Compact preempt with an emergency open-cargo authority?"

"Only if gate readiness is confirmed."

"Can you confirm?"

He looked at the board. Amber-red. With reasons. Cargo group possible after Morrow and Ashkey inspection, Vane Three drift acceptable, buffer cool-down not yet required, trust remnants visible, Blue Vault active. Possible. Not certified.

"Not under ordinary sequence."

"We are past ordinary."

Tamsa's voice from Blue Vault: "Do not rush cargo through because these cases are ugly."

"If we wait, the cases may control the cargo by law," Lena said.

"If we rush, the gate may kill it by physics."

Both true. Sivek could feel the old machinery of command assembling itself around him: incompatible truths, insufficient time, a board that would never be clean enough to absolve choice.

Om said, "Officer Hale, your recommendation."

Everyone waited.

Sivek had spent six years wishing Lena had been wrong to choose. Wishing the universe had offered a hidden fourth path at Nerio's Lamp that the inquiry had missed. It had not. Tonight had not forgiven her by repeating the problem. It had indicted everyone who pretended command could be made innocent by procedure.

He placed both hands on the manual scheduling plate.

"We proceed in parallel," he said. "Blue Vault team isolates cores one by one. Control sends immediate preemptive cargo authority packet to Dax and cargo captains: custody-core filing hostile, routeholder emergency claims rejected by court and Compact, cargo group remains under convoy command and Kestrel local jury. Morrow and Ashkey inspect and relaunch for bracket. Gate opens for cargo if and only if local jury remains no worse than amber-red and Blue Vault relay is contained or delayed. I will not wait for green. I will not hide amber."

Om held his gaze through the feed. "That is not a recommendation. That is an operational ruling."

"Yes."
