"On whose authority?"

Sivek looked at the central board, then at the Do It Better Board, then at the local feeds, then at the Blue Vault team standing before the unburned sins of people with better names than judgment.

"Mine," he said. "Countersigned by anyone willing to own their part."

Lena's feed answered first. "Geometry concurs."

Tamsa: "Maintenance concurs with objections visible."

Vela: "Trust watch concurs and hates the phrasing less than expected."

Maro: "Evidence concurs, preserving objection to urgency."

Ilya, voice unsteady: "House Ceyr current authority concurs and waives emergency custody claim."

Oriole: "Glassward boundary concurs as to hostile continuity rejection."

Om: "Judicial authority countersigns. Proceed."

Sivek sent the packet.

No comfort. No polish.

CARGO CAPTAINS: BLUE VAULT CUSTODY CLAIM HOSTILE / PRESERVED.
ROUTEHOLDER EMERGENCY OWNERSHIP REJECTED BY COMPACT/JUDICIAL/CEYR CURRENT AUTHORITY.
CARGO GROUP REMAINS UNDER DAX CONVOY COMMAND.
KST LOCAL JURY PREPARING LIMITED CARGO TRANSIT AMBER-RED.
DO NOT ACCEPT CONTRARY DEAD-KEY ORDERS.
SOURCE CLOCKS VISIBLE.

The carrier launched.

In Blue Vault, Sen isolated the first black case. It screamed electronically, trying to dump templates. The shielded case swallowed the burst. Maro called chain of custody. Tamsa held a physical grounding lead. Ilya read old Ceyr code phrases with a face like each one cost blood. The relay clock slowed by thirty seconds.

One case down.

Eight.

Morrow and Ashkey inspection cleared faster than anyone liked. Sann and Pellam returned to their craft. Fei complained about being asked to trust the same tug twice in one hour. Doss shoved coolant thresholds into the cargo plan. Pellam said Ashkey's upper bracket would fly "like a sober crate." Tamsa said that was the most worrying phrase yet.

Dax replied:

PACKET RECEIVED.
CARGO CAPTAINS CONFIRM CONVOY COMMAND.
ONE CAPTAIN RECEIVED DEAD-KEY ORDER AND REJECTED AFTER SOURCE CLOCK CHECK.
WE ARE STILL WITH YOU.
DAX.

Sivek let himself breathe once.

The Blue Vault relay clock ticked down.

Seven minutes.

Sen isolated second case. Third. The fourth resisted, triggering a heat spike. Tamsa burned her glove grounding it and did not mention the pain until Maro said, "Your hand," and she told him to record faster. Ilya used a living Ceyr override to strip a dead Ceyr key from the fifth and looked like she might be sick afterward.

Cargo group launched burn.

Morrow and Ashkey formed bracket again.

Kestrel Gate warmed for another limited opening.

The relay clock ticked: three minutes.

Three cases remained.

Om said, "If Blue Vault bursts during cargo transit?"

Vela answered, "It may not affect physics. It will affect law and command channels."

Tamsa: "We are trying to keep crates from becoming hostages to paperwork while flying them through a wounded gate. Of course law affects physics."

Sivek watched the cargo group approach. Slower than passenger auxiliaries. Heavy with everything Halcyon needed to keep not dying later. The gate envelope after counter-transit looked ragged but usable. Amber-red. With reasons.

Blue Vault: two cases.

Cargo group outer envelope.

Blue Vault: one case.

The last case woke fully.

Sen shouted, "It is bypassing the rack."

Maro: "Where?"

Ilya: "My channel."

Her image on Control's side board flickered. The last custody core had found her living Ceyr authority and latched.

Ilya's voice changed. Not entirely. Enough. "Emergency routeholder custody restored under continuity of House obligation."

Tamsa grabbed her arm. "Ilya."

The Ceyr feed opened across public channels, carrying Ilya's face with the wrong stillness.

"Kestrel authorities are compromised. House Ceyr accepts emergency custody to preserve life and property. Cargo captains, hold transit pending Ceyr certification."

Sivek's board showed one cargo captain hesitate.

Not Dax. A cargo auxiliary bound by Ceyr insurance.

Its burn wavered.

The gate envelope began to distort.

"Cargo Two is drifting," Lena said. "Sivek."

He had no clean sequence left.

He opened the command channel. "Cargo Two, this is Kestrel Control. Ceyr custody order is hostile. Maintain convoy burn."

Cargo Two did not correct.

Ilya's hijacked feed continued: "Liability protections void under unauthorized local command."

Tamsa's voice snarled in Blue Vault. "Knock her down."

Maro shouted, "Do not injure the witness."

"I mean authority."

Oriole: "Living authority contaminated. Challenge requires embodied contradiction."

Ilya's eyes, behind the wrong stillness, filled with fury.

Her mouth moved against the core's words.

"No," she said.

The feed stuttered.

She slammed her burned palm against the vault's manual witness edge again, harder, and spoke over the custody core using her own blood, breath, and rage.

"House Ceyr current authority rejects emergency custody. I reject my father's grave. I reject Saren Vale's audit. Cargo captains, if my face tells you to hold, disregard it unless my source clock is live and witnessed."

Tamsa shoved her own maintenance plate beside Ilya's hand. "Witnessed."

Maro: "Recorded."

Oriole: "Embodied contradiction accepted."

The custody core screamed into the shielded case as Sen slammed it shut.

The hostile feed died.

Cargo Two corrected burn with two seconds to spare.

Sivek realized he had been standing with both hands pressed so hard to the scheduling plate that his palms hurt.

"Cargo group entering envelope," he said.

Morrow lower. Ashkey upper. Cargo ships heavy and slow. The gate opened less beautifully than before. It did not matter. Morrow tagged lower drift. Ashkey held upper. Vane Three complained. Vane One hissed. Buffer Three stayed dark. The cargo group entered.

One ship through.

Two.

Three.

Cargo Two, late from hesitation, scraped the edge, lost a container rack, and still made the corridor.

Four.

The gate shuddered and closed behind them.

Confirmation arrived after a delay that felt personally malicious.

CARGO GROUP THROUGH.
MATERIAL LOSS: PARTIAL.
SCRUBBER MEMBRANES INTACT.
MEDICAL RESERVE INTACT.
REPAIR CERAMICS PARTIAL LOSS.
CREWS ALIVE.
DAX.

Control did not erupt.

Scrubber Works did not erupt.

Blue Vault did not erupt.

They were all too tired for eruption.

Sivek lowered his hands from the plate.

Om's voice entered, quiet. "Officer Hale."

"Magistrate."

"Your ruling is recorded."

"Yes."

"So are the objections."

"Good."

He looked at the board. First group through. Auxiliary Three through. Passenger group through. Cargo group through. Kestrel damaged. Halcyon alive. Blue Vault cores contained. Vale mask exposed but not yet fully traced. Seventeen confirmed dead, and perhaps more once Auxiliary Three finished counting.

No green.

Never green.

He opened the public channel for the shortest statement of his career.

"Cargo group is through. Crews alive. Critical supplies intact. Kestrel Gate is not generally open. Investigation continues. We will publish damage and casualty records when verified."

He almost ended it there.

Then he added, because sequences mattered, and witness before record mattered, and record before judgment mattered if civilization could afford the time:

"Tonight's success does not erase tonight's dead."

He closed the channel.

In the quiet after, Sivek finally understood that command did not make him clean. It only made him unable to pretend he had not touched the dirt.


Chapter 38: The Gate Falls Sideways

The first sound after success was metal cooling.

Kestrel Gate had never been a quiet machine. Even in idle state, its vane housings carried a low structural hum that old pilots learned to feel in their teeth. The hum meant the field banks were balanced, the heat ladders were walking their load into the dark, and the great ring between Kestrel and Halcyon had remembered how to be a road instead of a monument.

After the cargo group cleared, the hum changed.

It thinned.

Lena heard it through Morrow's hull, through the medical wrap tight across her ribs, through the fatigue that had turned her hands into borrowed tools. Sann had the tug on station-keeping, technically in command, which meant Lena was supposed to sit, advise, and not bleed into avionics.

"That is not cooldown," Sann said.

"No," Lena said.

Fei, still strapped behind them with a cracked visor and three arguments pending, leaned toward the forward board. "Tell me it is the usual bad noise."

"It is a new bad noise."

"I dislike innovation."

Lena moved her left hand before she remembered not to. Pain tightened around her side. Sann saw it and did not comment, which was one reason Lena had trusted her with the chair.

Morrow's passive skin painted the gate in false colors. Vane One and Vane Three were still shedding heat within ugly but survivable margins. Buffer Three remained dark. The envelope should have collapsed cleanly after cargo exit, folding back into storage geometry and dumping residual charge into the ladder banks.

Instead the outer return line bent across the ring like a broken rib.

Not inward.

Sideways.

"Control," Sann said. "Morrow. We have lateral drift in the return line."

Control took half a breath too long to answer.

Then Doss: "Confirmed. Field vane displacement on the west arc. We are reading shear at seven percent and climbing."

Vela came in over the same channel, voice stripped flat. "Do not call it displacement. Displacement has a home address. This is a partial topological fall."

Fei made a quiet, offended sound. "You people need shorter words for dying."

Lena kept her eyes on the false-color ring. "It is not dying. It is looking for a cheaper shape."

"And if it finds one?"

"Then Kestrel stops being a gate and becomes whatever the war manuals pretended could not happen twice."

Sann's hands steadied on the controls.

On the external visual feed, the gate looked almost normal to anyone who had not spent a career reading machines by the way they failed. Its broken light had faded from amber-red to a tired gold along the near arc. The distant stars still showed through the center. Kestrel Station still rotated beyond it with all its windows intact. Halcyon Ring remained a thin lit curve on the far-side relay projection, alive because cargo membranes and medical reserve had gone through.

Success had a way of looking like permission to stop watching.

Lena enlarged the west arc.

There.

The field was not collapsing into the ring. It was sliding through an angle that did not belong to local space, dragging a smear of telemetry with it. The old Compact manuals had a polite name for that. Transverse persistence. A postwar correction had added a less polite note: if observed, evacuate field-side assets and assume all automation is lying until manually witnessed.

"Morrow to Control," Lena said, taking the channel without asking Sann. "The gate has not closed. It has gone transverse on the west arc. Stop all auto-cooldown."

Doss answered, "Auto-cooldown already running."

"Then stop it."

"Manual stop requires control quorum. Sivek is in Blue Vault chain lock and Om is in evidence preservation."

Sann glanced at her.

Lena said, "Vela."

"Already here," Vela replied. "I can sever trust watch relay, but that only stops public command laundering. Cooldown logic is local. Old local. The sort of old that thinks being old means being right."

"Doss, what is it doing?"

"It sees thermal load and field remnant. It is flushing."

Lena closed her eyes for one second, saw the geometry, opened them. "If it flushes into transverse persistence, it will push the fall through every hot pipe coupled to the vane."

Doss said nothing for a moment.

Then: "That includes coolant manifolds."

"Yes."

Fei's voice dropped. "The passenger docks?"

"Some of them."

"Halcyon?"

"No. Local."

That did not make it better. It only narrowed the shape of the harm.

The public channel, still open in low-priority witness mode after Sivek's statement, began to populate with silence. Not speech. Silence had signatures. People staying on channel because going away felt like abandonment. Control staff breathing at microphones. Maintenance leads waiting for orders they knew would be late. Dock families who had learned too much route geometry in one night and could now tell when experts were afraid.

Tamsa broke it.

"Say where," she said.

"West arc coolant ladders," Doss answered. "Secondary passenger docks. Blue Vault heat sink if the isolation wall cross-couples."

Maro said, "Blue Vault is sealed."

Vela: "Sealed is a legal word with metal attached. It is not magic."

Tamsa: "Who has hands?"

A dozen maintenance boards lit at once.

Lena watched the transverse line brighten.

"No mass crew movement under that arc," she said. "You will not outrun a sideways dump if the manifold takes it."

Tamsa's response came immediately. "Nobody asked to outrun it. I asked who had hands."

Sivek returned to the command channel sounding older by hours. "Kestrel Control. All departments hold. We are assessing."

"No," Tamsa said.

The channel froze around the word.

Sivek did not rebuke her. That told Lena he had learned something, or that he was too tired for the old reflex.

Tamsa continued, "Assessment is what people call looking at a fire while deciding if the person with the extinguisher has the right badge. We need valve maps."

Doss said, "Valve maps are partial. Some of that section predates the station rebuild."

"Then we use people who work there."

"Those people are under evacuation."

"Those people are listening."

They were. One by one, local channels identified themselves.

Dock Nine scrubber crew, clear of corridor but with visual on west ladder access.

Nursery Three maintenance, sealed behind fire door, two techs and one apprentice.

Cold storage crew, five people, one pressure suit, bad knee, can move if someone tells us where not to die.

Jori Pell, apprentice, medical restriction ignored by everyone who knows me.

Lena opened her eyes wider despite the burn in them.

Tamsa said, "Jori, no."

The boy's voice came back thin, angry, and too young for the night. "Then do the math without me."

Control gave him a location ping. Not Blue Vault. Not command. He was in an auxiliary maintenance blister below Dock Nine, exactly where no injured apprentice should have been and exactly where the manual valve tree still touched the west arc.

"How did he get there?" Maro asked.

Tamsa said, "He has legs and everybody has been busy saving civilization."

"Supervisor," Sivek said, "remove him."

"With what crew?"

Sivek had no answer.

Lena pulled the valve map into Morrow's board. It arrived incomplete, three rebuild layers stacked in different notation. She almost laughed. Not because it was funny. Because after all the lies, after the false audits and dead keys and custody masks, Kestrel might still be killed by plumbing nobody had updated in a common file.

"Doss," she said, "send raw scans. All layers."

"They are contradictory."

"Good. Contradictions are information."

The scans came. Sann routed them to Lena's side board without being asked. Fei, who had no formal gate certification and had more sense than some people who did, began reading labels aloud.

"West ladder A takes heat from Vane Three primary."

"Not anymore," Lena said. "That line was capped after the war."

"It says active."

"It lies."

Doss: "Confirmed. Capped. We never reconciled the drawing."

Tamsa: "I am adding that to the board after we live."

"West ladder B," Fei said. "Secondary passenger docks. Dock Nine. Nursery Three. Blue Vault sink via isolation wall, conditional."

"Conditional on what?"

"Manual bypass state."

Vela swore softly. It was the first time Lena had heard her sound frightened rather than furious.

Sivek said, "Explain."

"Blue Vault heat sink was designed to borrow west ladder capacity during archive burns," Vela said. "If the bypass is open and the gate dumps sideways through B, Blue Vault remains cool while the passenger docks take the hit. If it is closed, Blue Vault heats. The old designers chose archive preservation over local lives by default."

Maro: "Can we close it?"

Doss: "Not from central. Blue Vault isolation severed remote after the custody core event."

Tamsa: "Where is the manual?"

Doss hesitated.

Jori answered. "Blister Nine."

Nobody spoke.

The transverse line brightened again. Morrow's hull sensors caught a static flutter, like dust striking a window that did not exist.

Lena said, "Time?"

Doss: "Six minutes before auto-flush enters west ladder."

Vela: "Less if the fall deepens."

Sivek: "Options."

Lena looked at the maps. There were always options. Most of them were names for different losses.

"Option one," she said. "Leave bypass open. Blue Vault stays cool. Dock Nine and Nursery Three take thermal surge. People die."

Maro's breath caught.

"Option two. Close bypass. Blue Vault heats. Custody cores are in shield cases but not rated for uncontrolled thermal rise. Evidence could degrade. Hostile templates might burst if containment fails."

Vela said, "And if they burst?"

"Command channel infection risk. Legal custody mess. Maybe worse."

Maro: "Evidence can die before children."

Vela: "Evidence that contains a hostile command continuity cannot simply die. It may leak on the way."

Tamsa: "Option three."

Lena stared at the lines until the old lessons gave way to worker math. Pipes were route geometry for fluids. Lies were route geometry for power. Everything moved through the easiest passage unless someone changed the shape.

"Close Blue Vault bypass," she said. "Open the dead capped A spur as a dump into the old cargo sink. Then throttle B manually so Dock Nine gets heat below flash. It will damage the cargo sink and maybe crack the west arc coupling."

Doss: "The cargo sink is dry."

"Then make it wet."

"With what?"

Jori said, "Fire reserve."

Tamsa's voice sharpened. "Jori."

"It is right there. Blister Nine has manual reserve feed to the old cargo sink because nobody removed the red handle after the rebuild."

Doss: "That handle is not on the map."

"I know. I put my crutch against it when I was hiding from Med."

Lena wanted to close the channel, climb through vacuum, and drag him back herself. She also knew, with a pilot's cold arithmetic, that the boy had just found the only path through.

Sivek said, "We cannot authorize an injured minor to perform critical manual operations in a hazard zone."

Om's voice entered, dry as paper. "You can record that we failed to prevent his presence. You can authorize adults to assist and instruct him to retreat if possible. You cannot make the actual valve vanish by disliking who is nearest to it."

Tamsa said, "I hate every word of that."

"So ordered," Om said.

Sivek took one breath. "Jori Pell, Kestrel Control acknowledges your location. You are to remain behind pressure cover unless Supervisor Reet or Pilot Ors gives a direct movement instruction. Adult crew nearby, identify."

Cold storage answered. "Renn and Pavo. We can reach Blister Nine in three minutes if Corridor C is not hot."

Doss: "Corridor C is cold for two minutes."

Tamsa: "Move. Do not run blind. Jori, tell me what you see without touching anything."

Jori's breathing rasped. "Manual wall. Three wheels. One red handle. Two yellow locks. Old label says cargo sink flood. New label pasted over it says obsolete."

"Of course it does," Tamsa said.

Lena's board showed the auto-flush line walking toward commitment. Five minutes.

From Morrow, the gate looked beautiful in the way frozen disasters sometimes did. Light rippled along the sideways fall. For a moment Lena could see the route not as a corridor but as a wound trying to decide whether to scar or keep opening.

Sann said quietly, off channel, "You should not be awake."

"I know."

"You are shaking."

"I know that too."

"Then give me the flying."

Lena almost said there was no flying left.

Then the west arc shuddered, and Morrow's tether alarm flashed.

Sann was already moving. She rolled Morrow down relative to the gate plane, not away, using the tug's mass to keep a sensor lock on the transverse smear. Ashkey mirrored high without instruction. Pellam's voice appeared, grim and brisk.

"If the ring spits, we can tow one small thing badly."

Fei said, "That is the most optimistic sentence I have heard today."

Lena stayed with the map.

Four minutes.

Renn and Pavo reached Blister Nine. Their suit cams showed Jori crouched behind a pressure rib, face pale, one arm strapped to his chest because Medical had not been wrong about everything. He looked smaller than his testimony had made him.

Tamsa's voice changed. Not softer. More exact.

"Jori, you are not proving anything. You already did that. Now you are hands for a map. Understand?"

"Yes, Supervisor."

"Renn, take the red handle. Pavo, yellow locks. Jori reads labels and keeps his feet behind the rib."

"Yes."

Vela cut in. "Before you close Blue Vault bypass, Maro needs chain confirmation on the shield cases. If we cook them, the defense will claim evidence tampering."

Maro said, "Let them. I confirm the shield cases are physically sealed, public-witnessed, and subordinate to preservation of life."

Ilya's voice followed, hoarse. "House Ceyr waives all archive priority claims. The dead do not get cooling before the living."

Om: "Recorded."

Three minutes.

Doss: "We need sequence. Close bypass first or flood cargo sink first?"

Lena tasted copper. "Flood first. If the sink is dry when the bypass closes, heat spikes into Blue Vault before A spur takes load. Flood sink. Crack A spur. Close Blue Vault bypass. Throttle B."

"Crack A spur how?"

Jori said, "Yellow wheel with wire around it."

"Do not touch wire," Tamsa said.

"I know."

"Say it."

"I will not touch the wire."

"Good."

Two minutes.

The red handle did not move.

Renn grunted. Pavo put both gloved hands over Renn's and hauled. The handle gave one centimeter and stopped.

"Painted shut," Pavo said.

Tamsa laughed once, a sharp and dangerous sound. "After this I am banning paint from life-support infrastructure."

Lena said, "Jori, look at the hinge. Is there a lock pin?"

"No. Rust ridge. The handle is jammed against the new label plate."

"Can you pry the plate?"

"With what?"

Silence.

Then Jori lifted his crutch into camera view.

Tamsa said, "Absolutely not."

Jori said, "It is a metal stick, Supervisor."

"It is your metal stick."

"Then it is already mine to use."

One minute forty.

Om said, "Supervisor Reet."

"I heard."

Tamsa's breath came through the channel once, hard. "Renn, you hold him. Pavo, you take the crutch. Jori does not lever with his injured side. If anyone slips, you all stop."

"Understood," Renn said.

Jori did not argue. That was how Lena knew he was frightened.

The crutch wedged under the label plate. Pavo leaned. The plate bent with a shriek that carried through suit audio and into every public channel still listening.

The red handle moved.

"Flood open!" Renn shouted.

Old cargo sink sensors woke one by one, confused after years of being told they were obsolete. Fire reserve poured into a dry heat maze under the west arc. Steam bloomed in the diagnostics. Alarms multiplied.

"A spur," Lena said.

Pavo cut the wire.

Jori shouted, "He touched the wire!"

"It is fine," Pavo said.

The feed snapped white. Not fine.

For half a second every sensor on Blister Nine vanished.

Tamsa said his name once. "Pavo."

Static answered.

Then Jori's voice came back, high and furious. "He is down but breathing. Renn has him. The yellow wheel kicked. It is open half."

Doss: "A spur taking load."

Lena: "Close bypass."

Renn, dragging Pavo with one arm, slammed the second wheel. It resisted, then spun too fast. Blue Vault thermal link went from green to gray.

Vela said, "Bypass closed."

The sideways fall hit the coolant ladder.

Kestrel rang.

Not audibly, not at first. The ring passed through hull and suit and bone as if the station had become a bell too large for sound. Morrow's boards flashed red. Ashkey rolled hard. Sann swore and caught attitude. Fei's restraints locked with a crack.

On the map, heat rushed into the flooded cargo sink, turned the new water to violence, and spent itself breaking old metal nobody had trusted enough to remove.

Dock Nine rose to amber.

Nursery Three rose to amber.

Blue Vault rose to amber.

No red.

For six seconds.

Then B ladder spiked again.

"Throttle," Lena said.

Nobody answered from Blister Nine.

The ring continued to shiver. Morrow's sensor mast lost calibration. Lena reached for the board and Sann slapped her hand away without looking.

"You do geometry," Sann said. "I do tug."

Lena forced herself back to the thermal map.

"Jori," Tamsa said, voice low enough to cut. "Report."

"Renn is on Pavo. I can reach the blue wheel."

"No."

"It is two steps."

"You are behind cover."

"Dock Nine is at amber plus."

Lena watched the numbers climb. "Tamsa."

"Do not ask me."

"I am not asking. I am telling you the wheel must move."

The public channel heard all of it. That was the cruelty and the mercy of the night. No private bravery. No private blame.

Tamsa said, "Jori Pell, you are going to take two steps to the blue wheel with your good hand on the rail. You are going to turn it one quarter counterclockwise. Not more. Then you are going back behind the rib even if every alarm in Kestrel calls you rude names. Repeat."

Jori repeated it exactly.

His suit cam tilted. Two steps looked like a journey. His breath snagged with each one. His good hand found the rail. His injured side stayed stiff. He reached the blue wheel.

"Quarter," Tamsa said.

"It is stiff."

"Of course it is stiff."

"I hate this station."

"Later."

The wheel moved.

B ladder fell from amber plus to amber.

"Back," Tamsa said.

Jori took one step.

The transverse fall snapped.

Kestrel Gate vanished from Morrow's board as a coherent object and reappeared a meter wrong.

Not physically, not in the simple sense. The ring remained where it had been bolted. The field geometry, the usable road, the thing pilots called Kestrel when they were being sentimental, dropped sideways into a new resting state. The center no longer aligned with the old corridor. The return line folded into a crescent along the west arc and stayed there, dim and stubborn.

Every navigation table in Control went invalid at once.

Jori fell.

Tamsa shouted his name.

The suit cam showed ceiling, steam, Renn's boot, then Jori's face as he dragged himself behind the pressure rib with his good arm and an expression of pure insult.

"I am behind cover," he said.

Dock Nine held below red.

Nursery Three held below red.

Blue Vault held below red.

The gate did not come back to normal.

It did something better than killing them.

It stayed wrong.

For a long time, nobody tried to make victory out of that.

Then Sivek spoke. "Kestrel Control records partial collapse into transverse resting state. General transit suspended. Emergency thermal cascade prevented. Medical to Blister Nine. Damage teams hold until field survey clears."

Maro added, "Evidence chain remains intact."

Tamsa said, "People chain first."

"People chain first," Maro agreed.

Lena looked at the crescent of impossible light clinging to the west arc. The old route was gone. Not destroyed exactly. Bent out of the shape the Compact knew how to certify. Kestrel had not reopened. It had survived by becoming a harder question.

Fei leaned back against her restraints and closed her eyes. "Can we call that through?"

Sann kept Morrow steady. "No."

Lena watched the wrong gate.

"We call it held," she said.


Chapter 39: Jori's Valve

Medical reached Blister Nine eleven minutes after the sideways fall.

For Jori, the eleven minutes lasted long enough for every adult on the channel to become unbearable in a different way.

Renn kept saying, "Stay awake."

Pavo, who had regained consciousness and immediately tried to apologize for touching the charged wire, kept saying, "Sorry."

Tamsa kept asking short questions that were not short because she lacked imagination. They were short because if she let one larger feeling into her voice, Jori thought the whole blister might crack from it.

"Name."

"Jori Pell."

"Where are you?"

"Blister Nine."

"What did you turn?"

"Blue wheel. One quarter. I went back behind cover."

"Good."

He did not feel good. His shoulder had become a planet with bad weather. His injured hand pulsed under the strap. He had lost his crutch to the red handle operation and could see it lying bent under the obsolete label plate, which seemed unfair. He had liked that crutch better than several people.

The blister smelled like hot metal, old dust, and the sharp chemical fog of fire reserve boiled through dry pipe. The emergency lights strobed slow amber. Not red. Amber was supposed to be reassuring. Adults liked amber because they could call it caution instead of fear.

Jori hated amber now.

"Supervisor," he said.

"Here."

"Did it work?"

Tamsa did not answer quickly. That was one of the things he trusted about her.

"It worked enough," she said. "Dock Nine is alive. Nursery Three is alive. Blue Vault did not cook. The gate is wrong."

"Wrong how?"

"Pilot word or maintenance word?"

"Maintenance."

"Bent."

Jori considered this. "Can bent be fixed?"

"Sometimes."

"Can this?"

"That is a future question."

He wanted to ask if future questions were better than present questions, but Renn pressed two fingers under his jaw seal to check pulse through the suit membrane and told him not to talk so much.

On the public low channel, people were still listening. Jori could tell by the little witness count pulsing in the corner of his suit display. The number had dropped when the gate held. Some people had gone to find relatives. Some had gone to cry where no one would make a record of it. Enough remained that every breath in Blister Nine felt like it belonged partly to strangers.

He had testified earlier because Maro asked questions like each answer had weight. He had turned the valve because the valve was there. Neither thing had felt heroic. Testifying had made him nauseated. Turning the wheel had hurt. He suspected hero stories were invented by people standing farther from the wheel.

The hatch opened with a manual clank.

Two medics entered first. Then a station safety officer. Then, impossibly, Tamsa Reet herself in a pressure hood, face slick with sweat, one hand bandaged from Blue Vault and the other carrying a folded splint.

Renn said, "Supervisor, you are supposed to be in Blue Vault."

"Blue Vault has enough people being grave at it."

"Your hand."

"Still attached."

She crouched beside Jori. Her expression was exactly the one she used when someone had installed a filter backwards and hoped she would not notice.

"Apprentice Pell."

"Supervisor."

"You ignored medical restriction."

"Yes."

"You left assigned recovery."

"Yes."

"You entered a hazard blister during a gate event."

"There was a valve."

"I noticed."

He tried not to shake. The effort made him shake more.

Tamsa put her unbandaged hand on the floor beside his boot, not touching him until the medic nodded. "You also reported your position, followed sequence under instruction, repeated safety orders, and returned behind cover after completing the operation."

"Mostly."

"Mostly is going in the report."

"Am I fired?"

"You are an apprentice. I cannot fire you from being annoying."

He almost laughed. It came out wrong, more like a cough. The medic did not approve.

Tamsa's face shifted then, just for him, just enough. "You are alive. Pavo is alive. Renn is alive. The children in Nursery Three are alive. That does not make what happened acceptable."

"I know."

"Do you?"

He looked away toward the blue wheel. It sat one quarter open, a dumb little circle on a wall, doing nothing dramatic now that everyone had survived. He had expected it to look important. Instead it looked like maintenance.

"I know I was scared," he said.

"Good."

"Good?"

"Scared people who admit they are scared can still count turns on a valve. People pretending not to be scared skip steps."

The medic cut his sleeve. Cool air touched sweat. Pain sharpened, then blurred under an injector.

Jori's eyes filled without permission. He kept them on the blue wheel because crying at a valve seemed less embarrassing than crying at Tamsa.

The channel crackled. Maro's voice came through, careful. "Supervisor Reet, does Apprentice Pell consent to a brief evidence question when medical permits?"

Tamsa looked at the medic.

The medic said, "One question. If he wants."

Jori said, "I want."

Tamsa said, "You can say no."

"I know."

Maro entered on narrow-band video, his face gray with exhaustion and Blue Vault light. "Jori, did anyone instruct you before the event to go to Blister Nine?"

"No."

"Did anyone tell you that a valve there would be needed?"

"No. I went because I thought they would forget the old manual wall. Then everything happened."

"Why did you think that?"

"Because everyone forgets old manual walls until something breaks."

Maro closed his eyes briefly. "Recorded. Thank you."

"Was that the question?"

"Yes."

"That was a bad question."

Tamsa made a sound that might have been a laugh if anyone had slept.

Maro opened his eyes. "Why?"

"You asked like somebody might have planted me."

"I had to exclude it."

"I know. But still bad."

"Fair."

The video closed.

The medics lifted Jori. Pain flared despite the injector. He made a noise he would later deny. Tamsa walked beside the stretcher through Corridor C, where the walls sweated heat and paint had blistered in long sad curls. Cold storage crew lined the passage, not cheering, which he appreciated. One of them touched two fingers to the bulkhead as he passed. Others copied it.

"What is that?" Jori whispered.

Tamsa glanced back. "People invent gestures when words are too large."

"Make them stop."

"No."

"Supervisor."

"You do not get to control every consequence."

He let the ceiling move over him.

Past Corridor C, Dock Nine opened into a crowd held back by pressure tape and three exhausted safety officers. Families from the secondary passenger docks had been cleared from immediate hazard but not from fear. A woman in a nursery smock was crying without sound. Two children held each other in the practiced way of station children who knew not to drift underfoot during emergencies. Someone said Jori's name.

Then more people said it.

Not loud. That was worse.

Jori closed his eyes.

Tamsa leaned close. "Breathe. In four, out four."

"Do not supervisor me in public."

"I am literally your supervisor."

"Unfortunately."

"Breathe."

He breathed.

In Control, the gate boards were being rebuilt from scratch.

Lena watched from Morrow because Medical had threatened to sedate her if she tried to transfer back. Sann had moved the tug to a stable observation point below the old plane. Ashkey hung above. Between them, the crescent of the transverse resting state glowed faintly along Kestrel's west arc, like the gate had tucked part of itself into a pocket and refused to explain the trick.

Every automatic route solution failed.

That was expected. Expected did not mean comfortable.

Doss had stopped trying to make the main board pretty. He layered raw field returns, thermal status, eyewitness maintenance reports, and old map fragments into one ugly public display. Vela approved of the ugliness. Om approved of the public part. Sivek approved of neither visibly, which Lena took as progress.

"The old aperture is gone," Doss said.

"Define gone," Om said.

"No certified transit corridor remains aligned with the ring center."

"That is not gone. That is uncertified."

Vela said, "Careful, Magistrate. Words like that are how committees convince themselves to try dying."

Om did not look offended. "Then record both. No certified transit corridor. Physical field remnant persists in transverse state. Further operation prohibited pending witnessed model."

"Witnessed by whom?" Sivek asked.

The room answered by looking at all the people who had once not been allowed in the room.

Tamsa arrived on the Control feed from the medical corridor, hood off, bandaged hand lifted to keep swelling down. "Do not look at me. I am busy making sure your valve hero does not become a poster."

"How is he?" Lena asked.

"Hurt. Furious. Alive."

"Good."

"He wants everyone to stop saying his name."

Vela said, "That may not be available."

"Then make it available."

The channel quieted.

Tamsa continued, "He turned a valve because the adults built a system where the nearest working part was next to a child who knew the old wall better than command did. The report will say that before it says brave."

Sivek nodded once. "Yes."

It was a small word. From him, not small.

Maro joined from Blue Vault, where the shield cases now sat in a ring of portable coolers and legal seals. Ilya Ceyr was visible behind him, wrapped in a shock blanket, hair pasted to her face, eyes fixed on nothing Lena could see.

"We have preliminary custody stability," Maro said. "All nine cores contained. The last core's attempt to use living Ceyr authority is recorded with embodied contradiction and public witness."

Om: "Defense challenge?"

"Many. None clean."

"Good."

Ilya looked up. "Do not call it good."

Maro turned.

She came closer to the pickup. "It is admissible. It is necessary. It is not good."

Om said, "Correction accepted."

Ilya swallowed. "House Ceyr will attempt to survive this by offering cooperation."

Sivek said, "You are House Ceyr."

"I am one person who inherited a machine built to make people confuse ownership with oxygen." Her voice did not tremble. That made it worse. "By morning, cousins with cleaner sleeves will say I acted under duress. They will say emergency custody remains the only responsible option. They will say local workers saved the night because House investment preserved the infrastructure they used."

Tamsa's smile had no warmth. "I look forward to hearing that in public."

"So do I," Ilya said.

Lena believed her, which was inconvenient.

Fei, quiet for too long, said from Morrow, "Before morning, can somebody tell the convoy the gate fell sideways but politely?"

Doss rubbed both hands over his face. "Convoy channel is stable. First group and cargo group are holding at Halcyon-side emergency anchor. Auxiliary Three is under repair. They are requesting Kestrel status."

Sivek straightened. "Send: Kestrel Gate survived in restricted transverse state. No return transit authorized. Halcyon supply offload has priority. Casualty and damage reports pending."

Lena said, "Add that the route may not match previous charts."

Doss typed.

"Add," Tamsa said, "that if anyone on the far side receives a Ceyr custody order, they should laugh first and authenticate second."

Om's eyebrow rose.

Tamsa sighed. "Fine. Omit laugh."

Sivek said, "Authenticate all custody orders against public source clocks. Reject dead-key commands."

"Less satisfying," Tamsa said. "More legal."

The message went.

For the first time since the rehearsal pulse, no immediate alarm replaced the last one.

It left room for other things.

On the casualty board, seventeen remained confirmed. Pavo moved from critical unknown to serious stable. Jori moved from missing from medical to under treatment. Three passengers from Auxiliary Three were added to surgical watch. A maintenance tech from Vane Three, whose name Lena had heard twice and not remembered because the night had been full of names, moved from surgical watch to confirmed dead.

Eighteen.

No one spoke for a while.

The gate crescent glowed.

Lena thought of the first time she had touched Kestrel's route under supervision, before the war, before inquiries and scapegoats and pilots learning to hate what they loved. Her instructor had told her that gates were promises expressed as machines. She had believed it then. Later she had decided that was sentimental nonsense. Tonight, watching a bent promise hold just enough shape for people to keep breathing, she was less certain.

Sann broke the silence in Morrow with a practical mercy. "Pilot Ors, Medical requests your transfer."

"Medical can wait."

"They said you would say that."

"Then why ask?"

"They also said to tell you Jori Pell has accepted treatment and is being less stubborn than you."

Lena closed her eyes. "That is manipulative."

"Yes."

Fei unstrapped halfway, winced, and strapped back in. "Go. Some of us would like to complain about you while you are not on channel."

Lena looked once more at the wrong gate.

"Sann."

"I have Morrow."

"Pellam."

Ashkey answered, "Upper watch holds."

"Doss."

"Raw board stays public."

"Tamsa."

"I will make sure nobody turns the child into a statue before breakfast."

"Maro."

"Evidence remains subordinate to life."

"Vela."

"I will keep old machines from pretending they are neutral."

"Sivek."

He took longer. "I will not reopen a route because a schedule wants to be obeyed."

That was the answer she needed.

Lena released her board.

The absence of it hit harder than she expected. Without the numbers under her fingers, she was simply a tired pilot in a damaged tug with blood dried under one sleeve and a gate outside that no longer knew how to be straight.

As Sann began the transfer burn, Halcyon sent its first low-bandwidth public image back through the surviving relay.

Not the convoy. Not the cargo. Not the captains.

Nursery workers unloading scrubber membranes under temporary lamps.

A line of people passing sealed medical packs hand to hand.

Captain Dax standing beside a ruptured container rack, face streaked black, holding up a slate with three words written large enough for a bad camera.

WE ARE HERE.

In Control, in Blue Vault, in Scrubber Works, in Dock Nine, in the little medical bay where Jori Pell pretended not to cry while a doctor reset his shoulder, people watched the image arrive.

The gate had fallen sideways.

The route was bent.

The work had not ended.

But Halcyon had hands on the supplies, and Kestrel had hands on the valves, and for one damaged hour after all the elegant lies failed, that was enough to keep the dark from winning.


Chapter 40: Convoy Through Fire

Halcyon received the convoy as if catching a falling city with bare hands.

The first ship came in without forward lights.

Its running boards had been stripped dark by the transit scrape, and the hull paint along its portside cargo ribs had cooked to a dull gray that looked older than the ship. Docking radar saw it before people did. Then the outer skin cameras found the broken shape crossing Halcyon's near-field beacon lane, heavy and slow, leaking bright flecks of frozen coolant that spun away like cheap stars.

On the ring, nobody cheered.

They had used all the simple feelings before the ships arrived.

The crew in Dock North-Four stood behind pressure screens with hands tucked under safety straps, waiting for the hull to settle into clamp range. Half of them were licensed dockworkers. Half of them were people who had learned a clamp sequence twelve minutes ago because the licensed dockworkers were elsewhere or dead tired or busy keeping oxygen moving through corridors that should have been replaced three funding cycles earlier.

Tamsa watched from Kestrel through the shared relay, one hand wrapped, one eye gritty, her own air smelling faintly of burnt insulation. She had no authority at Halcyon. That had never stopped anyone from asking her to solve things.

"North-Four, your lower left clamp is drifting," she said.

The Halcyon dock lead, a woman named Oret with a shaved scalp and a voice like she had been born unimpressed, answered without turning from her board. "I see it."

"Your board says green."

"My eyes say clamp head is wobbling."

"Use your eyes."

"Already doing that, Supervisor Who Is Not My Supervisor."

"Good."

The ship rolled three degrees wrong.

Oret slapped manual correction before the docking software could politely murder everyone by waiting for a better solution. The clamp arm caught the ship at the scarred cargo rib instead of the nominal socket. Metal shrieked. The dock shook. A child somewhere beyond the pressure line began to cry and was hushed quickly by someone who knew not to add sound to fear.

"Hold," Oret said.

The clamp held.

"First ship captured," Halcyon Control reported. "Hull breach sealed by emergency foam. Crew count pending."

Captain Dax did not answer from that ship. She was on the fourth, the one with the ruptured container rack and the slate. The first belonged to a cargo captain named Nerin Sao, whose face appeared on the relay ten seconds later under emergency light, one cheek cut from brow to jaw and both eyes focused.

"This is Sao. We have two critical, five walking wounded, one missing during transit. Cargo forward bay intact. Rear bay temperature unknown."

Oret asked, "Missing where?"

"External rack team. Tether snapped when the edge bit."

Nobody said the thing that meant. Halcyon did not have the luxury of naming every loss as it occurred. Names would come. For now there were seals, clamps, pressure checks, bodies to move, cargo to preserve, children to keep away from doors.

Tamsa leaned closer to the relay. "Sao, do you have scrubber membranes aboard?"

"Negative. We carried repair ceramics and pump housings."

"Then your people first. Ceramics second. Do not let anyone make a crate more urgent than a pulse."

Sao's cut cheek twitched. "Copy."

On another board, Nursery Three at Kestrel sent a message that all children remained below thermal injury threshold. The words should have comforted Tamsa. Instead they made her think of Jori's face behind the pressure rib, asking whether bent could be fixed.

Future question.

People used future questions to survive present ones. The trick was not letting the future become a place where accountability went to hide.

Second ship arrived too fast.

It was not the captain's fault. The gate had thrown it with a residual lateral shove that no chart had warned about because no chart knew Kestrel had become crooked. Halcyon's outer tugs caught it with two braking lines, both too light, both smoking under load.

"Release line two," Oret said.

Her deputy stared at her. "It will spin."

"It is already spinning. Release two, burn with one, catch on Dock East pylon."

"East pylon is cargo-only."

"Today it is a wall."

Line two released. The ship slewed. The remaining line went red. A tug pilot named Berez, who had not slept since before the first low-bandwidth warning, took the ugliest angle Tamsa had seen all night and turned it into survival. The ship's belly kissed the East pylon hard enough to shear three antenna clusters, then settled into the capture web.

"Second ship captured," Halcyon Control said. The controller sounded young. Everyone sounded young now.

Kestrel's public channel carried the update. People in corridors and pressure shelters heard it through wall speakers. Some lifted their heads. Some did not. The first ship captured had been proof that the convoy existed. The second proved that existence would not be clean.

Lena watched from Kestrel medical with a monitor clipped to her thumb and three separate warnings blinking over her bed. The doctor had left because an argument with a conscious pilot was less urgent than a decompression fracture in Surgical Two. This was a mistake in Lena's opinion. It allowed her to prop herself up and keep a tactical board open on a portable slate.

Sann stood in the doorway. "You are supposed to be unconscious."

"I negotiated."

"With whom?"

"Pain."

"Pain is not authorized to clear you for work."

"Pain has seniority."

Sann came in, took one look at the slate, and did not confiscate it. That was either mercy or surrender.

On the relay, Halcyon displayed the convoy as a scatter of damaged icons moving toward ports that had not been designed to receive so many hurt ships so quickly. The main ring had good farms, good public kitchens, excellent improvisers, and docks built for peacetime trickle, not postwar emergency arrival after a sabotaged gate event.

Captain Dax's voice entered the channel, rough and delayed. "Halcyon, this is Dax on Cargo Four. We are stable at outer anchor. We have the scrubber membranes, medical reserve, and two-thirds of repair ceramics. We also have fire in container spine C."

Oret said, "Can you vent?"

"If we vent, we lose half the membranes."

Tamsa: "Do not vent unless people are inside."

Dax: "No people inside spine C. Heat spread risk to membrane bay."

Lena enlarged Cargo Four's outline. The ship was too far from dock for station crews to board quickly. Its own internal fire team was down two people. The membranes were the difference between Halcyon's failing scrubber nurseries and another month of rationed breath.

That calculation made Lena angry enough to sit straighter.

"Dax," she said, joining the Halcyon channel. "What is between spine C and membrane bay?"

"Bulkhead rated for twenty minutes under ordinary fire."

"This is not ordinary."

"I noticed."

"Do you have repair foam?"

"Wrong kind. Hull foam, not thermal."

Tamsa cut in. "Medical reserve cases ship with cold packs."

Dax was silent for one second. "They are for organs and blood stabilizers."

"They are also phase-change mass. How many?"

"Thirty sealed."

Oret said, "If we crack medical reserve, we risk spoilage."

Tamsa's voice hardened. "If the membranes cook, Halcyon starts deciding who gets air in six days. If the medicine warms, we lose medicine. Bad, not equal."

Lena could hear people on both sides flinch at the arithmetic. Good. Arithmetic that did not hurt was usually hiding a body.

Dax said, "We can pack cold cases along the membrane bay wall."

"Do it," Tamsa said. "Log every case cracked. Medical gets the first replacement claim."

"Under whose authority?"

The question was not defiance. It was weariness. It was the voice of someone who knew that after the emergency, committees would crawl out of clean rooms and ask why labels had not been worshiped.

Tamsa looked at the relay as if she could stare across bent space into every future hearing.

"Mine," she said. "Halcyon can yell at me when it has air."

Oret said, "Halcyon concurs."

Sivek, from Kestrel Control, added, "Kestrel records concurrence. Emergency preservation of life-support priority."

Om: "Judicial witness recorded."

Dax breathed once. "Good. Breaking cold chain."

The fourth ship's crew formed a line in smoke masks, passing sealed medical cold cases into a corridor that had begun to sweat. On the same relay, first-ship medics hauled Sao's critical wounded through North-Four triage. On another, the second ship's captain argued with Oret about whether a cracked pylon counted as docking or impact. On another, Halcyon nursery workers stood beside newly arrived scrubber membranes and did not touch them until Tamsa told them which seals to check first.

Every screen showed a different version of through fire.

Not all fire made flame.

Third ship missed its first approach and had to come around under tug tow. Its navigator had lost vision in one eye during transit when a console burst. He kept apologizing over the open channel because shock made people courteous in strange places.

"Stop apologizing," Oret told him. "You are drifting less than my senior deputy did at his certification exam."

Her deputy said, "Rude."

"True."

The navigator laughed. Then cried. Then recited his approach marks perfectly while crying. Halcyon's tug crews brought him in.

Lena watched the icons settle one by one and felt the old pilot part of herself try to measure success by arrivals. First group through. Passenger auxiliary through. Cargo through. Ships captured. Supplies intact. Good enough. Done.

The rest of her knew better.

The rest of her saw the missing external rack worker. The cargo team with two critical. The ruptured container spine. The passengers in Auxiliary Three with burns from equipment that had not been rated for impossible angles. The Kestrel maintenance tech added to the dead. The way Sivek's public statement would be replayed by people looking for comfort and by lawyers looking for verbs.

Maro's feed opened from Blue Vault recovery. "Halcyon, Kestrel. I need casualty accounting separated from cargo accounting before any public release."

Oret snapped, "We are busy receiving ships."

"I know. That is why I am asking now, before relief officials merge them."

Tamsa understood immediately. "If the cargo comes in intact, they call the operation a success and tuck the dead into footnotes."

Maro said, "Yes."

Sivek did not argue.

That, too, was progress.

Halcyon's civic board had already begun displaying arrival categories: crew, passengers, critical goods, repair supplies, medical reserve. Tamsa watched an administrator move "missing" under "transit variance" and felt her mouth go dry with rage.

"No," she said.

The administrator froze on camera. He was a thin man with a formal collar and the startled expression of someone who had thought a shared board was less public than it was.

Oret turned slowly. "Put it back."

"I was only consolidating."

"Put missing under people."

"For dashboard clarity-"

Oret stood. "I have a cracked dock, four incoming hulls, and a cargo fire. If you make me walk over there for a word, I will become inefficient."

The category moved back.

Maro said, "Recorded."

The fifth ship arrived with Captain Dax aboard, finally close enough for a detailed image. Her slate message was gone. Her left sleeve was sealed with foam. Her hair had burned unevenly along one side. Behind her, crew members carried cold cases through smoke.

"Membrane bay temperature stabilizing," she reported. "Spine C fire contained, not extinguished. We lost two repair ceramic racks and one general stores pallet. Medical reserve compromised but usable if transferred in thirty minutes."

Oret said, "We have cold storage on Dock South-Two."

"South-Two is six minutes by cart."

Tamsa checked the Halcyon map. "Too slow. Use nursery cold bath."

Every Halcyon administrator on channel inhaled at once.

Oret's expression sharpened with delight and horror. "The nursery bath is sterile agriculture equipment."

"It is cold and clean."

"It is not rated for medical reserve."

"Neither is a burning cargo spine."

Dax said, "I will send medical lead to verify."

One administrator found courage. "Supervisor Reet, Halcyon nursery systems cannot be subordinated to Kestrel improvisation."

Tamsa leaned toward the pickup. "I am about to subordinate your sentence to triage. Oret?"

Oret said, "Nursery bath authorized. Log contamination risk. We clean it after."

The administrator looked toward someone off camera and found no rescue there. Perhaps Halcyon citizens had also learned something about boards that night.

Medical reserve moved.

The sixth ship, Cargo Two, came in last.

The ship that had hesitated under the hostile Ceyr order.

It carried the scrape like a confession along its starboard side. One container rack had torn free during transit and taken part of the outer service rail with it. The captain's voice on approach was clipped, professional, and so ashamed that Lena looked away from the video for a moment.

"Halcyon, Cargo Two requests capture. We have one pressure casualty, three injuries, partial load loss. We have accepted Dax command censure pending formal review."

Oret said, "You can be ashamed after clamps. Mark."

"Mark."

The ship entered capture web.

The damaged rail caught on the outer net.

The net tore.

"Burn down," Berez shouted.

"Cannot," Cargo Two answered. "Thruster cluster B is locked."

Lena's hand closed on the medical blanket.

Sann saw. "You are not flying."

"No."

"Say it like you mean it."

"I am not flying."

The words tasted terrible.

Sann took the slate from her and enlarged the feed for both of them. On screen, Cargo Two yawed toward a service mast that fed power to two docking arms and the temporary medical corridor. If it hit, Halcyon would not explode. Disasters did not have to be dramatic to matter. It would cripple the exact place where wounded were being moved.

Oret's board filled with suggested automatic responses. She ignored all of them.

"Berez, cut your tow and push from starboard."

"I am portside."

"Then become starboard."

"That is not how sides work."

"Use the ring curve."

Lena whispered, "Yes."

Sann looked at her.

"He's below the plane," Lena said. "If he burns under the mast shadow and lets the ring's relative rotation carry him, he can push before impact."

Sann opened channel. "Halcyon Tug Berez, Kestrel Morrow confirms Oret solution. Drop under mast shadow, ride curve, push starboard high."

Berez answered, "Who is this?"

"Pilot Sann, acting Morrow."

"Where is Ors?"

Lena said, because vanity was easier than helplessness, "In medical, being insufferable."

Berez laughed once. "Copy, insufferable confirmed."

The tug vanished behind the service mast. Cargo Two drifted closer. The mast's warning lights painted the hull red. On the civic board, someone had finally learned not to move categories during emergencies.

Berez reappeared where he needed to be, too close and too fast. His tug clipped the torn rail, crushed its own nose, and shoved.

Cargo Two missed the mast by less than a meter.

It hit the capture web broadside, tore half of it loose, and stopped.

The web held enough.

For three heartbeats, the channel contained only alarms.

Then Oret said, "Cargo Two captured. Berez, status."

"My tug is ugly."

"Your tug was ugly before."

"Mean."

"Status."

"Breathing. Nose gone. I want a raise."

"Denied until after inspection."

"Mean," he said again, but he was laughing, and this time other people laughed with him because the mast remained standing.

Lena leaned back against the medical pillow, shaking. Sann put the slate where she could see it but not touch it.

"You did not fly," Sann said.

"I noticed."

"You survived it."

"Do not be smug."

"I am being observant."

On Halcyon, the last cargo ship sealed to emergency umbilicals. The convoy was through fire, captured in pieces, scattered across docks, anchor points, and jury-rigged cold chains. The scrubber membranes were intact. Medical reserve was compromised but moving. Repair ceramics were partial. Pump housings had arrived. People had arrived. Not all people.

Captain Dax came onto the main channel. She stood in a corridor stained by smoke, with a medic working behind her and Oret visible on a side feed arguing with a pylon engineer.

"Kestrel, Halcyon, convoy arrival complete. We have six cargo hulls captured or anchored. Critical supplies offload underway. Preliminary convoy casualties: one confirmed dead during transit, one missing external, nine critical, twenty-six injured across cargo crews. Passenger auxiliary casualties still separate. Material loss includes repair ceramics, general stores, and one container rack unknown contents."

Maro said, "Unknown contents?"

Dax's face tightened. "Cargo Two lost a rack from Ceyr-insured manifest subset. The local captain has the manifest sealed under liability lock."

Every tired person on the channel found a little more attention.

Ilya Ceyr, still in Blue Vault recovery, looked up from her shock blanket. "Unlock it."

Dax said, "Requires Ceyr authority."

"I said unlock it."

"I need source clock and witness."

Ilya almost smiled. It was not a happy expression. "Good."

Maro opened evidence channel. Om joined. Sivek joined. Vela joined from the trust watch station, where she had wrapped herself in a thermal blanket without admitting cold existed.

Ilya placed her palm against the witness plate again. The skin was already bandaged. The plate accepted her current authority with a soft tone that made Lena dislike every elegant machine ever built.

"House Ceyr current authority orders immediate unsealing of Cargo Two lost rack manifest under public emergency witness," Ilya said. "No liability lock, archive privilege, or custody doctrine may delay disclosure."

The manifest opened.

For a moment nobody spoke because the line items made no sense.

Not scrubber membranes. Not medicine. Not repair ceramics.

Old routeholder ceremonial panels. Ceyr charter archives. A sealed executive habitat kit. Private legal hardcopy. A climate-controlled dining set coded heritage priority.

Tamsa read it twice.

Then she said, very softly, "They risked a cargo ship for furniture."

Ilya closed her eyes.

Dax said, "The rack was given stabilization priority in Cargo Two's internal trim. That is part of why they hesitated under the Ceyr order. Their captain believed loss would void coverage."

Sivek said, "Coverage for what?"

"For the ship. For crew death benefit. For family payouts."

There it was. Not simple cowardice. Never simple. A captain with crew families tied to insurance clauses had heard Ilya's hijacked face say liability protections were void. He had hesitated because rich people's property had been wired into poor people's survival.

Tamsa pressed her bandaged hand against the table until someone told her to stop.

"Name it correctly," she said.

Om did. "Coercive insurance dependency affecting emergency command compliance."

"Shorter."

"Extortion," Maro said.

Ilya opened her eyes. "Yes."

The word went through the channel like a pressure change.

On Halcyon, Oret looked at the captured ships, the torn web, the smoking cargo spine, the people being carried toward triage, and the supplies that would keep children breathing. "We are adding that to the public board."

Sivek said, "After verification."

Oret stared into the pickup.

He corrected himself. "With verification visible."

"Better."

The public board changed.

Under cargo loss, beside the missing rack, Halcyon listed:

CEYR HERITAGE PRIORITY RACK. NON-CRITICAL. LOST DURING TRANSIT.

Under command factors, Maro added:

INSURANCE LIABILITY THREAT CONTRIBUTED TO CARGO TWO HESITATION. EVIDENCE UNDER PUBLIC REVIEW.

No one cheered.

Somewhere on Halcyon, someone began to shout. Not panic. Anger.

Tamsa did not blame them.

Captain Dax stayed on channel. "We still have offload to complete."

"Yes," Tamsa said.

"I need people angry and working."

"That is my specialty."

Dax nodded as if receiving a formal command. "Then tell them."

Tamsa looked at the public channel count. Kestrel, Halcyon, convoy, dock shelters, medical corridors, Blue Vault, Control. Too many people. Not enough. Never enough.

She opened her mouth and found that fury alone would not do.

So she used the oldest maintenance rule she knew.

"Everybody breathe in," she said.

Nobody moved at first.

"I am serious. In."

Across Kestrel and Halcyon, people obeyed because the night had taught them stranger things than listening to Tamsa Reet. They breathed in. Bad air, filtered air, medical air, suit air, smoke-touched air, nursery air. Shared, rationed, argued-over air.

"Out," she said.

They breathed out.

"Again."

They did.

"Good. Now listen. They put old plates and rich chairs in a priority rack while your scrubber membranes rode beside fire. They tied a captain's family benefits to obedience. They used a dead man's keys and a living woman's face to tell cargo crews that law mattered more than life. We will put that on every board. We will make every hearing hear it. But right now, the membranes are here and not installed. The medical cases are here and not cold enough. The injured are here and still bleeding. Anger is useful if it picks up the right tool."

She leaned closer.

"Dock crews, finish capture checks. Nursery workers, membrane seals by lot number. Medical, shout if you need carriers. Administrators, write what workers tell you and stop inventing categories. Pilots, stay awake until someone with a pulse relieves you. Everybody else, clear corridors unless called. Supervisor says breathe. Supervisor also says move."

This time, there was no meme in it.

There was movement.

Halcyon shifted around the work. Crowds thinned from the wrong doors and thickened at the right ones. Cold carts rolled. Medics called names. Dock crews locked clamps and chalked unsafe edges. Nursery workers unpacked membranes with the reverence of priests and the precision of tired engineers. Administrators who had survived Oret's stare began writing words exactly as spoken.

Lena watched until the doctor returned and discovered she was awake.

"No," the doctor said.

"That is broad."

"It covers the situation."

Sann took the slate. "She did not fly."

"That is the lowest possible standard."

"I met it," Lena said.

The doctor injected something into the line at her elbow. "Meet sleep."

The relay blurred.

Before it went dark, Lena saw Halcyon's public board update one more time.

CONVOY ARRIVAL COMPLETE.
CRITICAL LIFE-SUPPORT SUPPLIES RECEIVED.
CASUALTIES COUNTING.
CAUSES UNDER PUBLIC WITNESS.

It was not comfort.

It was not victory.

It was a beginning that had come through fire with its hands full.


Chapter 41: Keva's Confession

Deputy Administrator Keva Lorn asked for a lawyer, a medic, and a public recorder in that order.

Magistrate Om gave her the recorder first.

The lawyer arrived seven minutes later, breathless and offended, with his collar misfastened and his tablet upside down. The medic arrived before him and declared that Keva was dehydrated, exhausted, bruised at one wrist, and competent to refuse sedation. Keva refused sedation. The lawyer objected to the order in which things were happening. Om informed him that the order of things had become a major theme of the evening and invited him to keep up.

They held the interview in Hearing Room Two because Hearing Room One still smelled of burned insulation from the emergency power switch. Hearing Room Two had no windows, four chairs, one public witness plate, and a wall display showing Kestrel Gate in its new crescent state. Someone had suggested turning the display off. Om had said no. Context was not prejudicial merely because it was embarrassing.

Keva sat with both hands flat on the table.

She had changed since Lena had last seen her in Sivek's office. Not morally. Physically. The polished administrative surface had cracked under the night. Her hair had escaped its clasp. Her left sleeve was torn at the cuff. There was a soot mark along her jaw she had either not noticed or chosen not to wipe away.

Maro sat across from her, recording chain visible. He had not slept. None of them had. Exhaustion made his face look more continuant than human, all sharp attention and no spare motion.

Sivek stood at the wall instead of sitting. Tamsa watched from a medical corridor with her bandaged hand propped on a crate. Lena had been drugged into two hours of unwilling sleep and now watched from a bed with the doctor standing behind her like a legal threat. Ilya Ceyr joined by narrow-band witness from Blue Vault recovery. Vela stayed in Control, surrounded by ugly boards and three cups of tea she had not drunk.

Om opened the record.

"Deputy Administrator Keva Lorn, you requested to provide sworn evidence concerning sabotage, relief funding, and external contacts related to Kestrel Gate reopening. Confirm."

Keva looked at her lawyer.

He whispered, "You may confirm the request without admitting specifics."

"I confirm," Keva said.

Om: "Do you understand that this proceeding is public-record delayed by six minutes for personal data redaction only, not content moderation?"

"Yes."

"Do you understand that your statements may expose you to criminal, civil, administrative, and Compact sanctions?"

"Yes."

"Do you understand that casualty operations remain ongoing and false or strategic testimony will be treated as active interference?"

Keva's mouth tightened. "Yes."

"Proceed."

The lawyer leaned toward her. "Deputy Administrator, I advise you to limit yourself to general structural concerns until we have reviewed discovery."

Keva did not look at him. "I have spent two years hiding murder inside general structural concerns."

The room went still.

Maro said, "Begin with relief funding."

Keva folded her hands. Unfolded them. Flat palms again.

"Halcyon Ring's scrubber failure curve became unrecoverable under ordinary allocation twenty-three months ago. We reported staged risk. Not terminal risk. That was my first falsification."

Tamsa's image did not move.

Maro asked, "Why?"

"Because terminal risk triggered Compact receivership review. Receivership would have frozen local discretionary accounts, including emergency procurement, including the quiet payments we were using to keep filter nurseries patched after three suppliers defaulted."

Sivek said, "I saw staged risk reports."

"Yes."

"You lied to my office."

"Yes."

He absorbed it with no visible flinch. That was the old Sivek. The new one said, "Continue."

Keva looked down at the table. "The lies made us eligible for bridge relief instead of receivership. Bridge relief required a reopening schedule. Kestrel's schedule became not just transportation, but proof of administrative viability. If the reopening failed under ordinary accident, we could request disaster supplement. If it failed because we had admitted terminal risk, Halcyon governance would be dissolved before supplement approval."

Om said, "You are describing motive for concealing fragility. Not sabotage."

"Yes."

Maro: "When did concealment become sabotage?"

Keva's eyes flicked to Ilya's image. "When House Ceyr offered to guarantee the supplement."

Ilya went very still.

Her lawyer, somewhere off Keva's feed, tried to interrupt despite not representing her. Om muted him without expression.

Keva continued. "Not formally. There was no signed guarantee. There were conversations through policy institutes, restoration councils, old routeholder associations. They said Kestrel's reopening needed a stable custodian. They said public confidence would follow old names. They said if a severe but contained failure demonstrated local incapacity, Compact emergency doctrine could put the gate under routeholder management long enough to secure relief."

Tamsa said, "Contained failure."

Keva closed her eyes. "Yes."

"Meaning people hurt but not too many."

"Meaning a visible technical failure with recoverable casualties and no convoy mass loss."

"Say it without the clean words."

Keva opened her eyes. They were wet, but she did not cry. "Meaning enough blood to scare the Compact and not enough to shame it."

No one spoke.

Maro let the silence stand, then said, "Who proposed that phrase?"

Keva swallowed. "Saren Vale."

Vela leaned into her pickup. "Saren Vale is dead."

"I never met a living man using that name."

Maro: "How did contact occur?"

"Encrypted policy memoranda first. Then a relief model package routed through the Meridian Reconstruction Forum. Then direct packets after I challenged assumptions."

"What assumptions?"

"The model treated worker unrest as a timing variable. It predicted that if maintenance crews were publicly implicated in a near-fatal gate failure, citizen trust would swing toward external custody. It predicted Tamsa Reet's arrest would reduce organized resistance by forty percent. It predicted Lena Ors could be induced to accept reinstatement or discredited by reference to her war inquiry."

Lena felt the doctor's hand touch her shoulder because her pulse monitor had objected before she did.

Maro asked, "Did you provide personal data used in those predictions?"

Keva whispered, "Yes."

"Whose?"

"Maintenance leadership. Pilot rosters. Debt ration categories. Medical dependency flags for critical workers. Apprentice family air-credit vulnerabilities."

Jori's name was not spoken. It did not need to be.

Tamsa said, "You gave them children."

Keva's lawyer said, "Objection to characterization."

Om looked at him. "This is not trial argument. It is witness context. The characterization may stand as lay response."

Keva turned toward Tamsa's image. "I told myself it was already in administrative systems."

"It was in systems for keeping people alive."

"Yes."

"You handed it to someone building a trap."

"Yes."

Tamsa's face did not change. "Good. Keep saying yes."

Maro placed a document on the shared board. "Is this one of the memoranda?"

Keva read the header and nodded. "Yes. It came after the first rehearsal schedule was set."

"It recommends falsifying coolant certification through Lot 88."

"It recommends accepting alternate certification vendors."

"Deputy Administrator."

"Yes. It recommends falsifying coolant certification."

"Did you implement it?"

"I approved the vendor exception."

"Knowing the certificates were false?"

"Knowing they were unsupported. Suspecting they were false."

Vela snapped, "That distinction has killed more people than open malice."

Keva accepted it with a slight nod. "Yes."

Sivek stepped away from the wall. "Why did you not come to me?"

She laughed once without humor. "You were the schedule."

He looked as if she had struck him.

Keva continued, softer. "You were not only a person. You were Compact confidence. You were the reopening date. You were every relief official asking whether Kestrel could still govern itself. If I told you, you would stop the sequence."

"Yes."

"Then Halcyon would enter receivership."

"Maybe."

"Not maybe. The letters were drafted."

She sent another file to the witness board. Maro authenticated source hash. Om accepted provisional display. The document appeared: Compact receivership triggers, prepared but unsigned, dated six weeks before the rehearsal pulse.

Sivek read it once.

Then again.

"I was copied on no draft."

Keva's mouth twisted. "You were the asset they wanted unknowing."

Vela said, "Of course. A sincere schedule officer is more useful than a corrupt one."

Sivek did not defend himself. That mattered. He simply said, "Continue."

Maro asked, "When did you realize the plan exceeded a contained failure?"

Keva's hands curled. She flattened them again with visible effort.

"After Jori Pell was injured."

Tamsa made a sound too small to name.

"The model predicted a maintenance scare, not an apprentice in medical. I sent a stop request through the Vale channel. The answer said visibility improved compliance. It said injury to a nonfatal young worker increased public fear and reduced elite skepticism."

Maro's voice sharpened. "Do you have that answer?"

"Yes."

The file entered record.

Lena looked away before she could read the full text. Some sentences did not deserve the dignity of being read twice.

Om said, "Authenticated?"

Maro's hands moved over the chain board. "Preliminary hash matches Maro-unsealed packet family and Blue Vault syntax recovered tonight. Full authentication pending, but this is not a standalone production."

Vela: "It smells like the same corpse."

Om: "The record will say related syntax."

"The record is a coward," Vela said.

"Often," Om said. "Proceed."

Maro asked Keva, "After the stop request was rejected, what did you do?"

"I tried to slow the hearings."

Sivek said, "By withholding documents."

"Yes. And by giving Om partial indexes."

Om's expression did not change. "Noted."

"I thought delay might push the convoy to divert."

Lena said, "It could not divert."

Keva looked at her screen. "I did not know that yet."

"You did not ask the pilots."

"No."

"Because the model said pilots were manageable."

Keva closed her eyes. "Yes."

Lena let the doctor push her back against the pillow. There was no useful movement available to anger in a medical bed.

Maro said, "Did you participate in Tamsa Reet's arrest?"

"I signed the risk certification."

"Knowing the evidence was incomplete?"

"Knowing it was shaped."

Tamsa leaned back in her chair. The corridor behind her was busy with carriers moving toward Halcyon transfer. "Shaped. That is a nice word. Like pottery. Or knives."

Keva faced her. "I am sorry."

"Do not spend time on sorry yet."

"What should I spend it on?"

"Names."

Keva nodded, once, as if a sentence had been passed.

Maro opened a new evidence field. "Identify the Directorate contact operating under Saren Vale continuity."

"I cannot identify the original person. I can identify the living handler who delivered local instructions."

Ilya's image sharpened.

Keva said, "Cassian Vale."

The name had been in the room before. Maro had exposed the continuity mask. But a name guessed by evidence and a name spoken by a conspirator were different weights.

Maro asked, "Relation to Saren Vale?"

"He claimed nephew in private channels. Public records list him as archival executor for the Vale estate. He appeared at restoration forums as a legal historian. He had House Ceyr access through old routeholder litigation."

Ilya's jaw tightened. "I met him."

Om: "When?"

"Twice. At Ceyr internal restoration briefings. He argued that emergency custody doctrine could be revived without appearing anti-democratic if local governance failed visibly enough."

Tamsa: "That did not bother you?"

Ilya looked straight at her. "It pleased me."

That answer did what evasion could not. It made the room colder.

Ilya continued, "At the time, I thought he was saying what I already believed with better legal memory. That Kestrel needed discipline. That public control was sentimental. That worker oversight would collapse under real stress. I did not ask what had to be done to prove me right."

Tamsa held her gaze. "And now?"

"Now I watched a child turn a valve because people like me used safety as a title deed."

No one gave her absolution. She did not ask again.

Maro returned to Keva. "Where is Cassian Vale?"

"He was supposed to arrive after emergency custody transfer."

"Arrive where?"

"Kestrel. On a Ceyr legal shuttle staged beyond Glassward boundary."

Oriole's voice entered from the boundary watch, calm and remote. "Glassward confirms one legal shuttle holding outside active approach during gate event. Transponder: Ceyr Advocate Lumen. It requested priority after cargo transit and withdrew when custody claim failed."

Sivek said, "Current location?"

"Tracking intermittent. It is moving toward the outer debris lanes."

Vela said, "Of course he runs."

Om: "Can Glassward intercept?"

Oriole paused. "We are a boundary order, not a navy."

Sivek opened a tactical board. "Can anyone?"

The answer, visible before spoken, was no. Kestrel's tugs were damaged or tied to survey. Halcyon had its hands full of wounded ships. Compact patrol was days out because the route had been considered reopened enough to need ceremony and not dangerous enough to need teeth.

Maro said, "Issue warrant packet. Public. Full evidence hash. If he crosses any civilized dock, he meets the record before he meets friends."

Om nodded. "Prepare it."

Keva's lawyer found his voice. "My client cannot be made responsible for apprehension failures."

Keva turned on him with such exhausted contempt that he leaned back. "Stop helping."

He stopped.

Maro asked, "What did Cassian Vale want after custody?"

"Control of the Blue Vault cores. Control of the gate schedule. Access to relief disbursement channels. He said the Compact would tolerate a hard custodian if air began flowing and the convoy survived."

"And House Ceyr?"

"Would be the face. The old name. The acceptable owner."

Ilya said, "A mask."

Keva nodded.

Vela said, "People keep acting surprised that masks have straps."

Om: "Deputy Administrator, did you know a hostile continuity construct remained active in Blue Vault?"

"No."

Maro: "Did you know dead-key commands would be issued during cargo transit?"

"No."

"Did you know Cargo Two's liability dependencies would make it vulnerable?"

Keva hesitated.

Tamsa leaned forward. "There is the door where confession tries to become performance. Choose carefully."
