Lena picked up the slate.

Her hands were steadier than she expected.

She did not answer Ara Pellon privately. Private answers were how clean stories got manufactured. She opened the public charter channel, tagged the Compact offer, the old inquiry record, the casualty ledger, the route jury, and the medical restriction note because Tamsa insisted the note had legal beauty.

Then she wrote:

Pilot Lena Ors acknowledges receipt of Compact recommendation for reinstatement and appointment as Acting Technical Chair for Kestrel Gate Recovery.

I accept immediate correction and reopening of my prior adverse inquiry record. The record was used to discredit valid safety warnings and to isolate technical authority from public witness. Its correction belongs in the public evidence chain, not in private apology.

I decline appointment as Acting Technical Chair.

Kestrel's recovery cannot be led by a restored chair replacing a disgraced chair. The gate failed through concentrated authority, hidden maps, dead keys, coercive insurance, and technical knowledge kept inside ranks. Rebuilding under a single prestige office would preserve the shape of the failure.

I offer service as route geometry lead under the provisional Public Charter, subject to Public Route Jury review, cross-discipline signoff, medical clearance, and removal by charter process. Pilot Sann held Morrow during transverse emergency operations and should be named tug operations co-lead if she consents. Maintenance, life-support, evidence, trust-watch, passenger safety, cargo crew, and public witness signatures must remain equal conditions of model validation.

I want the work. I refuse the chair.

She stopped.

The room was quiet.

Then Jori said, "That is longer than five minutes."

The doctor looked at the wall clock. "Reading time does not count."

"Convenient."

Lena sent the message.

For three seconds, the network did nothing dramatic.

Then the public board copied it.

Then Halcyon copied it.

Then someone in Scrubber Works tagged the last line:

I WANT THE WORK. I REFUSE THE CHAIR.

Tamsa groaned. "Now it is a thing."

Vela said from the speaker, "It was always going to become a thing. At least this one has verbs."

Sann took the slate from Lena before anyone could ask her to type more. "I consent, with the note that my name is not decorative."

Maro added it to the thread.

Sivek read the public response count rising and looked pained in the specific way of an officer watching a process escape its filing cabinet. "Ara Pellon will need a formal version."

Om's voice entered from the hall. "The public answer is formal. We may add formatting."

The doctor pointed to the door. "All of you out."

Tamsa stood. "Supervisor says obey medical."

Jori looked betrayed. "Now?"

"Especially now."

The room emptied slowly, because exhausted people had become bad at transitions. Sivek paused at the door.

"Pilot Ors."

"Officer Hale."

"For what it is worth, I believe you should have had correction years ago."

"That is not worth nothing."

He nodded.

"It is also not enough," she said.

"No."

"Good."

He left.

Sann remained, because the doctor had decided she was furniture or useful. Possibly both.

Lena lay back and discovered that refusing a chair could take as much out of a body as accepting one.

"Did I just make my life worse?" she asked.

Sann sat again by the door. "Yes."

"Good."

"Also better."

"That is not a pilot answer."

"I am expanding under charter."

Lena closed her eyes.

Sleep did not come immediately. The old chair stood in her mind, polished by resentment and longing. She let herself look at it properly for the first time in years. It had not only been taken. It had also been built too high. From it, a pilot could see routes and miss valves. Could see schedules and miss nurseries. Could see doctrine and miss a boy with a crutch prying an obsolete label off the only handle that mattered.

She did not forgive the people who had dragged her from it.

She did not forgive herself for wanting it back.

She only set it down, which was different from being free.

Outside medical, Kestrel kept working.

The charter boards updated her refusal. Halcyon membrane installation reached twenty-eight percent. Vela opened a deletion-log architecture task and assigned herself too much work. Maro linked the Compact offer to the old inquiry evidence. Tamsa added "chairs are not routes" to a maintenance board and then denied doing it. Jori asked whether training modules could include jokes and was told no by three adults who were all lying.

At the edge of the ring, the gate crescent held its crooked light.

It did not care who sat where.

It would open again only if the work became true enough.


Chapter 46: Maro Continuant

Maro Venn learned that he was legally alive from a clerk who could not stop apologizing.

The clerk was named Tes Ol. She had been borrowed from passenger licensing because the evidence office had become a place where ordinary schedules went to be eaten. She stood in the doorway of Blue Vault recovery with a slate held against her chest, eyes fixed on the floor, and said, "Continuity determination is back, Investigator Venn. I am sorry."

Maro looked up from the chain log. "For the determination?"

"For bringing it while you are working."

"That depends on the determination."

Tes swallowed. "Continuant status preserved."

Maro sat very still.

Behind him, the nine Blue Vault shield cases hummed inside their portable coolers. Each case carried seals from Kestrel court, evidence office, trust-watch systems, and public witness. The last hostile core had gone quiet twelve hours ago after Vela routed its every attempted handshake into a sandbox that displayed the word NO in thirty-two obsolete Directorate protocols. Vela had called that immature. Then she had saved the sandbox.

Maro had been awake for most of it.

He had expected exhaustion to blunt the determination when it came. It did not. The words entered him with surgical clarity.

Continuant status preserved.

The forked memory record he had unsealed under emergency testimony had not, in the court's provisional view, created a legally separate person. The private continuity gap had not invalidated his testimony. The hostile construct had used copied patterns, dead keys, and context grafts, but Maro Venn remained the recognized continuant of Maro Venn for legal purpose.

He was alive, singular, and admissible.

He should have felt relief.

Instead he felt as if someone had returned his skin with public exhibit tags attached.

Tes extended the slate. "There is a required acknowledgment."

"Of course there is."

"It says you have received the determination and understand it is provisional pending external preservation review."

"I understand."

"There is also a note from Magistrate Om."

That made him look up.

Tes offered the slate. Maro read:

This finding preserves your standing. It does not restore your privacy. I am sorry that law has no better verb.

Om.

Maro read it twice. Then he signed the acknowledgment.

Tes lingered.

"Was there something else?"

"No. I mean yes. I mean, people are saying continuant like it is good news."

"It is good news for the case."

"Is it good news for you?"

The question was too direct for a clerk, which meant she had been carrying it from someone else or from herself and had not yet learned to hide human concern inside procedure.

Maro considered giving the kind answer. He was very tired of kind lies.

"I do not know," he said.

Tes nodded, relieved by uncertainty more than comfort. "I will file the acknowledgment."

"Thank you."

After she left, Maro closed the chain log and opened the public determination. His name sat at the top:

MARO VENN, ACCIDENT INVESTIGATOR, CONTINUITY STATUS PROVISIONALLY PRESERVED.

Below it, the court summarized the evidence in careful language. Fork record voluntarily unsealed. Memory discontinuity authenticated. Hostile construct used related but non-identical pattern fragments. No evidence of separate active Maro-derived legal person. Testimony remains valid. Privacy harm substantial and ongoing. Future use of unsealed fork record limited to Kestrel sabotage, Blue Vault, relief-fund manipulation, Compact doctrine inquiry, and derivative proceedings authorized by public court order.

Limited.

The word sat there like a paper wall in vacuum.

He knew how evidence spread. Not because people were evil, though some were. Because relevance branched. Because lawyers found new contexts. Because historians arrived with moral appetite. Because systems copied what they should have referenced. Because someone, someday, would decide that Maro's private memories were necessary to prove something adjacent to something adjacent to the dead.

His fork record contained more than the missing hour.

It contained his mother's last message before the Pelagos blackout, preserved because he had replayed it during the wrong audit window. It contained the smell of rain in a dome city where rain was a civic performance. It contained the face of the person he had loved and left because accident investigators learned to arrive after ruin and forget how to stay before it. It contained small humiliations, private cruelties, jokes that only worked in rooms that no longer existed, and the texture of being a self before that self became useful.

Now parts of it belonged to the record.

Maro opened the access log.

Om had viewed the required segments. Vela had viewed technical pattern boundaries. Sivek had viewed command-relevance excerpts. Ilya had viewed the Ceyr-linked continuity mask. External preservation had received hashed references but not content. Tamsa had viewed none.

That last line surprised him.

He sent a message:

You declined access?

Tamsa answered from Halcyon scrubber coordination, where the background noise suggested people were either installing membranes or dismantling a kitchen.

To your brain? Yes.

It is evidence.

It is also your brain.

You were entitled under public witness.

Supervisor says not everything entitled should eat.

He stared at the line until his eyes burned.

Then another message arrived from Lena:

Determination posted. Standing preserved. How bad?

He typed:

Manageable.

He deleted it.

He typed:

Worse than expected. Better than invalidation.

He sent that.

Lena answered:

That sounds true enough to work with.

Maro set the slate down.

Blue Vault had no windows. It had never needed them. Archives did not require sky, only temperature discipline and people willing to pretend that preservation was neutral. The portable coolers had turned the air sour. The evidence seals blinked at different rates. A public board in the corner displayed the charter clause in large text because Tamsa had insisted:

PEOPLE BEFORE EVIDENCE. RECORD THE COST.

Maro wondered where he fit in that sentence when he had become both.

Ilya Ceyr entered with two guards, one medic, and a tablet under one arm. The guards were not for him or for her exactly. They were for the legal fact that House Ceyr had suspended her authority and then sent three incompatible notices demanding return of "misappropriated archive assets," meaning the hostile cores. The charter barred Ceyr access, but guards made legal theory less likely to become a person with a crate.

Ilya looked at the public board. "Continuant status preserved."

"Yes."

"Congratulations?"

"Interrogative accepted."

She came to stand beside him, leaving a respectful distance from the cases. "House Ceyr has filed an objection to the limitation order."

"On what ground?"

"They claim the fork record may contain privileged routeholder communications."

Maro almost laughed. It came out as air. "Do they want to own everyone's memories or only those that embarrass them?"

"They prefer not to distinguish."

He opened the objection. It was exactly as bad as promised. Ceyr counsel argued that any communication between Maro and Ceyr representatives, including informal or remembered exchanges, could be subject to privilege review before public disclosure. The filing did not quite claim title over his mind. Its courtesy made it worse.

Ilya said, "I can answer publicly."

"You are suspended."

"From active House authority. Not from being irritating."

"That seems correct."

She sent a response before he could advise against it:

Ilya Ceyr, witness to Blue Vault contradiction, objects to House Ceyr attempt to extend privilege into Investigator Venn's private memory. The House may defend documents it created. It may not claim custody over another person's continuity because its representatives spoke where they should not have.

Maro read it. "Thank you."

"Do not become grateful. It makes future disagreement awkward."

"Noted."

She glanced at him, then away. "Does the determination help?"

"The case, yes."

"You?"

"People keep asking."

"Because the legal answer is visibly insufficient."

He looked at her. "You lost your crest authority."

"Yes."

"Did it help?"

She considered. "It clarified pain."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the only one I have."

They stood among the sealed cases, two people legally preserved in forms neither had chosen.

Vela's voice came from the trust-watch channel. "Maro, your access wall is leaking."

He straightened. "Where?"

"Not content. Metadata. Someone outside Kestrel is querying the determination index by emotional tags."

Ilya frowned. "Emotional tags?"

Maro's stomach went cold. "Fork archives sometimes classify memory segments by affect load for trauma review."

Vela: "And some charming institution thinks affect tags are not private because they are not content."

"Who?"

"Route Authority archive mirror. Not Pellon's office. Older node. Maybe automated discovery, maybe a person hiding behind one."

Maro opened the access wall. The query pattern appeared in thin red lines. No segment content had left. But the external node had requested index entries associated with fear, grief, shame, attachment, and moral injury.

Not his words. Not his images.

Only the map of where they lived.

He felt suddenly and completely tired.

Vela said, softer than usual, "I can block it."

"On what authority?"

"Mine, if I am quick and rude."

"Charter authority requires cause."

"Cause: someone is licking the outside of your skull."

Ilya said, "Put that in different language."

Maro opened the charter emergency clause. Evidence access conflict. Living person before evidence. Record cost. He could invoke harm. He could block the query and invite review. He had written similar rules for other people. Now the rule waited to see if he believed it when the person was himself.

"Block it," he said.

Vela's hands moved somewhere beyond the channel. The red lines cut off.

"Done. Logged as privacy harm prevention under charter clause four. I used cleaner wording than my first instinct."

"Thank you."

"Do not thank me yet. The archive mirror has filed automatic protest."

Om joined the channel thirty seconds later, as if summoned by legal ugliness. "Investigator Venn, did you authorize trust-watch block?"

"Yes."

"Cause?"

"External metadata query sought affect classification from unsealed fork index beyond immediate evidentiary need, creating privacy harm not justified by current proceeding."

"Good sentence."

"I have practice."

"Temporary block upheld pending review. Vela, preserve query logs."

"Already did."

"Ilya Ceyr, why are you in Blue Vault?"

Ilya looked briefly like a student caught in a restricted corridor. "Delivering objection context."

"Are you medically cleared?"

"Partially."

"That is not a status."

The medic behind her said, "No."

Om closed her eyes. "Everyone on this station treats medical instruction as decorative."

Maro said, "I am cleared."

Tes, the clerk, passing the doorway at exactly the wrong moment, said, "No, you are not."

Silence.

Om: "Explain."

Tes froze. "I mean, his work clearance says evidence-limited with rest cycles required."

Maro turned slowly.

Tes looked as if she wanted the floor to become a profession. "It was on the routing note."

Vela laughed once over the channel. "Continuant status preserved; self-preservation status doubtful."

Om said, "Investigator Venn, you will take a rest cycle after this review."

"Magistrate-"

"That was not phrased as discussion."

Tamsa entered the channel uninvited. "Supervisor concurs."

Maro looked at the ceiling. "Is everyone watching Blue Vault?"

Vela: "The public board is not a window. But also yes."

Om's tone changed, not softer, exactly. More exact. "Maro. The charter you helped draft applies when evidence tries to consume a person. That includes the person who opened the file."

He had no procedural answer to that.

So he said, "Understood."

The review of the metadata breach took forty minutes. The archive mirror protest claimed standard preservation sweep. Om denied ongoing access. Vela built a tighter wall around affect tags. Maro filed a personal harm statement because the charter required cost, and because if he did not file one, someone later would call the harm theoretical.

The statement took longer than the legal block.

He wrote:

The unsealed fork record contains private memory material beyond the evidentiary segments relevant to Kestrel sabotage. Affect index queries reveal structure of private experience even where content remains sealed. Such structure can expose grief, attachment, fear, and shame patterns. I experience this as continuing privacy harm. I accept limited evidentiary use necessary to preserve life, prove sabotage, and prevent recurrence. I do not consent to exploratory, administrative, historical, or reputational mining of my continuity record.

He stopped there.

Then added:

Being useful is not consent.

He nearly deleted it. Instead he sent it.

The public board posted the harm statement under restricted summary. The full version remained sealed by his choice. That choice felt small and enormous.

Messages came afterward.

Lena sent: Good line.

Tamsa sent: Put it in training.

Vela sent: Already building a rule named after it. Will deny if asked.

Ilya sent nothing. She stood beside him while the medic checked her palm and pretended not to listen.

Sivek arrived in person after the review, carrying two sealed packets and the expression of a man who had been arguing with external authority until language began to peel.

"Pellon's office upheld the block," he said. "The older archive node has been isolated from the Kestrel evidence mirror."

Maro nodded. "Good."

"Also, Compact route authority accepts your continuant status for the doctrine challenge."

"Useful."

"Yes."

Sivek did not leave.

Maro waited.

Finally Sivek said, "I reviewed the segments tied to my office."

"You were authorized."

"That is not what I came to say."

Maro folded his hands.

Sivek looked toward the Blue Vault cases. "Your private recollection of my first interview with you after the rehearsal pulse differs from my report."

"Yes."

"My report says I provided full cooperation under schedule constraint."

"I remember you telling me not to widen the inquiry unless I could prove sabotage before I found it."

Sivek's face tightened. "That is accurate."

"I did not include it in public summary because it was not central."

"It is central to me."

Maro did not answer.

Sivek placed one packet on the table. "Amendment to my command statement. I name the pressure I applied. I also state that the evidence arose from your continuity record and that further detail should not be disclosed unless necessary."

Maro took the packet but did not open it. "Why?"

"Because if your memory bears the cost of my words, my record should bear the responsibility."

The sentence was stiff. It was also true enough to stand on.

"Thank you," Maro said.

Sivek nodded and left.

After him came silence, or what passed for it in Blue Vault. Cooler hum. Seal ticks. Distant boards. The low murmur of Kestrel learning to keep public record without becoming public spectacle.

Ilya finally spoke. "You should take the rest cycle before Om sends guards."

"You too."

"I am not continuant evidence."

"No. You are suspended heritage, hostile-authority contradiction, and living Ceyr witness."

"That was unkindly accurate."

"Investigator."

She made a small sound. "Fine."

They left Blue Vault together under guard escort and medical disapproval. At the corridor junction, she turned toward recovery and he turned toward the borrowed office that had become his sleeping room. Before they parted, Ilya said, "Maro."

He looked back.

"I did not view your fork because I was afraid of what it would prove about my house. Not because I was noble."

"I know."

"Does that matter?"

"Yes."

"How?"

"It makes your later choice more expensive."

She absorbed that. "Good night, then, if the phrase still functions."

"Rest cycle," he corrected.

"Ugly."

"Accurate."

They separated.

Maro's borrowed office contained a narrow cot, two sealed evidence lockers, a wall display he turned off, and a cup of cold tea someone had abandoned during the charter drafting. He removed his jacket and sat on the cot without lying down.

Continuant status preserved.

The phrase came back, less sharp now. The law had pointed to him and said: this person remains this person. It had not known what else to say. Maybe no law could.

He opened a private file. Not evidence. Not public. Private, with local encryption and no mirror. A dangerous comfort.

He recorded:

I am Maro Venn. I remember the missing hour now in pieces I would not have chosen to share. I remember being afraid that my own memory had been made into a corridor for someone else's lie. I remember opening the file anyway. Today the court says I remain myself. I do not know whether selfhood is something a court can preserve. I know only that tomorrow I will decide again which parts of me the record may use, and I will try to keep enough unrecorded to come home to.

He stopped.

Then added one more line:

Being useful is not consent, but sometimes consent is choosing what usefulness costs.

He sealed the file.

For the first time in two days, Maro lay down before collapsing.

Outside, Kestrel's public boards continued without him. That felt like loss. It felt like proof.

He slept.


Chapter 47: Halcyon Opens

Halcyon opened Dock North-Four at seventy-two percent scrubber recovery.

The administrators wanted ninety.

The medics wanted eighty.

The nursery leads wanted one hundred and a signed promise from physics that no filter would clog during the ceremony, though they refused to call it a ceremony. Oret wanted to open when the clamp alarms stopped lying. Tamsa wanted to open when the work required people to stop treating Halcyon as a patient behind glass and start treating it as a station with doors. The public charter required Halcyon consent for any shared route governance step that affected life-support, cargo priority, or public access.

Halcyon voted through corridor boards, dock terminals, kitchen slates, nursery panels, and one paper ballot box because a retired teacher named Sola Im insisted that civilization had not survived the Fracture Wars to forget pencils.

The vote was not about reopening the gate. The gate was still bent, watched by Morrow and Ashkey crews, modeled by Lena's new geometry group, and insulted daily by Vela's trust-watch diagnostics. The vote was about opening the dock receiving district to Kestrel maintenance observers, convoy crew representatives, and the first joint governance assembly under the public charter.

It sounded procedural until Tamsa saw the line outside Dock North-Four.

People came carrying tools, children, repair tablets, food packets, names, complaints, and pieces of damaged equipment they wanted someone to see. Halcyon residents stood on one side of the pressure cordon. Kestrel workers and convoy crew stood on the other. Between them sat a temporary witness arch built from cargo frame, source-clock projectors, and two badly painted signs:

AIR FIRST.

NO PRIVATE MAPS.

Niko claimed he had nothing to do with the signs. The paint on his sleeve disagreed.

Tamsa arrived with Lena, Sann, Maro, Sivek, Om, Vela, Ilya, and Jori, which sounded like a delegation only if one ignored the medical braces, borrowed jackets, pressure bruises, and the fact that Vela had brought her own chair because she did not trust public furniture with her back. Jori was walking with a new support frame and the intense resentment of someone being supervised by four adults and a nurse.

"Do not make a speech," Tamsa told him.

"I was not going to."

"You were thinking one."

"Thinking is still legal."

Om said, "Mostly."

The dock smelled of sealant, boiled coolant, human fatigue, and fresh membrane plastic. The damaged capture web from Cargo Two hung retracted along the far wall, tagged and unrepaired because Oret wanted it visible for the first assembly. The service mast Berez had nearly lost stood under a lattice of temporary braces. Cargo Four's smoke stains still marked the deck near the cold-transfer route. Someone had painted a small line around the dent where Berez's ugly tug had clipped the torn rail and written RAISE DENIED.

Berez stood beside it, accepting condolences for his tug with the dignity of a martyr.

Captain Dax waited near the witness arch. Her burned hair had been cut short on one side. Her uniform was clean enough to show how badly it had been repaired. Beside her stood Sao with a bandaged cheek, Cargo Two's captain Ren Hal with both hands clasped behind his back, and three crew representatives elected by ships that had never before elected anything except who had to clean the filters after long burn.

Oret stood at the center, helmet under one arm.

"Dock North-Four opens under provisional charter," she said. "Not for spectacle. Not for forgiveness. For work. Halcyon residents consented by local vote. Kestrel delegates enter as observers and co-workers, not rescuers, owners, or inspectors with clean shoes."

Tamsa looked down at her boots.

Oret caught it. "Your shoes are acceptable by filth standard."

"Honored."

The pressure cordon lowered.

No one moved for one breath.

Then a nursery worker stepped forward carrying a failed membrane seal, and a Kestrel scrubber technician stepped from the other side with an inspection lamp. They met under the witness arch without ceremony, leaned over the seal, and began arguing about gasket compression.

Halcyon opened like that.

Not with applause. With disagreement over a part.

The dock loosened around them. People crossed in controlled groups. Source clocks tagged each work table. Public boards showed not only tasks but who had authority to change them. Membrane installation. Medical reserve triage. Cargo loss claims. Dock repair. Casualty ledger support. Map reconciliation. Insurance coercion testimony. Gate model witness training. Food distribution. Childcare. Sleep enforcement, which Tamsa had opposed until Oret threatened to create a Supervisor Reet Rest Compliance Board.

Lena found herself at the gate model table with Sann and two Halcyon nursery engineers who had never touched route geometry but understood airflow through old systems better than any pilot in the room. The tabletop display showed the transverse crescent as a flexible contour threaded through coolant dependencies, cargo mass histories, and field vane drift.

Engineer Pel Emon stared at the contour. "This is a pipe with delusions."

Lena considered. "Yes."

Sann said, "That may be our best summary."

Pel pointed to the west arc. "If the field remnant wants the easiest thermal route, why are you modeling it like a pilot wants to go somewhere?"

Lena opened her mouth.

Closed it.

Sann smiled without mercy.

Pel continued, "Your old model assumes intention along transit axis. The sideways fall behaved like pressure finding a bad wall."

"Route geometry is not fluid mechanics," Lena said.

"No. But the station made it act impolitely like both."

Lena looked at the contour again. The old pilot instinct wanted to correct Pel's language. The new charter required her to ask whether the language saw something certification had missed.

"Show me the wall," she said.

Pel did.

Across the dock, Maro sat with cargo crew witnesses under a sign that read EVIDENCE IS NOT A TRAP, which he had not approved but had not removed. Ren Hal, Cargo Two's captain, gave his statement with his eyes on the deck.

"When the Ceyr feed said liability protections were void, I thought of my crew families first," Ren said. "Not the rack. Not the furniture. The benefits. I had two seconds. I hesitated. The ship scraped. We lost Varo Chen's rack team and almost hit Halcyon mast."

Dax stood behind him, arms folded. She had censured him. She had also insisted he testify before anyone else turned him into a simple coward.

Maro asked, "Did you know the heritage rack contents?"

"No. Manifest subset was locked. I knew it had high priority and Ceyr insurance."

"Would you have assigned trim differently if contents were visible?"

Ren laughed once, no humor. "I would have thrown the dining set out before the burn."

The crew representatives murmured. Maro let the sound enter the record.

"What do you need the charter to change?" he asked.

Ren looked up then. "No captain should learn during transit that their crew's death benefits depend on obeying a voice they cannot authenticate."

Maro entered it as proposed rule language. "Anything else?"

"Yes. If I am to be reviewed, review the people who wrote the clause before reviewing the two seconds."

Dax nodded once.

Maro wrote that too.

Near the casualty support table, Om worked with Sola Im, the retired teacher, on the relational witness rule. The unnamed cargo cook had become a pressure point large enough to move law by millimeters. His crew had submitted a sealed memorial file with twenty-three statements, three recipes, and a recording of him shouting at a pressure oven. His legal brother still refused public naming.

Sola tapped the draft with a pencil. "You are using 'household' too narrowly."

Om said, "Inheritance law uses it narrowly."

"People do not die according to inheritance law."

"I am aware."

"Then write what you know."

Om, who intimidated officers, pilots, and lawyers, looked mildly outmatched by a retired teacher with a pencil.

"Suggested language?"

"A chosen operational household may attest memorial relationship when legal kin withhold or cannot be reached, subject to privacy review and later challenge."

Om wrote it down.

Sola added, "And do not use subject twice in one sentence. It sounds like you are afraid."

Om removed the second subject.

Tamsa saw all this while failing to stay at her assigned station.

Her assigned station was "maintenance governance intake," which meant everyone brought her things no one else wanted to decide. A Kestrel pipefitter wanted to know whether Halcyon gasket standards outranked Kestrel emergency stock. A Halcyon nursery lead wanted to know whether Kestrel workers had to log tool contamination twice. A convoy engineer wanted to join a map audit despite not being resident. A child wanted to know if the bent gate meant stars had corners now.

"Ask a pilot," Tamsa told the child.

The child looked at Lena, who was arguing with Pel Emon, and wisely left.

Jori was supposed to be seated at the training map table, where Niko had given him a harmless job matching old labels to current photographs. Ten minutes later he had corrected four labels, insulted two legends, and attracted six adults who wanted to tell him about undocumented panels.

Tamsa went over. "This is not rest."

"I am sitting."

"Your brain is standing."

"That is not in the medical order."

The nurse behind him said, "It is now."

Jori sighed with the suffering of the righteous. "People keep bringing me bad labels."

Niko held up a slate. "Because you find the worse ones."

"That is not my fault."

"It is becoming your job."

Tamsa pointed at both of them. "No jobs for injured apprentices."

Jori and Niko exchanged a look that made her distrust the future.

"Training review," Niko said.

"Temporary label audit," Jori added.

"Under supervision," the nurse said.

Tamsa looked at the nurse. "Do not encourage them."

"I am documenting them. Different."

It was not different enough, but the map table was working. Old contradictions were becoming visible. Blister Nine's obsolete red handle had already been photographed, tagged, and placed beside Jori's revised open question. Under it, someone had added:

THE MAP SHOULD NOT REQUIRE AN INJURED APPRENTICE TO BE RIGHT.

Tamsa pretended not to feel that in her throat.

At midday, Halcyon opened the nursery corridor to a small Kestrel delegation.

No cameras went in except a local witness feed controlled by the nursery leads. No speeches. No touching the new membranes. Everyone scrubbed hands twice. Tamsa, Lena, Sivek, Om, and Ilya were allowed through. Maro declined; he had evidence intake. Vela declined by saying nurseries made people sentimental and she was allergic.

The nursery was quieter than Tamsa expected.

Not peaceful. Peace was too large a claim. But the air was different. Less metallic. The new membranes sat in their frames like pale lungs behind transparent covers, each tagged with lot number, inspection hash, installing crew, and next check time. Children slept in low hammocks along the inner wall while two caregivers sorted clean cloths. A boy with a shaved patch for a medical sensor watched the adults with solemn suspicion.

Nursery Lead Aven said, "Do not thank the membranes."

Sivek blinked. "I was not going to."

"People do. They come in and look at equipment like it is mercy. It is work."

Tamsa liked her immediately.

Aven led them to the scrubber bank. "Fourteen frames still need replacement. Six seals are questionable. We are running lower than comfort because installing too fast risks tearing old mounts. Cargo reserve gives us a month if no new failure occurs."

"If a new failure occurs?" Om asked.

"Then comfort leaves."

Ilya stood behind the others, hands folded, eyes on the children. She had insisted on coming though no one had insisted on inviting her. Her presence made several Halcyon workers stiffen. She noticed. She did not retreat into apology.

Aven looked at her. "You are the Ceyr witness."

"Yes."

"Not authority."

"No."

"Good."

Ilya accepted that too.

They reached the donor plaque outside Nursery Two, now removed and laid flat on a workbench. Behind it, the place where Keva Lorn had hidden the physical wafer sat exposed, edges bright from hurried prying. Someone had taped a temporary label beneath it:

EVIDENCE FOUND HERE BECAUSE SOMEONE EXPECTED WORKERS TO FIND WHAT OFFICIALS WOULD NOT SAY.

Sivek read it in silence.

Ilya said, "Will you leave the hole?"

Aven's expression sharpened. "Do you object?"

"No. I think you should leave it until everyone knows why it is there."

Aven studied her. "That is the first useful Ceyr sentence I have heard."

"I will try to have a second eventually."

Tamsa almost smiled.

One of the sleeping children coughed.

Every adult in the room looked toward the sound.

The caregiver by the hammocks checked the child's monitor, adjusted a blanket, and gave the universal station gesture for not alarming yourselves into stupidity. Shoulders lowered one by one.

Sivek looked wrecked by the tiny cough.

Aven saw it. "You people on Kestrel heard air as numbers."

"Yes," he said.

"Halcyon hears numbers as children coughing."

"I am learning."

"Learn faster."

"Yes."

The tour ended at the membrane control board. Aven placed a local access token on the rail.

"Under the charter, Halcyon life-support grants Kestrel maintenance read access to membrane performance, not control access. Control stays here unless we explicitly ask for remote assist. We grant the Public Route Jury authority to audit our logs for shared route decisions. We do not grant any route officer authority to trade nursery stability for schedule."

Sivek said, "Accepted."

"Say it like an order you are giving yourself."

He straightened. "Kestrel Control accepts that Halcyon nursery stability cannot be traded for route schedule."

Aven nodded. "Better."

Tamsa watched him take the lesson without defense. That did not undo anything. It did make him less immediately irritating.

Back in Dock North-Four, the first joint governance assembly began with food because Halcyon voted that people drafting rules while hungry should be considered a preventable hazard. The meal was simple: protein cakes, pickled greens, heated grain, and fruit paste from a cargo crate that had survived better than several legal doctrines. Kestrel workers ate beside convoy crew. Halcyon nursery leads argued with pilots. Cargo captains sat under the public board where their errors and courage were both visible.

No one made it harmonious. Harmony would have been suspicious.

Oret called the assembly to order by hitting a cracked clamp housing with a wrench.

Om winced. "Must we?"

Oret said, "This dock recognizes impact."

The agenda appeared:

1. Life-support status and shared priority.
2. Dock repair and cargo offload completion.
3. Public Route Jury seat confirmation.
4. Map contradiction audit.
5. Cargo and insurance testimony schedule.
6. Memorial coordination.
7. Gate model witness training.
8. Sleep rotation enforcement.

Vela, remote from Kestrel, objected to sleep rotation being last.

Oret moved it to first.

Tamsa objected on principle.

Oret said, "You are the reason."

The assembly voted sleep rotation enforcement into immediate effect. Tamsa lost. Lena lost. Sivek lost. Maro lost in absentia. Vela abstained because she claimed she was not subject to laws made by hypocrites, then fell asleep on audio for seven seconds and was marked compliant.

After that, real work began.

Halcyon life-support presented the membrane recovery curve. Kestrel maintenance presented replacement capacity. Convoy crews presented cargo still sealed. Dock repair presented the capture web damage. The Public Route Jury seats were confirmed provisionally:

Tamsa for Kestrel maintenance until workers could elect a replacement or punish her by keeping her.

Aven for Halcyon life-support.

Sann for tug operations.

Lena for route geometry, medical restrictions noted.

Maro for evidence continuity, rest cycle restrictions noted over his written protest.

Vela for trust-watch systems, with a requirement to explain at least half her insults in technical terms.

Dax for convoy cargo crews.

Sola Im for resident public witness.

Niko as map audit coordinator, after he attempted to decline and was overruled by too many people who had seen his signs.

Jori Pell was not given a seat. He glared until the assembly created a rotating apprentice observer role with medical clearance required. The nurse approved this because it gave her a formal way to remove him.

Ilya Ceyr was listed as institutional claimant witness, nonvoting, with right to submit evidence and objections.

She said, "That is an ugly category."

Om said, "Earn a better one later."

"Fair."

When the assembly reached memorial coordination, the room changed. Tools quieted. People straightened or looked down. The casualty ledger appeared in public-facing mode, names visible only where permission had been granted, withheld rows marked with dignity instead of blankness.

Sola Im spoke.

"We will not hold one memorial and call it complete," she said. "People died in different places, for different reasons, with different families and crews. Some names are public. Some are not. Some bodies are missing. Some questions remain open. We need a form that does not pretend grief is tidy."

Om asked, "Proposal?"

"Three parts. First, a quiet ledger room on Kestrel and Halcyon, linked but not broadcast. Second, a work memorial at the damaged systems: Vane Three, Blister Nine, Dock North-Four, Nursery Two, and the cargo web. Third, when families consent, names on the repaired field vane before any transit certification."

Tamsa looked at Lena.

Lena said, "Names on the vane was in the old pilot memorial practice."

"Before or after pilots became unbearable?"

"During."

"Then we can steal it."

Sivek said, "Not steal. Continue."

Tamsa looked at him.

He corrected, "Share."

"Better."

The assembly accepted the three-part memorial plan.

The unnamed cook remained unnamed. His crew's sealed file would be represented by a place marker until the legal dispute changed or the crew chose another form. Varo Chen's marker would remain in missing status until the search ended. Eda Malk's family allowed her bad singing note to be included in the quiet ledger room, not on the public board. Ruun Adali's age was corrected everywhere.

Small accuracies became acts of care.

Toward the end of the assembly, Ren Hal stood again.

He looked as if he had not slept, which meant he fit in. "Cargo Two crew requests to assist with capture web repair."

Oret folded her arms. "Because you hit it?"

"Because we hit it."

"You are under review."

"Yes."

"Review is not exile."

He looked startled.

Dax said, "His crew knows the damage path."

Oret considered, then nodded. "Accepted under dock lead supervision. If anyone turns this into penance theater, I will throw them into paperwork."

Ren's shoulders lowered.

That was how Halcyon opened, hour by hour. Not by becoming generous in an abstract way, but by finding forms where responsibility could work without becoming a cage.

Late in the cycle, after the assembly had dissolved into task groups, Tamsa stepped out through the witness arch and leaned against the pressure rail. Beyond the dock windows, Kestrel's damaged ring was a distant bright scar. Between stations, the relay traffic pulsed with new channels: charter logs, membrane reports, map audits, condolence packets, cargo claims, evidence hashes, jokes, insults, sleep orders, recipes, and one public argument about whether "pipe with delusions" should become official gate terminology.

Lena joined her, moving slowly but without the medical monitor for the first time.

"You are supposed to be resting," Tamsa said.

"So are you."

"I am leaning in a restful manner."

"Same."

They stood together.

After a while, Lena said, "Halcyon is louder than I expected."

"It was always loud. Kestrel just had bad microphones."

"Fair."

Below them, Jori and Niko were arguing over a label photograph while the nurse hovered at a range calculated to preserve both supervision and dignity. Oret was showing Ren Hal the torn capture web. Dax was eating standing up. Sivek was listening to Aven explain membrane failure with the expression of a man trying to rebuild his soul through technical detail. Ilya was helping Sola Im sort witness categories, which seemed both useful and mildly punitive. Om was writing with a pencil because Sola had given her one and dared her to refuse.

Maro appeared at the dock entrance, paused under the witness arch, and looked around as if surprised the work had continued without him.

Tamsa lifted her chin in greeting. "Rest cycle?"

"Completed."

"Liar?"

"Partial."

"Accepted provisionally."

He came to stand near them. "The assembly posted its first decisions."

"Do they look like governance?"

"They look like work with signatures."

Lena said, "That may be governance."

Maro looked toward the nursery corridor. "Halcyon opened."

Tamsa nodded. "Not all the way."

"No."

"Enough for hands."

The phrase stayed there, practical and exact.

Across the dock, a group of Halcyon children emerged under caregiver supervision to deliver paper tags to the map audit table. Each tag had a place name written in uneven letters: Nursery Two, Cold Bath, North-Four, The Bad Clamp, The Singing Lady's Hall, The Hole Behind the Plaque, Jori's Not Valve.

Jori protested the last one immediately.

The children ignored him with the natural authority of people who had survived adults.

One child approached Tamsa and handed her a tag that read AIR FIRST in blue pencil. Tamsa took it with the care she would have given a fragile seal.

"Where does this go?" the child asked.

Tamsa looked at the dock, the boards, the people, the damaged route, the new charter fields, the old arguments learning new names.

"Everywhere," she said.

The child considered this and decided it was an acceptable logistical challenge.

Halcyon did not become safe that day.

Safe was not something a station became because a gate had held, a charter had posted, or people had found enough courage to stand in public with their failures. Safe was maintained. Checked. Argued for. Repaired. Paid attention to when attention was boring. Protected from anyone who tried to turn it into ownership.

But Dock North-Four stayed open.

The membranes kept seating.

The witness arch held.

For the first time since the convoy warning, people moved between Kestrel and Halcyon work tables without asking who owned the air between them.


Chapter 48: The New Schedule

The first new schedule did not contain a single transit.

Sivek hated it for five full minutes before admitting that was why it worked.

He stood in Kestrel Control with the schedule wall blank except for one title line and a public timestamp. The old wall had been built to soothe. Its arcs had once shown departure lanes, gate warm windows, convoy slots, tug support, buffer maintenance, and ceremony blocks in a calm blue order that implied the future would obey if given enough columns. Officers had loved that wall. Investors had loved it more. Citizens had learned to read it the way hungry people read menus.

Now the wall said:

KST-HALCYON TRANSVERSE EMERGENCY PUBLIC WORK SCHEDULE / VERSION 0.1 / NO TRANSIT CERTIFICATION.

Under it, nothing.

Tamsa stood with a cup of station coffee so bad it had become a legal category. "Looks honest."

"It looks empty."

"Also honest."

Sivek folded his arms. "A schedule must schedule."

Vela, from the trust-watch console, said, "A wall can also confess."

"That is not a scheduling principle."

"It should be. Most schedules are lies with dates."

Om sat at the side table reviewing the charter compliance fields. She had insisted that Version 0.1 be drafted in Control instead of a conference room because the old schedule wall was one of the compromised instruments, even though it had done nothing more active than display human confidence too beautifully.

"Begin with prohibitions," Om said.

Sivek almost objected on instinct. Schedules began with permissions. Departures. Arrivals. Windows. Tasks. Prohibitions were footnotes, warnings, boxed exceptions placed where people could pretend not to see them.

That had been part of the failure.

He touched the input rail.

1. NO GENERAL TRANSIT.
2. NO CARGO TRANSIT.
3. NO PASSENGER TRANSIT.
4. NO ROUTEHOLDER CUSTODY ACTION.
5. NO DEAD-KEY COMMAND ACCEPTANCE.
6. NO AUTOMATED MODEL CLOSURE WITHOUT PUBLIC WITNESS.

The wall looked severe.

Tamsa nodded. "Better."

"It is not a work schedule yet."

"It tells people what will not happen to them while they work."

That was also a schedule, he realized. A promise of absence. No surprise burn. No hidden custody switch. No old key ordering your ship to hold while a gate bent around you. No leadership declaring victory on a wall without the dead beside it.

He added:

FOUNDATION: LIFE-SUPPORT STABILITY, CASUALTY ACCOUNTING, EVIDENCE PRESERVATION, MAP RECONCILIATION, TRANSVERSE MODELING, DOCK REPAIR.

Oret, on Halcyon video, read the line while signing a work order with her other hand. "Put life-support first in every view, not just foundation."

"It is first."

"Scroll views cut foundation on small terminals."

Vela said, "Confirmed. Old display hierarchy privileges route operations."

Sivek changed the template so life-support appeared in the fixed header.

The wall flickered.

LIFE-SUPPORT STATUS: HALCYON MEMBRANE RECOVERY 73 PERCENT / KESTREL FILTER RESERVE 41 DAYS AT CURRENT LOAD / NO SCHEDULE MAY OVERRIDE NURSERY OR MEDICAL AIR LIMITS.

Tamsa's eyebrows lifted. "Look at you, learning where to put air."

"Do not sound surprised."

"I am surprised."

He accepted that because it was fair.

Lena joined late, moving slowly enough that even Vela did not comment at first. Sann came with her carrying two slates and an expression that warned everyone against asking whether Lena should be in Control. Medical had cleared Lena for "limited advisory participation, seated, no emergency command, no elevated voice." Tamsa had immediately asked what counted as elevated. The doctor had answered, "Anything I hear from another deck."

Sivek had placed a chair at the route geometry station.

Lena looked at it. "Is that a trap?"

"Medical compliance."

"Same question."

She sat.

Sann put the slates down. "Morrow and Ashkey observation cycles. No active tug approach inside crescent hazard boundary without route jury signoff."

"Good," Sivek said.

Lena looked at the blank transit section of the wall. "You did not put test drone windows."

"Not yet."

"Good."

He looked at her. "I expected the pilot to ask for probes."

"The pilot wants probes. The geometry lead under charter wants the model to earn them."

Tamsa muttered, "That sentence is going to become training material."

Lena ignored her. "We need three things before even a cold drone crosses the inner hazard line. First, Morrow and Ashkey passive survey agreement. Second, Pel Emon's thermal-wall model incorporated. Third, map audit on west arc coolant and field dependencies. The gate fell sideways through more than physics."

Vela said, "I heard my language in that and feel violated."

"You will survive."

"Often against my preference."

Sivek added a modeling block:

TRANSVERSE MODEL PHASE 1: PASSIVE SURVEY ONLY.
REQUIRED INPUTS: Morrow/Ashkey observation; Halcyon thermal-wall review; west arc map audit; Blue Vault isolation status; casualty-linked damage questions.
OUTPUT: Public witnessed model draft, not certification.

Maro arrived as the block posted, rested just enough to look more human and less willing to tolerate nonsense. "Add evidence dependency."

Sivek did.

Maro shook his head. "Not that. The schedule must show which tasks depend on evidence that may be contested. If a model input comes from a damaged sensor whose log is part of sabotage review, people need to see uncertainty before the model makes it look clean."

Lena nodded. "Yes."

Sivek opened a new field.

EVIDENCE STATUS: raw / witnessed / contested / preserved / excluded.

Vela said, "Add 'politely suspicious.'"

Om: "No."

"History will vindicate me."

The schedule grew.

Not elegantly. Elegance had become suspect. The wall filled with blocks that looked more like exposed wiring than a command plan:

LIFE-SUPPORT:
Halcyon membranes: install, inspect, rest cycle, repeat. Public status every six hours. No celebratory language until Aven approves.
Kestrel filters: inspect lot variance. Publish deferred replacement list. Worker signoff required for any load increase.

CASUALTY LEDGER:
Family notifications. Relational witness rule trial. Missing search update. Unnamed crew memorial dispute. Ledger room setup on both stations.

EVIDENCE:
Blue Vault core preservation. Fork privacy wall. Relief-fund ledger authentication. Cargo insurance clause extraction. Ceyr objection tracking. Cassian Vale warrant distribution. Compact doctrine inquiry supplement.

MAPS:
Blister Nine physical audit. West arc coolant dependency. Old cargo sink. Nursery cold bath emergency use. Dock North-Four capture web. Morrow/Ashkey sensor mast recalibration. All obsolete labels challenged by photograph.

GATE MODEL:
Passive only. No drone. No pulse. No warm test. No simulated certification language. Public explanation draft required before technical closure.

GOVERNANCE:
Public Route Jury seated. Charter petition intake. Sleep rotation enforcement. External preservation arrival plan. Halcyon/Kestrel separate consent procedure.

The last category made Sivek pause.

ECONOMY.

No one had wanted to write it. Everyone knew they had to.

Repair required parts. Parts required contracts. Contracts required money, credit, labor, and the kind of legal clarity people with ships could show to insurers who were not currently being named on public boards. House Ceyr had made its threat plainly: without them, Kestrel would not be rebuilt. The new schedule had to answer without pretending answer and funding were the same thing.

Sivek typed:

ECONOMIC RECOVERY:
Emergency supply accounting. Public procurement board. Coercive insurance disclosure. Non-Ceyr repair bids. Compact relief petition. Halcyon labor credit protection. Kestrel worker hazard compensation. Cargo crew benefit escrow.

Tamsa stepped closer. "Benefit escrow?"

"Cargo Two testimony. If family benefits can be used to coerce command, they need protected escrow during review."

Oret said, "Can you do that?"

"Not alone."

Om said, "Provisional charter can recommend. Compact preservation can support. Halcyon and Kestrel can refuse to certify future cargo under contracts without escrow."

"Write that," Tamsa said.

Sivek did.

Vela whistled softly. "That will make insurers chew walls."

"Good," Oret said.

Maro added, "It also gives captains a clean reason to reject coercive clauses before launch."

Dax joined from Halcyon, still in the same repaired uniform, though someone had made her eat since last shift. "Cargo crews will back it."

Ren Hal appeared beside her, looking thinner than before. "Cargo Two crew will testify first."

No one congratulated him. No one forgave him. They made a slot.

The schedule wall began to look less empty and more dangerous.

That was how Sivek knew it was close.

He had built schedules his whole adult life. He had thought a good schedule reduced danger by putting tasks in order. This one did something else. It displayed danger in the order people had to meet it. It refused to smooth. It refused to turn uncertainty into a date. It placed life-support above transit, casualty names beside work, and evidence status beside technical models. It kept saying no where old walls would have said pending.

A public comment arrived from someone on Kestrel Deck Twelve:

WHEN DOES THE GATE OPEN?

The question replicated across the channel in more polite, angry, frightened, and practical forms. It was the question under all the work. Halcyon had supplies for now, not forever. Kestrel had a route remnant, not a route. The Ash Belt and Near Reach had families separated by a bent promise. Every schedule that did not answer when sounded like evasion to someone waiting.

Sivek looked at the wall.

His old hands wanted to make a window. Not a false one. A careful one. A phrase like earliest possible survey pulse in nine days. A range. A hope with caveats. The kind of answer that would calm markets, reassure families, and become a knife the moment anyone needed to cut through caution.

He did not type it.

Lena watched him.

Tamsa watched him.

Om watched him.

Even Vela stopped muttering.

Sivek opened the public response field.

Kestrel Gate does not have a reopening date.

He stopped. The sentence looked brutal.

Then he added:

This schedule publishes the work required before any responsible date can exist. The first possible dates will be for survey, not transit. Each date will name its uncertainties, evidence status, signoff requirements, and cancellation conditions. A date without cancellation conditions is not a promise. It is a pressure tactic.

He sent it.

The public board held the response. Some people approved. Some cursed. Some asked whether no date meant no hope. A Halcyon resident wrote:

HOPE WITH A FAKE DATE ALMOST KILLED US.

The comment spread faster than the official response.

Tamsa said, "Put it in findings."

Om looked tired enough to become law by osmosis. "Not every good sentence belongs in findings."

"Coward."

"Frequently."

But Om copied it into the public schedule rationale under citizen comment.

The schedule still needed time blocks. Work without times became fog. Sivek began assigning cycles, and this was where the old skill helped without ruling. He knew crew fatigue limits, tug maintenance intervals, evidence review bottlenecks, dock shift changes, and how long a legal packet took to reach a relay under damaged conditions. Tamsa knew which maintenance crews could be asked for one more hard shift and which would start making dangerous mistakes. Oret knew when Halcyon dock corridors would clog if med carts and cargo haulers used the same pressure doors. Aven knew membrane rest periods. Lena and Sann knew when Morrow's sensor drift would make another observation pass worthless. Vela knew which automation tasks needed human witness because the old systems were too polite when lying. Maro knew which evidence extractions could occur in parallel and which had to wait for chain order. Om knew which approvals could be provisional and which, if rushed, would poison everything that followed.

For the first time, Sivek built a schedule by not being the center of it.

It was slower.

It was better.

At hour three, Jori sent a message:

MAP AUDIT TRAINING MODULE SCHEDULED WHEN?

Tamsa answered:

AFTER APPRENTICE MEDICAL CLEARANCE.

Jori:

THAT IS NOT A TIME.

Tamsa:

EXACTLY.

Sivek added:

Map audit training module draft: Cycle 3, contingent on medical clearance of participants, worker instructor availability, and review of Blister Nine case. No injured apprentice required for schedule validity.

Jori replied:

RUDE BUT ACCEPTED.

Niko sent:

I am screenshotting this historic surrender.

The exchange remained on the side board until Om moved it to training notes with unnecessary dignity.

By the fourth hour, the schedule had version 0.7 and a spine:

Cycle 0: Stabilize life-support and evidence walls.
Cycle 1: Complete urgent map contradiction audits tied to life safety.
Cycle 2: Publish passive transverse model draft with uncertainty.
Cycle 3: Train first resident witness cohort on maps, source clocks, and cancellation conditions.
Cycle 4: Conduct unmanned cold sensor drift check outside hazard boundary if all prerequisites hold.
Cycle 5: Review whether survey drone proposal may be drafted. Not launched. Drafted.

No transit.

No pulse.

No promise that the gate would behave because people were tired.

Sivek posted Version 0.7 for public objection.

Objections came like weather.

Pilots wanted clearer criteria for advancing from passive survey to cold drone. Halcyon residents wanted membrane recovery to reach eighty-five percent before any gate-facing operation. Kestrel merchants wanted cargo planning windows. Convoy crews wanted benefit escrow before any ship accepted a new manifest. Families wanted memorial timing not to be subordinated to schedule optics. House Ceyr objected to the entire economic section. Compact route authority asked why Kestrel had used the phrase "pressure tactic" in an official schedule. Vela answered that one before Sivek could stop her:

BECAUSE IT IS ACCURATE.

Om made her add:

Technical and legal rationale attached.

Vela attached a devastating paragraph that Sivek privately admired.

One objection was anonymous at first, then unmasked by choice.

Keva Lorn, medical-supervised custody, wrote:

The schedule should include relief reporting deadlines. Scarcity lies began before sabotage. If relief status is not visible, future administrators will learn to hide risk until emergency makes them powerful.

The room went quiet.

Tamsa read it twice.

"She is right," Tamsa said, as if the words tasted bad.

Om added a RELIEF TRANSPARENCY category:

Risk curves, relief applications, receivership triggers, and supply exhaustion forecasts shall be published with uncertainty and responsible signatory. No staged-risk language without visible underlying model.

Sivek looked at Keva's line on the comment board. "Usefulness is not forgiveness."

Tamsa nodded. "But we do not throw away useful because we hate the source."

"No."

"Annoying."

"Yes."

Version 0.8 posted.

By then, Control had filled with people who would not have been allowed near the old schedule wall. A Halcyon nursery lead with a gasket stain on her sleeve. A cargo quartermaster. Niko with paint still under one fingernail. Sola Im with her pencil. Dax. Ren Hal. Pel Emon. A compact preservation deputy on delayed relay, observing without yet claiming authority. Two citizen observers selected by lot, one of whom kept asking devastatingly simple questions.

"What happens if the public board goes down?" she asked.

Vela smiled like a blade. "Excellent."

Redundant boards were added to the schedule.

"Who tells people in sleep rotation?" the observer asked.

Tamsa stopped smiling.

Sleep rotation briefing packets were added.

"If House Ceyr offers parts cheaper than anyone else, who decides whether cheap is a trap?"

No one smiled.

Procurement review criteria were added, including conflict scoring, dependency risk, and public explanation of rejected bids.

Sivek began to understand that public witness did not make expertise weaker. It made assumptions work for a living.

Version 0.9 posted after seven hours.

Medical arrived for Lena at six and stayed because the schedule had become relevant to patient compliance. At seven, the doctor announced that any version not finalized in the next twenty minutes would be reviewed without Lena because she would be unconscious by professional force. This created consensus faster than legal theory had managed.

Version 1.0 posted at the end of the eighth hour.

It was ugly.

It was long.

It had no transit date, no triumphant title, no blue arcs, no ceremony block, and no place where a routeholder could insert ownership by sounding calm. It began with air and ended with cancellation conditions. It linked every work cycle to evidence, casualty, map, life-support, and governance fields. It showed who could say no. It showed who had to explain yes.

Sivek stood before it after everyone else had started arguing about implementation.

Om came to stand beside him.

"You look bereaved," she said.

"I am."

"For the old schedule?"

"For what I thought schedules were."

She considered the wall. "A schedule is a moral document pretending to be a logistical one."

"You could have told me that years ago."

"You would not have believed me."

"No."

On the public board, the first line of Version 1.0 began replicating across Kestrel and Halcyon:

NO GENERAL TRANSIT. PUBLIC WORK CONTINUES.

Sivek expected despair.

There was some.

There was also something else. A mechanic signing up for map audit training because the time was real. A Halcyon parent thanking the schedule for saying nursery air could cancel gate work. A convoy crew member asking how to file coercive contract language. A pilot objecting to a cancellation threshold and explaining why. A family member asking when the quiet ledger room would open. A merchant complaining about cargo uncertainty and being answered by three people who had almost died bringing cargo through.

The schedule did not calm everyone.

It gave them a place to put their fear where it could be answered by work instead of used by power.

Tamsa finished her coffee and grimaced. "This is still terrible."

"The schedule?"

"The coffee. The schedule is irritatingly decent."

Sivek almost smiled.

Lena, being escorted out by medical and Sann, stopped by the wall. She read the first line, the life-support header, the model phases, the cancellation conditions.

"Good," she said.

From her, it felt like a medal and an audit.

"Thank you," Sivek said.

"Do not get sentimental. Version 1.1 will hurt."

"I expect so."

She left.

Sivek stayed until the wall dimmed for cycle change. The old blue arcs were gone. The future no longer looked like a clean road.

It looked like a list of things people had agreed not to hide.

For the first time in his career, he trusted the blank spaces.


Chapter 49: Names on the Vane

The field vane had to be cooled before it could remember anyone.

Vane Three still held heat in its inner ribs, not enough to burn through a suit but enough to make the memorial crew move slowly and the safety officers rude. The vane had been shut down since the sideways fall, isolated from the transverse crescent by three physical breaks, two software locks, and Vela's handwritten sign taped over the access terminal:

IF THIS WAKES WITHOUT WITNESS, I WILL HAUNT THE LIVING.

Om had objected to haunt as imprecise.

Vela had written a footnote:

FIGURATIVE BUT ENTHUSIASTIC.

The sign stayed.

Names on the vane had started as a line in the ending resolution of old pilot custom, then become Sola Im's memorial proposal, then become a charter task, then become a public argument large enough to require three rooms and two rest breaks. Not everyone wanted names on equipment. Some families wanted quiet. Some wanted no association with the machine that had killed their dead. Some wanted names on Halcyon, not Kestrel. Some wanted names only after full investigation, because memorials could become a way of sealing blame too early. Some wanted everything now because waiting felt like abandonment.

So the memorial plan split again, as all honest plans seemed to do.

The vane would not carry every name.

It would carry only names or markers consented for work memorial placement by family, crew household, or relational witness under Om's provisional rule. It would also carry open markers for the unnamed and the missing, not as placeholders to be tidied later but as visible refusals to pretend the ledger was complete.

The first engraving would be temporary.

Not carved into the vane itself. That came after full repair, if families still consented. For now, a heat-rated memory strip would be bolted to the outer maintenance spine beside the manual inspection rail. It could be removed, corrected, expanded, or replaced. Tamsa liked that. Lena liked it more than she admitted. Sivek had argued for permanence until Sola Im asked whether he wanted accuracy or stone.

He chose accuracy.

The memorial crew assembled in Lock Seven.

Tamsa led because maintenance owned the rail work. Lena came because pilot custom owned part of the promise and because she had stopped pretending medical could keep her away from everything. Sann came to make sure Lena obeyed suit limits. Maro came as evidence witness, though he looked uneasy around grief that did not need cross-examination. Om came to certify consent. Sivek came without title beyond schedule officer and carried the bolt kit. Oret joined by remote from Halcyon, where a twin memory strip would be mounted near Dock North-Four. Aven watched from the nursery corridor with the children off camera. Dax came for convoy crews. Ren Hal came for Cargo Two and stood near the back. Ilya came as Blue Vault and Ceyr witness, not because anyone had asked her to but because the heritage rack had been lost and the dead had not.

Jori tried to come.

Tamsa said no.

He argued for sixteen minutes through medical channel, citing apprentice observer status, Blister Nine relevance, and the fact that his new support frame had magnetic boots.

Tamsa let him finish. Then she said, "No."

"Supervisor."

"No."

"I turned the valve."

"Yes. You are not installing the memorial to prove it."

"That is not why."

"Then you can witness from medical."

He glared through the screen. "This is unjust."

"Yes."

The nurse behind him gave Tamsa a grateful look.

Before the lock cycle, Jori sent one request:

Do not let them make the unnamed marker smaller.

Tamsa answered:

I won't.

The suit channel opened. Lock Seven bled down.

Kestrel's exterior waited beyond the hatch, lit by work lamps and the faint crooked glow of the transverse crescent along the west arc. The gate no longer looked like a road. It looked like a question held open by broken metal. Vane Three stretched beneath the crew, a long dark curve ribbed with conduits, radiator scars, and fresh isolation clamps. Far off, Morrow and Ashkey kept watch in their new positions, lower and upper, no longer heroic tugs in a desperate burn but patient instruments of public doubt.

Lena stepped onto the vane first and had to stop.

The last time she had been outside on the field vane, the gate had been warming toward disaster and every second had carried the shape of a decision. Now there was time. That made it worse in a different way. Time allowed detail. Scorch marks. Bolt heads. The repair patch where Ruun Adali's access spine had collapsed. A tiny fleck of red paint from a tool handle caught in a seam. The gap where old labels had been stripped for audit.

Sann's voice entered her helmet, private. "Breathe."

"I am."

"With conviction."

Lena breathed with conviction.

Tamsa moved ahead along the rail, suit gloves precise despite the bandage still visible under the wrist seal. "Memorial strip mount point is six meters forward. Watch the rib. It is still lying about being flat."

Sivek carried the bolt kit like it weighed more than metal.

Maro followed with the strip case clipped to his harness. The case contained the temporary memory strip, consent log, name order, pronunciation file, and open marker text. It also contained amendments from three families received in the last hour. One changed a middle name. One removed a job title. One added a chosen household witness. The third asked that the deceased not be called brave because, in the family's words, "she was angry and doing her job." Om had approved the amendment immediately.

At the mount point, Tamsa checked the rail temperature. "Within range."

Vela, remote through trust-watch, said, "Terminal remains dead. My sign remains legally superior."

Om: "Your sign is not legal."

"Emotionally superior, then."

Sivek opened the bolt kit.

The first problem appeared exactly when dignity would have preferred otherwise.

The mount holes did not align.

Tamsa stared at them.

Sivek said nothing, which was wise.

Lena looked at the rail, then at the strip bracket. "Old vane pattern?"

"New bracket pattern," Tamsa said. "Procurement said universal."

Oret laughed over the Halcyon link. "Universal means wrong in more places."

Tamsa closed her eyes inside her helmet. "I am going to memorialize the procurement office next."

Maro asked, "Can we redrill?"

"Not here. Not with heat in the rib and suit time ticking."

The silence that followed was not large, but it carried all the ways memorials became impossible through small incompetence.

Then Niko's voice entered from the map audit table. "There is an adapter."

Tamsa's head snapped up. "Where?"

"Old cargo sink retrofit kit. Same bolt offset. We tagged two brackets obsolete but active, because apparently that is our civilization."

Tamsa looked toward Kestrel's hull as if she could see through it to wherever Niko stood being useful and irritating.

"Can someone bring one to Lock Seven?"

"Already running."

Om said, "Record delay cause."

Tamsa said, "Record that obsolete saved us again."

Maro recorded both.

They waited on the vane.

Waiting outside a damaged gate was different from waiting in a hearing room. The suit made every breath audible. The stars showed too many places where no one had arrived in time. The crescent glow painted the vane edges with a color Lena's eyes disliked. Below, Kestrel's habitation bands turned slowly, full of people watching through public feed or refusing to watch because grief did not owe participation.

Sivek said, "I should have checked the bracket pattern."

Tamsa replied, "Yes."

He accepted it.

"Procurement should have checked. Maintenance should have checked. The schedule should have included fit verification. Memorial crew should have had a spare adapter. Put all of that in the after-action, not your private guilt drawer."

"I have a private guilt drawer?"

"You have a warehouse."

Lena laughed before she could stop herself. It came out rough over the channel. Even Sivek's mouth moved.

The adapter arrived by courier drone twenty minutes later, escorted by more attention than some heads of state deserved. Tamsa inspected it. Old cargo sink retrofit, cleaned, verified, photographed, source-clocked, blessed by Niko's smug silence.

The holes aligned.

The memorial strip seated against the rail.

Sivek handed bolts to Tamsa one by one. She set them. Lena held the strip steady. Sann watched suit time. Maro verified chain. Om certified consent state. Dax read convoy markers from Halcyon link. Oret supervised the twin strip installation at Dock North-Four. Aven kept the nursery feed open but did not show the children.

When the strip was mounted, no one spoke for a moment.

Then Om said, "Proceed with names and markers."

Maro opened the file.

His voice was not theatrical. That would have been intolerable. He read as if each word had a job and the job mattered.

"Eda Malk. Passenger systems nurse. Family note attached by consent: She sang badly on purpose when people were scared."

The strip lit. EDA MALK appeared in pale letters along the rail.

On Halcyon, the twin strip lit near Dock North-Four.

Maro continued.

"Ruun Adali. Kestrel maintenance technician. Age thirty-three, corrected by family. Killed under Vane Three access spine collapse."

RUUN ADALI.

Tamsa's hand tightened on the rail.

"Varo Chen. Cargo external rack worker. Missing, presumed dead. Search active by family request. Marker consented by sister and crew household."

VARO CHEN / MISSING / SEARCH ACTIVE.

Dax bowed her head.

"Unnamed cargo crew cook, Sao ship. Public name withheld by legal next of kin. Crew household memorial sealed by participant consent. Marker approved under provisional relational witness rule."

The marker appeared:

UNNAMED CREW / SAO SHIP / CREW REMEMBERS.

Jori's voice entered from medical, quiet. "Same size."

Tamsa looked at the letters. Same size. "Same size."

Maro read the rest. Names from Auxiliary Three. Names from Kestrel vane crews. A Halcyon dockworker who had died after the convoy arrived, counted because the emergency had not ended when the ships stopped moving. Two passengers whose families consented only to initials. One maintenance apprentice from another shift whose official role had been misfiled and corrected by coworkers. The list did not include every injured person, every witness, every life bent by the event. Memorials were always partial, which was why they had to admit it.

At the end, Om read the open marker:

FOR THOSE NOT YET NAMED, NOT YET FOUND, OR NOT YET READY FOR PUBLIC MEMORY.

The letters appeared last.

They were the same size.

The suit channel held a silence that no one tried to fill.

On Kestrel, the quiet ledger room opened at the same moment. On Halcyon, the twin room opened beside the nursery corridor, close enough to air to make the connection plain and far enough from sleeping children to let adults break privately if they needed to. The rooms contained no heroic mural. Just names, markers, family notes, crew notes, unanswered questions, and seating.

Maro closed the file.

Om said, "Memorial strip installed under provisional consent. Corrections remain open."

Tamsa added, "Work memorial does not close investigation."

Sivek said, "Recorded."

Lena looked down the curve of Vane Three toward the crooked glow. She had expected the names to make the machine feel accused. Instead they made it feel less alone and more responsible. A gate was a promise expressed as a machine. The old instructor's sentence returned again, unwanted and not entirely wrong. Promises failed when people confused them with guarantees. Promises mattered because they could be broken and remade with witnesses.

She touched two gloved fingers to the rail beside the strip, not on any name.

Sann did the same.

Tamsa did.

Sivek hesitated, then did.

Maro did not at first. Then, carefully, he touched the rail beside the open marker.

Across the Halcyon feed, Oret touched the twin strip with two fingers, then pretended she had only been checking adhesion.

Dax touched Varo Chen's marker.

Ren Hal touched nothing. He stood back, face wet, and let that be true.

The public channel began to populate with gestures. Not applause. Images of hands on bulkheads, tools set down, cups raised in quiet rooms, a child's blue AIR FIRST tag taped beside a family note, a cargo crew gathered around an empty galley station, Kestrel workers standing in front of a corrected map, Halcyon nursery leads pausing between membrane checks. Some people sent no image. Silence was accepted as participation or refusal; no one demanded to know which.

Then House Ceyr filed an objection.

It arrived under formal emergency review, marked urgent, because cruelty often believed itself punctual. The objection claimed that the work memorial placement on Vane Three might prejudice custody proceedings by implying institutional responsibility before adjudication. It requested removal or anonymization of markers tied to cargo insurance, heritage priority, or Blue Vault evidence until liability could be established.

For three seconds, nobody spoke.

Then Vela said, "I will handle this."

Om said, "No."

Tamsa said, "Let me handle this."

Om said, "Also no."

Lena said nothing. Her hand remained on the rail.

Ilya Ceyr joined the channel from the quiet ledger room on Kestrel. She stood before the public display, no crest authority, no family frame, only her own tired face.

"I will answer."

Om considered. "As witness?"

"As witness. As suspended Ceyr. As someone whose family just asked the dead to wait for liability."

The channel opened.

Ilya read the objection in full. She did not summarize. People heard every clean word.

Then she said, "House Ceyr's objection is noted. It asks that names and markers be removed or anonymized because they may affect custody and liability claims. This request illustrates why custody and liability claims cannot govern memory. The memorial strip does not adjudicate responsibility. It records people and open questions. If House Ceyr believes a marker is factually wrong, it may submit correction with evidence. If House Ceyr believes being seen beside the dead is prejudicial, it should examine the conduct that made proximity uncomfortable."

Maro, on the vane, whispered, "Good."

Ilya continued. "I am not authorized to bind House Ceyr. Today that is useful. I cannot withdraw their objection. I can object to it."

Om said, "Witness objection recorded."

Tamsa said, "Put the Ceyr objection in the ledger room."

Ilya looked at her.

"Not hidden," Tamsa said. "Beside the memorial explanation. Let people see what they asked."

Om approved.

House Ceyr's objection became part of the memorial record.

It did not have the effect they intended.

More families consented to markers over the next hour.

Not all. Never all. But enough that Maro had to reopen the file while still on the vane and add two names from previously withheld rows. A cargo crew household submitted the unnamed cook's favorite recipe as sealed memorial material. Varo Chen's sister sent a correction to his marker: he preferred Var, not Varo, except on official documents, and he hated official documents. The strip changed:

VAR CHEN / MISSING / SEARCH ACTIVE.

Sivek watched the correction with an expression Lena could not read.

"What?" she asked privately.

"Schedules do not like corrections after posting."

"Memorials require them."

"So do schedules, apparently."

"Yes."

Suit time ran low. The crew prepared to return.

Before leaving, Tamsa attached one more item to the rail beside the memory strip. Not official. Not permanent. A small blue tag in a child's handwriting:

AIR FIRST.

Om said, "Is that consented?"

Tamsa said, "No idea."

"Supervisor."

"Fine. Temporary non-ledger object, source noted, subject to removal if it becomes a problem."

Om sighed. "Accepted."
